b'<?xml version=\'1.0\' encoding=\'UTF-8\'?><rss version="2.0" xmlns:atom="http://www.w3.org/2005/Atom" xmlns:blogger="http://schemas.google.com/blogger/2008" xmlns:gd="http://schemas.google.com/g/2005" xmlns:georss="http://www.georss.org/georss" xmlns:opensearch="http://a9.com/-/spec/opensearchrss/1.0/" xmlns:thr="http://purl.org/syndication/thread/1.0"><channel><atom:id>tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490</atom:id><lastbuilddate>Fri, 08 Apr 2016 15:19:47 +0000</lastbuilddate><category>Music Reviews</category><category>Life Stories</category><category>Interviews</category><title>The World\'s First Internet Baby</title><description></description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/</link><managingeditor>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</managingeditor><generator>Blogger</generator><opensearch:totalresults>167</opensearch:totalresults><opensearch:startindex>1</opensearch:startindex><opensearch:itemsperpage>100</opensearch:itemsperpage><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1113371815037892122</guid><pubdate>Sat, 12 Mar 2016 02:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2016-03-11T18:15:52.006-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Wayne Shorter "JuJu" (1964)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uIX0lMuVc-M/VuN2CPKjCdI/AAAAAAAABSQ/kHSG_B3EqwUVkmsccfReS3YPPyWMKd1FA/s1600/MI0001670468.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="314" src="https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uIX0lMuVc-M/VuN2CPKjCdI/AAAAAAAABSQ/kHSG_B3EqwUVkmsccfReS3YPPyWMKd1FA/s320/MI0001670468.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;It\'s not surprising that the great saxophonist Wayne Shorter was often slated as being a dutiful student of John Coltrane when he first emerged in the early 60\'s, an assessment that was bolstered by his use of Coltrane\'s rhythm section on his first two LP\'s for the fabled Blue Note label. But as the second of these albums, 1964\'s &lt;i&gt;JuJu&lt;/i&gt;, clearly demonstrates, Shorter was far more than a mere imitator; yes, his and the group\'s playing often recalls the exploratory spirit of where Coltrane was at then, what with Shorter\'s zig-zagging sax lines and Elvin Jones\' rumbling, otherworldly percussion, but his six compositions here are just as challenging, ambitious and surprising as any of Trane\'s stuff.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Most notably, the opening title track is flecked by more than a hint of African music, as Shorter himself acknowledged in the album\'s liner notes, and "House of Jade" has an unmistakable oriental flavor. All six pieces that the formidably strong quartet runs through, from "JuJu" to "Twelve More Bars to Go", are filled with unpredictable twists and turns, and unique melodic phrases. Shorter played with well-established giants like Miles Davis and Coltrane in the 60\'s, and like many others who played with them in their early careers, he no doubt suffered early on when first beginning to present himself as a leading creative force on his own, but if his previous album (1963\'s &lt;i&gt;Night Dreamer&lt;/i&gt; set) didn\'t make the case for him as such, then &lt;i&gt;JuJu&lt;/i&gt; certainly did. Moreover, he would only continue to expand and improve on his own highly unusual skills for composition by leaps and bounds over the next several years, and although it isn\'t as if his previous recordings were inferior, it was &lt;i&gt;JuJu &lt;/i&gt;that undoubtedly set in motion a string of high-quality recordings that would continue until the end of the decade.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2016/03/wayne-shorter-juju-1964.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="https://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uIX0lMuVc-M/VuN2CPKjCdI/AAAAAAAABSQ/kHSG_B3EqwUVkmsccfReS3YPPyWMKd1FA/s72-c/MI0001670468.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1534533159025955683</guid><pubdate>Wed, 03 Feb 2016 07:04:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2016-02-02T23:16:49.633-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pavement "Wowee Zowee" (1995)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-wi3PGhhvk6s/VrGbVuWCVMI/AAAAAAAABPs/Z0CyNX1MbPE/s1600/MI0002036632.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="314" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-wi3PGhhvk6s/VrGbVuWCVMI/AAAAAAAABPs/Z0CyNX1MbPE/s320/MI0002036632.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With wry, hooky singles like "Cut Your Hair", "Gold Soundz" and "Range Life", 1994\'s &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain, Crooked Rain &lt;/i&gt;brought Pavement a small measure of unexpected commercial recognition, matched by the critical appraisal and indie-world popularity that they\'d earned well with two of the best, most distinctive rock records of the 90\'s, or any era really. But despite the increased attention, the group would deftly retain their position as the premier act of an increasingly distinct "indie rock" underground, one that was distancing itself more with each passing year from the now-mainstream, far more palatable "alt-rock" of Stone Temple Pilots (heh heh), Smashing Pumpkins (ha! ha!) or Pearl Jam. And without really having to &lt;i&gt;try&lt;/i&gt;, no less; no matter how much more accessible and conventionally structured the tunes on &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain&lt;/i&gt; were compared to the fractured, lo-fi &lt;i&gt;Slanted and Enchanted&lt;/i&gt;, Pavement were still far too weird and adventuresome to fit comfortably into the post-Nirvana modern-rock landscape.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Remaining on the legendary Matador Records, they followed the effervescently melodic &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain&lt;/i&gt; with a sprawling, disjointed, weary double-album in the form of 1995\'s &lt;i&gt;Wowee Zowee&lt;/i&gt;, which, with little bits of various musical styles sprinkled liberally across eighteen songs, has often been likened to The Beatles\' &lt;i&gt;White Album&lt;/i&gt;. When you get inside of it, though, it\'s not really all that similar to The Beatles\' famously chaotic work. (And not just because it\'s actually a much shorter album; if you have the album on vinyl, it might be the only double-LP in your collection in which the album contained is too long to fit on two sides, but not long enough to spread across four.) Sure, Pavement is expanding their musical range here, opening with the surprisingly vulnerable, introspective singer-songwriterism of "We Dance", which was apparently influenced directly by Britpop contemporaries "The London" Suede , and also channels a bit of the late, great David Bowie as heard on &lt;i&gt;Hunky Dory&lt;/i&gt;; it appropriately features Stephen Malkmus affecting something of a British accent. "Range Life" certainly brought country music, or country-rock to mind, but "Father to a Sister of Thought" actually has a pedal-steel guitar part straight out of The Byrds\' &lt;i&gt;Sweetheart of the Rodeo&lt;/i&gt;. The sublime "Grave Architecture" is flecked by 60\'s pop, the lumbering "Half a Canyon" by both blues-rock &lt;i&gt;and&lt;/i&gt; Krautrock, and the raucous minute-long "Serpentine Pad" sounds like a lost Sex Pistols track rewritten by a pissed-off university student railing against "this corporation attitude!"&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;But unlike &lt;i&gt;The White Album&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Wowee Zowee&lt;/i&gt;\'s songs don\'t shapeshift from one to another, and you get no sense that the group was in disarray during its recording, or that it feels at any time like a compilation of each band member\'s prospective solo efforts. Pavement here sound more like a group that\'s confident enough at this point to start really expanding their musical vocabulary, and even though it features various styles that Pavement had never really played with before, &lt;i&gt;Wowee Zowee&lt;/i&gt; ultimately plays like something of a careening, egg-scrambled mix of their first two albums: reflecting the melodies and exquisite songcraft of &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain&lt;/i&gt; with outstanding numbers like "Rattled By the Rush", "Black Out", "Grounded" and "Motion Suggests", but also marking a return to the willfully obscure, esoteric, experimental proclivities that marked their earliest recordings, with offbeat detours like Scott Kannberg\'s closing "Western Homes" and the funky "Brinx Job". Their third album is certainly not as strong as its two predecessors, but its playful, limitless feel only served to confirm Pavement\'s standing as one of the very best and most surprising rock bands of their time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2016/02/pavement-wowee-zowee-1995.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-wi3PGhhvk6s/VrGbVuWCVMI/AAAAAAAABPs/Z0CyNX1MbPE/s72-c/MI0002036632.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2072266526809437364</guid><pubdate>Tue, 26 Jan 2016 20:07:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2016-01-26T12:07:49.324-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pixies "Trompe le Monde" (1991)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-62kKuzv8dck/VbiD5DY1h2I/AAAAAAAAA3E/vy4aEV090qs/s1600/MI0002447612.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="314" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-62kKuzv8dck/VbiD5DY1h2I/AAAAAAAAA3E/vy4aEV090qs/s320/MI0002447612.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Tensions in the Pixies camp had already reached critical mass before they entered the studio to record what would end up being their last album for over two decades, 1991\'s &lt;i&gt;Trompe le Monde&lt;/i&gt;. In English, this means "fool the world", and that might just be the reason it compelled one writer (namely, Michael Bonner of Lime Lizard) to dub it "one of the best albums you may very well ever hear" and "a strong contender for best album of the 20th century". To each their own, but it\'s quite bizarre praise, on account of how even among diehard Pixies fanatics, their then-swan song was commonly regarded as their weakest album; "was" regarded, because 2014\'s absurdly belabored "comeback" album &lt;i&gt;Indie Cindy&lt;/i&gt;, comprised entirely of material which had already been released over three EP\'s to much critical disdain, must surely take the cake as &lt;i&gt;the &lt;/i&gt;turkey in the group\'s relatively small body of work. But nonetheless, &lt;i&gt;Trompe le Monde&lt;/i&gt; is still most definitely the least-compelling and most inconsistent of their four original studio albums with Kim Deal in the lineup.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;She may have still been in the band and playing her bass like a champ, but just like its predecessor &lt;i&gt;Bossanova&lt;/i&gt;, it contains no songs written by her or lead vocal spots, and close to none of her backing vocals. You\'d hardly believe she was even there with the rest of them. This isn\'t at all the reason why it\'s not as impressive as the three previous records, or even the debut EP &lt;i&gt;Come on Pilgrim&lt;/i&gt;, especially since by this time Kim was already preoccupied as the dominant singer and songwriter in her own band (The Breeders), but it did lead yet another writer to quip that this gives it the feel of being Black Francis\' first solo album rather than a band effort. His fascination with science fiction takes center stage in the songs\' lyrics, as opposed to the lurid sexual and religious elements of earlier albums. In addition, after the surfy, spacey sound of &lt;i&gt;Bossanova&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Trompe &lt;/i&gt;is by comparison a harder-rocking, more straightforward affair, a shift which was apparently motivated by the presence of Ozzy Osbourne recording in a different part of the studio they were working in at the same time. This does not, however, signal a whole-hearted return to the sheet-metal abrasion of &lt;i&gt;Surfer Rosa&lt;/i&gt;, or the short, sharp, lethal tunesmithing of &lt;i&gt;Doolittle&lt;/i&gt;. Although the group are augmented on several songs by the swirling keyboards of former Captain Beefheart sideman and Pere Ubu member Eric Drew Feldman, they often sound like a more conventional "college rock", or even hard-rock band than ever before, even on great songs like the swaggering, nasty "U-Mass".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;None of these things are what make &lt;i&gt;Trompe le Monde&lt;/i&gt; a lesser album than its predecessors, nor do they make it an out-and-out &lt;i&gt;bad&lt;/i&gt; album at all, but they are worth noting when considering why it is what it is. Now, it\'s down to the strength of the songs themselves, which (just like &lt;i&gt;Bossanova&lt;/i&gt;, as a matter of fact) is pretty high for the material that makes up its first half, like the raw, abrasive "Planet of Sound"; the quirky single "Alec Eiffel", which was moderately successful on the modern rock chart; an explosive cover of The Jesus and Mary Chain\'s "Head On"; "The Sad Punk", which puts a whole new spin on their famous \'loud/quiet/loud\' dynamics, alternating between the full-bore hardcore punk of its verses and its sweet, melancholic choruses; and "U-Mass", with Francis\' hilarious-yet-acidic, screamed refrain of "It\'s EDUCATIONAAAAL!" Like its predecessor, the quality of its material runs out of steam during its second half, drawing to a close with decidedly unremarkable songs like "Lovely Day", "Motorway to Roswell" and "Navajo Know".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;At the end of the day, it was a pretty good way to go out for the Pixies, wrapping up their initial run after just four LP\'s and before they might\'ve stumbled even worse on a follow-up album at a time when their members (especially Deal and Francis, of course) weren\'t really getting along anymore. The only thing you can really say is a shame about the way the Pixies initially broke up is that they disbanded just before they almost certainly would\'ve been able to bask rightfully in the glow of the alternative rock bonanza of the early-mid 90\'s, which ironically would be kick-started by a song that Kurt Cobain admitted was an attempt to "rip off the Pixies".</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2016/01/pixies-trompe-le-monde-1991.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-62kKuzv8dck/VbiD5DY1h2I/AAAAAAAAA3E/vy4aEV090qs/s72-c/MI0002447612.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5099004870408469066</guid><pubdate>Fri, 08 Jan 2016 00:40:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2016-01-07T16:40:12.193-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Smiths "Meat is Murder" (1985)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-xLGir5eez58/Vo7xYjKetsI/AAAAAAAABOs/dFN_KejK4hE/s1600/MeatMurder.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-xLGir5eez58/Vo7xYjKetsI/AAAAAAAABOs/dFN_KejK4hE/s1600/MeatMurder.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;As unexpected as it was, it didn\'t take long for The Smiths to rise to fame in Britain; though "Hand in Glove", their debut single, made little impact when it was first released in mid-1983, it only took them a now-legendary appearance on Top of the Pops six months later to make the band and their leader Morrissey into the nation\'s newest, most unlikely pop stars. Their eponymous debut album, released just three months after that, climbed all the way to an astonishing No. 2 on the UK album charts, amidst controversies over lyrics that touched on homosexuality, pedophilia and murdered children. After a 1984 that was jam-packed with Peel Sessions and fantastic singles, culminating in the November release of the excellent &lt;i&gt;Hatful of Hollow&lt;/i&gt; comp, it should perhaps be of no surprise that their second proper album would reach No. 1 upon its release in February 1985.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Meat is Murder &lt;/i&gt;boasts a fuller, more polished production than its predecessor, which was criticized in some quarters for John Porter\'s murky, flat rendering, and from the thunderous beginning of "The Headmaster\'s Ritual" it\'s clear that this is an angrier, more indignant album than the first one, starting it off with an acidic condemnation of the rigid hierarchy and corporal punishment that informed life at the school that one Steven Morrissey had the misfortune of attending as a child. Even the delightfully bouncy rockabilly of "Rusholme Ruffians" is occupied with violent hooliganism on the last night of the fair, while also acknowledging that "my faith in love is still devout!" After all, on a night like this, "someone falls in love, and someone\'s beaten up". Morrissey gets political for the first time in "Nowhere Fast", wherein he states: "I\'d like to drop my trousers to the Queen/Every sensible child will know what this means/The poor and the needy are selfish and greedy/On her terms!" "Barbarism Begins at Home" expands on the themes of "Headmaster\'s Ritual" to thrilling effect, over seven minutes of tense disco/funk, with its incessant refrain of "a crack on the head is what you get!"&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This scabrous streak culminates in the closing title track, whose stern anti-meat proselytizing may have been bold coming off of a chart-topping album in 1985, but which is also quite possibly The Smiths\' weakest song. Over a musically uncharacteristic, plodding dirge, and six minutes, Morrissey melodramatically condemns "the flesh you so fancifully fry", "the calf that you carve with a smile" and "the turkey you festively slice" as "MURDER!" It\'s admirable on some level that Morrissey sang about something which was (and still is) obviously very important to him, at a time when PETA was only five years old and just beginning to attract substantial attention, but the song confirms that the base-level "Finger-Pointin\' Song" was not The Smiths\' forte, easily the least inventive (both musically and lyrically speaking) and most tedious song the group recorded in its brief lifespan. It\'s a big part of the reason that &lt;i&gt;Meat is Murder&lt;/i&gt; as an album isn\'t the equal of &lt;i&gt;The Smiths&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Hatful of Hollow&lt;/i&gt; or the follow-up &lt;i&gt;The Queen is Dead,&lt;/i&gt; but there\'s no doubt that it\'s got plenty of worthy moments, including one of their very best ballads in "That Joke Isn\'t Funny Anymore", and the other highlights mentioned earlier.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2016/01/the-smiths-meat-is-murder-1985.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-xLGir5eez58/Vo7xYjKetsI/AAAAAAAABOs/dFN_KejK4hE/s72-c/MeatMurder.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1979760618572443997</guid><pubdate>Sat, 21 Nov 2015 09:40:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-21T01:40:25.047-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>David Bowie "The Man Who Sold the World" (1970)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-COEWQG_EAlc/VlAzdBUpg9I/AAAAAAAABOI/mwJGObykZKA/s1600/MI0003889912.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="316" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-COEWQG_EAlc/VlAzdBUpg9I/AAAAAAAABOI/mwJGObykZKA/s320/MI0003889912.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In the span of about five years, a young, hungry Bowie shifted his musical focus from straight, R&amp;amp;B-derived rock &amp;amp; roll, to mod pop, to lite-entertainment music hall, to hippie folk-rock, and all he had to show for it by the time the sixties ended was essentially a novelty hit in the form of "Space Oddity", although that was certainly an uncommonly dark and existential song to be considered as such. It hinted at his immense potential as a singer/songwriter, but as critics Roy Carr and Charles Shaar Murray once said of his third album, &lt;i&gt;The Man Who Sold the World &lt;/i&gt;is where the story &lt;i&gt;really &lt;/i&gt;starts. The cover art for the album\'s British release, which curiously came several months after its American debut, was a statement unto itself, depicting a very convincingly ladylike Bowie in a dress and sporting long, curly blonde locks. Accordingly, the album\'s sweeping, complex opener, "The Width of a Circle", harbors thinly veiled references to male homosexuality, which had essentially only been decriminalized in the UK a mere three years earlier. It was most certainly not a staggering, singular breakthrough as a song, as good as it is, but the more important thing is that it immediately announces that the former David Jones was definitely onto something, here.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;To this day, &lt;i&gt;The Man Who Sold the World&lt;/i&gt; is still nothing short of a glaring anomaly in Bowie\'s extensive discography, second only perhaps to his Anthony Newley-esque 1967 debut in how surprising it is to many who hear it after becoming accustomed to the stuff that made him a superstar in the 70\'s and 80\'s, which is saying something considering just how unexpected and challenging his work during that time could often be. The predominant sound here is a heavy rock stomp not far from that of Led Zeppelin or Black Sabbath, which is nonetheless offset by Bowie\'s nascent science-fiction themes and Nietzschean bent. For better or for worse (most likely the former, honestly), he would never return to these heavy metal waters, these waters which have him yowling songs like the Zeppelin-esque "She Shook Me Cold" and "Black Country Rock". As convincing as it all is, and respectably as he puts on the Robert Plant or Ozzy Osbourne hat here, one still can\'t help but get the feeling that there was only so far he could go with it, and better on both him and all of us that he saved that particular side of his musical character for this particular album only.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The title track, which is the album\'s obvious showpiece, was probably the single best and most spellbinding song he\'d written (or at least recorded) yet, matched with an ominous performance that radiated dreamlike mystique and intrigue, and as startling as Nirvana\'s brooding, bone-chilling reading was at their performance for MTV\'s Unplugged series over two decades later, it\'s honestly something of a shame that many people only know the song by their cover version. It has to still be one of Bowie\'s finest moments as a singer, performer and songwriter. There\'s also the particularly underrated, haunted lullaby "After All", with Bowie barely rising above a whisper as he sketches out something that resembles a particularly sinister, half-recalled childhood nightmare, joined in the verses by a truly creepy chorus line of "Oh by jingo".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All in all, it\'s no surprise that &lt;i&gt;The Man Who Sold the World&lt;/i&gt; would not be Bowie\'s true breakthrough, creatively or commercially, but it remains an important and slightly cast-aside item in that long list of albums and side-projects he\'s been involved in since its release, and as mentioned before, it is &lt;i&gt;the&lt;/i&gt; crucial turning point in his career, the album that started him on the road to the dizzying heights of Ziggy Stardust and beyond, and it\'s no accident that the ultimate success turned out to be just around the corner.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/david-bowie-man-who-sold-world-1970.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-COEWQG_EAlc/VlAzdBUpg9I/AAAAAAAABOI/mwJGObykZKA/s72-c/MI0003889912.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-201352093962707398</guid><pubdate>Fri, 13 Nov 2015 08:25:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-13T00:25:22.259-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Mott the Hoople "All the Young Dudes" (1972)/"Mott" (1973)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-zX-78i6_27g/VkWMM03cQNI/AAAAAAAABM4/kDBbfJeqdjI/s1600/MI0001533774.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="285" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-zX-78i6_27g/VkWMM03cQNI/AAAAAAAABM4/kDBbfJeqdjI/s320/MI0001533774.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In 1972, David Bowie was not only England\'s biggest new pop star, having revitalized his own once-lagging career with &lt;i&gt;The Rise and Fall of Ziggy Stardust and the Spiders from Mars&lt;/i&gt;, but he also became a notoriously generous benefactor to the success of artists he admired. He lent a seemingly magical touch to Lou Reed with the unlikely breakthrough of &lt;i&gt;Transformer&lt;/i&gt;, Iggy and the Stooges\' epochal swan song &lt;i&gt;Raw Power&lt;/i&gt;, and a little-known, just-disbanded rock &amp;amp; roll outfit called Mott the Hoople. They returned to Great Britain from Sweden in the spring of \'72, having called it a day as a band after putting out several albums that endured a tepid commercial reception, when Bowie got in touch with them, wanting to produce an album for them.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;He offered them his classic "Suffragette City" to record, which they turned down while asking for "Drive-In Saturday" instead, and they wound up recording a tune called "All the Young Dudes". Standing where it does today as a regular of classic rock radio, it can be hard for casual listeners to believe that this song, which Ian Hunter renders so convincingly like a heartfelt ode to young guys sticking together through adversity as they enter adulthood, is actually tied up with the apocalyptic themes of &lt;i&gt;Ziggy Stardust&lt;/i&gt;. "The news" that "the dudes" are carrying in that swelling, moving chorus is the same news that made the newscaster weep in an earlier Bowie song, in other words the news that the planet Earth only has five years left to live. You might remember it? Whether people understood that aspect of the song didn\'t matter; the song when released as a single shot to #3 on the UK Singles Chart, and even went to #40 in a glam-suspicious United States, and became a ready-made anthem for all those bedraggled kids who clung to the early-70\'s glitter &amp;amp; glam rock aesthetic as a way of life, and the Hoople were resurrected.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As for the Bowie-produced album of the same name, the title track inevitably overshadows everything else on it, but it\'s the most consistent full-length that the group had yet put together, save maybe for the hotwired rock &amp;amp; roll of the immediately preceding &lt;i&gt;Brain Capers. &lt;/i&gt;Yes, the rather pedestrian run-through of The Velvet Underground\'s "Sweet Jane" is hardly the best or most striking cover version of one of Lou Reed\'s most widely beloved songs, let alone the most thrilling album opener they could\'ve had for their most successful LP to date. But there\'s also the swampy, cowbell-flecked "Sucker", with its quirky introductory line, "Hi there/Your friendly neighborhood sadist/Wanna take you for a ride", plus the Stonesy "Jerkin\' Crocus" and a fine early version of "Ready for Love", written and sung by guitarist Mick Ralphs before it became a bigger hit with his later group Bad Company. The sorrowful "Sea Diver" represents another fine example of Ian Hunter\'s much-remarked-on sentimental streak, and makes for a more fitting and effective album closer than "Sweet Jane" did as an album opener.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All in all, &lt;i&gt;All the Young Dudes&lt;/i&gt; may not quite be the equal of Bowie\'s own &lt;i&gt;Ziggy Stardust&lt;/i&gt; album, or even Reed\'s &lt;i&gt;Transformer &lt;/i&gt;for that matter, but it\'s still one of the key works of that brief period where theatrical, gender-bending glam rock seized the English pop audience. That\'s only sartorially, though, and via the association with Bowie; Mott were easily the least arty, least avant-garde and least androgynous artists to benefit from Bowie\'s assistance at the time. In that respect, &lt;i&gt;All the Young Dudes&lt;/i&gt; is, musically speaking, best heard as simply one of the finest, most underrated, and most idiosyncratic hard rock records of its time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-50hixXhoUlU/VkWV7zy8WGI/AAAAAAAABNM/Ib9QWsCYpjw/s1600/MI0001675736.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="313" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-50hixXhoUlU/VkWV7zy8WGI/AAAAAAAABNM/Ib9QWsCYpjw/s320/MI0001675736.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;What do you do when you\'re in your early thirties and have finally connected with your last-ditch grab for rock stardom? Well, if you\'re like Ian Hunter, you apparently put together a follow-up album laden with withering sarcasm about the stress of fame and the road, including a devastating ballad written after the night that your band decided to call it quits before unexpectedly finding success. 1973\'s &lt;i&gt;Mott&lt;/i&gt; comes barreling in with the 50\'s throwback "All the Way from Memphis", which apparently makes reference to real-life incident when Mick Ralphs lost his guitar on tour, with a variable, infectious chorus that includes choice words like "as your name gets hot, your heart grows cold" and "you gotta stay young, man, you can never be old!"&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s the perfect lead-off track for an undoubtedly stronger album than its predecessor, even if it lacks something quite as moving and memorable as "All the Young Dudes". It\'s a looser, funnier, more ramshackle album, at least if you\'re talking about songs like "Memphis" or the soaring, jubilant "Honaloochie Boogie", or the zinger-hook chorus of "Drivin\' Sister" or the rumbling shuffle of "Whizz Kid". Elsewhere, you have the exceedingly odd, decidedly slight "Violence", which Hunter starts off by hollering "missing link/poolroom stink/I can\'t talk", mumbling the refrain of "violence, violence/it\'s the only thing that\'ll make you see sense" as a busy violin accentuates the melody. "I\'m a Cadillac", sung by the soon-to-depart Ralphs, is a respectable enough rocker, but then, much like how his "Ready for Love" morphed into the wandering "After Lights" on the previous album, it meanders off into a jazzy, murky instrumental ("El Camino Dolo Roso"), which is a bit of a drag.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This means that like its predecessor, &lt;i&gt;Mott &lt;/i&gt;doesn\'t always live up to its tremendous promise, but it doesn\'t make it anything less than the group\'s finest, most solid album. Hunter expands upon the melancholy of "Sea Diver" with the grand, orchestral lament "Hymn for the Dudes", and then with the even more startling "Ballad of Mott the Hoople (March 26, 1972-Zurich)", the aforementioned song written after what the group thought would be its final gig, just months before hitting it big with Bowie\'s help. He admits that "rock &amp;amp; roll\'s a loser\'s game", but "it mesmerizes, I can\'t explain the reasons for the sights and for the sounds". If that isn\'t one of the most touching, heartfelt and brutally honest statements any songwriter has ever come up with to describe what dedicated rock &amp;amp; rollers will go through to live as musicians, I for one haven\'t heard much else quite like it. Hunter\'s heart-on-the-sleeve masterstroke comes in the form of the closing "I Wish I Was Your Mother", a sad yet sweet song about a man coming to a sobering realization that his relationship is doomed to failure, and that it\'s got a lot to do with his own faults, too.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If &lt;i&gt;Mott&lt;/i&gt; could stand up to some improvement and isn\'t as great an album as it could\'ve been, it\'s still a considerably overlooked gem, appropriate perhaps for the best work of a band which in itself is certainly one of the most widely overlooked and underappreciated in rock &amp;amp; roll history.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/mott-hoople-all-young-dudes-1972mott.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-zX-78i6_27g/VkWMM03cQNI/AAAAAAAABM4/kDBbfJeqdjI/s72-c/MI0001533774.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4561661779532289643</guid><pubdate>Thu, 12 Nov 2015 19:01:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-12T11:04:33.858-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Cheap Trick "Cheap Trick" (1977)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-NMmHSXr2NJE/VkTXCyc7LkI/AAAAAAAABMk/alKAznNFtw8/s1600/MI0001714118.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="316" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-NMmHSXr2NJE/VkTXCyc7LkI/AAAAAAAABMk/alKAznNFtw8/s320/MI0001714118.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;From the time they formed and started relentlessly woodshedding their material in Illinois bars, Cheap Trick was exactly what American hard rock needed at a time (mid-late 70\'s, to be exact) when it was getting ever blander and more predictable. With their mix of roaring guitars and swelling melodies, they certainly weren\'t punk rock as it has come to be defined in the decades since it first emerged, at roughly the same time as these boys from Rockford, but it\'s no surprise that many observers lumped Cheap Trick in with the punk/new wave uprising upon the release of their eponymous, initially low-selling 1977 debut album. It\'s packed with tighter, better songs than, say, &lt;i&gt;Frampton Comes Alive! &lt;/i&gt;or the latest Foghat album, and is coursing with the kind of giddy excitement that a lot of the more commercially successful FM-dwelling rock acts had seemingly forgotten how to generate.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Produced by Jack Douglas, who was then known primarily for his work with Aerosmith, the Tricksters\' first album often rocks harder and feels more aggressive than the gleaming power-pop sound that they would soon become successful with. It\'s not just Douglas\' production, which simply hits harder than the slicker, more melodic sound Tom Werman would give them on their next two albums, the disappointingly emasculated &lt;i&gt;In Color&lt;/i&gt; and the spectacular &lt;i&gt;Heaven Tonight&lt;/i&gt;. The subject matter of the songs is often considerably darker than the later hits, including a song dedicated to mass-murderer Richard Speck ("The Ballad of TV Violence"), another one alluding to pedophilia ("Daddy Should Have Stayed in High School"), and the album\'s only single at the time of its release, the surging "Oh Candy", which is about the suicide of a photographer friend of the band\'s. With the exception of "Candy", these songs are sung with considerable sarcasm and black humor, while not exactly turning them into mere gallows humor either. They had picked up a sense of oddball, deviant humor from cult pop heroes like The Move, and would continue to extrapolate on this most valuable attribute even in bigger, more well-known hits like "Surrender", what with its lyrics making reference to venereal disease, junkie Vietnam vets and parents smoking their kids\' pot.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It goes without saying that the band itself, as well as singer Robin Zander, had gigged so much, from their bar band days through tours with acts like Rush, Kiss and Santana, that the performances on their first album were almost guaranteed to be top-notch, and indeed they are. Rick Nielsen, who\'s also the primary writer and composer of all the group\'s original songs here, was already a fantastic guitarist, versatile and capable of both ripping it up and spinning out sophisticated melodies. Goofball drummer Bun E. Carlos and bassist Tom Peterson hold down a formidable rhythm section, from the Gary Glitter stomp of the opening "ELO Kiddies" and the punky, amphetamine charge of "He\'s a Whore" to the subdued, hypnotic pulse underneath the dreamy, druggy "Mandocello". They would only continue to improve over the next two or three years, but there\'s no doubt that Cheap Trick were strong enough just two years into their existence to cut a fully-formed, near-perfect album as the very first one to hit the racks, if not quite yet the upper echelons of the singles or album charts.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If the band wasn\'t a real part of the true-blue punk revolution, they did end up being an equally influential force of their own, an influence which revealed itself over a considerably lengthy period of time. Most of the fashion-victim hair metal bands which took over rock radio in the mid-late 80\'s owed a huge debt to Cheap Trick, but so did most of the alternative rockers which took their place in the early-mid 90\'s, which is the kind of duality that makes for a truly exceptional and influential band, whose best music has stood the test of time astonishingly well compared to many (if not most) other hard-rock bands that were successful in the late 70\'s.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/cheap-trick-cheap-trick-1977.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-NMmHSXr2NJE/VkTXCyc7LkI/AAAAAAAABMk/alKAznNFtw8/s72-c/MI0001714118.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4226320197169798448</guid><pubdate>Wed, 11 Nov 2015 05:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-10T21:15:09.612-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>T. Rex "The Slider" (1972)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-apf1I4seZH8/VkLJYzSKW_I/AAAAAAAABMQ/00-Tr1Sof28/s1600/MI0003035834.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="291" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-apf1I4seZH8/VkLJYzSKW_I/AAAAAAAABMQ/00-Tr1Sof28/s320/MI0003035834.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior &lt;/i&gt;quickly catapulted Marc Bolan and T. Rex to superstardom in the UK, not to mention a lone US Top Ten hit with "Bang a Gong", so it\'s only fitting that their breakthrough album\'s successor boasts a more polished production and just &lt;i&gt;feels &lt;/i&gt;like the sound of a guy who\'s on top of the world and reveling in every moment of it. It starts right off with one of two selections on &lt;i&gt;The Slider&lt;/i&gt; that shot to #1 on the UK Singles Chart, the irrepressibly cheerful "Metal Guru", which is surely one of the best openers for any rock album of the early 70\'s. The other top-selling single, "Telegram Sam", is even better, a gleefully hedonistic ode to Bolan\'s dope suppliers and various other hangers-on, not to mention Bob Dylan.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As on &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior&lt;/i&gt;, he still gives over a considerable amount of space to melancholy acoustic songs, and once again he sounds just as poignant in these songs as he does confident in the rockers. In "Spaceball Ricochet", he admits "With my Les Paul/I know I\'m small/But I enjoy living anyway", a surprisingly vulnerable sentiment from a guy who was the leader of a band which took England by storm like almost no other since The Beatles (whose Ringo Starr he was now close friends with, and who took the photos that appear on &lt;i&gt;The Slider&lt;/i&gt;\'s LP jacket to boot). Bolan and the band then surge full-bore into the uncommonly heavy, Zeppelin-esque "Buick MacKane" immediately thereafter, which is certainly a neat flip of the switch to pull off with such ease.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Slider&lt;/i&gt; treads upon the same basic territory as its predecessor, only it has even more exceptional album cuts and an even stronger smattering of smash-hit singles. In other words, it may not have any basic sonic elements that were missing from &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior&lt;/i&gt;, but it does everything that album did louder, more joyously and ultimately even better than before. Just as The Beatles, eight years earlier, had hit the peak of their early years with &lt;i&gt;A Hard Day\'s Night&lt;/i&gt;, which radiates profusely and forevermore on wax with the initial glow and giddy thrill of Beatlemania, &lt;i&gt;The Slider&lt;/i&gt; captures the glorious, woefully short-lived height of&amp;nbsp; "T. Rextasy", and you can still hear that excitement in nearly each and every song. T. Rex would never be better than they were here.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/t-rex-slider-1972.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-apf1I4seZH8/VkLJYzSKW_I/AAAAAAAABMQ/00-Tr1Sof28/s72-c/MI0003035834.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-590624038413376932</guid><pubdate>Tue, 10 Nov 2015 20:02:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-10T12:31:12.867-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>T. Rex "Electric Warrior" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-W0rz5qumLks/VkERBH8xR-I/AAAAAAAABL4/Q7bQKDvrZKA/s1600/MI0003282349.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-W0rz5qumLks/VkERBH8xR-I/AAAAAAAABL4/Q7bQKDvrZKA/s320/MI0003282349.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Marc Bolan always believed that one day he would be a star, and so did many people who knew him, and it most certainly did not come to him overnight, but with 1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior&lt;/i&gt;, he got his wish. It\'s just as well that it would be the album that made both him and T. Rex as a band into a sensation, because with &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior &lt;/i&gt;the group arrived at a tremendously influential, distinctive sound that took Great Britain by storm. The album\'s down-and-dirty, hard-rockin\' sound had been glimpsed before, but it wasn\'t until songs like "Mambo Sun", "Jeepster" and of course "Bang a Gong (Get It On)" that it reached perfection.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And "T. Rexstasy" couldn\'t have come at a better time. Just when the serious-minded, denim-and-bearded aesthetic of prog-rock had become the dominant force on British charts and in music weeklies, Bolan came along with a truly outrageous fashion sense and a bevy of infectiously catchy, sexually-charged rock &amp;amp; roll singles, loaded with warm, crunchy guitars, massive hooks and seemingly nonsensical lyrics. It was surely a far cry from the group\'s earlier incarnation as cult heroes Tyrannosaurus Rex, which consisted only of Bolan and percussionist Mickey Finn, and once put out an album entitled &lt;i&gt;My People Were Fair and Had Sky in Their Hair...But Now They\'re Content to Wear Stars on Their Brows&lt;/i&gt;. But even on &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior&lt;/i&gt;, Marc hasn\'t completely abandoned all traces of his hippie, freak-folk past; not with words like "O God/High in your fields above Earth" or "the throne of time is a kingly thing". With all the ease he gets acquainted with the lean, mean rock of the hit singles, "Lean Woman Blues", "The Motivator" and "Rip-Off", he shines just as brightly in melancholy acoustic ballads like "Cosmic Dancer" and "Life\'s a Gas", both of which sound a lot sadder now, especially when a man that had just six more years left to live is singing "Life\'s a gas/I hope it\'s gonna last". In "Dancer", he seemingly writes his own epitaph when he says "I danced myself right out the womb", and then later on "I\'ll dance myself into the tomb", and I\'ll be damned if that\'s not just exactly what he did.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As the sunshine-pop optimism and idealism of the 60\'s faded further into the setting sun of rock &amp;amp; roll at the beginning of the 70\'s, Marc revamped Rex at precisely the right time, penning terrifically sexy pop songs that dripped with the sleaze and hedonism that were now front and center of the new rock culture. It gave inspiration for David Bowie to craft his flamboyant Ziggy Stardust persona, bringing him even greater success than Bolan, and "glam rock" was born, bringing that intoxicating sense of the outrageous that rock &amp;amp; roll had started off with back where it belonged. &lt;i&gt;Electric Warrior &lt;/i&gt;is without a doubt one of the very best, most influential and most emblematic albums of its time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/t-rex-electric-warrior-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-W0rz5qumLks/VkERBH8xR-I/AAAAAAAABL4/Q7bQKDvrZKA/s72-c/MI0003282349.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8623320466430568130</guid><pubdate>Thu, 05 Nov 2015 20:44:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-11-05T12:48:09.832-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bad Brains "Rock for Light" (1983)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dmQCKdqOUIE/VjvAfwlNQYI/AAAAAAAABLU/54FsztJSN58/s1600/MI0000679271.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dmQCKdqOUIE/VjvAfwlNQYI/AAAAAAAABLU/54FsztJSN58/s320/MI0000679271.jpg" width="319" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The Bad Brains\' classic eponymous 1982 debut cemented the fabled D.C. band\'s reputation as one of the very best bands to see live in the American punk underground. It also unexpectedly captured the attention of none other than Cars mastermind Ric Ocasek, who soon approached the band about producing for them. The result of their collaboration, 1983\'s &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light&lt;/i&gt;, would be their first full-length vinyl release at the time, being as the first album was originally released only on cassette tape for ROIR.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Owing both to Ocasek\'s involvement and a lack of the limitations imposed by the previous album\'s recording circumstances, &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light&lt;/i&gt; boasts a much fuller, more ferocious sound than its tinny predecessor. In light of this, it\'s not surprising that the Brains would want to take another shot at a few songs they\'d already done for the first album, so there\'s six songs here ("Attitude", "F.V.K.", "Banned in D.C.", "Sailin\' On", "Right Brigade" and "Big Takeover") that already appeared on the ROIR cassette. To be honest, their presence slightly hampers &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light &lt;/i&gt;as a whole, an obvious side effect of recycling old material, but you\'d have to be the most vehement adherent of lo-fi recording to think that the improved sound does anything but good to them. It\'s also a plus that all six of them are from the fast &amp;amp; furious side of their musical character, because the louder, fuller sound makes them sound even more vicious than before, taken along with how H.R. is even more unhinged as a vocalist and the band is playing even faster.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The same goes for the other hard &amp;amp; fast numbers, like "Coptic Times", "How Low Can a Punk Get?", the title track and "At the Movies", but that\'s not the only facet of the Brains\' immense talents that is nourished here; "Rally Around Jah Throne", "The Meek Shall Inherit the Earth" and especially "I and I Survive" are even better as reggae selections than the ones that appeared on the previous album. Ocasek makes them all sound sweeter, and H.R.\'s voice more soulful than the earlier recordings. &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light&lt;/i&gt; would be the Bad Brains\' last recording for several years, as they broke up for the first of several times to follow shortly after its release and would remain inactive until they re-emerged with the acclaimed &lt;i&gt;I Against I &lt;/i&gt;in 1986. By that time, the early-80\'s American hardcore scene that they\'d helped foster was essentially a thing of the past. Because of this, even though &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light &lt;/i&gt;isn\'t quite the equal of the ROIR cassette, it\'s still a valuable piece of their work, the last gasp of the Brains as they were in their early years, the band that would go from the most furious hardcore punk to the smoothest reggae on the drop of a dime.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/11/bad-brains-rock-for-light-1983.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dmQCKdqOUIE/VjvAfwlNQYI/AAAAAAAABLU/54FsztJSN58/s72-c/MI0000679271.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5318231782337119384</guid><pubdate>Sat, 31 Oct 2015 01:23:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-10-30T18:23:34.683-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Syd Barrett "The Madcap Laughs" (1970)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-MYMvXx40w_4/VjQJDAm4FCI/AAAAAAAABKY/2VNgVbN7S4A/s1600/MI0001389173.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="311" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-MYMvXx40w_4/VjQJDAm4FCI/AAAAAAAABKY/2VNgVbN7S4A/s320/MI0001389173.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;It must\'ve been quite a bizarre thing for early fans of the burgeoning Pink Floyd to see the group\'s leader, Syd Barrett, leave the fold just months after the release of their 1967 debut album, &lt;i&gt;The Piper at the Gates of Dawn&lt;/i&gt;. Like the later disintegration of American psychedelic contemporaries Skip Spence and Roky Erickson, Barrett\'s mental state seemed to deteriorate at an extraordinary rate; by all accounts, by the time the Floyd began recording &lt;i&gt;Piper&lt;/i&gt;, Syd already seemed totally disconnected from what was going on around him, having become a regular user of LSD at the beginning of that year. In their regular live performances at the UFO Club, he\'d gotten into the habit of standing onstage, motionless with his Telecaster hanging round his neck, and when they went to America for their first appearances both onstage and on television there, he flummoxed in equal measure audiences at venues like the Fillmore in San Francisco, and TV hosts Dick Clark and Pat Boone, to whom he was totally unresponsive when it came to the standard pop-show questionnaire. The jaunt went so poorly that management aborted the tour, and had them flown back to England within a month. Another aborted tour of the UK with Jimi Hendrix followed at the end of the year, at which point the far more stable David Gilmour joined the group, and by January 1968, the near-impossible-to-work-with Barrett was officially gone from the Floyd as a recording and performing unit.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;He was far from finished as a singer/songwriter in his own right, however, as evidenced by the other four members\' initial resolve to keep him in the band as a songwriter and creative force, as opposed to a performer, as The Beach Boys had done when Brian Wilson could no longer handle their touring schedule. Although this quickly proved not to be a viable option, Gilmour and Floyd co-founder Roger Waters did serve as the producers for some of the sessions for what turned out to be &lt;i&gt;The Madcap Laughs&lt;/i&gt;, released at the beginning of 1970 as the first of only two proper solo albums Barrett would ever record. The album\'s genesis was remarkably convoluted, with a wildly erratic series of sessions that spanned a fifteen-month period from May 1968 to August of \'69, featuring production-wise input from Floyd manager Peter Jenner and Malcolm Jones prior to Waters and Gilmour coming in towards the end of this period of time. The final product is, appropriately, a wildly erratic collection of songs which range from Barrett alone with his guitar, howling the desolate "Dark Globe" with harrowing desperation, to him and a passel of overdubbed accompanists (including the whole of the Soft Machine\'s then-current lineup, on two tracks) skipping through whimsical pop songs like "Love You" and "Here I Go". It goes without saying that &lt;i&gt;Madcap &lt;/i&gt;is all over the map, which is certainly a fitting reflection of Barrett\'s completely disheveled state, but does not result in a completely consistent album. Despite the presence of positively bewitching songs like "Octopus", "Golden Hair", "Dark Globe" and "Terrapin", it gets rather tediously incoherent towards the end with the loosely grouped "suite" of "She Took a Long Cold Look", "Feel" and "If It\'s In You", united only by the false starts and studio banter going on between them, and for these three songs being without a doubt the most unfocused, and unfinished, takes that made it to the final LP.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Make no mistake that &lt;i&gt;The Madcap Laughs&lt;/i&gt; is certainly not as strong as &lt;i&gt;The Piper at the Gates of Dawn&lt;/i&gt;, but as one of only two proper albums Barrett would put out after his woefully brief tenure with the Pink Floyd came to a fractious end, it\'s a valuable document of the man who has been called the greatest cult figure in rock history, without many arguments to the contrary. Syd was an undeniably talented and unique artist whose career was thoroughly derailed just when it was starting to flourish, and then the man just gave it all up and retired to a completely anonymous life until he died at 60, and consequently there\'s not much of his work that ultimately made it to tape. With that in mind, anything you can hear from his small body of work is worth listening to, and &lt;i&gt;Madcap&lt;/i&gt; remains a thoroughly compelling, fascinating product of a mind that went through a kind of tumult that most of us will never really be able to understand.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/10/syd-barrett-madcap-laughs-1970.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-MYMvXx40w_4/VjQJDAm4FCI/AAAAAAAABKY/2VNgVbN7S4A/s72-c/MI0001389173.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2288160706829371703</guid><pubdate>Sat, 24 Oct 2015 02:41:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-10-23T21:16:21.837-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Silver Apples "Silver Apples" (1968)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-sTVAwHH_cY0/VirwYCeit5I/AAAAAAAABJY/HLnW83q5WcA/s1600/MI0002785351.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="310" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-sTVAwHH_cY0/VirwYCeit5I/AAAAAAAABJY/HLnW83q5WcA/s320/MI0002785351.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Like contemporary releases by The Velvet Underground, The Stooges and Captain Beefheart, the Silver Apples\' eponymous first album was one of those late-60\'s oddities which went largely ignored in its day, but later found recognition as a touchstone for much of what was to come, in terms of cutting edge pop and rock music, after the fire of The Beatles and the acid generation went out. In an age where the vast majority of mainstream pop is almost entirely electronic, and record-making is almost exclusively done with digital equipment, &lt;i&gt;Silver Apples&lt;/i&gt; feels especially prescient, not really presaging the future of rock as The Velvets and Stooges did, but pointing the way to a future where the electric guitar would no longer be the predominant instrument in pop music.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s funny, then, that the Apples had their genesis in a forgotten New York rock &amp;amp; roll band, the Overland Stage Electric Band. Their lead vocalist, Simeon Coxe, started fiddling around with a few oscillators during breaks between songs in the group\'s live performances, and soon became deeply fascinated with the sounds they would make. Drummer Danny Taylor started digging what Simeon was getting up to, and before long the other three members of the group dropped out of the picture, leaving the twosome that introduced, among their other innovations, the duo format that would come to permeate synthpop and much electronic-based music in general, from their fellow New Yorkers Suicide to any number of British acts that came to commercial prominence in the early 80\'s (Soft Cell, Yaz, Bronski Beat, etc.) Simeon, an enigmatic figure that normally went under just his first name, assembled a formidable noise-making machine of nine oscillators with eighty-six manual controls, dubbing it The Simeon, while Taylor\'s tribal percussion drove their songs as much as The Simeon\'s blips and burbles. Simeon\'s own singing voice, sometimes in harmony with Taylor, was emblematic of 60\'s folk rock, much like their quintessentially Age of Aquarius lyrics, many of which on their first album were actually composed by a poet friend of theirs named Stanley Warren, but the music behind those words and that voice was anything but. Electronic instruments had already been worming their way into pop music for some time, like the theremin heard in The Beach Boys\' "Good Vibrations" or the Moog synth in Simon &amp;amp; Garfunkel\'s "Save the Life of My Child", but the Silver Apples were among the very first artists for which electronic sounds were the basis of their material.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And the songs here are terrific, memorable ones, too; the opening "Oscillations", which was apparently (and fittingly) the first song that Simeon ever wrote, immediately settles into the hypnotic pulse that runs through all of the remaining eight songs, in which Simeon intones in his fragile wisp of a voice that "my only world is sound". As cosmically blissed-out as the lyrics come across, for better or worse, it\'s a spooky, haunting sound going on behind them, especially in the downright ominous "Lovefingers", "Velvet Cave" and "Dancing Gods", which directly predict the otherworldly drone of Krautrockers like Can, Neu! and Faust.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It took many years for a large enough amount of people to understand how groundbreaking &lt;i&gt;Silver Apples &lt;/i&gt;really was, about 25 years after the duo released their second album (1969\'s &lt;i&gt;Contact&lt;/i&gt;) and then promptly disappeared, at which point in the mid-90\'s Simeon decided to revive the name with a new partner to begin performing again. Now, with close to fifty years\' hindsight, the Silver Apples\' first album looks like one of the most audacious and hugely influential albums of the decade, a record which still sounds fresh and mysterious all these years later.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/10/silver-apples.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-sTVAwHH_cY0/VirwYCeit5I/AAAAAAAABJY/HLnW83q5WcA/s72-c/MI0002785351.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4544037516614793446</guid><pubdate>Tue, 06 Oct 2015 02:46:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-10-05T19:46:10.850-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "Bringing It All Back Home" (1965)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LBhAtln3eNs/VhMrszXMdUI/AAAAAAAABIY/KEBRxndepQ8/s1600/MI0002424512.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="284" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LBhAtln3eNs/VhMrszXMdUI/AAAAAAAABIY/KEBRxndepQ8/s320/MI0002424512.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Dylan began 1964 as America\'s most notorious folk troubadour and protest singer, as represented by &lt;i&gt;The Times They Are A-Changin\'&lt;/i&gt;, and ended the year with a considerable backlash against him slowly brewing in the folk music community that had nurtured him and brought him the recognition that he had at that point. &lt;i&gt;Another Side of Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt; was just about totally devoid of the sort of solemn moral messages that most people seemed to associate with his name, prompting Sing Out! magazine editor Irwin Sibler to chastise Dylan for getting caught up in "the paraphernalia of fame" and "losing touch with the people". Nobody who was disappointed with his change of lyrical direction could\'ve predicted the outrage they would then feel when &lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home&lt;/i&gt;, released in January 1965, led off with seven songs recorded with a full, electric band behind him, the first of them being the sarcasm and absurdity-laced, rough &amp;amp; ready blues-rock of "Subterranean Homesick Blues". It sounded like no other folk, blues or rock &amp;amp; roll song that had come before it, and was the perfect opening song for the album on which Dylan completely turned his back on the trappings of folk music\'s traditions and timeworn subject matter. And though it was the best album he\'d made up to that point, quite a few people were positively pissed.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s quite amusing to look back fifty years later on the sincere moral outrage that so many of Dylan\'s fans and peers expressed at the time about his decision to boldly incorporate elements of hard-driving rock &amp;amp; roll and electric blues into his songs, but the fact of the matter is that a lot of those people thought he\'d simply sold out to the commercial interests of the time. Before Dylan did what he did then, folk music (along with jazz, classical and the blues) was viewed as "serious music" in the eyes of \'sophisticated\' music aficionados, and rock &amp;amp; roll was viewed as mindless, disposable teenage noise. Folk in particular was associated with a kind of left-liberal moral purity, and socially conscious idealism, which made it something of a sacred cow in the eyes of many of its contemporary followers and performers. The fact that pop and rock &amp;amp; roll music had suddenly received a huge shot in the arm mattered little; to them, the British Invasion spearheaded by The Beatles and The Stones was just a newer manifestation of an inherently juvenile, passing trend, what with all those woefully delusional teen girls who screamed their heads off at their concerts. With the entirely electric first half of &lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home&lt;/i&gt; as the starting point, Bob Dylan in 1965 did more than any other performer or songwriter to completely, and irreversibly, alter this perception. It\'s not only the watershed and the turning point of Dylan\'s career, but also one of a handful of truly epochal, game-changing albums in the history of popular music.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although the electric half inevitably received more attention at the time, the second, entirely acoustic one is just as important; "Mr. Tambourine Man" followed the lead of some of the more mystical songs on &lt;i&gt;Another Side&lt;/i&gt;, a gorgeously moving number that feels personal and introspective in a lot of places, like a reflection on the demands of being a popular performer, but also on the other hand like a surreal daydream, brimming with abstract imagery. It would also lead to the advent of "folk-rock" as the biggest new trend in pop music, when a new band from L.A. called The Byrds got ahold of an early acetate of the song and transformed it into a Beatlesque pop hit, aided by a chiming 12-string guitar and the group\'s marvelous harmony singing. "Gates of Eden" and "It\'s Alright, Ma (I\'m Only Bleeding)" are even more cryptic, loaded with striking turns of phrase like "the relationships of ownership", "money doesn\'t talk, it swears", "sometimes even the president of the United States has to stand naked". The album comes to a close with the failed romance of "It\'s All Over Now, Baby Blue", but it\'s the elliptical, seemingly indecipherable imagery of the earlier three songs which Dylan would only continue to pursue on his following two albums.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home&lt;/i&gt; is a startling piece of work, the bold, brash introduction of a very new kind of artist in the pop music arena, but in hindsight many view it as just the prelude to the even better &lt;i&gt;Highway 61 Revisited&lt;/i&gt;, released later in \'65. It\'s a testament to just how on top of his game Dylan was in the mid-60\'s to think that an album as masterful as &lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home&lt;/i&gt; was just a warm-up exercise.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/10/bob-dylan-bringing-it-all-back-home-1965.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LBhAtln3eNs/VhMrszXMdUI/AAAAAAAABIY/KEBRxndepQ8/s72-c/MI0002424512.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-549221413677590720</guid><pubdate>Fri, 02 Oct 2015 01:30:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-10-03T20:53:26.313-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "The Times They Are A-Changin\'" (1964)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lLkngY5MfMk/Vg3SHk2B-EI/AAAAAAAABIA/LZ2p68DSc-g/s1600/MI0002208215.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="317" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lLkngY5MfMk/Vg3SHk2B-EI/AAAAAAAABIA/LZ2p68DSc-g/s320/MI0002208215.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Freewheelin\' Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt; introduced the former Bobby Zimmerman to the world as not only the biggest new star of the American folk revival of the early 60\'s, but also as one of the leading figures of the burgeoning left-wing political activism that swept college campuses of the time and would continue to flower as the decade progressed. &lt;i&gt;Freewheelin\'&lt;/i&gt; was a richly diverse effort that ranged from the socially conscious leanings of "Blowin\' in the Wind", "Masters of War" and "A Hard Rain\'s a-Gonna Fall" to the tender love songs like "Girl from the North Country" and "Corinna, Corinna", with plenty of absurdist humor sprinkled along the way. Nonetheless, it was the socially conscious, leftist side that the public at large seemed to fixate on at the time; in the minds of many, Dylan was definitively a "protest" folk singer.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Perhaps with this in mind, perhaps even in an uncharacteristic attempt to "give the people what they wanted", 1964\'s &lt;i&gt;Times They Are A-Changin\'&lt;/i&gt; was almost entirely dominated by political material, right from the opening title track, which is every bit the standard that "Blowin\' in the Wind" is today, and just as good a song. If there were ever just one near-supernaturally prescient song in popular music history, here it is; Dylan himself stopped performing it for a number of years after he made his move to electric rock accompaniment, just a year after its release as the leadoff track on the album, but it truly took on a life of its own, and would be recorded by a veritable laundry list of diverse artists in the mid-to-late 60\'s, an early signpost for a tremendously turbulent era. The times really &lt;i&gt;were&lt;/i&gt; a-changin\', and more than they had in America for a very long time, and five decades later America is still grappling with the ramifications of those changes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There\'s also the fantastically pointed "Only a Pawn in Their Game", which was perhaps the most thematically advanced political song he\'d yet come up with, describing how poor and/or uneducated white people (like the white man who\'d just recently assassinated civil rights leader Medgar Evers) are, in their own way, victimized by America\'s racially prejudiced socioeconomic system and its crooked racist politicians just as much as "negro" Americans are. "With God on Our Side" is especially potent, a lengthy examination of American history and its wars, from when white Europeans killed and displaced Native Americans once upon a time, all the way up to the then-current Cold War, questioning all of the commonly-held preconceptions of all these things. "The Lonesome Death of Hattie Carroll" isn\'t as bluntly condemnatory as the similarly-themed, then-unreleased "Death of Emmett Till", but it\'s a brutally incisive account of how in 1963 a wealthy, well-connected heir of a tobacco farming family (Billy Zantzinger) murdered an African-American barmaid (Hattie Carroll) and got off with no more than six months in the lockup. In a year in which debates about sentencing disparities, police brutality, and racism in general in America have become more heated than they have been in a long time, it remains quite bitterly relevant.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As strong as this material is, the album as a whole falls short of the standard set by its stunning predecessor, much of which is owed to how much less diverse it is both thematically and musically; it features a lot of the same ancient folk melodies and song structures being repeated more than once, and of course for many the dour, moralizing tone of much of Dylan\'s material this time around can be a bit tedious. Two songs, however, stand apart from the predominant theme, those two being the absolutely gorgeous, heartbroken "Boots of Spanish Leather" and the equally delicate "One Too Many Mornings". As it would turn out later, he essentially crafted this particular record on purpose as a protest-focused album, leaving several worthy, not-so-political songs that were cut during the sessions off of the final set. Maybe he needed to get all of his social-justice warrior (to use a most likely soon-to-be-dated, rather dumbass term) ambitions out of his system with a single LP, an idea bolstered by the fact that it\'s well-established that he was already growing tired of so-called "finger-pointin\' songs" by the time the album was released in January of 1964. The following &lt;i&gt;Another Side of Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt;, with lyrics inspired by French symbolism and Beat poetry and with his sense of humor fully restored, would be recorded just five months after that, setting the stage for his rediscovery of the kind of white-hot rock &amp;amp; roll he had loved as a teenager with 1965\'s &lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Highway 61 Revisited&lt;/i&gt;. None of this, however, should be taken to mean that &lt;i&gt;The Times They Are A-Changin\'&lt;/i&gt; is not a worthy or important item in his discography; in fact, it should be looked towards as a pioneering, early example of a concept album, being as Dylan quite clearly engineered it to have the recurring political themes that it has.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If you have any appreciation for Dylan\'s not-unimportant role as, for a time, a well-known white ally of the Civil Rights movement of the 60\'s, and by extension as an unwittingly galvanizing figure of the other social &amp;amp; political upheavals that would occur over the rest of the decade, his third album captures this side of him better than anything else he would ever record again.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/10/bob-dylan-times-they-are-changin-1964.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lLkngY5MfMk/Vg3SHk2B-EI/AAAAAAAABIA/LZ2p68DSc-g/s72-c/MI0002208215.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2931599933361642840</guid><pubdate>Thu, 24 Sep 2015 19:54:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-24T12:54:18.517-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Curtis Mayfield "Super Fly" (1972)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-_89WHA7_y_Y/VgRKh00prBI/AAAAAAAABHE/099AC2VSbiw/s1600/MI0002323191.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="311" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-_89WHA7_y_Y/VgRKh00prBI/AAAAAAAABHE/099AC2VSbiw/s320/MI0002323191.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Curtis Mayfield\'s soundtrack for the 1972 blaxploitation flick "Super Fly" is one of those soundtracks that stands apart from the film itself as a superb work of art on its own. The Beatles\' nominal soundtracks for &lt;i&gt;Help! &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;A Hard Day\'s Night&lt;/i&gt; are two well-known examples, of course, not to forget the one for &lt;i&gt;Saturday Night Fever. Super Fly &lt;/i&gt;is particularly extraordinary for how it completely transcends the film it was meant to accompany; commercially, the LP and its two singles ("Freddie\'s Dead" and the title track) outgrossed the film itself, but more importantly, Mayfield was at the peak of his powers as a keenly socially-conscious songwriter, taking the film\'s subject matter and spinning it into a tour de force, a song cycle about the players, pimps and dealers portrayed in the film.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;His songs decry the problems befalling black America in the early 70\'s, but are rich with pity for pushers like the one in "Pusherman" ("a victim of ghetto romance", who\'s "been told I can\'t be nothin\' else/Just a hustler in spite of myself") or "Eddie You Should Have Known Better", and convey his righteous anger towards the apathy that surrounds the fall of the hopeless junkie in "Freddie\'s Dead". He\'s joyously happy that life is his natural high, and that the man can\'t put "No Thing On Me", but he doesn\'t sound arrogant. The title track encapsulates all the glamour surrounding the lead character Youngblood Priest\'s life of crime, but also his desire to get out from the drama and the danger that come with it, and because of that, to this day "Super Fly" is still one of the greatest songs anybody\'s ever written about ghetto crime or gangsters, even after a quarter century of gangsta rappers recycling some of the same themes. From beginning to end, Curtis has his eye dead-locked onto the mean streets of the ghetto, and he simply tells it like it really is, without sensationalism or excessive moralizing.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Of course, he would have gotten nowhere with his magnificent words if he didn\'t have equally spectacular musical backing, and with its horns, wah-wah-drenched guitar, bongos and percolating bass lines, &lt;i&gt;Super Fly&lt;/i&gt; is not only one of the greatest touchstones of the sound of early 70\'s funk and R&amp;amp;B, but also a seminal influence on future TV and movie soundtrack producers. It established a subgenre unto itself for blaxploitation soundtracks, and would ultimately stand as the apotheosis of this loose category. As a matter of fact, it would represent the highest peak of Curtis Mayfield\'s extraordinary talents, the culmination of over a decade spent as one of the relatively few 60\'s soul singers to write most of his own material, progressing over the back half of that decade from the moving civil rights anthem "People Get Ready" to the more open-ended questions of "Choice of Colors", then hitting a stride when he split from The Impressions with the apocalyptic, chaotic funk of "(Don\'t Worry) If There\'s a Hell Below, We\'re All Gonna Go" and 1970\'s &lt;i&gt;Curtis&lt;/i&gt;
