album. &lt;i&gt;Super Fly&lt;/i&gt; is nothing short of his masterpiece, a summation of the immense musicality and excellent songwriting that would never again be quite as wonderfully on display as it was here.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/curtis-mayfield-super-fly-1972.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-_89WHA7_y_Y/VgRKh00prBI/AAAAAAAABHE/099AC2VSbiw/s72-c/MI0002323191.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-932860949429490845</guid><pubdate>Wed, 23 Sep 2015 15:59:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-23T08:59:13.897-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bruce Springsteen "Nebraska" (1982)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Ek-xhm2tRiM/VgKtqaYfQ7I/AAAAAAAABGw/MBHTahyvbz8/s1600/Bruce_Springsteen_-_Nebraska.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Ek-xhm2tRiM/VgKtqaYfQ7I/AAAAAAAABGw/MBHTahyvbz8/s1600/Bruce_Springsteen_-_Nebraska.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With a few decades\' hindsight, when you think about it, Bruce Springsteen putting out something as virtually unadorned and resolutely grim as 1982\'s &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt; on Columbia Records was one of the most audacious, almost shocking moves ever made by an artist of his commercial stature. \'The Boss\' was coming off of the huge success of his 1980 double-album &lt;i&gt;The River&lt;/i&gt;, which would go on to be his third-best selling album and was supported by one of his most rapturously received concert tours. In the first days of 1982, he recorded what were intended to be demos for his next album at his home, on a Teac four-track machine, and eventually entered the studio with The E Street Band to make a \'proper\' album. But Springsteen and the engineers seemed to unanimously agree that his demo recordings actually sounded better for this particular set of songs than the full-band takes. Listening to the ten songs that make up &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt;, it really comes as no surprise that the E Street band version wouldn\'t have  worked quite so well for many of these songs, being as most of them, in  terms of their melodies and their structures, are rooted in traditional  country and American folk music, and therefore sound as though they were  meant to be performed alone anyway. There does exist an as-yet unreleased, complete alternate version of the album, recorded in the studio with the band, but it\'s Springsteen\'s home demos which became the final, released version of the album, and of course it sounds nothing like anything he had done before, or for that matter like anything any of his closest commercial contemporaries released in 1982.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The raw, lo-fi audio quality of the album was startling enough, but the real key to &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt; is just how grim it really is in terms of Springsteen\'s songs, which had been getting progressively more pessimistic on &lt;i&gt;Darkness on the Edge of Town &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;The River&lt;/i&gt;, and now he opened his latest album with a song about cold-blooded murder. The title track fades in with a desolate, wailing harmonica, leading into a pretty-sounding, well-worn traditional folk melody; it recounts the real-life tale of Charles Starkweather, who embarked on a brief murder spree stretching from Lincoln, Nebraska through Wyoming in 1958 with his fourteen-year-old girlfriend in tow, and whose story had already popped up in "Badlands" off of &lt;i&gt;Darkness&lt;/i&gt;. It\'s the song where that pessimism that had begun to surface more and more in Springsteen\'s writing turns to outright hopelessness, as he sings without emotion, in the voice of Starkweather, that "through the badlands of Wyoming, I killed everything in my path", and that "I can\'t say that I\'m sorry for the things we done/at least for a little while, sir, me and her we had some fun". Springsteen had just begun to focus more on the lives of those who had bleak, far-from-prosperous futures ahead of them, and here was a narrative about someone who had no future at all, about to be executed for committing ten brutal murders. Needless to say, it is absolutely bone-chilling, and it sets the tone for one of the most harrowing albums ever to reach the Top Five on the Billboard 200.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"Atlantic City", the one moderate hit single to be pulled from &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt;, is only marginally more hopeful, with that so typically Springsteen-esque chorus line of "Everything dies, baby, that\'s a fact/But maybe everything that dies someday comes back", but only after it starts right off with a lyric about a mob boss being blown up along with his house in Philadelphia. After the sepia-toned nostalgia of "Mansion on the Hill", there\'s also "Johnny 99", a fast rock &amp;amp; roller (albeit played solo, like everything else, and on an acoustic guitar) about a guy who loses his job at an auto plant, can\'t find a new one, so he gets drunk one night and shoots a night clerk dead for no real reason. This is followed by a sweet country-folk ballad about Joe Roberts, an honest farmer-turned-highway patrolman from Michigan who finds out that his no-good brother is responsible for a shooting at a roadhouse, chases him through country roads and then knowingly "watches his tail lights disappear" near the Canadian border, because "man turns his back on his family, well he just ain\'t no good". All of this is front loaded into the first half of the album, and then at the beginning of side two (on the original vinyl) is its most frightening song, the ominous "State Trooper". This time, we don\'t know what this guy did, he\'s "got a clear conscience" about the things he\'s done, but he desperately wishes "please don\'t stop me", getting impatient with talk-show radio and saying he\'s got something that\'s been "botherin\' him (his) whole life". We never find out exactly what\'s going on here, but the seething, deathly quiet intensity of Springsteen\'s performance suggests nothing good, and the startling howl that distorts the hissing tape to its limit at the end of the song is likely to make you jump the first time you hear it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As disturbing as all of this is, there are a few respites from the pile of corpses and unflinching despair that seem to haunt most of &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt;, especially its last few songs, like the Chuck Berry-esque "Open All Night", the one song that\'s powered by an electric rather than acoustic guitar, and the deeply personal ghostly visions of "My Father\'s House". "Reason to Believe" (not the often-covered Tim Hardin song) ends the album on what initially appears to be an almost reluctantly positive note, a winding rumination where Springsteen doesn\'t even understand why people still find just that, all the time, whether it\'s a man poking a dead dog\'s eye as if it will bring it back to life, a submissive housewife still waiting for the man who ditched her long ago, or a groom waiting for a bride who ain\'t gonna show up. In the face of their undying faith, The Boss scoffs, and although it\'s not quite as sordid or anywhere near as violent as the opening title track, it closes out the album on a note that\'s ultimately just as bitter.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Of course, the next album would ultimately pull a drastic 180-degree turn on all of this, that album being the ultra-polished, ultra-mega-hit &lt;i&gt;Born in the U.S.A.&lt;/i&gt;, and the stark, spare gloom of &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt; would seemingly become a distant memory even for some of Bruce\'s most loyal fans. But with this much-loved anomaly in his discography, a sound which he has revisited on only a few occasions (1995\'s &lt;i&gt;Ghost of Tom Joad&lt;/i&gt;, for example) throughout his career, Springsteen had unknowingly presaged the advent of lo-fi indie rockers that would come to prominence in the later 80\'s and 90\'s, who would adopt his method of recording these would-be demos as an essential part of their musical aesthetic. This gives &lt;i&gt;Nebraska&lt;/i&gt; a certain context in rock history, but it\'s the strength of his songs, and the immense bravery he had to have in turning his back on the gleaming, uplifting rock sound that he\'d made his trademark, which make it possibly his finest work, and one of the best "mainstream" rock albums of the 1980\'s.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/bruce-springsteen-nebraska-1982.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Ek-xhm2tRiM/VgKtqaYfQ7I/AAAAAAAABGw/MBHTahyvbz8/s72-c/Bruce_Springsteen_-_Nebraska.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3148993261276495918</guid><pubdate>Mon, 21 Sep 2015 00:44:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-20T17:45:54.489-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>John Lennon "Imagine" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-we3iKwSrLII/Vf9TOKdGHmI/AAAAAAAABF8/U2tfYe-tnrI/s1600/ImagineCover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-we3iKwSrLII/Vf9TOKdGHmI/AAAAAAAABF8/U2tfYe-tnrI/s1600/ImagineCover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Although &lt;i&gt;John Lennon/Plastic Ono Band&lt;/i&gt; was a shockingly brave work, unlike any album that had come before it and still as harrowing and revealing as just about any rock record has ever been since it, it\'s no surprise that many listeners would be put off by its completely uncompromising aesthetic. With that same token, it\'s perhaps equally unsurprising that Lennon himself seemed to have felt it the right time to make something a lot more melodic and accessible, being as his first proper solo album stood on its own as a singular statement that he couldn\'t truly repeat.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Imagine&lt;/i&gt; was, inevitably, far more popular and more successful than its predecessor, with its title track of course becoming an instant standard that remains the most-covered song he ever wrote. Perhaps his audience, or the pop/rock audience in general, felt reassured by the song\'s ostensibly unironic idealism after Lennon had already declared that "the dream", presumably referring to that of the utopian sixties counterculture, was over in the previous album\'s "God". It was certainly a refreshingly sweet song, informed by socialist ideas of "no possessions", "no religion", "no countries" and "a brotherhood of man", that came out in an era of unprecedented social, racial and political division in the tumultuous aftermath of that turbulent decade. Those who take note of him singing "imagine no possessions" while being filmed playing his song in a lavish mansion, on a piano that must\'ve cost an absolute fortune, are right to point out how flawed some of the ideas in "Imagine" really are, but it takes nothing away from just what a gorgeous and ageless piece of music it is.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The album it opens is a far cry from the stark, deathly spare &lt;i&gt;Plastic Ono Band&lt;/i&gt;, boasting production and instrumentation that, at times, is nearly as lush and full-boded as The Beatles\' &lt;i&gt;Abbey Road&lt;/i&gt; or &lt;i&gt;Let It Be&lt;/i&gt;. But it does nothing to take away from its quality, as Lennon\'s songs are all superb; running the gamut from the delicate beauty of "Jealous Guy" and "How", which are as vulnerable and sincere as anything from &lt;i&gt;Plastic Ono Band,&lt;/i&gt; to the bitterly sarcastic "Crippled Inside", the McCartney-baiting "How Do You Sleep?", the hardscrabble blues-rock of "It\'s So Hard" and "I Don\'t Wanna Be a Soldier", and the rabble-rousing anger of "Gimme Some Truth". "Oh Yoko!" is surely one of the most joyous and infectious songs penned by a man in dedication to his wife, and it ends the album on a warm, fuzzy note, which is indeed in stark contrast to the despair of the previous album\'s "My Mummy\'s Dead". Ultimately, &lt;i&gt;Imagine&lt;/i&gt; isn\'t anywhere near as startling as &lt;i&gt;Plastic Ono Band&lt;/i&gt;, but it\'s no less great an album, and in hindsight, it\'s hard to tell which one of the two albums is really his finest solo effort.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/john-lennon-imagine-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-we3iKwSrLII/Vf9TOKdGHmI/AAAAAAAABF8/U2tfYe-tnrI/s72-c/ImagineCover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6226861940789675033</guid><pubdate>Sun, 20 Sep 2015 04:34:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-10-23T21:43:43.567-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The United States of America "The United States of America" (1968)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-NvFLMF849u0/Vf4jmJi8oWI/AAAAAAAABFA/dZJyR9ecUeg/s1600/MI0002016262.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="318" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-NvFLMF849u0/Vf4jmJi8oWI/AAAAAAAABFA/dZJyR9ecUeg/s320/MI0002016262.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Ignored by both Columbia Records\' marketing department and the general public at the time of its release in early 1968, The United States of America\'s only album represented one of the earliest and most ambitious forays into electronic music yet attempted by a rock group, recorded at a time when the only proper synthesizer available was the uber-expensive Robert Moog model and making extensive use of oscillators which were built specifically for the short-lived group by a fellow named Richard Durrett. Historically speaking, this album is perhaps most notable for being one of the very first rock albums, if not &lt;i&gt;the &lt;/i&gt;first, that featured not one note of electric guitar in it, which almost guarantees that it remains an object of much fascination by aficionados of both experimental rock and psychedelic music, and it remains a deserved cult favorite.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Aside from their music, which was radically different from even what was considered cutting-edge rock of the time, and difficult for much of the late-60\'s rock audience to embrace &lt;i&gt;en masse&lt;/i&gt;, the band itself was a rather unusual grouping; leader/conceptualist Joseph Byrd was a former ethnomusicology instructor at UCLA, who was in his early thirties at the time he put together this outfit with vocalist and former lover Dorothy Moskowitz and several other UCLA students. They were schooled in the likes of Stockhausen and Cage, but were enamored with the rock &amp;amp; roll-fueled, leftist counterculture that shook the whole world in the 60\'s, resulting in music that was undoubtedly a product of its time, but also disconnected from the usual trappings of psych-rock or any other type of rock music as it was understood up to that time. It says a lot about how surprisingly willing major record companies were in those days to take chances that Columbia signed The United States of America, largely due to the good word of the label\'s ever-reliable rock &amp;amp; roll producer David Rubinson, when the group had played live just once beforehand, saying nothing of how deliberately anti-commercial they really were.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"The American Metaphysical Circus" starts off with a burst of funhouse calliope music, which is gradually joined by four other tracks all playing at the same time to create a disorienting wall of seemingly unrelated pieces, then dissolves into an oceanic wash of eerie, space-age sounds and Moskowitz\'s heavily distorted vocal, a striking effect achieved by her singing into a ring modulator, which is just one of the devices that her voice is treated with throughout the album. Not that she\'s the only one who gets to sing lead; Byrd himself sings lead on four of the ten songs, although for some reason these songs tend to be the ones with lyrics that have not aged well, like the attempted Mad Magazine-style, "anti-bourgeois" satire of "I Won\'t Leave My Wooden Wife for You, Sugar" and the po-faced, self-serious Latin chant that begins "Where Is Yesterday". All of that aside, one gets the idea that it\'s a good thing the lead singer spot was primarily the domain of Dorothy Moskowitz, whose vibrant voice ranges from the Nico-like coo of the gorgeous "Cloud Song" and "Love Song for the Dead Che" to the Grace Slick-esque snarl of&amp;nbsp; the harder-charging "Garden of Earthly Delights" and "Hard Coming Love". Byrd\'s real contributions and talents lay elsewhere; the final two or so minutes of the album, for example, with an impressively complex collage of tape loops and excerpts culled from the previous nine songs.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;His vision for this type of avant-rock, where the primitive electronic equipment is utilized in such an ear-prickling fashion as to make you forget there\'s no guitars to be found, was most certainly way ahead of his and the group\'s time, regardless of whether or not their songwriting was as exceptional as, say, Frank Zappa or Lou Reed. In the accepted history of challenging, unconventional music in general, The United States of America remain quite little-known, even in this age of exhumed obscurities-turned-forgotten masterpieces, and their one and only album is both underrated and under-recognized for how groundbreaking it really was for rock music in 1968.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/the-united-states-of-america-united.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-NvFLMF849u0/Vf4jmJi8oWI/AAAAAAAABFA/dZJyR9ecUeg/s72-c/MI0002016262.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6043886255680068849</guid><pubdate>Sun, 20 Sep 2015 02:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-19T19:15:40.666-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>A Tribe Called Quest "The Low End Theory" (1991)</title><description>&lt;br /&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-V5A-SWzIPp0/Vf4H7s9mXTI/AAAAAAAABEY/C1zK6uCFnN8/s1600/MI0001719592.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="316" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-V5A-SWzIPp0/Vf4H7s9mXTI/AAAAAAAABEY/C1zK6uCFnN8/s320/MI0001719592.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;1990\'s &lt;i&gt;People\'s Instinctive Travels and the Paths of Rhythm&lt;/i&gt; was one of the most distinctive debut albums by any hip-hop act of the music\'s first decade, but the ensuing &lt;i&gt;Low End Theory&lt;/i&gt; was even more self-assured and impressive, mixing A Tribe Called Quest\'s established social commentary and penchant for jazzy samples with slammin\' beats that often sound like they\'re coming from a drumkit as opposed to a drum machine, even though the group never used a percussionist. They did enlist the services of old-school jazz bassist Ron Carter for the sublime "Verses from the Abstract", though they achieve a similar upright bass sound (via sampling) in many other spots on their sophomore album. To this day, &lt;i&gt;The Low End Theory &lt;/i&gt;is one of the most successful jazz-rap hybrids ever waxed, successfully establishing a smoky, late-night vibe and taking full advantage of jazz music\'s broad, boundlessly eclectic range of sounds and melodies, as well as contributing a stellar set of cool, concise songs that encompass Q-Tip and Phife Dawg\'s impeccably smooth, near-telepathic flow and a broad range of subject matter.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The rumbling, ominous "Excursions" functions as a statement of purpose for the group, as the lackadaisical Q-Tip casually recalls how his father, upon hearing the hip-hop his son was enamored with, was reminded of bebop. From there, he and the (slightly brasher) Dawg never sound less than fully confident on the mic, without undue posturing or any of the sexism and homophobia that permeated the lyrics of the West Coast gangsta rappers that were just beginning to take over the rap music world then. It\'s ironic, then, that Tribe once cited N.W.A.\'s &lt;i&gt;Straight Outta Compton&lt;/i&gt; as an influence on the making of their second album, but at the same time it\'s surprisingly understandable, given that &lt;i&gt;The Low End Theory&lt;/i&gt; has harder-hitting beats and ever so slightly more adrenaline than most other records by members of the so-called Native Tongues collective. The music is stripped-down, relying far more on an incredibly strong rhythm section than on any of the samples that are present in the mix, which was notably something of a departure from the norm for an East Coast act in an era that was dominated by the dizzying, kaleidoscopic aesthetic of Public Enemy and (fellow Natives) De La Soul. There\'s no filler material in the form of skits or an overriding conceptual arc, just fourteen songs that are all chock-filled with cerebral, wordy rhymes, which take aim at the rap music game ("Show Business", "Rap Promoter"), ask a head-spinning array of rhetorical questions ("What?"), tackle an issue that\'s still very rarely discussed among rappers ("The Infamous Date Rape"), and plenty of other heady topics without ever sounding preachy or sanctimonious.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The fusion of jazz and rap music is like bread and butter on paper, being as both forms of music constitute indigenous African-American art and value both improvisation and, in one form or another, flow. However, few acts out of the jazz-rap movement that sprung up in the early 90\'s got the feeling and the music down so well as A Tribe Called Quest did here, and &lt;i&gt;The Low End Theory&lt;/i&gt; is not only unquestionably their finest hour, not to mention probably the best album this side of De La\'s &lt;i&gt;3 Feet High and Rising&lt;/i&gt; ever to come from any Native Tongues group, but also one of the greatest and most influential hip-hop recordings of all time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/a-tribe-called-quest-low-end-theory-1991.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-V5A-SWzIPp0/Vf4H7s9mXTI/AAAAAAAABEY/C1zK6uCFnN8/s72-c/MI0001719592.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4639234991503282394</guid><pubdate>Sat, 19 Sep 2015 04:22:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-18T21:22:31.952-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Ice Cube "Death Certificate" (1991)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-0wbcppYc0Rk/VfyB_Qlbk5I/AAAAAAAABDs/Aty1f-fedxA/s1600/330px-Ice_Cube-Death_Certificate_%2528album_cover%2529.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-0wbcppYc0Rk/VfyB_Qlbk5I/AAAAAAAABDs/Aty1f-fedxA/s320/330px-Ice_Cube-Death_Certificate_%2528album_cover%2529.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Ice Cube\'s solo debut proved many critics and fans wrong in establishing not only that the ex-N.W.A. member was a formidable talent on his own, but also that the group\'s unexpected star power rubbed off on him commercially when the album sold 95,000 copies in the first week of its release. Buoyed by the critical and commercial success of &lt;i&gt;AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted, &lt;/i&gt;as well as by Cube\'s surprising initial foray into film acting with his acclaimed leading role in John Singleton\'s "Boyz in the Hood", 1991\'s &lt;i&gt;Death Certificate&lt;/i&gt; was one of the most anticipated releases of the year, hitting a million pre-orders before it was released on October 29th. It incurred much controversy with far more overt misogyny than he\'d ever delved into before, as well as the newfound, unapologetic racism and antisemitism that surfaces elsewhere. Shocking, negligible lyrical content was all over &lt;i&gt;Most Wanted&lt;/i&gt;, and N.W.A.\'s &lt;i&gt;Straight Outta Compton&lt;/i&gt; for that matter, but &lt;i&gt;Death Certificate&lt;/i&gt; considerably upped the ante on all of it, resulting in an absolutely unrelenting, prolonged firebomb directed at a multitude of targets, running through nearly every single song from the "oreo cookie"-baiting of "True to the Game" to the underage sex of the infectiously funky "Givin\' Up the Nappy Dugout", with its refrain of "I\'m gonna do my thing with your daughter" sung almost ironically by a chorus of soulful female vocalists.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cube explains in the introductory "The Funeral" that the album is divided between "death" and "life" sides, the first of those basically being concerned with the problems blighting the black community, and the second essentially focusing on how the community can take action against them. On record, this translates into a first half dominated by imagery of STD\'s, dope-dealing, crushing poverty, racism and gang warfare, and the remainder detailing a mostly violent response to all of these things. Taken together, &lt;i&gt;Death Certificate &lt;/i&gt;has close to none of the sense of humor that popped up on its predecessor, replaced by an all-pervading, glowering anger that runs over the listener like a tank. This music sounds more genuinely dangerous than anything hip-hop had produced since &lt;i&gt;Compton&lt;/i&gt;. Needless to say, the relentless misogyny and hell-to-pay condemnation of those "oreo cookie" black men who sell out their race and try to be white, sprinkled with a dose of homophobia and antisemitism, can grow a bit tiresome at times, but through it all, Cube continues to be perhaps the best, most clever gangsta rapper and songwriter of all time. Like its predecessor, it\'s absolutely loaded with great songs, albeit without the Bomb Squad\'s production work and therefore less of the jarring sonic barrage that characterized the first record. Instead, it\'s all hard, deep-as-a-canyon funk, like a much more bleak precursor to Dr. Dre\'s game-changing &lt;i&gt;The Chronic&lt;/i&gt; with its heavy reliance on P-funk samples.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s only fitting that an album this provocative and inflammatory should come to an end with probably the best song on it, the scabrous "No Vaseline", where Cube viciously denounces his old homies from N.W.A. and takes the potshot at manager Jerry Heller that warrants the "antisemitism" tag mentioned in the laundry list of easily offensive lyrics. It may be a good deal less cerebral or thoughtful than the &lt;i&gt;AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted&lt;/i&gt;, but by any measure &lt;i&gt;Death Certificate&lt;/i&gt; is equally exceptional, and Cube would never come up with something as formidable as this ever again.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/ice-cube-death-certificate-1991.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-0wbcppYc0Rk/VfyB_Qlbk5I/AAAAAAAABDs/Aty1f-fedxA/s72-c/330px-Ice_Cube-Death_Certificate_%2528album_cover%2529.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-241379009241500810</guid><pubdate>Fri, 18 Sep 2015 21:27:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-18T14:27:08.861-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Ice Cube "AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted" (1990)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-hwMVJa7Cc8c/VTMqO5dnHAI/AAAAAAAAAh0/y20DRRq9R8Y/s1600/AmeriKKKa%27s_Most_Wanted_(Ice_Cube).jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-hwMVJa7Cc8c/VTMqO5dnHAI/AAAAAAAAAh0/y20DRRq9R8Y/s1600/AmeriKKKa\'s_Most_Wanted_(Ice_Cube).jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Just as Niggaz With Attitude hit its stride as a most unexpected commercial entity, the one out of the fivesome who delivered the group\'s most unadulterated, aggressive rhymes left the group amid business disputes and struck out on his own with a vengeance in late 1989. Many had doubts about Ice Cube\'s solo clout, especially given that Eric "Eazy-E" Wright was pegged as N.W.A.\'s star character by much of the national media and the group\'s fans, but the blistering &lt;i&gt;AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted &lt;/i&gt;silenced most of the naysayers with a hard-hitting Bomb Squad production that bridges the George Clinton-derived sounds common to West Coast hip-hop and the dense, sample-heavy collage techniques that the production team had debuted with the East Coast\'s own Public Enemy. For good measure, it also boasted almost as much incendiary rhetoric as N.W.A.\'s &lt;i&gt;Straight Outta Compton, &lt;/i&gt;or for that matter PE\'s &lt;i&gt;Fear of a Black Planet &lt;/i&gt;(on which Cube made a guest appearance in "Burn Hollywood Burn"); after an intro bit where Cube plays a man about to be executed, he comes out of the chute like a bull with the vicious "Nigga You Love to Hate" ("Why more niggas in the pen than in college?"), and the attack doesn\'t let up until the album is through.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Of course, the title track is a manifesto for a rapper who did indeed seem like a marked man in the first Bush era, and in an age where young black men undoubtedly served as the preeminent punching bag for the right-wing, white male power structure, thanks largely to an incessantly sensationalized crack cocaine epidemic, he was essentially positioning himself as AmeriKKKa\'s ultimate boogeyman. He fits the bill, and throughout his first (and probably best) solo album, he\'s waving it and his middle finger in the face of those who fear him and everything he represents.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s far from an ideologically dense opus on par with &lt;i&gt;It Takes a Nation of Millions to Hold Us Back &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Black Planet&lt;/i&gt;, as evidenced by the much-discussed misogynistic streak that runs through the album and the images of inner city violence that Cube portrays in never less than the crudest, rudest terms imaginable. In "Endangered Species", however, he attacks the dehumanization of black men, and in "Turn Off the Radio" he savages play-it-safe R&amp;amp;B DJ\'s with a damn-near infectious contempt. What\'s most important is how &lt;i&gt;AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted &lt;/i&gt;makes it perfectly clear that Cube was perhaps the most articulate "gangsta" rapper around, with even the mindless woman-abusing of "You Can\'t Fade Me" sketched out with undoubtedly clever turns of phrase. As a statement of purpose and a tour-de-force for one of the most intimidating MC\'s ever to rock the mic, &lt;i&gt;AmeriKKKa\'s Most Wanted &lt;/i&gt;is just about perfect, and on every level he would never truly top this incendiary debut. &lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/ice-cube-amerikkkas-most-wanted-1990.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-hwMVJa7Cc8c/VTMqO5dnHAI/AAAAAAAAAh0/y20DRRq9R8Y/s72-c/AmeriKKKa\'s_Most_Wanted_(Ice_Cube).jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6321919150585816668</guid><pubdate>Fri, 11 Sep 2015 15:10:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-24T21:45:49.743-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "The Freewheelin\' Bob Dylan" (1963)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-x0bcfNRtC0w/VfLcQCb6TsI/AAAAAAAABC4/Xbbs7mQg4YQ/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_The_Freewheelin%2527_Bob_Dylan.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-x0bcfNRtC0w/VfLcQCb6TsI/AAAAAAAABC4/Xbbs7mQg4YQ/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_The_Freewheelin%2527_Bob_Dylan.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With the hindsight of over half a century since University of Minnesota student Robert Zimmerman first began delving into traditional American folk song after being enamored with rock &amp;amp; roll as a high-schooler, soon becoming a fixture of the Dinkytown folk scene under the name Bob Dylan, it\'s clear where he began building his reputation as the most exceptional talent to emerge from the folk revival of the late 50\'s and early 60\'s. His eponymous 1962 debut album, a rather weak seller for Columbia Records that many in the recording industry at the time dubbed "(John) Hammond\'s Folly" and which contained only two original compositions, was clearly the work of a particularly unique and talented disciple of the likes of Woody Guthrie and Jesse Fuller. In light of what was to come just a few years later, it now looks almost like his first demo tape as opposed to a full-fledged debut album. It would instead be its 1963 follow-up which marked his true arrival into both the consciousness of a wider audience than the folk circles he\'d come up in, and into the framework of American pop culture.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It was with the release of &lt;i&gt;The Freewheelin\' Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt; that Bob Dylan was introduced to the world the way the world first got to know him, as the protest-singing troubadour who would soon be anointed as the "Voice of a Generation", that generation being made up of people in their early twenties who were against the militarism of the Cold War-era U.S. government, supported the ever-intensifying civil rights movement, and who would, by the end of the 60\'s, be at the forefront of one of the most pivotal time periods in American history. The protest folk movement of the time, which had an enormous presence on college campuses in the early 60\'s, constituted the first rumblings among young Americans that would eventually blossom into the new-leftist counterculture that shocked, titillated and left an indelible impact on American society. Although singers like Dylan\'s close friend and onetime lover Joan Baez and Phil Ochs beat him to the punch by either making their first recordings several years before his (Baez), or being far more sincere about political causes than he was even at this stage (Ochs), Dylan would be the only member of this loosely affiliated group of young singer-songwriters to retain a crucial and important position in American pop music at large for the remainder of the decade. With that in mind, &lt;i&gt;Freewheelin\'&lt;/i&gt; is not just Dylan\'s first great artistic breakthrough, not only perhaps the most startling long-player that any contemporary American folk singer had released up to that point, but also a tremendously prescient signpost for the substantial cultural shifts that were to occur over the next decade after its recording.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Track one, "Blowin\' in the Wind", was, of course, the first Dylan song that the vast majority of music listeners became familiar with, and the first of many songs he wrote in the 60\'s which would become bigger hits when recorded by other acts; Peter, Paul and Mary recorded a version that would be more commercially successful than Dylan\'s original recording and brought the 22-year-old singer/songwriter a great deal of national recognition. Even though Dylan would progress much farther, and very quickly, from its almost naive or sanctimonious idealism, it remains nothing short of one of the most enduring pop music standards of the 1960\'s. "Girl from the North Country", adapted in large part from the traditional "Scarborough Fair", would later be re-recorded as a duet with Johnny Cash for 1969\'s &lt;i&gt;Nashville Skyline&lt;/i&gt;, which is appropriate in that although it\'s primarily indebted to the traditional form of American folk ballad, it has something of a country &amp;amp; western air to it, or at least its lyrics. It\'s no surprise that a young, pre-outlaw Waylon Jennings would be one of the first artists to perform their own version of the song after Dylan\'s original, which can be heard on &lt;i&gt;The Restless Kid-Live at JD\'s&lt;/i&gt;. "Masters of War" is unquestionably one of the most vicious and unflinching protest songs, regardless of musical genre, ever written, wherein Dylan compares the conniving, war-mongering politicians of its title who "play with (his) world" to "Judas of old", hoping that their death will come soon and that he\'ll stand over their graves to make sure they\'re dead. It\'s one of the most powerful anti-war statements made by any artist in the years prior to the formal beginning of America\'s decade-long involvement with the Vietnam War, which would occur two years later.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As beautifully succinct and eloquently phrased as "Blowin\' in the Wind" is, "A Hard Rain\'s a-Gonna Fall" has more depth and complexity as a sociopolitical statement, but furthermore "Don\'t Think Twice, It\'s Alright" is an even better song, which, as Bob pointed out in comments quoted in the album\'s liner notes, seems at first like a tender romantic ballad, but it\'s really more of a good motto to keep to yourself if you\'re ever in doubt. The closing "I Shall Be Free", among other numbers here, demonstrates the surprisingly strong, absurdist sense of humor that would be unfortunately lacking in the ensuing &lt;i&gt;Times They Are-a Changin\'&lt;/i&gt; album, making a return in &lt;i&gt;Another Side of Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt; later in 1964.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;The Freewheelin\' Bob Dylan&lt;/i&gt; captures just the first phase of Dylan\'s long career as an ever-shapeshifting artistic force, but even with the triumphs of later albums like &lt;i&gt;Highway 61 Revisited &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Blonde on Blonde&lt;/i&gt;, it still holds up as one of his best and most consistent albums, and one of the best albums of the pre-Beatles era when, for the most part, LP\'s were merely collections of songs, as opposed to being considered artistic statements. Although it wasn\'t his first album, it\'s still startling to hear just how good a singer and songwriter he was, at the age of just 21 at the time of its recording, and how even before he officially turned his back on the trappings and political ideals of the folk movement, he was already in a much different league as a writer of original material at such an early stage.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/bob-dylan-freewheelin-bob-dylan-1963.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-x0bcfNRtC0w/VfLcQCb6TsI/AAAAAAAABC4/Xbbs7mQg4YQ/s72-c/Bob_Dylan_-_The_Freewheelin%2527_Bob_Dylan.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1370247184930098018</guid><pubdate>Thu, 03 Sep 2015 22:02:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-09-03T17:02:56.152-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Rolling Stones "Beggars Banquet" (1968)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-vxPxOilyPqY/VejEHHqMGnI/AAAAAAAABBs/1mdaTqB0ZKM/s1600/MI0000367339.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-vxPxOilyPqY/VejEHHqMGnI/AAAAAAAABBs/1mdaTqB0ZKM/s320/MI0000367339.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The Stones spent 1967 experimenting with Kinks-esque British pop and psychedelia on the superb &lt;i&gt;Between the Buttons &lt;/i&gt;and the fascinating yet more flawed &lt;i&gt;Their Satanic Majesties Request&lt;/i&gt;. As Keith Richards tells it, he\'d been listening to a veritable collection of records that he\'d picked up on jaunts to America in the mid-60\'s, but never had time to listen to until their touring slowed way down in 1967 and \'68, and the sounds of American roots music would inform the back-to-basics &lt;i&gt;Beggars Banquet &lt;/i&gt;album. A troubled Brian Jones was largely absent from the studio when work began on their seventh British (and ninth American) album in March 1968, which is part of the reason that it has far less of their experimentation of the past couple of years. Some at the time might\'ve grumbled that The Stones were copping out of the increasingly progressive rock vanguard of the late 60\'s by going back to the roots of their music, but from beginning to end, &lt;i&gt;Beggars Banquet &lt;/i&gt;was the strongest album statement the group had yet produced, beginning a startling string of classic albums that would continue with 1969\'s apocalyptic &lt;i&gt;Let It Bleed&lt;/i&gt;, 1971\'s hungover &lt;i&gt;Sticky Fingers&lt;/i&gt;, and 1972\'s ragged masterpiece &lt;i&gt;Exile on Main St.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Unorthodox arrangements certainly were not gone from the band\'s musical character at this stage, as demonstrated by the six-plus minute epic "Sympathy for the Devil", which is the perfect calling card for the sinister elements that would run through everything they made thereafter up to &lt;i&gt;Exile&lt;/i&gt;; The Stones had certainly been controversial before, with their well-earned bad boy image and the lasciviousness of songs like "Let\'s Spend the Night Together" and "Satisfaction", but flirting with the image of Lucifer suggested a new kind of danger and malevolence that went hand-in-hand with the disintegration of late 60\'s "flower power". It\'s rather interesting in hindsight that it opens the same album that also features the ostensibly revolutionary, summer-of-\'68 anthem "Street Fighting Man", which is actually more in step with the position of John Lennon\'s "Revolution" than one might expect when you scan its lyrics. In any case, "Sympathy" is far more prescient not just for the future direction of The Rolling Stones, but also for the direction rock &amp;amp; roll itself would take in the 1970\'s, when bands like Led Zeppelin and Grand Funk Railroad got heavier and nastier, and hedonistic decadence took center stage in hard rock. The leering "Stray Cat Blues" considerably upped the ante on the sexual sleaze that had always been a crucial part of The Stones\' persona, where Mick Jagger is coming onto a fifteen-year-old groupie, shrugging that "it\'s no hanging matter/it\'s no capital crime" and that "I\'ll bet your mama don\'t know you can bite like that/I\'ll bet she never saw you scratch my back". It\'s one of the few songs, from the time when rock could actually shock people, that would actually be considered far &lt;i&gt;more &lt;/i&gt;offensive and/or outrageous if it were released today than it was at the time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;By contrast to all this, looking through that same retrospective lens, "No Expectations" is perhaps the saddest and loneliest song Jagger or Richards ever penned; it certainly wasn\'t intended as such, but with Jones\' lonesome slide guitar part, it sounds like an elegy in advance of his tragic death, which would occur just about a year after the recording of the album wrapped up. The song\'s very first live performance, which was filmed for the then-unreleased "Rock &amp;amp; Roll Circus" TV film in December of 1968, would represent Jones\' very last performance with the group he named and helped form. "Take me to the station/Put me on a train/I\'ve got no expectations/To pass through here again"...&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;More consistent than any LP that they had done beforehand, &lt;i&gt;Beggars Banquet&lt;/i&gt; signaled the dawning of a new era for The Stones, one that would produce their greatest albums and some of their finest songs, and its first installment would forever remain one of their finest accomplishments on record.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/09/the-rolling-stones.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-vxPxOilyPqY/VejEHHqMGnI/AAAAAAAABBs/1mdaTqB0ZKM/s72-c/MI0000367339.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7552180871423457878</guid><pubdate>Sun, 30 Aug 2015 01:59:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-29T19:11:19.657-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Clash "London Calling" (1979)</title><description>&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-X5nIYRZSoqI/VeJNWxuPVCI/AAAAAAAABAo/KIUchIqafLU/s1600/TheClashLondonCallingalbumcover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-X5nIYRZSoqI/VeJNWxuPVCI/AAAAAAAABAo/KIUchIqafLU/s1600/TheClashLondonCallingalbumcover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;With 1978\'s &lt;i&gt;Give \'Em Enough Rope&lt;/i&gt;, The Clash first began to make significant inroads into the US rock audience, many of whom first got to see the group live when they embarked on the tour supporting their sophomore album. In the wake of the Pistols\' implosion, they were now officially the flagbearers for a waning punk movement in Britain, but at the end of the blighted 70\'s, they were in turmoil. They had parted ways with manager Bernie Rhodes, and as a consequence had to leave their longtime rehearsal studio in Camden Town to find a new place to brainstorm new music. In mid-1979, they holed up in a place called Vanilla Studios to write and demo new songs, before entering Wessex Studios in August to begin recording their third album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They had already proven as early as their very first album, with the rocking-yet-reverent version of Junior Murvin\'s reggae classic "Police and Thieves", that unlike the Sex Pistols, they weren\'t confined to a basic 4/4 rock &amp;amp; roll sound, and by the time of their second LP, that basic punk-rock sound had been adopted by scores of derivative, dumbed-down groups in Britain. By 1979, for all intents and purposes, the original punk movement there (and the older scene for that matter in New York\'s CBGB\'s, for slightly different reasons, but at about the same time) had quickly frittered away in a veritable shitstorm of media hysteria and increasing commercial success. Nobody in The Clash ever really put it like this, but one can\'t help but think that this emboldened them to almost completely eschew the basic sound and angry sloganeering of their early days, and embrace a thick gumbo of music that was still filtered through the urgency and immediacy of punk rock.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Produced by the volatile Guy Stevens, as opposed to the previous album\'s slick and professional Sandy Pearlman, &lt;i&gt;London Calling&lt;/i&gt; is nothing short of a tour de force, as admittedly tired and universally-acknowledged a statement as that is to make about &lt;i&gt;London Calling &lt;/i&gt;by now. The very first double-album of the post-punk era, there is absolutely none of the paltry filler material that significantly hampered &lt;i&gt;Give \'Em Enough Rope&lt;/i&gt;, and it kicks right off with one of the absolute greatest album openers in rock history. The ominous title track is nothing short of an apocalyptic siren, Joe Strummer\'s warning to all whose ears are open that the "phony Beatlemania" and placid idealism of the 60\'s is long extinct, and he\'s howling it with more fire and desperation than any number of other punk groups who\'d been making similar statements for the past three or four years by that point. It\'s a particularly potent lyric especially when the album\'s UK and US release dates are taken into consideration: in Britain, it was released on 14 December, 1979, at the very end of the poverty-stricken, grim decade which the Pistols and The Clash were such an insolent, disorderly product of, and the end of the year when Margaret Thatcher was elected Prime Minister, foreshadowing yet another decade of economic and social turbulence in the country. In America, it was released a few weeks later, in early January 1980, ten months before the election of the similarly onerous, ultraconservative Ronald Reagan as president, the beginning of one of the most influential and divisive administrations in US political history, and with it a gradual erosion of the liberal thinking that the social upheavals of the 60\'s had engendered on the nation. "London Calling" could hardly have been recorded at a more appropriate time, as the opening number on what is at once the last great album of the 1970\'s and the first great album of the 80\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;On side one alone, in vinyl terms, they move from the razor-sharp martial stomp of the title song, to the careening rock &amp;amp; roll of Vince Taylor\'s 50\'s oldie "Brand New Cadillac", to the evasive career criminal of the languid, weary "Jimmy Jazz", the unrepentant dope fiend of the rampaging "Hateful", and the happily hedonistic rude boy of "Rudie Can\'t Fail". The wildly eclectic sounds are positively intoxicating through those first five songs alone, and then there\'s the rest of its first half; the ebullient "Spanish Bombs" sounds like it was plucked off of Spector\'s assembly line, only tied together with a fiercely political lyric, and with the disco-fevered "Lost in the Supermarket" the group reach a new plateau of lyrical maturity, as Mick Jones sings with a mixture of sympathy and scorn Strummer\'s portrait of Jones\' own childhood in the dreary suburbs. It\'s at once the most vulnerable song they\'d written up to that point, and one of their greatest slices of acerbic social commentary. With the furious "Clampdown", as with the title track, they pull off the neat feat of writing another one of their patented anti-establishment anthems without reverting back to the sound of early rabble-rousers like "Garageland" or "Career Opportunities", rocking in a way that no one but them could with an angry, impassioned call for their audience to resist the pull of the \'adult\' world\'s compromises and lost motivation. Bassist Paul Simonon takes the lead with "The Guns of Brixton", with the grim question of "When they kick at your front door, how you gonna come?/With your hands on your head, or on the trigger of your gun?" It\'s even more ominous than "London Calling", in light of the racially-motivated rioting that would take place not long after its recording in Brixton, among other areas.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While the heroin-addled Sid Vicious died just as everybody expected, checking out at the age of 21 before he could face trial for supposedly murdering his repulsive girlfriend in a drugged-out stupor, a 27-year-old Joe Strummer lashed out at this mindless nihilism that was so commonly associated with punk, with the uplifting "Death or Glory". You know a guy is a gifted songwriter when he writes something like "He who fucks nuns will later join the church"! Although disc two is inevitably, ever so slightly less strong than the first one, nothing on it drags and everything only further fleshes out the album\'s ambitious wealth of diverse material. It wasn\'t originally supposed to be on the album, added at the last minute to the end of it and not originally listed on its sleeve, but everything perfectly culminates in "Train in Vain", the poppiest thing they\'d written yet, with an irrepressibly joyous lead vocal from Mick Jones married to a relentlessly catchy lyric about his own relationship with The Slits\' Viv Albertine. When released as a single in the US, it would be their first to enter the Top 30 there, buoyed by both widespread critical enthusiasm from the American press towards &lt;i&gt;London Calling&lt;/i&gt; as a whole and The Clash\'s growing visibility as "The Only Band That Matters".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Whether they really were or not, it\'s no wonder that &lt;i&gt;London Calling&lt;/i&gt; would attract such a tag for the group, because it\'s not only indisputably their finest hour, but it\'s also undoubtedly one of the greatest rock albums of all time. Of all the albums so frequently piled on with endless critical hosannas, few of them come so perfect and fully-formed as this one; quite simply, in terms of rock &amp;amp; roll, this is pretty much as good as it could ever get, and it has stood the test of time like only an elite group of the very best LP\'s of all time have ever been able to do, never mind the trappings of its era or any other in popular music history. It\'s wonderfully rich with creative freedom and a joyful celebration of music of all kinds, which is plainly obvious from beginning to end and is probably one of the biggest reasons that it\'s endured so well over a third of a century since it was recorded, and will continue to do so to whatever end this earth may have in the future.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/the-clash-london-calling-1979.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-X5nIYRZSoqI/VeJNWxuPVCI/AAAAAAAABAo/KIUchIqafLU/s72-c/TheClashLondonCallingalbumcover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-327158158727058894</guid><pubdate>Fri, 28 Aug 2015 02:33:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-27T19:33:33.448-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>X "Under the Big Black Sun" (1982)/"More Fun in the New World" (1983)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/--4b2XCIFsRk/Vd-3Ga9XTRI/AAAAAAAAA_0/8804HnJWjXE/s1600/330px-XUnderTheBlackSun.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/--4b2XCIFsRk/Vd-3Ga9XTRI/AAAAAAAAA_0/8804HnJWjXE/s320/330px-XUnderTheBlackSun.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In 1982, X did what almost no other band from the initial wave of punk rock in Los Angeles got to do, which is make the jump to a major label and go into the studio to make an album with major label money. It\'s no surprise that after putting out the terrific &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt; on Slash Records, the surprisingly widespread critical acclaim would reach the point where someone like Elektra Records would take notice, although one might speculate that it may have also had something to do with the fact that X\'s producer Ray Manzarek happened to have once been a member of one of Elektra\'s most successful acts. He returned back behind the boards for the group\'s Elektra debut, &lt;i&gt;Under the Big Black Sun&lt;/i&gt;, and if a segment of their fanbase was surely crying "sellout!" and expecting a polished piece of watered-down crap, this record sure as hell wasn\'t it. In fact, it might actually be their finest hour, where the already versatile band essentially left the sartorial and social trappings of L.A. punk behind and continued to expand their musical palette. Whether it\'s true or not, it\'s no wonder that Exene Cervenka, for one, considers it to be X\'s best album, or at least her personal favorite.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;An absolutely ferocious opener "The Hungry Wolf", whose extremely low-budget music video actually garnered some notable airplay in the early days of MTV, is a Zeppelin-strength hard-rocker that was unlike anything they had done previously, an evocative likening of a romantic partnership to two restless animals roaming through life, living in the moment and never knowing what to expect. The preceding &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt; had a preoccupation with John Doe and Exene Cervenka\'s fractious real-life marriage, but although it certainly rears its head once more in songs like "Wolf" and "Riding With Mary", &lt;i&gt;Under the Big Black Sun&lt;/i&gt; is more haunted by grief, which also had its roots in real life. Cervenka\'s sister Mary had died in a car crash a couple of years previously, which is explicitly referred to by the title of "Riding With Mary", the anguish of the tear-stained torch song "Come Back to Me", and the despair of the hopelessly attached ghost in the throttling "Because I Do". Of course, the foursome\'s exquisite rendition of Leadbelly\'s "Dancing With Tears in My Eyes" is inevitably colored by this sadness, as well. It\'s rather surprising that X waited until their third album to record the punchy "Blue Spark", which by the bandmembers\' own account was actually the first song they ever played together, but it seems to fit in a whole lot better here than it would have on either of their first two records.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although X were always a thoroughly American band, as evidenced by their fondness for American traditional music and disdain for the fashion-conscious London sensibility that many outside observers readily associated with \'punk rock\' at the time, "The Have Nots" is where they began to align themselves with a kind of rustic Americana sensibility that would come to the forefront in later years. A perfect closing number for X\'s strongest and most satisfying album, it\'s a moving hymn to America\'s legion of blue-collar barflies, the disenfranchised working class that was being marginalized more than ever by Reaganomics in the early 80\'s. With over thirty years\' hindsight, it also pulls of the neat trick of being a perfect lead-in to the lead-off track of their next album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Mq3NS3FsCvQ/Vd-_uTVV5kI/AAAAAAAABAQ/z9mWjboIpWY/s1600/MI0002807688.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="312" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Mq3NS3FsCvQ/Vd-_uTVV5kI/AAAAAAAABAQ/z9mWjboIpWY/s320/MI0002807688.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;When looking at X\'s first four albums individually, you begin to see thematic threats that run through each one, even if none of them are strict \'concept albums\' per se. &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt; was laden with imagery of&amp;nbsp; "Sex and Dying in High Society", &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt; found John Doe absconding with a "White Girl" (who, as an aside, has long been rumored to have been Germs bassist Lorna Doom), &lt;i&gt;Under the Big Black Sun &lt;/i&gt;was burdened by painful loss, and &lt;i&gt;More Fun in the New World&lt;/i&gt; is often given over to sharp commentary on Reagan\'s America. The anthemic opener "The New World" is as explicitly political as they had ever been; it\'s really like a direct continuation of the previous album\'s "The Have Nots". Where they had previously given their empathy to those for whom "dawn comes soon enough" and "it keeps getting sooner or later", they now spoke in the voice of one of those down-on-their-luck working men, irate that things were better "before they voted for what\'s-his-name" and that all the bars are closed, cause "they must be votin\' for a new president or somethin\'".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As good a song as it is, this undercurrent of disenchantment with the Reagan administration really reaches its full flower with the magnificent "I Must Not Think Bad Thoughts", which also includes a jab at the radio\'s early-80\'s preoccupation with "The Second British Invasion" and namechecks fellow American underground stalwarts the Minutemen, Flesh Eaters, D.O.A., Big Boys and Black Flag in the process, asking, "Will the last American band to get played on the radio please bring the flag?" Many had uneasy feelings about this kind of nationalistic sentiment, which wasn\'t at all dissimilar to the contemporary songs of mainstream, "heartland" rock superstars like Bruce Springsteen or John Cougar Mellencamp, but they certainly made it sound heartfelt and convincing as opposed to mindlessly jingoistic, in one of their greatest songs.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As a whole, &lt;i&gt;More Fun in the New World&lt;/i&gt; is definitely a shade weaker than the three fantastic records that came before it, but it certainly has its fair share of good-to-great songs, including the melodic rocker "True Love", the Stonesy strut of "Hot House" and the white-hot rock &amp;amp; roll of "Devil Doll". One would think that closing out with a funky, four-on-the-floor version of "True Love" would kill the album\'s momentum and considerably mar its overall quality, but the experiment actually pays off quite marvelously well given just how completely unexpected it was. Many folks still don\'t know that Billy Zoom actually had almost as strong an R&amp;amp;B background as he did rockabilly chops in the years before X\'s formation. &lt;i&gt;More Fun&lt;/i&gt; was the end of an era for X, their last album to be produced by Ray Manzarek, as the group would rather inexplicably pair up with heavy metal producer Michael Wagener for 1985\'s &lt;i&gt;Ain\'t Love Grand!&lt;/i&gt;, and though there would certainly be strong material scattered liberally throughout their subsequent albums, it was definitely the final installment of their most creatively fertile period.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/x-under-big-black-sun-1982more-fun-in.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/--4b2XCIFsRk/Vd-3Ga9XTRI/AAAAAAAAA_0/8804HnJWjXE/s72-c/330px-XUnderTheBlackSun.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1037652241461536359</guid><pubdate>Fri, 28 Aug 2015 01:16:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-27T18:16:14.993-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>X "Los Angeles" (1980)/"Wild Gift" (1981)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-zmVomnDYvOQ/Vd-mRbT5bxI/AAAAAAAAA_M/6FhdcKTDU5w/s1600/XLosAngeles.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-zmVomnDYvOQ/Vd-mRbT5bxI/AAAAAAAAA_M/6FhdcKTDU5w/s1600/XLosAngeles.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;L.A.\'s late-70\'s punk explosion, for the longest time, didn\'t get the critical kudos that was rewarded the bursts of rage and energy that blossomed in New York and London at the same time, even though the Los Angeles scene (and that of San Francisco, for that matter) was almost inarguably just as vibrant, exciting and intense as the other two primary hubs of punk rock in the genre\'s early days. One band, however, managed to transcend not only the stigma that was attached to the city it was formed in, but also the inherent musical limitations of punk rock in general, and was rewarded with heap-tons of much-deserved critical appraisal by the likes of Robert Christgau and Rolling Stone magazine. X made it clear with their 1978 debut single "Adult Books/We\'re Desperate" that they were the most artistically promising act amongst the many punk bands that formed in 1976 and \'77 in L.A. With 1980\'s &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt;, produced by ex-Doors keyboardist Ray Manzarek, they not only put forth a mission statement out to the wider rock world that they were a force to be reckoned with, but also perhaps defined the L.A. scene itself with its fractured post-Beat prose, and much in the same way that Manzarek\'s old group belied the paisley platitudes of the Age of Aquarius, its refusal to deny the seedy underbelly that had always lied underneath the glitz and glamour of Hollywood celebrityism.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Bassist/vocalist John Doe, his girlfriend Exene Cervenka, thirtysomething guitar wiz Billy Zoom and hard-hitting drummer D.J. Bonebrake all came from backgrounds quite at odds with the suburban discontent that was giving rise to the advent of hyper-speed L.A. hardcore when the band recorded their debut album. Zoom and Doe were children of the sixties who watched rock become increasingly bloated and corporate as the seventies dragged on, and their salvation finally came when they heard The Ramones and Patti Smith and realized that they wanted to join forces with the musical revolt of punk. Cervenka was a frustrated young poet who had never really sung before basically tagging along as a second vocalist with her boyfriend\'s new band, and Bonebrake&amp;nbsp; simply fit the bill of someone who could hit the snare drum hard. Although all nine songs on &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt; are fueled by punk\'s aggression and energy, bits and pieces of other musics naturally crept in; Zoom\'s guitar was dripping with the twangy clip-clop of rockabilly, while Doe and Cervenka\'s unkempt harmony singing uncannily recalled Appalachian folk music. The result of all this were songs that were far more cerebral, poetic and intelligent than most other punk bands, and a sound that was entirely their own.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The pure venom of "Your Phone\'s Off the Hook, But You\'re Not" scowls right out the gate, but "Johnny Hit and Run Pauline" is out-and-out disturbing, a detached portrait of a serial rapist powered by a drug that powers him to have sex, every hour, for 24 hours. It sounds like something William S. Burroughs would have written in "Naked Lunch", plastered onto a slice of positively feral, menacing rock &amp;amp; roll. X throws in a sped-up cover of The Doors\' "Soul Kitchen" for good measure, as their little tribute to the man behind the boards, who also contributes his distinctive organ work on songs like the appropriately queasy, slow-burning "Nausea". The title track, which had already been released as a single on Dangerhouse Records the year beforehand, would remain the group\'s nastiest character study, of an apparently real-life woman who "started to hate every nigger and Jew." "The Unheard Music", which would later provide the title to a fine documentary about the band, is perhaps the most mature and accomplished musicianship on the record, a mid-tempo rumination on a disenfranchised populace, "laughed out of the public eye". After all the darkness and decadence of the preceding eight songs, "The World\'s a Mess; It\'s in My Kiss" is nothing short of a celebratory finale, as rousing an album closer as any band could ever hope for and a perfect capper on what is surely one of the most audacious and accomplished rock debut albums ever recorded.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-sL7DewKzZFc/Vd-wDCiEQPI/AAAAAAAAA_g/5fh5nEtrx98/s1600/XWildGift.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-sL7DewKzZFc/Vd-wDCiEQPI/AAAAAAAAA_g/5fh5nEtrx98/s1600/XWildGift.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With Ray Manzarek again behind the boards, 1981\'s &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt; builds on the strengths of &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt; and winds up as an equally impressive album, a robust refutation of the "sophomore slump" that befalls many artists who put out a debut album as startlingly brilliant as X\'s. If their first record was infused with the urban desperation and sprawl of Los Angeles, the second one is something of a concept album about strained romance, inspired by the real-life romance between John Doe and Exene Cervenka, who had tied the knot in 1980 and would eventually divorce in 1985. But make no mistake; this is anything but a melodramatic, &lt;i&gt;Rumours&lt;/i&gt;-esque rock soap opera. X still possess all the firepower and versatility of &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt;, and spin out a collection of songs that amounts to a whole that is easily the equal of its predecessor.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It might seem that re-recording "Adult Books" and "We\'re Desperate", the two songs featured on their \'78 debut single, was the result of needing material to fill the album to capacity, but although the original recordings are superior, the versions here both tie well into the overarching theme of many newer songs on &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt;. Strong indications of infidelity suffuse the tightly-coiled tension of the superb "White Girl", which is probably the strongest song here, and Cervenka continually puts off a real conversation with her husband in "Some Other Time". All of this aside, however, this loose "concept" does not dominate every lyric line by any means, as they spit out the frenzied hobo fever dream of "Back 2 the Base" and the resolutely minimalist "I\'m Coming Over", the latter of which started life as a poem of Cervenka\'s that she\'d introduced to the band when they first came together. It\'s not quite as startling as its predecessor, but on the strength of these songs alone, X still sound as unique and at the top of their game as they ever would again.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And there\'s no question that things would never be quite the same again, after releasing their second album for the then-indie Slash Records. The considerable critical attention garnered by their first two records soon facilitated a move to the major Elektra Records in 1982, something that most of their original L.A. punk contemporaries never had the opportunity to do. In that sense, &lt;i&gt;Wild Gift&lt;/i&gt; also functions as a fantastic summation of X\'s first half-decade as a band. They would never make another record quite like it again.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/x-los-angeles-1980wild-gift-1981.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-zmVomnDYvOQ/Vd-mRbT5bxI/AAAAAAAAA_M/6FhdcKTDU5w/s72-c/XLosAngeles.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1527786260479418251</guid><pubdate>Thu, 27 Aug 2015 10:36:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-27T03:36:59.958-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Wipers "Youth of America" (1981)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-aS-d-2xy5H0/Vd7eUM2pY6I/AAAAAAAAA-0/gfPkgLOwbHM/s1600/Wipers_-_Youth_of_America.png" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-aS-d-2xy5H0/Vd7eUM2pY6I/AAAAAAAAA-0/gfPkgLOwbHM/s1600/Wipers_-_Youth_of_America.png" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;If the auspicious &lt;i&gt;Is This Real?&lt;/i&gt; immediately established that Greg Sage and the Wipers had a sound unlike any other punk rock band on either side of the Atlantic, 1981\'s follow-up &lt;i&gt;Youth of America&lt;/i&gt; was even more unique, branching out into more complex melodies and song structures while retaining the group\'s anthemic, scrappy energy and the angst-ridden themes of Sage\'s lyrics. At a time when most bands at the punk vanguard of the American underground were trying to strip the music down to its barest, most unrelenting essence, Sage (who for what it\'s worth was about 29 by the time of &lt;i&gt;Youth of America&lt;/i&gt;\'s release, much older than the era\'s hardcore kids) was pushing the sound and attitude in unprecedented new directions. His twangy, distorted guitar sound was already distinctive from the get-go, but here it\'s spitting out ever-more ambitious, spidery webs of harmonics, moving towards a kind of prog-punk that no one else was doing at the time. And yet most of the time, the rhythm section is still chugging along at the expected 4/4 pace behind all of this, grounding everything in impeccably tight, impressive songs. Everything is still in service of the song, and at just a brisk 30 minutes long, the album has not one tedious note or half-baked idea.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"Taking Too Long" kicks things off and indicates off the bat that this is a very different album from its predecessor. Another thing that changed this time out is that after a bad experience with the professional studio in which &lt;i&gt;Is This Real?&lt;/i&gt; had been cut, Sage took full control of the sound and circumstances of the second album\'s recording. As a result, &lt;i&gt;Youth of America&lt;/i&gt; has a much darker, eerier atmosphere than the first album, and "Taking Too Long" is a perfect example, with Sage\'s whispered verses and the group\'s restraining of the trademark chug, not to mention a piano that complements the (just-on-the-verge-of) cathartic choruses. The soaring power-pop "Can This Be" is the sole reprise of their earlier, more conventionally melodic sound, and the dramatic, six-plus-minute "When It\'s Over" is a stunning capper to the album\'s first half. The closing title track is the most talked-about part of the record, clocking in at an unheard-of ten-and-a-half minutes, and it\'s still startling to listen to it pulsing on pure manic urgency all the way through, never losing momentum as Sage calls out "it is time we rectify this now!" to the alienated, disaffected kids that were always his primary thematic concern in those early Wipers songs.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although 1983\'s ensuing &lt;i&gt;Over the Edge&lt;/i&gt;, which brought them back down to the sleek, pounding pop-punk anthems of &lt;i&gt;Is This Real?&lt;/i&gt;, would be just as strong an album, &lt;i&gt;Youth of America&lt;/i&gt; is without a doubt the Wipers\' masterpiece, a stunningly impressive piece of work that sounded like absolutely no other record of its time and would continue to reverberate across the spectrum of American underground rock for years to come. It\'s a doggone shame that even after the acknowledgement of the Wipers\' massive influence on the Pacific Northwest grunge-rock that would blow up the charts a decade later, &lt;i&gt;Youth of America &lt;/i&gt;(and much of Sage\'s work, with or without the Wipers in general) is still often overlooked.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/wipers-youth-of-america-1981.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-aS-d-2xy5H0/Vd7eUM2pY6I/AAAAAAAAA-0/gfPkgLOwbHM/s72-c/Wipers_-_Youth_of_America.png" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5100295336373510878</guid><pubdate>Fri, 14 Aug 2015 20:40:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-17T23:32:14.758-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Replacements "Sorry Ma, Forgot to Take Out the Trash" (1981)/"Stink" (1982)"Hootenanny" (1983)/"Let it Be" (1984)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NrMK1U5c21k/Vc1QmX6GRwI/AAAAAAAAA7M/baQrAcm7jbY/s1600/IMG_0347.JPG" imageanchor="1" style="margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="239" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NrMK1U5c21k/Vc1QmX6GRwI/AAAAAAAAA7M/baQrAcm7jbY/s320/IMG_0347.JPG" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I just picked up a copy of Rhino Entertainment\'s brand-new vinyl box set, which collects all four records The Replacements cut while on the Minnesota indie Twin/Tone. It\'s arrived at quite an interesting time, being as Paul Westerberg announced onstage at a concert of the reunited Replacements in June that it was to be their last show. It\'s obviously a fantastic coup for vinyl junkies everywhere to have these four records all in one box, after years of only being able to track down pricey individual copies of each of them, but it\'s also positively thrilling to hear them in this context, evolving from the drunk-punk snarl of their first album to the full-fledged masterwork &lt;i&gt;Let it Be&lt;/i&gt;, in the span of just three years no less. I was rather surprised to find out that I was the first person to buy a copy at the Record Collector, even though copies had only arrived about two days earlier. I thought, \'Shouldn\'t this thing be flying out of the store?\' These records (with the exception of the &lt;i&gt;Stink &lt;/i&gt;EP, which was recently reissued in standalone form) haven\'t been pressed on vinyl in over two decades, and one of them (1984\'s &lt;i&gt;Let it Be&lt;/i&gt;, their last for the label) happens to be one of the most widely beloved and critically acclaimed rock albums of the 80\'s. I\'ve seen old vinyl copies of it go for about the same price, on its own, as the cost of this entire set (about $80).
