But hey, maybe a few folks\'ll be swayed by a sweeping overview of all the material contained in this excellent, and much-needed set, which also doubles as an overview of the first phase in the career of a band who continue to be tremendously influential on any number of punk or alternative/indie rock artists to this day.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-5YPNcYfDOVI/Vc1W1tP-AqI/AAAAAAAAA7g/fOlpq1vbfSs/s1600/MI0001668088.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="314" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-5YPNcYfDOVI/Vc1W1tP-AqI/AAAAAAAAA7g/fOlpq1vbfSs/s320/MI0001668088.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;From the very beginning, The Replacements were an anomaly in the American punk underground of the early 80\'s. Although their earliest material certainly bespeaks a desire to be a part of the hardcore scene, they always played too loose, too sloppy, and above all else too sloshed to really fit comfortably into a scene that quickly adopted a rather absurd set of \'rules\' and an ostensibly humorless outlook. Obviously, 1981\'s &lt;i&gt;Sorry Ma, Forgot to Take Out the Trash&lt;/i&gt; is the polar opposite of Minor Threat\'s contemporary song/manifesto "Straight Edge", but in addition to that, there\'s none of the teeth-gnashing rage of Black Flag or the Circle Jerks. And for that matter, the band didn\'t possess the dense, sheet-metal sonic assault of their fellow Minnesotans H\xc3\xbcsker D\xc3\xbc, with whom they had something of a friendly rivalry and who are namechecked in "Somethin\' to D\xc3\xbc", a highlight of this debut. The Replacements sounded like a good-old-fashioned rock &amp;amp; roll bar band, the differences being that this one was made up of a bunch of kids who were all hopped up on punk rock fury and cheap booze, and who couldn\'t play their instruments anywhere near as \'professionally\' as your average bar band.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Side one is a relentless series of pummeling rockers, kicking off with the absolutely ferocious, cheerfully reckless "Takin\' a Ride" and rampaging through songs about the joys of irresponsibility, a crush on the girl that works at the store, getting bored with the bad weed one buys at an arena-rock show, and hating music cause it\'s "got too many notes". All spontaneous, unpretentious bursts of aggression concerning all manner of fleeting post-adolescent gripes, and all of it is a whole lot of fun. Then, at the beginning of its second half, comes the downright shockingly midtempo ballad "Johnny\'s Gonna Die", which is like Westerberg is writing a letter of concern to Johnny Thunders, the legendary former New York Dolls guitarist who was actually enough of a substance-abusing fuck-up to warrant concern from this notoriously fucked-up quartet. And truth be told, it doesn\'t sound like &lt;i&gt;that&lt;/i&gt; much of a wild departure, starting a trend that can be observed on all their Twin/Tone recordings where the \'Mats would regularly take stylistic detours, and rarely did it sound at all like they were trying to be musically diverse. Immediately afterward, they turn in another one of their fuck-up anthems, "Shiftless When Idle", and through yet more endlessly entertaining bursts of noise they manhandle until the record\'s over. Although the three other Replacements tended to have certain preferences for how the band should sound, Westerberg was clearly the type of songwriter who just liked what he liked; "Johnny\'s Gonna Die" was the first indication of that unwillingness to be tied down to any one sound that would manifest itself continuously in the remainder of his Replacements years, and beyond as a solo artist.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although the group, and Westerberg in particular, would obviously mature light-years away from this initial sound, &lt;i&gt;Sorry Ma &lt;/i&gt;is as perfect an encapsulation of the untamed, untrained impulse to just rock out, with no overwrought political or social baggage to be found, as any band could ever hope to get on tape. And so they were off to a great start, and they were just getting started.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/--eyEOW34svA/Vc2yVd5n7eI/AAAAAAAAA74/HlHellM1O1M/s1600/MI0001792149.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="310" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/--eyEOW34svA/Vc2yVd5n7eI/AAAAAAAAA74/HlHellM1O1M/s320/MI0001792149.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&amp;nbsp;This \'82 EP, or perhaps something of a mini-album, is essentially an adjunct to &lt;i&gt;Sorry Ma&lt;/i&gt;, overflowing with drunken, reckless abandon and songs with titles like "Fuck School", "God Damn Job" and "Dope Smokin\' Moron". The only real difference here is that it has a much fuller, more assaulting sound than the first album, and that much of it approximates straight hardcore more accurately than its predecessor As even the cover of &lt;i&gt;Stink &lt;/i&gt;indicates off the bat, the opening anthem "Kids Don\'t Follow" is the obvious high point of these eight songs; it might even be better than most of the bona-fide anthems that appeared on the first album, but everything else is equally enjoyable, and the whole thing avoids overstaying its welcome at a brisk fifteen minutes long. Amid the raw rock &amp;amp; roll fury is one particularly atypical track, the moody, angst-ridden "Go". Even moreso than the earlier "Johnny\'s Gonna Die", this song explicitly points the way towards the band\'s introspective, emotionally complex future. Fun fact, about the recording of Minneapolis cops shutting down a show that appears at the beginning of the EP: the guy who you can hear yelling "Fuck you, man!" in the background is Dave Pirner, the future frontman of Soul Asylum, who were long considered the little brother band of Minneapolis heavyweights like the \'Mats until they eventually hit it big in the alt-rock rat race of the early 90\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-ciOryWRCxqI/Vc22QdA7_1I/AAAAAAAAA8I/WBq2a2MGe1s/s1600/MI0001662618.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="317" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-ciOryWRCxqI/Vc22QdA7_1I/AAAAAAAAA8I/WBq2a2MGe1s/s320/MI0001662618.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;By the time 1983\'s &lt;i&gt;Hootenanny &lt;/i&gt;rolled around, a sizable portion of the American independent underground was growing increasingly frustrated with the predominance of strictly regimented hardcore punk, sowing the seeds of the wildly diverse "Amerindie" landscape that would flourish from the mid-80\'s onward. Unlike a lot of musicians and music fans at the time, The Replacements had never actually cared about whatever sonic limitations punk rock (hardcore or otherwise) put on itself. In that light, it\'s easy to see their second proper album as the moment where they gave up any pretense of trying to fit in with the hardcore scene, seeing that a substantially larger "alternative" audience was now more than willing to listen to a band that didn\'t restrict their playing to breakneck speed-riffage.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In reality, though, that\'s much too contrived and deliberate an idea when you listen to &lt;i&gt;Hootenanny&lt;/i&gt;; by all accounts, the group played the entire recording process off as a laugh, tossing out songs of all different types without much care, a ragbag of crazy notions thrown up in the air to see where they\'d land. In other words, it may be the most diverse collection of songs they\'d yet come up with, but &lt;i&gt;London Calling &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Sandanista!&lt;/i&gt; this ain\'t. The eclecticism never comes off as "ambitious", and the whole thing plays a lot differently than a famously sprawling work like the aforementioned Clash albums, or &lt;i&gt;The White Album&lt;/i&gt;, or H\xc3\xbcsker D\xc3\xbc\'s later &lt;i&gt;Zen Arcade&lt;/i&gt;. The title track and sleeve art add up to one big piss-take of folk music, with the bandmembers swapping instruments for an inebriated, bluesy shuffle. That opening title track may not be the funniest parody or the most compelling slice of music you\'ll ever hear, but it perfectly sets the tone for an album that\'s truly all over the map, which in turn perfectly reflects the notoriously wayward, erratic nature of The Replacements\' live performances.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The hellacious "Run It" and "You Lose" would fit in comfortably on their previous two releases, and Westerberg turns in yet another of his classic mid-tempo rock anthems with "Color Me Impressed", while the jaunty rockabilly of "Take Me Down to the Hospital" is another fine showcase for The Replacements\' often overlooked aptitude as a bloozy boogie band, a particular proclivity which would soon all but disappear from their musical character. A couple of these songs don\'t really sound anything like anything else they\'d later do, most notably "Willpower", a downright ominous, moody track with Westerberg\'s vocals drowning in a pool of almost dub-like echo. No one on the planet earth would\'ve expected these rowdy boys to come up with something that The Cure might do, but the real standout here just might be "Within Your Reach", which is even more startlingly atypical. Westerberg plays all the instruments here, a drum machine (!) and synth (!!) accompanying sparse electric guitar, and on top of this most unusual backdrop is the most personal, vulnerable lyric he\'d put on a \'Mats album yet. It\'s the kind of wounded, heartfelt song he would perfect several times over, which eventually became the songwriter\'s primary focus, and here was its earliest manifestation. Many other tracks are given over to odd, amusing toss-offs like the haphazard Beatles/Chubby Checker pastiche/pisstake "Mr. Whirly" and "Lovelines", where Westerberg reads saucy personal ads from the newspaper over a sleazy mock-jazz backing.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It all closes out with the country-fried "Treatment Bound", a fantastically grumpy tune about "gettin\' nowhere, quick as we know how", in terms of popularity. In hindsight, it\'s quite an ironic cap-off. As uneven as it obviously is, the album was a crucial turning point for the band, both in terms of their music and their national popularity; &lt;i&gt;Hootenanny &lt;/i&gt;was their first release to garner any widespread attention from college radio, and it led to their first proper national tour in mid-\'83. The seamless wild-card sound and the increased critical attention would set the stage for their true artistic breakthrough, which, unbeknownst to anyone at the time, would eventually hasten the end of their Twin/Tone years and grease the wheels on the expressway to their signing with Warner Bros. Records.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NOJo5UY9v-Y/Vc5Bo9H4i2I/AAAAAAAAA8g/h-dUya2I-Fw/s1600/MI0002079514.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="310" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NOJo5UY9v-Y/Vc5Bo9H4i2I/AAAAAAAAA8g/h-dUya2I-Fw/s320/MI0002079514.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;If &lt;i&gt;Hootenanny &lt;/i&gt;was a surprising, eccentric effort, pointing the way to a band that had a lot more to offer musically than most observers would\'ve guessed from &lt;i&gt;Sorry Ma &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Stink&lt;/i&gt;, 1984\'s damn-near-as-it perfect &lt;i&gt;Let it Be&lt;/i&gt; was a stunning fulfillment of all the promise that The Replacements and Westerberg as a songwriter had previously displayed. All of Westerberg\'s growth as a songwriter, the band\'s growth as musicians, and all of their cards both musically and aesthetically are summed up here. Songs like "Androgynous", "Sixteen Blue" and "Answering Machine" are the kind of things most songwriters would absolutely kill to be able to come up with, and here they sit with songs like "Gary\'s Got a Boner" and a speedy punk blast about the sixteen-year-old bass player having his tonsils removed. And all of it absolutely sounds like it was meant to be here, on the same album; part of its genius is how a song like "Androgynous" can be credibly sandwiched between "Tommy Gets His Tonsils Out" and a fine but far from essential cover of Kiss\' "Black Diamond". Because of this quality, this refusal to take everything so god-damned seriously even when baring the most vulnerable parts of their soul, &lt;i&gt;Let it Be &lt;/i&gt;is unique in the annals of classic, much-ballyhooed rock albums.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1984 was nothing short of a landmark year for the burgeoning, worldwide alternative/indie rock underground, yielding other fantastic records like the Minutemen\'s &lt;i&gt;Double Nickels on the Dime&lt;/i&gt;, H\xc3\xbcsker D\xc3\xbc\'s &lt;i&gt;Zen Arcade&lt;/i&gt;, the Meat Puppets\' &lt;i&gt;II&lt;/i&gt;, R.E.M.\'s &lt;i&gt;Reckoning&lt;/i&gt;, The Smiths\' eponymous debut and Echo &amp;amp; the Bunnymen\'s &lt;i&gt;Ocean Rain&lt;/i&gt;. In true Replacements fashion, &lt;i&gt;Let it Be &lt;/i&gt;isn\'t comparable to any of those other albums. The frequently applied (and very much deserved) "masterpiece" tag will inevitably indicate a major, ambitious piece of work, but despite the unprecedented poetry of Westerberg\'s best songs here, it certainly doesn\'t feel like it was designed as any sort of grand statement. The jangly, buoyant pop of "I Will Dare", featuring an exquisite mandolin part from R.E.M.\'s Peter Buck, kicks things off with the most mature song the band had come up with up to that point, but they immediately follow it with the joyous Eddie Cochran-esque rocker "Favorite Thing", which may be a wide-eyed love song but also boasts the infectious refrain of "I don\'t give a single shit", showing that this bunch of rough-housing, hard-drinking malcontents hadn\'t outgrown their wild ways one bit. If they had, &lt;i&gt;Let it Be&lt;/i&gt; wouldn\'t be as beloved by so many people or be as uniquely relatable to succeeding generations of teenagers to the present day.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;When he sang about being "Unsatisfied", about the pain, confusion and boredom of youth in "Sixteen Blue", and when he howled "I hate your answering machine" over only the sound of his own distorted electric guitar (not to mention loops of that faceless telephone operator giving pre-recorded directives), Paul Westerberg officially became one of rock &amp;amp; roll\'s true poets of alienation and disenchantment. Few other rock songwriters have ever been as painfully accurate about teenage growing pains, and what\'s more, fewer still have been able to render them in such a fashion that listeners can connect emotionally with the material long into their adulthood. As it would turn out later, his band wasn\'t always eager to follow along his path of introspective songwriting, but here they follow his lead with surprisingly restrained playing and just the right amount of sloppy charm. A mark of true greatness is when an album captures a peak in creativity as lightning in a bottle, and as unlikely as many would\'ve found it when they first stumbled onto a stage back in 1980, The Replacements would become one of the few bands of any era to successfully achieve that. For both band and lead singer alike, this would remain their finest hour, and there\'s a reason that it\'s still regarded as one of the greatest and most enduring rock albums of the 1980\'s, and for all time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/the-replacements-sorry-ma-forgot-to.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-NrMK1U5c21k/Vc1QmX6GRwI/AAAAAAAAA7M/baQrAcm7jbY/s72-c/IMG_0347.JPG" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7602955945201656194</guid><pubdate>Sun, 02 Aug 2015 06:19:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-08-01T23:44:43.109-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Joy Division "Unknown Pleasures" (1979)/"Closer" (1980)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-nxyetXT6fn0/Vb2A_dELziI/AAAAAAAAA4U/Vb-NrhlS2yc/s1600/330px-Unknown_Pleasures_Joy_Division_LP_sleeve.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="319" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-nxyetXT6fn0/Vb2A_dELziI/AAAAAAAAA4U/Vb-NrhlS2yc/s320/330px-Unknown_Pleasures_Joy_Division_LP_sleeve.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Coming from a band that emerged from the gray, dreary landscape of Manchester, in a time before it was a notorious music mecca on par with London or Liverpool, it should come as little surprise that Joy Division\'s two proper albums sound like they came out of nothing that was happening in the aftermath of punk at the end of the 70\'s. Even in the creatively fertile, musically diverse post-punk era, the first of them, 1979\'s &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures&lt;/i&gt;, sounded nothing like anything being made by Mancunian peers like The Fall or the Buzzcocks, or for that matter any other band in England or America. It has endured and continued to be a huge influence on new bands like few other records of its time, but no one has ever made anything quite like it in over a third of a century since its release.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The two albums, both released in the month of July at the beginning and end of a single 12-month period, form an astonishingly perfect arc in the trajectory of the doomed lead singer Ian Curtis, and as part one, &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures &lt;/i&gt;is a perfect document of a young band that had progressed in quick succession from the raw, yet cerebral, punk-rock precursor Warsaw into a more developed, dynamic sound that was entirely their own and would be enormously influential. Curtis\' lyrics are relentlessly grim and filled with psychic tumult, but despite the album\'s gloomy, spacious sonic atmosphere (courtesy of producer Martin Hannett), they seem to take a backseat here to the palpable energy and adventurousness of the band itself. The precise, Motorik drumming of Stephen Morris and Peter Hook\'s signature, melody-carrying bass lines are overlaid with Bernard Sumner\'s heavy riffing, from the deceptive warmth of the opening "Disorder"; the jittery epileptic dance of "She\'s Lost Control"; the rip-roaring stampede of "Interzone"; the death march of "Day of the Lords" and "I Remember Nothing". From beginning to end, this was the sound of the future, and folks have only recently begun to really catch up to it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In light of Curtis\' suicide at the age of 23, just a year after these recordings were made, their debut album is above all else foreboding and suggestive of a troubled mind, never fully revealing the true nature of its darkness. His ghost haunts everything Joy Division ever did in hindsight, and this album in particular, as Kurt Cobain\'s suicide hangs over even Nirvana\'s least depressive songs and gives everything an ominous feel because of what would eventually transpire. One can imagine how mysterious and indeterminable it must\'ve seemed to those who heard it upon its release, before Curtis was anointed one of rock\'s quintessential tortured souls. He hadn\'t yet reached the bottom of his hole, but he had stumbled upon a unique gift for detailing despair with an inner-directed sense of rage and frustration, a quiet storm that was no easy fit with the external fury and sociopolitical rhetoric of punk, but supported by fantastically exciting music that was deeply indebted to punk\'s unbridled aggression. The end result of all this, despite some initial grumblings by bandmembers about Hannett\'s production work, is one of the great rock albums, for sure, and most definitely one of the very best and most auspicious debuts of all time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-bi6JKsBGCTs/Vb2eQJb2vzI/AAAAAAAAA4o/ARxVJeUIGsI/s1600/330px-Joy_Division_Closer.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-bi6JKsBGCTs/Vb2eQJb2vzI/AAAAAAAAA4o/ARxVJeUIGsI/s320/330px-Joy_Division_Closer.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Upon release as Factory Records\' very first long-player, &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures&lt;/i&gt; was not a particularly big seller even by indie label standards and was not immediately recognized as the great, tremendously important work of art that it is. But throughout the remainder of 1979, Joy Division\'s profile steadily grew, as an increasingly numbered following of gray-overcoated fans latched onto the band and they began touring more. At the same time, the young life of Ian Curtis, who had obviously never been that much of a happy camper, really started to unravel, brought on by an ever-worsening case of epilepsy and an increasingly complicated romantic life.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The sense of shame and failure he\'d always been plagued by only increased as his medical condition progressed to where his \'fits\' became uncontrollable, and would even occur onstage as he was performing. Doctor\'s orders to lead a decidedly sedate and complacent life didn\'t jibe with the physically expressive lead singer of an exceptionally aggressive, intense band whose popularity was increasing at the dawn of the 80\'s. While his songwriting had always been startlingly dark and morose, it became much more overtly desperate and desolate in the songs Joy Division recorded for their second album in March 1980. If &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures&lt;/i&gt; was the suggestion of a very troubled, disturbed creative mind, &lt;i&gt;Closer&lt;/i&gt; is a still-unsettling, vividly personal representation of that mind and the man\'s final descent into what he viewed as an inescapable and overarching despair, the closing denouement before the ultimate demise.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With Hannett once again behind the boards, the uncompromisingly harsh, brutally despondent &lt;i&gt;Closer&lt;/i&gt; is a much different affair from the ethereal, otherworldly omen of &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures&lt;/i&gt;, right from the start with the queasy chainsaw guitars and primal, tribal percussion of the opening "Atrocity Exhibition". Here, Curtis is describing a twisted theater of pain-as-entertainment, perhaps anticipating the scores of writers and fans who would try to figure him out forevermore, sending out to them his own personal invitation of "This is the way, step inside..." Then there\'s the synthpop of "Isolation", musically speaking a prescient foreshadowing of Steve Morris, Bernie Sumner and Pete Hook\'s imminent New Order future, where Curtis nakedly admits "I\'m ashamed of the things I\'ve been put through, I\'m ashamed of the person I am." Joy Division never rocked as hard or cut so incisively as in the savage "Colony", and the band\'s considerable growth as musicians comes to the forefront with the tension and not-quite-release of the masterfully understated "Heart and Soul". The surging, emotionally overwhelming "Twenty Four Hours" spells out his fruitless search for a rescue from the darkness in as plainspoken and exacting terms as he would ever manage: "Just for one moment, thought I\'d found my way/Destiny unfolded, I watched it slip away." This is the last stand of that earlier-mentioned internal rage and frustration, subsiding forever into the hopeless, soul-sucking abyss of "The Eternal", where he imagines his own funeral procession with little doubt that life is soon to imitate art for him. One would be hard-pressed to name a more devastating conclusion to most any album than the epochal "Decades"; the spectral, synthesizer-driven music is almost a sweet lullaby in comparison with some of the stuff that\'s been heard earlier, and then again also the backdrop of a bitter requiem for Curtis\' soon-to-be lost young life, and a loss of innocence in the overall ugliness of the outside world, one that the singer was now completely convinced he just couldn\'t exist in anymore.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In the annals of popular music, there aren\'t a whole lot of places you can go that are darker than where Joy Division took rock &amp;amp; roll on their two masterful albums, and in the deepest, darkest trench of rock history, Joy Division are on an artistic par with the likes of The Velvet Underground and The Doors in how they shaped this into some of the most compelling, striking and influential music of its time, and for all time. With all their doom and gloom, they were never corny or mawkishly maudlin; their high drama was not a bombastic embellishment, which was lost on a great many of the band\'s goth-rock progeny. As tempting as it is to view Ian Curtis as one of rock\'s great tortured geniuses, as he\'s been built up so convincingly to be ever since the day he hung himself in the early hours of May 18th, 1980, one would do well to remember the man as Stephen Morris once described him: "An ordinary bloke just like you or me, liked a bit of a laugh, liked a bit of a joke." This ordinary, working-class lad also just so happened to be one of the great singing poets of depression and desolation, who succumbed to mounting personal problems and cut short a life that could\'ve gone quite differently if he\'d just realized that even what seemed unendurable was in reality quite temporary. When you keep that in mind, and in spite of the cold, distant, mechanical sounds the group was synonymous with, the unshakeable humanity of Joy Division\'s rather small, damn-near-as-it perfect body of work fully reveals itself and becomes all the more extraordinary.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/08/joy-division-unknown-pleasures.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-nxyetXT6fn0/Vb2A_dELziI/AAAAAAAAA4U/Vb-NrhlS2yc/s72-c/330px-Unknown_Pleasures_Joy_Division_LP_sleeve.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8441900201356423830</guid><pubdate>Fri, 24 Jul 2015 03:52:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-23T20:52:41.539-07:00</atom:updated><title>Sonic Youth "Sister" (1987)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-pL_MhEkDv1c/VbGst3n1WmI/AAAAAAAAA14/RggmupXU0hI/s1600/MI0002799247.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="313" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-pL_MhEkDv1c/VbGst3n1WmI/AAAAAAAAA14/RggmupXU0hI/s320/MI0002799247.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;While it may have seemed that 1986\'s &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt; represented Sonic Youth\'s breakthrough in terms of their substantial growth as song composers and musicians, the stunning follow-up &lt;i&gt;Sister &lt;/i&gt;was nothing short of a Great Leap Forward moment, a seismic shift in the feel and cadence of their music that perhaps for the first time made them sound as much a great rock band as a great avant-garde project. Leaving behind the gloom and doom that hovered over &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Sister &lt;/i&gt;crackles with excitement and brute force; not only does the record sound much warmer and more approachable than their previous recordings, on a pure sound level, the band sounds more relaxed and confident than ever before, like they know they can now merge rock and the avant-garde seamlessly like it was meant to be, and do it like no one else but them could. Lee Ranaldo and Thurston Moore continue to spit out sheets of white noise from their guitars, but the razor-sharp, concise song structures whip them into shape and make the abrasive noise only that much more bracing.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They had already proven with songs like "Expressway to Yr. Skull", the previous album\'s showstopping closer, that they were developing a unique gift for incorporating pop melody into their experimental music in a way that could, at the same time, produce earworm hooks and still manage not to sound at all like conventional pop-rock. But the idea had not yet produced a song as simultaneously memorable, yet completely left-field, as "Schizophrenia", which starts off with Thurston singing a solitary, amusing verse about his friend\'s crazy sister and sounding like the group\'s most straightforward song to date, before it soon divulges into a dissonant instrumental break. The basic melody returns, somewhat, but not the simple three-chord pattern of its earlier section, slowly morphing into a wash of droning, needling guitars, propulsive drums and ambient feedback without abandoning the key or the original melodic tangent of the song. All in all, it\'s the most impressive thing the group had come up with yet, and it\'s just the opening track on what was the most impressive album of their career to date.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Sonic\'s earlier work had already proven that bassist Kim Gordon was as interesting and unique a songwriter as her then-husband Moore, but the clattering, ominous "Pacific Coast Highway", one of her two contributions to the record, boasts one of the most unsettling lyrics that any of the band\'s three songwriters (Ranaldo, Gordon and Moore) had ever penned, or ever would again. It\'s something of an anomaly on an album that showcases the group sounding a lot more upbeat than they had been beforehand, which is clearly illustrated by the unabashedly melodic rocker "Tuff Gnarl" and their blistering take on "Hotwire My Heart" (an old \'76 punk-era obscurity by the long-forgotten Frisco band Crime), not to mention the gossamer "Kotton Krown", the first time Gordon and Moore shared the lead vocals. As rewarding and powerful as the group could be when delving into the atmospheric darkness that informed their previous two albums, it made for a remarkably refreshing (and perhaps even necessary) change of pace when they cranked up the amps and kicked out the jams on &lt;i&gt;Sister&lt;/i&gt;. While all of their previous work contained at least a hint or two that this band was something special, this was the first time they made an out and out masterpiece, one of their only two albums that you can really say are perfect, and in hindsight it\'s remarkable to think that the other one of those two was just around the corner and would be an even greater artistic triumph.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/sonic-youth-sister-1987.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-pL_MhEkDv1c/VbGst3n1WmI/AAAAAAAAA14/RggmupXU0hI/s72-c/MI0002799247.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7307938109887225752</guid><pubdate>Thu, 23 Jul 2015 22:45:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-23T15:45:33.838-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Sonic Youth "EVOL" (1986)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-9CVut-bzaoc/VbFQenA0kVI/AAAAAAAAA1k/2j7VEIu11sI/s1600/MI0000039821.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-9CVut-bzaoc/VbFQenA0kVI/AAAAAAAAA1k/2j7VEIu11sI/s320/MI0000039821.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising&lt;/i&gt;, Sonic Youth took their first steps toward making their avant-garde bursts of noise and feedback into proper song-form compositions. For 1986\'s &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt;, their first album for SST Records and the first to feature Steve Shelley on drums, the group were inhabiting the same eerie, mysterious atmosphere of its predecessor, but with far greater compositional focus. If &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon&lt;/i&gt; almost felt like one long piece at times, &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt; was most certainly a collection of songs, and a few of them are quite a bit better than the ones they\'d recorded previously. The addition of Shelley, who had once been the drummer of the wacked-out Michigan hardcore band Crucifucks, proved to play a huge role in making them a much tighter band than ever before, giving them greater dynamics and strengthening their overall performances. Where previous drummer Bob Bert had a rather caveman-like, trash-can percussion style that could occasionally sound a bit hollowed-out, Shelley was precise and lethal behind the kit, whether exploding into manic fills or tribally pounding those toms, and as uncompromisingly brutal as Sonic Youth had been before, this helped make them into a more visceral, physically powerful band.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising &lt;/i&gt;and the concurrently released "Flower" single certainly both had a hazy, dreamy feel, "Tom Violence" and especially "Shadow of a Doubt" feel much more like dreams (or nightmares) unto themselves, both in terms of the sound and the newly developed song structures. The latter is literally a&lt;i&gt;bout &lt;/i&gt;a dream, as Kim Gordon whispers its first lines "Met a stranger on a train/Bumped right into me/Swear I didn\'t mean it/Swear it wasn\'t meant to be"; there\'s no indication as to whether it\'s a good or bad dream, but "You kill me and I\'ll kill her" certainly gives one the impression that great violence lies underneath a layer of misremembered details and self-reassuring platitudes. Midway through this extraordinarily moody, evocative piece, the music explodes from the tense restraint of its verses, as Kim desperately shouts "No!/Swear it was just a dream!" over the clatter of sound, before returning to the eerie calm from whence it began and suddenly petering out. Elsewhere, the swirling, psychedelic "Green Light" and the epic closer "Expressway to Yr. Skull" (which is quite amusingly titled as "Madonna, Sean and Me" on the back cover, and "The Crucifixion of Sean Penn" on the lyrics sheet) bring the noisy, abrasive Sonic Youth closer to melodic pop than they\'d ever been, without losing one bit of their inherently challenging, no-wave heritage or sounding at all like conventional pop songs.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This very unique dichotomy and distinctive sound, which is essentially introduced in full shape for the first time here on album #3, would become a well-known and oft-copied trademark by the time album #6 rolled around (1990\'s major-label debut &lt;i&gt;Goo&lt;/i&gt;, to be exact). But in 1986, it was absolutely unprecedented, and would be a tremendously important influence on the development of what was to become known as "alternative rock". Seeing as it also happens to simply be the best album they\'d made up to this point in their career, &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt; is nothing short of a landmark release for both the band and rock &amp;amp; roll in general. And to think that they were just getting started...</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/sonic-youth-evol-1986.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-9CVut-bzaoc/VbFQenA0kVI/AAAAAAAAA1k/2j7VEIu11sI/s72-c/MI0000039821.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5785369484552888056</guid><pubdate>Fri, 17 Jul 2015 01:07:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-22T19:18:24.043-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Sonic Youth "Bad Moon Rising" (1985)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-i6fEJXCCQXQ/VahFgooD5oI/AAAAAAAAA0Y/s166Ux2nQKA/s1600/330px-SonicYouthBadMoonRising.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-i6fEJXCCQXQ/VahFgooD5oI/AAAAAAAAA0Y/s166Ux2nQKA/s320/330px-SonicYouthBadMoonRising.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Essentially a transitional album, 1985\'s &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising&lt;/i&gt; is one of Sonic Youth\'s most curious and unique outings. Nothing else in what was to become a very extensive discography would ever sound quite like it again. At this stage, they were very short on melody and "proper" song form, and long on treated instruments, ambient noise and atonal squall. They had not yet developed their distinctive avant-rock sound, the one that would make its initial appearance on their next album &lt;i&gt;EVOL &lt;/i&gt;and which they would expound on with the brilliant &lt;i&gt;Sister &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Daydream Nation&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With its near-total lack of conventional song structures, &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising&lt;/i&gt; is typical of that early phase. What makes it different from their previous two albums, though, aside from being a lot more dynamic than the skittering post-punk of their eponymous debut or the throttling brutality of &lt;i&gt;Confusion is Sex&lt;/i&gt;, is that it\'s actually something of a concept album about the dark, violent underbelly of America, conceived at a time in which the country was in turmoil thanks to Ronald Reagan\'s severely regressive socioeconomic policies, but its popular culture was in the middle of a particularly nationalistic phase. In other words, the catchphrase may have been "It\'s Morning in America", but coming from and looking at the desperate, dangerous, poverty-stricken New York City that birthed them, Sonic Youth couldn\'t help but point out that it was in fact well past midnight, even if they certainly weren\'t going to do it by shouting "Fuck Reagan!" over a bunch of 20-second thrash riffs, and by no means is it a "political" album. &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While it\'s true that their next three albums would undoubtedly be loaded with better and more surprising material, &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising&lt;/i&gt; is the best representation of the spooky, atmospheric side that their music always had. In its original form, eight songs mostly stitched together by dissonant instrumental segues, it plays like a hazy, foreboding dream, laden with mystery and ominous intent, laced with imagery of sex and death, because as Kim Gordon saw it, "America is really about death". A plangent guitar intro leads into the galloping "Brave Men Run", wherein Kim Gordon sings "Brave men run from me" like she\'s Lizzy Borden\'s murderous ghost, and then husband Thurston Moore takes the lead vocals with the droning dirge "Society is a Hole". The lengthy, hypnotic "I Love Her All the Time" is ostensibly Sonic\'s twisted version of a love song, but it actually belies a disturbing undercurrent of sexual dominance and submission, a theme which Gordon expounds on with the equally chilling "Ghost Bitch".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;At the end of the album proper, the chain-link of distant, half-remembered nightmares is broken when "Justice is Might" fades out completely and the chaotic "Death Valley \'69" comes crashing in. A primordial duet between Moore and Lydia Lunch, it brings all of the album\'s mordant themes to a fiery culmination with a slithering, sexually-charged evocation of the Manson family murders. It\'s the closest thing to a real rock &amp;amp; roll song here, and when released as their very first 45 RPM single, it would be their first song to make an impression on college radio, pointing the way towards the equally somber but much more song-oriented &lt;i&gt;EVOL&lt;/i&gt; that was to follow. In the end, &lt;i&gt;Bad Moon Rising&lt;/i&gt; certainly isn\'t as flat-out astonishing as its three successors, either in its original form or with the four fine bonus tracks tacked onto the end as has been the case with all CD/digital issues since 1993. But it creates a forbidding, nocturnal tapestry of sound that the group never quite approached again, and would indeed remain perhaps their darkest, most overtly foreboding work, which amounts to one of the most conceptually interesting and unique records in what was to become quite a large discography.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/sonic-youth-bad-moon-rising-1985.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-i6fEJXCCQXQ/VahFgooD5oI/AAAAAAAAA0Y/s166Ux2nQKA/s72-c/330px-SonicYouthBadMoonRising.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1263253883637110086</guid><pubdate>Tue, 14 Jul 2015 18:34:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-14T11:37:55.799-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Buzzcocks "Singles Going Steady" (1979)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-TkSOCN_3vVc/VaVPBsOKKrI/AAAAAAAAAz8/4-0QL0IvYi0/s1600/MI0000619095.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-TkSOCN_3vVc/VaVPBsOKKrI/AAAAAAAAAz8/4-0QL0IvYi0/s320/MI0000619095.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;While the Buzzcocks certainly managed several above-average long-players in their brief initial run from 1976 to 1980, the format in which they really excelled as one of the very best punk bands to come out of the United Kingdom (or anywhere else, really) was on a string of near-perfect, classic singles. All of them are collected, split between A-sides on the first side and B-sides on the second on the original vinyl, in &lt;i&gt;Singles Going Steady&lt;/i&gt;, which is easily one of the greatest singles or greatest-hits compilations ever assembled, and it actually plays much more like a \'proper\' album than a compilation.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The LP leads off with the smashing "Orgasm Addict", a sneering holdover from the group\'s earliest days when they were fronted by Howard Devoto, but guitarist Pete Shelley\'s impeccable pop sense soon took hold when Devoto left on the eve of the release of the groundbreaking &lt;i&gt;Spiral Scratch &lt;/i&gt;EP, and it\'s evident in the power-pop classic "What Do I Get?" From there is the effervescent "I Don\'t Mind", "Love You More", and the mini-masterpiece "Ever Fallen in Love?", which is quite simply one of the greatest and most timeless songs to come out of the entire punk/new wave revolution and stands as the Buzzcocks\' finest moment.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;One might think a song of such stature would dwarf everything that follows, but don\'t be daft; even the lesser-known B-sides ("Whatever Happened To?", "Noise Annoys", "Just Lust", et cetera) are compelling slices of atomic pop music. Another one of them, "Why Can\'t I Touch It?", also reveals a neglected side of the band that one would never expect from the masters of post-punk pop. Clocking in at an astonishing six and a half-minutes, it\'s an obvious homage to the repetitive, hypnotic grooves of German avant-rockers Can, Shelley, as it turns out, having always adored Krautrock just as much as romantic pop. Slow, droning, ponderous, it actually manages not to sound inappropriate or at all out of place amidst the all-pervading rush of pop-punk bliss. No doubt, this has much to do with it being the penultimate track in sequence, and just before the closing "Something\'s Gone Wrong Again", which itself is much more menacing, snarling rock than the rest of the album. But it also speaks to how surprisingly diverse the band were, and how they were clearly never afraid to dig into other sonic pastures that had little to do with the sound that made them famous. In their own way, these last two atypical songs are, as a matter of fact, as good as any of the more well-known cuts here.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If &lt;i&gt;Singles Going Steady &lt;/i&gt;feels more of a proper LP than just a compilation, then it would also have to be their best album, hands-down, and it has stood the test of time to become ultimately every bit as influential on countless successive waves of bands as &lt;i&gt;Never Mind the Bollocks&lt;/i&gt;, or the first Clash album, or anything by the Ramones for that matter. And not just on "punk" bands, either: everyone from The Fall to The Smiths to Nirvana, Green Day, Oasis and beyond owes a bit of gratitude to the dynamite music created by this classic quartet.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/buzzcocks-singles-going-steady-1979.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-TkSOCN_3vVc/VaVPBsOKKrI/AAAAAAAAAz8/4-0QL0IvYi0/s72-c/MI0000619095.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3107772263921766491</guid><pubdate>Thu, 09 Jul 2015 00:27:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-08T17:27:56.110-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pink Floyd "The Piper at the Gates of Dawn" (1967)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Hre-V4QQej8/VZ2zFULf1xI/AAAAAAAAAy4/FOidEJgcvvU/s1600/PinkFloyd-album-piperatthegatesofdawn_300.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Hre-V4QQej8/VZ2zFULf1xI/AAAAAAAAAy4/FOidEJgcvvU/s1600/PinkFloyd-album-piperatthegatesofdawn_300.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Pink Floyd\'s first album, and the only one to be recorded with the enigmatic Syd Barrett as their leader and primary songwriter, stands apart from the rest of the group\'s later discography in a way that few rock debuts do. When they emerged as part of a distinctly British psychedelic scene in the mid-60\'s, with unique sonic elements like Rick Wright\'s keyboards and Barrett\'s distinctive guitar sound, they were at once more menacing and boldly avant-garde than the majority of their British and American psychedelic counterparts, and on the other hand they weren\'t yet the space-rockers of contemporary yore. The Pink Floyd as heard here, and on their British hit singles "See Emily Play" and "Arnold Layne", is well and truly a different band from the one that would later create &lt;i&gt;Dark Side of the Moon&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Wish You Were Here &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;The Wall&lt;/i&gt;, and not just because of the obvious swapping of Barrett for David Gilmour that occurred shortly after the release of &lt;i&gt;The Piper at the Gates of Dawn&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Nearly every major rock &amp;amp; roll band had already begun to dip their toes into unprecedented sonic and lyrical territory by 1967, but the ominous outer-limits rumble of "Astronomy Domine" was something else entirely and sounded like just about nothing else that had come before it. It\'s definitely quite melodic and has audible roots in the otherworldly, echoing sound of early-60\'s&amp;nbsp; instrumental surf music, but it\'s also much eerier and creepier than most anything in the Top Ten of either the American or British pop charts at the time. The slightly more conventional "Lucifer Sam", whose rocking riff sounds a little like the old "Batman" TV theme, is another great example of the dichotomy between Syd Barrett\'s childlike whimsy and the ethereal noise created by the band and his guitar itself. 1966 and \'67 were banner years for rock albums boasting a single barrier-crashing, ten-plus-minute opus, and the epic instrumental "Interstellar Overdrive" might just be the very best one among that lot. It was the first recorded example of how the Floyd did more than nearly any other British band to pioneer the extended psychedelic "freakout"; it ends up sounding like a very lengthy version of something Dick Dale might have done in the early 60\'s, if he and his backing musicians had played much, much slower and included spooky organ work.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The strange confusion between cutesy psych-pop and the sheer lunacy that the group (and of course Barrett in particular) constantly hints at beneath the surface of the songs culminates in perhaps the wackiest song, "Bike", which seems like the Floyd\'s catchiest and most cheerfully inane number yet until it abruptly ends and enters into musique-concrete territory with a 90-second coda. This parade of complete randomness, and the album itself, concludes with what might well be the honking of diseased mallards. It\'s interesting to note that &lt;i&gt;The Piper at the Gates of Dawn&lt;/i&gt; was recorded at EMI Studios at the same time The Beatles were doing their work on &lt;i&gt;Sgt. Pepper\'s Lonely Hearts Club Band &lt;/i&gt;in the same place, and perhaps not coincidentally, both albums end with the music giving way to wacky sound effects. And so, &lt;i&gt;The Piper &lt;/i&gt;sounds from beginning to end like nothing the band would ever record again, and with that same token like absolutely nothing else around at the time of its release, one of the greatest and most inventive albums from an era in which the band had unbelievable competition in the innovation department of pop music.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/pink-floyd-piper-at-gates-of-dawn-1967.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-Hre-V4QQej8/VZ2zFULf1xI/AAAAAAAAAy4/FOidEJgcvvU/s72-c/PinkFloyd-album-piperatthegatesofdawn_300.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6792179438095763278</guid><pubdate>Mon, 06 Jul 2015 05:59:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-05T23:02:29.265-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Todd Rundgren "Runt" (1970)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-CxYq8MaA6Hk/VZoL-q_llhI/AAAAAAAAAxs/7YuZAUJaqnk/s1600/Rundgren_Runt_Cover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-CxYq8MaA6Hk/VZoL-q_llhI/AAAAAAAAAxs/7YuZAUJaqnk/s1600/Rundgren_Runt_Cover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The story goes that Todd didn\'t feel ready to immediately become a full-fledged solo artist after the breakup of The Nazz, so he formed an ad-hoc band called Runt, with the brothers Hunt &amp;amp; Tony Sales supplying the rhythm section in what was to be the first of many memorable supporting roles alongside other rock icons in years to come, and released what is for all intents and purposes his first solo album as a self-titled effort under that name. It\'s no surprise at all that the album today is always repressed as a Todd Rundgren album, not by "Runt", because it really is the first salvo in what was to be a string of wholly individual artistic endeavors during the 70\'s, and there\'s nothing here that you could discern as being any more like a band than like a one-man undertaking.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Regardless of all this, &lt;i&gt;Runt&lt;/i&gt; is a formidable first outing for one of the most fiendishly brilliant, idiosyncratic figures in the rock &amp;amp; roll pantheon, if in hindsight it may seem like a mere teaser for the even better follow-up &lt;i&gt;The Ballad of Todd Rundgren &lt;/i&gt;or the ensuing tour de force &lt;i&gt;Something/Anything?&lt;/i&gt;. The opening "Broke Down and Busted" is a gritty blues-rocker that somewhat recalls his days in The Nazz, but tender ballads like "Believe in Me" and "Once Burned" are prime examples of the kind of vulnerable, introspective songwriting, very heavily influenced at this point by Laura Nyro and Carole King, which Rundgren felt he hadn\'t gotten the chance to fully explore in his previous group (interestingly, despite having cut the original version of the classic "Hello, It\'s Me", which represents possibly the apotheosis of this side of his musical character, with them). He would only continue to develop this style on his next album and the one after it,, but it\'s surprising in retrospect how well formed it was in this initial stage of his solo career. On the other hand, the goofy "I\'m in the Clique" established his knack for willfully strange, offbeat material. All in all, add in the handful of tough rockers and his fondness for soul &amp;amp; R&amp;amp;B and you wind up with a most uncannily fully-formed debut album, introducing just about all the tropes that would define his more accessible material as a solo performer for a number of years.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The most famous song here is undoubtedly "We Gotta Get You a Woman", his first real chart hit and definitely the album\'s best cut. The one other Rundgren trademark not previously mentioned which the album also introduced comes with the pure pop mini-suite "Baby Let\'s Swing", which began the penchant for medleys and pastiches either of his own compositions or cover songs that would pop up several times throughout his 70\'s output. Unfortunately, immediately afterward, the record loses its steam with the closing, much longer (nine-plus-minute, to be exact) "Birthday Carol", another suite but one which is far less engaging than the one before it. This big mistake is probably just about the only one he made here, and one which would thankfully not be made again the next time around. Otherwise, &lt;i&gt;Runt&lt;/i&gt; is the kind of album most singer/songwriters would kill to have as their very first.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/todd-rundgren-runt-1970.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-CxYq8MaA6Hk/VZoL-q_llhI/AAAAAAAAAxs/7YuZAUJaqnk/s72-c/Rundgren_Runt_Cover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6144200583438354862</guid><pubdate>Sat, 04 Jul 2015 07:42:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-04T01:04:57.350-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Mothers of Invention "Freak Out!" (1966)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lyatWbWMtZE/VZeCYQTs_iI/AAAAAAAAAxQ/H8LPURMnEtI/s1600/FreakOut%2521.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lyatWbWMtZE/VZeCYQTs_iI/AAAAAAAAAxQ/H8LPURMnEtI/s1600/FreakOut%2521.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Like his close contemporary and onetime labelmate Lou Reed, Frank Zappa was a dyed-in-the-wool student of rock &amp;amp; roll, rhythm &amp;amp; blues, experimental music and free jazz when he formed The Mothers out of an earlier, Zappa-less group called the Soul Giants in Los Angeles, at the same time that Reed formed The Velvet Underground in New York in the mid-60\'s. Much unlike Reed, however, Zappa was both a skilled avant-garde composer and pop songwriter by this time, and under his direction, The Mothers (of Invention) were unlike any other rock band of their time when they made their debut on record with &lt;i&gt;Freak Out!&lt;/i&gt;, one of rock\'s very first double-albums, and suitably enough a record that sounded like absolutely nothing that preceded it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although the album starts off with the incendiary rallying call "Hungry Freaks, Daddy", Zappa clearly distanced himself from what would become the youthful counterculture of the era, well before it even became known as "the counterculture" in the pop culture lexicon and before it was anywhere near as well-defined as it would become within a few years. Instead of leading a charge into teenage rebellion, Zappa wrote some of the most unusual and self-deprecating pop songs of the time to make up the first LP. Disc one alone could well be called a concept album unto itself about the emotional bankruptcy of contemporary teenybopper pop and the staid conventions of mainstream America. "You Didn\'t Try to Call Me", for example, is without a doubt one of the best pop-rock songs to have been recorded in 1966; it also happens to be a hysterically funny, sneering mockery of all the romantic melodrama and self-pity that was/is so predominant on teen-oriented Top 40 radio, then and, to a somewhat lesser extent, in the case of whatever the real modern equivalent to that long-dead Top 40 radio format would be now. Although a robust, lighthearted sense of humor is most evident on the surface, there\'s also a real and palpable nastiness to it that bubbles underneath just about all of the album\'s first half, and that\'s what makes these songs so entertaining and engaging. There\'s also the completely wacked-out, early psychedelic classic "Who Are the Brain Police?", which boasted sound effects that had never been heard on a rock record beforehand and would soon be popularized as part of the arsenal of Jimi Hendrix, who as it happens would actually be inspired by a performance of Zappa &amp;amp; the Mothers to experiment with the wah-wah pedal for the first time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;After these doo-wop derived ditties wrap up at the end of disc one, the second half opens with the fiery "Trouble Every Day", which marks the only return to the sociopolitical overtones of "Hungry Freaks" and gives a detailed, acidic account of the 1965 Watts uprising. Then begins the bizarre "Help, I\'m a Rock", and at this point all notions of conventional pop music trappings are abandoned wholesale; Zappa\'s experimental leanings fully take hold with the almost entirely a-capella weirdness of "It Can\'t Happen Here" and the sound collage techniques of "Return of the Son of Monster Magnet", the latter of which takes up the entirety of side four on vinyl. Even with major rock artists like The Beatles beginning to dabble in avant-garde waters at the same time, nobody had ever shifted gears so dramatically like The Mothers of Invention did here.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;You often hear these days that a much-lauded, influential artist or album was "ahead of their/its time", and that\'s certainly true for both band and album alike in this case, but it really isn\'t enough to describe how completely and utterly unique they were in 1966. To call The Mothers ahead of their time seems to imply that the rest of rock eventually caught up with them, but that\'s hardly the case here, even considering the eventual flowering of progressive/art rock. There aren\'t a whole lot of albums of any kind of music where you can truly say there is absolutely no prior historical precedent for it, and &lt;i&gt;Freak Out!&lt;/i&gt; is in that very, &lt;i&gt;very &lt;/i&gt;small group.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/the-mothers-of-invention-freak-out-1966.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-lyatWbWMtZE/VZeCYQTs_iI/AAAAAAAAAxQ/H8LPURMnEtI/s72-c/FreakOut%2521.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1486921767119788076</guid><pubdate>Sat, 04 Jul 2015 05:54:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-03T22:54:50.332-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Alice Cooper "Love It to Death"/"Killer" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-TzyEpqurkns/VZdn4Yn7sMI/AAAAAAAAAwo/8k7NVXcMmOQ/s1600/MI0002481205.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="318" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-TzyEpqurkns/VZdn4Yn7sMI/AAAAAAAAAwo/8k7NVXcMmOQ/s320/MI0002481205.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;By 1971, Vince Furnier and the original Alice Cooper band (before Vince officially became Alice unto himself, of course) had put out two records on Frank Zappa\'s Warner Bros.-affiliated Straight/Bizarre imprint, and both of them not only passed near-unnoticed, but neither of them were fully consistent or possessed of a unique, identifiable sound. When the up-and-coming producer Bob Ezrin got ahold of them to commandeer their third record, which was essentially a sink-or-swim undertaking in terms of the group being able to stay on Warner Bros., all of the elements that had been floating around on their first two LP\'s and which surfaced in their outrageous stage shows finally came together to make for a dynamic, immaculately constructed collection of songs. For one thing, Ezrin had the group chop down an unfocused jam they would work out called "I\'m Eighteen", and the stripped-down, menacing final version was a smash-hit on the charts when released as a single in November 1970, almost single-handedly putting Alice Cooper on the map.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Love it to Death &lt;/i&gt;starts off with the propulsive "Caught in a Dream" and continues with "Eighteen" and the equally anthemic "Long Way to Go" before arriving at the creepy, Doors-influenced "Black Juju", a suitably theatrical psychedelic-horror opus that manages not to be needlessly indulgent or tediously overlong, or to stall the album\'s momentum coming at the end of side one of the original vinyl. The real mini-masterpiece here is the epic "Ballad of Dwight Fry", which began Alice\'s long-term fascination with mental illness and psychiatric hospitalization that he would continue to mine on his first solo album, &lt;i&gt;Welcome to My Nightmare&lt;/i&gt;, and on &lt;i&gt;From the Inside&lt;/i&gt;, another concept record which was actually an autobiographical account of his own hospital stay for acute alcoholism in the late 70\'s. Here, it was still the product of his own demented imagination, completely enveloping himself in the skin of his protagonist as he starts calmly explaining his two-week stay in the loony bin, and by the time it reaches its climax, he\'s desperately howling "I wanna get outta here!" over and over. Legend has it that Cooper actually wore a straightjacket when recording the vocal, and he has continually donned the jacket onstage to this day when performing the song along with a number of other tunes.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The album marked the first time the evil Alice character really came to life on tape, and it began a string of solid, if at the time critically panned, and commercially successful albums with the original group that would continue until Cooper parted ways with the band in 1974 and embarked on a much more successful solo career. Capturing such a classic example of vaudevillian theater on record is certainly not an easy thing to do when so much of the impact is visual, but as Alice has said himself innumerable times, it wouldn\'t have been so powerful or so shocking to a whole generation of rockers if he didn\'t have the band\'s terrific (and surprisingly diverse) music to back it up.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ZyFZeJ6b5A8/VZdv4mrQjNI/AAAAAAAAAw8/_V4xVMJxAKc/s1600/MI0002323410.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="312" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ZyFZeJ6b5A8/VZdv4mrQjNI/AAAAAAAAAw8/_V4xVMJxAKc/s320/MI0002323410.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The excellent &lt;i&gt;Love it to Death&lt;/i&gt; made for a crucial turning point in the career of a great rock act that nobody else looked or sounded quite like in the early 70\'s, but its follow-up &lt;i&gt;Killer &lt;/i&gt;is arguably an even better album, expanding on the sneering garage-rock, the grim humor, the surprisingly progressive side of the group\'s musical character and the Alice character itself all at the same time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If "Caught in a Dream" and "I\'m Eighteen" started its predecessor off with a bang, &lt;i&gt;Killer &lt;/i&gt;boasts an even better one-two punch with the classic "Under My Wheels" and the melodic guitar pop of "Be My Lover", and much like the previous album it wastes little time in delving into artier (and lengthier) waters, this time with the espionage-themed, King Crimson-esque "Halo of Flies". In-between the recording &amp;amp; release of these two records, the band lost a good friend and one of their earliest supporters in Jim Morrison, and they pay tribute here with the startlingly somber, literate "Desperado", with its "killer and clown" gunslinger narrative rounding out nothing short of a short story in rock-song form.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They\'d already become notorious in the rock &amp;amp; roll world with the infamous "chicken incident" at a Toronto concert back in 1969, but with the epic "Dead Babies" they whipped up an even greater firestorm of controversy that followed them from home over to Great Britain, which tried to ban them from performing when they made their way across the pond for their first shows there following this album\'s release in late \'71. As shocking as it was, if his morality-police critics would\'ve taken time to actually listen to Alice\'s disturbing lyrics, they would\'ve found a morality play at work; he\'s chastising neglectful parents who let their "Little Betty" stumble upon and ingest momma\'s aspirin, lamenting that they "didn\'t want you anyway". It\'s a gripping, chilling performance from band and singer alike, and another prime example of how well the returning producer Ezrin was able to help the group bring their unprecedentedly theatrical stage show into their recordings. Altogether, with only &lt;i&gt;Billion Dollar Babies&lt;/i&gt; as the other chief contender, &lt;i&gt;Killer &lt;/i&gt;is arguably the original Alice Cooper band at their very best.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/alice-cooper-love-it-to-deathkiller-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-TzyEpqurkns/VZdn4Yn7sMI/AAAAAAAAAwo/8k7NVXcMmOQ/s72-c/MI0002481205.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5549021787163394015</guid><pubdate>Fri, 03 Jul 2015 01:00:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-07-02T18:00:45.660-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Love "Love" (1966)/"Da Capo" (1967)/"Forever Changes" (1967)</title><description>Over the past few decades, more and more listeners have discovered how criminal it is that in their heyday, one of the best bands of the late 60\'s could barely get arrested commercially. Arthur Lee and Love were just one of the many fine rock &amp;amp; roll bands formed in Los Angeles and San Francisco during the mid-60\'s, but they were of a particularly high caliber in that regard, and stood out especially for being one of rock\'s very first racially mixed bands. Their first three albums, which are essentially the only albums that wound up being made by the classic lineup, constitute a few of the best rock records of their time, particularly the epic masterpiece &lt;i&gt;Forever Changes&lt;/i&gt;, the original band\'s swan song.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i9Xw2--Xvos/VZW_mkj0xNI/AAAAAAAAAvQ/EZkdnIO2KhE/s1600/MI0001979691.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="316" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i9Xw2--Xvos/VZW_mkj0xNI/AAAAAAAAAvQ/EZkdnIO2KhE/s320/MI0001979691.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In their early days, when they were regularly bringing down the house at homey L.A. clubs like Bido Lito\'s, Love\'s sound was essentially like a harder-rocking Byrds (for whom Love\'s own Bryan Maclean had once roadied) with Arthur Lee\'s quavering, Johnny Mathis-influenced lead vocals. They were a hard-rocking, R&amp;amp;B-inflected outfit, but they also made room for introspective folk-rock ballads, which would soon become the group\'s focus in addition to lush orchestral arrangements. You won\'t find anything like an orchestra on their eponymous 1966 debut, which uncorks itself with the relentless piledriver that is their version of Burt Bacharach\'s "My Little Red Book", a key proto-punk recording if there ever was one.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The record crackles with garage-band firepower like none of its successors ever did, except in select moments, but unfortunately it ends up being a clear indicator that both Lee and Maclean had yet to fully develop their idiosyncratic songwriting talents, making for an ultimately spotty piece of work when taken as a whole, like many first albums are. The handful of exceptions includes Arthur\'s brooding folk-blues lament, "Signed D.C.", a genuinely disturbing, painfully stark meditation on heroin addiction which in \'66 surely must\'ve been one of the darkest songs yet recorded by a rock group. This was just before The Velvet Underground\'s "Heroin" was loosed upon the land in recorded form, remember, and some five years before John Prine\'s "Sam Stone". The promise of Arthur Lee and the band may not have been on full display here, but at least half of its follow-up would turn that around.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ur_6DbZgZwY/VZXDnDtZIYI/AAAAAAAAAvg/std7tFrkvZE/s1600/MI0002938904.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ur_6DbZgZwY/VZXDnDtZIYI/AAAAAAAAAvg/std7tFrkvZE/s320/MI0002938904.jpg" width="310" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Sure, the cover of "My Little Red Book" was a fantastic opening track for Love\'s first album, but the astonishing Arthur Lee original "Stephanie Knows Who" managed to do it one better for the group\'s second long-player, 1967\'s &lt;i&gt;Da Capo&lt;/i&gt;.&amp;nbsp; Hitting a heady, unprecedented middle ground between rock &amp;amp; roll with all the ferocity of the early Who, jazz, harpsichords and a most unusual rising-falling, semi-operatic structure, it\'s the best original song the group had penned up to that point. But Lee isn\'t the only one of Love\'s two primary songwriters who\'s starting to really show his stuff here; right up next is Bryan Maclean\'s positively gorgeous "Orange Skies", which he supposedly wrote based on the melody of the guitar solo in The Byrds\' "Bells of Rhymney".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Though &lt;i&gt;Da Capo &lt;/i&gt;is where Love found their true artistic muse, bringing in the elements of everything from bossa nova to classical music that made the group so unique (apart of course from their mixed-race membership) in L.A.\'s rock scene in the 60\'s, their hardest-rocking song actually appears here. The atomic-strength "Seven &amp;amp; Seven Is" is one of the most ferocious compositions of its era, one of the most viciously driving rock &amp;amp; roll songs yet recorded up to that point, the tune people are evoking when they call Love progenitors of punk rock. The plaintive "She Comes in Colors" is said to have inspired The Rolling Stones\' "She\'s a Rainbow", which appeared on that band\'s &lt;i&gt;Their Satanic Majesties Request &lt;/i&gt;later that year. All six songs on the record\'s first side are better, or at least more distinctive than anything the group had written for the first album, but the same can\'t be said for side two (of the original vinyl), which is just one "song" in the form of the painfully tedious 19-minute blues jam "Revelations". It, too, was allegedly borrowed by The Stones when they caught Love live in concert before any of their recordings had yet been made, and they supposedly recorded their own barrier-crashing opus "Goin\' Home" for &lt;i&gt;Aftermath &lt;/i&gt;in \'66 after being inspired by an early performance of "Revelations" onstage. As boring as their (substantially shorter, 11-minute) song ends up being as well, it happens to actually be a lot more engaging in comparison. One gets the feeling that it\'s something they used to work out onstage, and that it may well have been sizzling in their early days, but it passed its due date by the time the group made their second LP. There would certainly be nothing like it the next time around.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/--yUi2YFrF24/VZXLnv4nuNI/AAAAAAAAAv0/WjFKnC-kDj4/s1600/MI0000350762.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="318" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/--yUi2YFrF24/VZXLnv4nuNI/AAAAAAAAAv0/WjFKnC-kDj4/s320/MI0000350762.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;While the front half of &lt;i&gt;Da Capo &lt;/i&gt;was made up of Love\'s strongest material yet, and marked a substantial stylistic shift from a lot of the rather undistinguished folk and garage-rock originals they\'d come up with for the first record, the third album was, as they say, the Great Leap Forward. On &lt;i&gt;Forever Changes&lt;/i&gt;, released just ten months after its predecessor, Arthur Lee and Bryan Maclean\'s writing grew more abstract and evocative, and the songs were augmented with orchestration that manages to weave directly in with the group\'s own instruments and never smothers the rest of the sound.&amp;nbsp; The result of this artistic growth is nothing short of a masterpiece, in the truest sense of the word, something that would paradoxically be heralded in later years as one of the greatest albums of all time and at the same time help to break apart the original Love.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Cryptic and inscrutable though many of the songs may be, as evidenced by wordy, grandiose titles like "The Good Humor Man He Sees Everything Like This", "A House Is Not a Motel" and "Maybe the People Would Be the Times or Between Clark &amp;amp; Hilldale", it amounts to something akin to a concept album concerning the burning societal tumult that lurked beneath the surface of the flower-power, peace-and-love aesthetic of their San Francisco and L.A. contemporaries. Many commentators have read it, with its somber, occasionally even grotesque imagery, as a prescient foretelling of the violent clashes between young people and the establishment that would take hold in cities across the world in the year following its creation, or the internal strife that began to come to the surface with the violence of the Manson Family or the Altamont Free Concert the year after that. Arthur Lee himself once said that at the time of the album\'s creation, he felt like he was about to die, for reasons that he didn\'t elaborate on, and that he thought his songs would constitute his last words to the world. All of this certainly lends a foreboding, yet often quite subtle, darkness to &lt;i&gt;Forever Changes&lt;/i&gt; that puts a different mark on it from most of 1967\'s other California-based, psychedelic landmarks.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Opening the album with Maclean\'s beautiful "Alone Again Or" is in many ways an acknowledgement that he was just as valuable a composer and singer as Lee, even though there\'s only one other song here that was written/sung by Maclean. Lee\'s ominousness definitely takes center stage here, nonetheless; "The Red Telephone" begins with "Sitting on a hillside/Watching all the people die", which might be the most striking single lyric line on the entire album. Similarly, the fiery "Live and Let Live", which is one of the few moments where the blazing electric guitar of Love\'s earlier work makes a prominent appearance, starts off with "Oh the snot has caked against my pants/It has turned into crystal/There\'s a bluebird sitting on a branch/I guess I\'ll take my pistol/I\'ve got it in my hand/Because he\'s on my land". This line, meant to satirize and decry simple-minded violence in a most sensational manner, gained a good bit of irony after Lee was convicted of negligent discharge of a firearm and sent to prison close to 30 years later.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Make no mistake, this is far from an entirely brooding, depressive piece of work; the bouncy, energetic "Bummer in the Summer", "Alone Again Or" and the unabashedly pretty "Good Humor Man" are all proof-positive of that. But Lee\'s epic, orchestral "You Set the Scene" ends the album with a number of bold statements about the chaotic state of the world in the immediate aftermath of the "Summer of Love": "There\'s a man who can\'t decide if he should fight for what his father thinks is right", "This is the only thing that I am sure of/And that\'s all that lives is gonna die" and finally, "Everything I\'ve seen needs rearranging/And for anyone who thinks it\'s strange/Then you should be the first to want to make this change/And for everyone who thinks that life is just a game/Do you like the part you\'re playing?" Most of the other psychedelic bands of the day made statements against the established social norms of their parents\' generation, but few put it in such open-ended, uncertain and yet direct terms.
