Whether it\'s a "concept album" in the conventional sense is definitely up for debate, but it seems that a recurring theme runs through these songs, particularly Arthur\'s, and that would be one of how the counterculture hadn\'t quite made for an overnight revolution, and might not end up changing the country or the world in quite the way many of its participants still hoped and believed it could in those halcyon days of 1967. In another sense, it\'s a vividly colorful summation of Los Angeles\' swinging 60\'s, the time and place Love was born in, and written just as it was coming to an end.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Forever Changes&lt;/i&gt; spent ten weeks on the Billboard 200 and peaked at a pitiful #154, even though billboard advertisements emblazoned with its cover stood all over Los Angeles, and it was much more successful in England where it made it to #24. Regardless, it marked the end of the original Love, which had been on the verge of splitting up all throughout its recording. Arthur Lee kept the name, but the remainder of the group that made 1969\'s respectable follow-up &lt;i&gt;Four Sail&lt;/i&gt; was a different band altogether with all-unknown new members and a much different, heavier rock sound. Not that the original group, or most any band for that matter, could really follow up something as complete, conclusive and just about perfect as &lt;i&gt;Forever Changes&lt;/i&gt;, which stands on its own with the kind of legacy that only an elite class of the greatest, most influential and game-changing albums in pop music history have attained.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/07/love-love-1966da-capo-1967forever.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i9Xw2--Xvos/VZW_mkj0xNI/AAAAAAAAAvQ/EZkdnIO2KhE/s72-c/MI0001979691.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7952771030531385415</guid><pubdate>Tue, 30 Jun 2015 05:32:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-29T23:04:19.783-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "Blood on the Tracks" (1975)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KGLo5hg8fY8/VZIhv47AD9I/AAAAAAAAAuw/AcmM8nD7Sos/s1600/MI0001462461.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="283" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KGLo5hg8fY8/VZIhv47AD9I/AAAAAAAAAuw/AcmM8nD7Sos/s320/MI0001462461.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;By 1974, Bob Dylan had been largely inactive and absent from the public eye for the better part of three years, with the exception of his soundtrack to the 1973 film "Pat Garrett &amp;amp; Billy the Kid" and the abysmal, completely incoherent &lt;i&gt;Dylan&lt;/i&gt;, but when he for all intents and purposes "came back", he did it in a big way. He reunited with The Band for the relaxed &lt;i&gt;Planet Waves&lt;/i&gt;, and then went on a critically lauded tour with them that was documented on the double live album &lt;i&gt;Before the Flood&lt;/i&gt;. With his profile again rising in the middle of a decade that had progressed so far with a number of critically lambasted missteps, he really surged back to the peak of his artistic powers with his most startling album in years, and the most intimately personal work he\'d ever created beforehand, or ever would again. &lt;i&gt;Blood on the Tracks&lt;/i&gt;, culled half-and-half from sessions conducted in New York in September and then in Minneapolis in December of 1974, certainly didn\'t mark a return to the fiery rock &amp;amp; roll of &lt;i&gt;Blonde on Blonde&lt;/i&gt;, but it was widely (and justly) hailed as his greatest album since that tour de force nearly a decade earlier.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As Dylan himself will attest, &lt;i&gt;Blood on the Tracks &lt;/i&gt;is hardly a \'confessional\' record in the mold of Joni Mitchell\'s &lt;i&gt;Blue &lt;/i&gt;or the early work of Jackson Browne or James Taylor; the riddles and allegories he\'s always employed to great effect are as much in evidence here as they are on any of his other albums, especially in the winding, lengthy "Lily, Rosemary &amp;amp; the Jack of Hearts". But "Tangled Up in Blue", "Simple Twist of Fate", "You\'re a Big Girl Now" and "If You See Her, Say Hello" are some of the most heartfelt and unabashedly emotional songs he\'s ever written, reflecting a time of genuine hurt in his personal life stemming from the drawn-out end of his marriage to his wife Sara, which would be finalized with their formal divorce in 1977. In particular, "If You See Her" is absolutely heartbreaking, and sadly beautiful in a way very few of his other songs approach. That said, "Tangled Up in Blue" mixes up ostensible bits of his personal life with a more ambiguous menage a trois narrative, saying nothing of how many configurations you\'ll encounter in the form of alternate takes and live readings of the song. And we haven\'t yet even gotten around to mentioning the extraordinary "Idiot Wind", which is possibly the angriest song he\'s ever penned, an absolutely ferocious indictment of the press and, again only ostensibly, his soon-to-be ex-wife.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While it is indeed far from a straight-ahead exercise in painful autobiography, it\'s become one of the great benchmarks of introspective melancholy in the rock canon, alongside Nick Drake\'s &lt;i&gt;Pink Moon&lt;/i&gt;, John Lennon\'s &lt;i&gt;Plastic Ono Band&lt;/i&gt; and Neil Young\'s closely contemporaneous &lt;i&gt;Tonight\'s the Night&lt;/i&gt;. Despite that, however, what really distinguishes &lt;i&gt;Blood on the Tracks&lt;/i&gt; as one of Dylan\'s masterworks is how warm and inviting this emotionally intense material winds up being, and how much at ease he sounds performing these songs, which by his standards are most likely a lot more revealing about his true personality than he\'d like to publicly admit. It\'s also tremendously ironic in hindsight that he\'s backed up by Nashville session pros. It wasn\'t the first time he had worked with these kind of guys, not at all, but he wound up producing one of the most "professional"-sounding recordings he\'d made up to that point, in startling contrast to the still-startling rawness of his lyrics and the way he sings them. A stunner that he\'d never top, and the kind of flat-out perfect piece of work most singer/songwriters simply don\'t have in them.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/bob-dylan-blood-on-tracks-1975.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KGLo5hg8fY8/VZIhv47AD9I/AAAAAAAAAuw/AcmM8nD7Sos/s72-c/MI0001462461.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7306591723289755263</guid><pubdate>Sun, 28 Jun 2015 07:49:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-28T00:49:50.705-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Flying Burrito Brothers "The Gilded Palace of Sin" (1969)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-xZmAsHfoFA0/VY-cCALo1lI/AAAAAAAAAuU/A6i3z0m0KDg/s1600/MI0000014522.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-xZmAsHfoFA0/VY-cCALo1lI/AAAAAAAAAuU/A6i3z0m0KDg/s320/MI0000014522.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;Though hardly anyone seemed to be paying attention to it at the end of the 60\'s, Gram Parsons had already very much got the ball rolling with what he called "Cosmic American Music" as a leading member of The International Submarine Band and a revamped Byrds. It didn\'t help his case that he\'d had all but one of his lead vocal parts on the latter\'s enormously influential &lt;i&gt;Sweetheart of the Rodeo&lt;/i&gt; overdubbed in the post-production stage, due to a contractual dispute with the label that had distributed The ISB\'s lone self-titled LP just months earlier, which by all accounts Parsons didn\'t seem to understand and led to much animosity towards his ex-bandmates. He wasn\'t yet recognized as a cutting-edge figure in both rock and country music, and unfortunately when he and Byrds bassist Chris Hillman paired off to form The Flying Burrito Brothers, there was no additional attention to be garnered. Upon its release in February 1969, &lt;i&gt;The Gilded Palace of Sin&lt;/i&gt; peaked at a woeful #164 on the Billboard charts, even as "country-rock" was already slowly beginning to take hold, but like The Velvet Underground or The Ramones, the Burritos\' rather dismal record sales didn\'t derail their massive influence over immediately succeeding generations of musicians. Within ten years, that "country-rock" had morphed into slick soft rock and taken the charts by the throat, Parsons was dead, and not one of the far more successful, later country-rock bands (i.e. The Eagles) had done the bastard child of a subgenre as much justice as the blighted Burritos did on their commercial flop of a debut album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Moreso than &lt;i&gt;Sweetheart of the Rodeo&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;The Gilded Palace of Sin &lt;/i&gt;truly is old-fashioned C&amp;amp;W brought up to date in the Age of Aquarius, satirizing the counterculture while embracing it in a most underemphasized and refreshing way. Parsons sings "Sin City, a loving tribute to (and send-up of) The Louvin Brothers\' sinners-and-saints country ballads, with both a knowing humor and a reverent veneration for the form, which is obviously hard to do, but of course he doesn\'t seem to have to try too hard to make it sound right. It just came naturally out of him, that vision of "Cosmic American Music" mixing folk, soul, gospel, country, rock, whatever, that he held so dear and which he realized as fully as he could during what would turn out to be a tragically brief career. It\'s that effortlessness and the undeniable sartorial ties to anti-establishment rock &amp;amp; roll which make &lt;i&gt;The Gilded Palace of Sin&lt;/i&gt; so remarkably different from what Music City was producing then, which is undoubtedly a big part of the reason it along with &lt;i&gt;Sweetheart of the Rodeo&lt;/i&gt; and even Dylan\'s &lt;i&gt;Nashville Skyline&lt;/i&gt; were received with considerable indifference from both the rock press and the country music establishment at the time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Gram once called George Jones "The King of The Broken Hearts", and most any true-blue old-school country music fan worth their salt would agree with him, but he rivals The Possum and any number of his other heroes with the gorgeously wounded "Hot Burrito #1", which is then promptly followed by the rollicking, uptempo R&amp;amp;B of "#2", showing that Parsons and the Burritos were far more than just the sum of country or rock music. It\'s not the only time that the group\'s soulfulness is on full display; just check out their take on "Do Right Woman, Do Right Man" or "Dark End of the Street", two standards both written by soul and R&amp;amp;B songwriters Chips Moman and Dan Penn. At just 22, you can\'t help but think Gram had already perfected his unique vision, but it\'s astonishing to think he was pretty much just getting started. As it would turn out, he never quite topped his mastery of a new kind of honky-tonk as heard on &lt;i&gt;The Gilded Palace of Sin&lt;/i&gt;. As such, it stands as his masterpiece, as well as one of the best and most unexpectedly influential records of its time, regardless of genre.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/the-flying-burrito-brothers-gilded.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-xZmAsHfoFA0/VY-cCALo1lI/AAAAAAAAAuU/A6i3z0m0KDg/s72-c/MI0000014522.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2077951055516845614</guid><pubdate>Wed, 24 Jun 2015 00:35:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-23T17:35:02.122-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "Another Side of Bob Dylan" (1964)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-bHZ1JvjXzWQ/VYnoRgCPhpI/AAAAAAAAAsA/jnz52REW9QU/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_Another_Side_of_Bob_Dylan.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-bHZ1JvjXzWQ/VYnoRgCPhpI/AAAAAAAAAsA/jnz52REW9QU/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_Another_Side_of_Bob_Dylan.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Recorded in just one night in the summer of 1964, Dylan truly began the process of an unprecedented shift not only in his own songwriting, but in the shape of popular music lyrics in general with his fourth album. If 1963\'s &lt;i&gt;The Freewheelin\' Bob Dylan &lt;/i&gt;was a substantial step up from the staunchly traditional folkie material of the eponymous debut album, &lt;i&gt;Another Side &lt;/i&gt;is Dylan liberated from the confines of the topical protest song form that dominated the preceding &lt;i&gt;Times They Are A-Changing&lt;/i&gt;. As he famously put it at the time, there are no "finger-pointin\' songs" here, though a similar social conscience managed to slip out in "Chimes of Freedom" and its references to the killing of John F. Kennedy the previous fall. Otherwise, in their place are songs heavily inspired by the demise of his relationship with Suze Rotolo, along with early manifestations of the dense, playfully inscrutable narratives that Dylan would only continue to perfect, right from the opening "All I Really Want to Do", which would be covered to much commercial success by both Sonny &amp;amp; Cher and The Byrds the following year as "folk-rock" took off on both the American and British pop charts. It\'s one of his funniest and sweetest love songs, as he rattles off an absolutely mind-boggling, random series of things he promises not to do to the subject of his affection. Ultimately, he just wants to be friends.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In another song, Bob kisses off both his own earlier self-seriousness and the sanctimoniousness of the folkie milieu he had come out of in "My Back Pages", yet another song that found greater success in the hands of The Byrds a few years later, and which would be a telling refutation of the strictures of traditional folk music that would come fully to the fore with the ensuing &lt;i&gt;Bringing It All Back Home&lt;/i&gt; and his infamous performance at 1965\'s Newport Folk Festival. There, he would smash down those strictures with searing electric rock &amp;amp; roll and a sneering "fuck you" attitude to back it up, but his words on this acoustic ballad are just as dynamite: "Ah, but I was so much older then/I\'m younger than that now". To be honest, some of its surreal imagery ("corpse evangelists", "confusion boats", "half-wracked prejudice leaped forth") doesn\'t stand up so well in comparison to the concurrently composed "Mr. Tambourine Man" or "Visions of Johanna", or any number of his other much-analyzed mid-60\'s songs, but that chorus is at once moving, memorable and absolutely brilliant. "Motorpsycho Nitemare" is even funnier, where Dylan scams on a girl named Rita and has to avoid getting killed or "turned into the FBI" by her dad when he proclaims that he likes "Fidel Castro and his beard".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It should be noted that as great and, in fact, essential as it ultimately is, &lt;i&gt;Another Side &lt;/i&gt;is very much a transitional album, with its feet firmly out the door from the traditional folk community and ethos but not yet completely on top of the enigmatic, self-effacing persona he cultivated over the next several albums, culminating in the drug-influenced, hard-rocking and wildly surreal masterpiece &lt;i&gt;Blonde on Blonde &lt;/i&gt;in 1966. Here, he made the particular misstep of the lengthy "Ballad in Plain D", a painfully (and uncharacteristically) autobiographical account of the obviously traumatic dissolution of his relationship with Suze Rotolo, which had ended with a violent confrontation between the two several months before the album\'s recording. It mixes regretful, rather melodramatic prose with a particularly vindictive view of Rotolo\'s older sister Carla, which by all accounts wasn\'t necessary and spoke of a guy who was still very upset and angry over what had happened when he wrote the song. As anyone from heartbroken teenagers to other iconic artists can tell you, art (in general, but poetry or songwriting in particular) created in this mindset is &lt;i&gt;very &lt;/i&gt;much hit or miss and can result in some ultimately cringe-worthy stuff. "Plain D" is hardly cringe-worthy, but just a bit immature and tedious both in its length and its lyrics, unlike the immediately preceding "I Don\'t Believe You" or the immediately ensuing closer "It Ain\'t Me Babe", which deal with the same subject matter.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Dylan wouldn\'t make another record with this kind of in-the-room intimacy till &lt;i&gt;Blood on the Tracks &lt;/i&gt;ten years later, which is why it\'s such a distinctive and consistently engaging piece of work despite whatever lyrical shortcomings are hiding here or there, and moreso than any other album it\'s laden with material that would be turned into pop chart success for numerous other artists. And all this from one recording session and by just Dylan himself, mostly just singing, blowing harp and playing guitar.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/bob-dylan-another-side-of-bob-dylan-1964.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-bHZ1JvjXzWQ/VYnoRgCPhpI/AAAAAAAAAsA/jnz52REW9QU/s72-c/Bob_Dylan_-_Another_Side_of_Bob_Dylan.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5707192608216114899</guid><pubdate>Tue, 23 Jun 2015 22:21:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-23T18:03:48.889-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bob Dylan "John Wesley Harding" (1967)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FiFckQOmYtk/VYnRCPesdTI/AAAAAAAAArs/nkgZyyo7beo/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_John_Wesley_Harding.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FiFckQOmYtk/VYnRCPesdTI/AAAAAAAAArs/nkgZyyo7beo/s1600/Bob_Dylan_-_John_Wesley_Harding.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;After reaching the peak of his "wild, thin mercury sound" with the careening, brilliant double-LP &lt;i&gt;Blonde on Blonde&lt;/i&gt;, Dylan was involved in a mysterious motorcycle accident outside his home in Woodstock, NY in the summer of 1966. The details and severity of this accident are still being debated to this very day, but in any measure Dylan went into near-total seclusion from the music industry for close to a year and a half afterwards, even as artists continued to cover and find success with his songs. Over that period of time, rock &amp;amp; roll music went psychedelic with the emergence of San Francisco hippie ballroom-dwellers like the Jefferson Airplane and Grateful Dead, The Beatles\' &lt;i&gt;Sgt. Pepper\'s &lt;/i&gt;and "Strawberry Fields Forever", and the mind-bending guitar pyrotechnics of (well-known Dylan fan) Jimi Hendrix. Dylan and his longtime backing group The Hawks, soon to begin recording on their own as The Band, cooked up a now-legendary series of recordings at a rented house known as Big Pink in Saugerties, New York that would become known as "The Basement Tapes" and take on a life of their own over the years, but were left officially unreleased at the time of their being committed to tape. With hindsight, they now reveal Dylan taking a looser and more laid-back approach to his songs in terms of both the words and the music itself, as opposed to the wiry, stream-of-consciousness rock &amp;amp; roll of &lt;i&gt;Highway 61 Revisited &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Blonde on Blonde.&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Following through on this musical path, Dylan quietly released &lt;i&gt;John Wesley Harding &lt;/i&gt;in the closing days of 1967, and as he had shocked the audience of the Newport Folk Festival two years earlier with his electric rock accompaniment, listeners were completely taken aback by its warm, gentle country-folk sound and how much more quiet it was compared to its three predecessors. What\'s also notable about the album is how much warmer and more compassionate Dylan\'s lyrics often are, pleading for love and understanding from an unspecified master hand in "Dear Landlord", turning the namesake Old West gunslinger into a real-life Robin Hood (in his own words, "a friend to the poor" who would "never hurt an honest man") on the title track, and all the while keeping his gift for inscrutability intact as ever in "Ballad of Frankie Lee and Judas Priest", "The Wicked Messenger" and the understated, open-ended original version of "All Along the Watchtower". Hendrix\'s positively apocalyptic (and much more famous) take on "Watchtower" may be quite frankly the greatest reinterpretation of any song in rock history, as agreed upon even by Dylan himself, but his original rendition has a captivating, ominous air all its own.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s not quite the equal of his previous two albums, but it would be a tremendously important landmark in the development of country-rock, which would take full shape over the course of the following two years with subsequent groundbreaking records like The Byrds\' &lt;i&gt;Sweetheart of the Rodeo&lt;/i&gt; and The Flying Burrito Brothers\' &lt;i&gt;Guilded Palace of Sin&lt;/i&gt;. It winds up being one of Dylan\'s most consistent and approachable records, and one of his best just because of how well he was able to take his songs in an entirely new direction, while keeping all of his inimitable songwriting and narrative flair.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/bob-dylan-john-wesley-harding-1967.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FiFckQOmYtk/VYnRCPesdTI/AAAAAAAAArs/nkgZyyo7beo/s72-c/Bob_Dylan_-_John_Wesley_Harding.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2395452025613491909</guid><pubdate>Fri, 19 Jun 2015 03:50:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-18T20:50:25.382-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Nick Drake "Pink Moon" (1972)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SpolXPbgl7c/VYN4sYbdpII/AAAAAAAAArU/c8I_IwcuNPo/s1600/NickDrakePinkMoon.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SpolXPbgl7c/VYN4sYbdpII/AAAAAAAAArU/c8I_IwcuNPo/s1600/NickDrakePinkMoon.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;By the age of 23, Nick Drake had recorded two albums that were indisputably excellent and sounded like the work of no other singer/songwriter of the late 60\'s or early 70\'s, but both of them had passed almost totally unnoticed by critics and the music-buying masses alike, not helped by Drake\'s near-total refusal to perform live, appear on television or give interviews. He also felt dissatisfied with certain aspects of the production of those first two outings, the debut &lt;i&gt;Five Leaves Left &lt;/i&gt;and its even more impressive follow-up &lt;i&gt;Bryter Layter&lt;/i&gt;, though both were hardly as egregiously overproduced as some of the material being put out by singer/songwriters who were getting bigger commercially at that moment than Drake could possibly have conceived of at any time in what would end up being just 26 years of life.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;He wanted to strip everything down to just him and his guitar, singing and playing with none of the modest, but very central accompaniment he\'d had from folks like Richard Thompson on his previous two records, a decision he\'d apparently made as soon as recording for &lt;i&gt;Bryter &lt;/i&gt;was finished up. Everything on &lt;i&gt;Pink Moon&lt;/i&gt;, all 28 and a half minutes of it, was recorded in two brief late-night sessions in October 1971, and the one and only exception to Drake\'s strictly voice-and-guitar concept is a lovely bit of piano on the title track, which decades later somewhat bizarrely appeared in a Volkswagen commercial. Legend had it for many years that he delivered the sixteen-track master recordings, which contained a brief instrumental cut of the ancient "Plaisir d\'amour" that he wanted left off the final album, anonymously in a plastic bag to the reception area of his label Island Records, without anyone immediately noticing it. This was proven untrue by eyewitness testimony to an obscure Drake-centric fanzine, namely that of Island press officer Dave Sandison, who rectified the rumor by saying he\'d delivered it (labeled "NICK DRAKE PINK MOON") directly to Chris Blackwell, albeit without telling anyone it was his new album or saying much of anything about it. The classic surrealist album art wasn\'t even his idea, rather a painting by an artist named Michael Trevithick (who actually happened to be a friend of Drake\'s sister) selected by the record company.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The rumor mill about its production speaks to how well solidified Drake\'s myth is, as a perpetually somber, brooding loner who seems much like he was always a ghost to the cult of fans that has grown steadily since the mid-80\'s. When you really get down to brass tacks, although Nick suffered from serious mental illness and severe clinical depression throughout his brief recording career, his small but priceless oeuvre speaks less to an eternally depressive mindset so much as it speaks to the mind and muse of someone who always existed outside of the music industry and apart from his contemporaries, not giving anywhere near as much of himself or his true personality to the audience as even most of the commercial-juggernaut "sensitive singer/songwriters" did then. The despair and the deep sense of isolation and alienation are all very much there, and indeed are central components of his work, but at least I\'ve never seen it as entirely bleak as much as it is one of the most elliptical and mysterious bodies of work in all of popular music.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Pink Moon&lt;/i&gt; is laden with the imagery of disease, disconnection and despair, but it has a quality very much like the recordings of the similarly mysterious, unknowable Robert Johnson of three-plus decades earlier; the darkness is just part of the music\'s character, rather than a cloud of anguish hovering over every note. The surprisingly bluesy "Know" is perhaps as simple and brutally direct as most any songwriter has ever been, consisting entirely of one repeated haiku of a verse: "Know that I love you/Know I don\'t care/Know that I see you/Know I\'m not there". "Parasite" is the full realization of his melancholy vision, wherein he paints a vividly evocative picture of himself as being nothing more than a parasite clinging to your skirt, or in the dirt, or in town, much like Iggy Pop\'s "Passenger" who stays under glass looking out at the world from Jim Morrison\'s modern-life-as-car-trip. Rarely had anyone spoken of pure disenfranchisement with such astonishing beauty in song, or like in any of the other songs on &lt;i&gt;Pink Moon&lt;/i&gt;. Indeed, it is very much an unintentional concept album about a state of complete and total isolation, both mentally and physically, brought out by the literal isolation of him being the only musician on the album, and being locked off from the rest of the world by his debilitating depression and terminally quiet, noncommunicative nature.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Enigmatic, spare and painfully brief, &lt;i&gt;Pink Moon &lt;/i&gt;is nothing short of a masterwork, which has only grown in stature in the decades since its release to very little critical (and basically no commercial) notice in early 1972.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/nick-drake-pink-moon-1972.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SpolXPbgl7c/VYN4sYbdpII/AAAAAAAAArU/c8I_IwcuNPo/s72-c/NickDrakePinkMoon.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3651595834484843225</guid><pubdate>Mon, 15 Jun 2015 08:59:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-15T02:05:44.010-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Germs "(GI)" (1979)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-CvmScjm7n2o/VX5TnhVw1kI/AAAAAAAAAqQ/g8w9s2KUe4g/s1600/330px-Germs_-_%2528GI%2529_cover.png" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="317" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-CvmScjm7n2o/VX5TnhVw1kI/AAAAAAAAAqQ/g8w9s2KUe4g/s320/330px-Germs_-_%2528GI%2529_cover.png" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;It wasn\'t supposed to be this way. The Germs began as a complete and total joke band, four teenagers getting onstage for an open-mic night and playing a few tedious minutes of incoherent noise and getting thrown offstage for fucking with a more "serious" band\'s gear (to be exact, smearing peanut butter on a bass amp that belonged to fellow Los Angeles punkers the Zeros). When first released, their home-recorded debut single ("Forming", backed with the live recording "Sex Boy") was seen as an amusingly bad novelty piece, and the band were widely dismissed by members of the nascent L.A. punk rock scene. None of the detractors would ever surmise that aside from X, The Germs would move to the forefront of L.A. punk and hardcore in terms of the way its history is recounted a third of a century later, or for that matter that guitarist Pat Smear would eventually end up in not one, but two of the most successful rock bands of the 1990\'s all thanks to his notoriety as a Germ.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although they were much more popular and locally acclaimed by the time they released a full-length LP in 1979, it was still a shock for many fans and critics alike to read and hear clearly the surprisingly wordy, intelligent lyrics of the maniac vocalist Darby Crash, who was well known for his intoxicated behavior onstage and particularly for either "singing" completely incoherently or not even singing into the microphone during performances. The fucked-up noise of their earliest days had become a still-noisy but far more determinedly furious sound, with Don Bolles\' manic drums and Lorna Doom\'s solid low-end work complementing Smear\'s blistering guitar and Crash\'s feral abandon. For her part, none other than Joan Jett, at the tail end of her Runaway days, sat behind the boards and (along with engineer Pat Burnette, of course) gave the album a crisp, roomy, and above all else savage sound, which they probably couldn\'t have attained if they\'d somehow managed to get first choice Mark Lindsay of Paul Revere &amp;amp; the Raiders to make the record with them.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;(GI)&lt;/i&gt;, titled after a moniker The Germs had begun to use to get booked in clubs which had banned them in L.A., starts off with one of the fastest and shortest punk songs that had yet come out of the US at the end of the 70\'s, the paranoid 43-second "What We Do is Secret" (later the title of a fine biopic about Crash\'s life and times), which aligned them as a favorite with the burgeoning suburban hardcore uprising that was beginning to splinter off from the original, artier Hollywood/L.A. scene. It\'s fitting that they bookend their shortest song, of both the album and their entire oeuvre, with their longest, closing out the album with the nine-minute, apocalyptic "Shut Down (Annihilation Man)", which proves that even as tight a band as they\'d become they still had a soft spot for pure, uninhibited mess.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In-between, both band and singer/lyricist are in top form. Darby Crash is positively spinning out of control on a syllabus cribbed from the ideas of Hitler, Bowie, Manson and Nietzsche, all coalescing into a searing account of alienation and reckless self-destruction. The group recut perhaps their greatest song, "Lexicon Devil", which had been released a year earlier as the title track of a sensational EP; with a more guttural performance from Crash and a faster, busier beat from Bolles (as opposed to the previous EP\'s Nickey "Beat" Alexander), its anthemic signature-song quality is offset by sheer urgency and mad desperation, like it\'s the last song the foursome will ever play and Crash is already about to head into his inevitable demise. "Manimal" starts off burning slow, simmering ominously before exploding into one of their most furious and intense songs, a startling view of the line between man and animal made all the more convincing by Darby\'s primordial growl. In "We Must Bleed", it sounds like the group could fall apart at any moment, rushing towards its shambolic non-climax with Darby screaming "I want out now" and the riff spiraling round &amp;amp; round like a spinning carnival ride about to break.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Darby wouldn\'t meet his actual demise till over a year after the album\'s release, on 7 December 1980 of a heroin overdose, and they made some recordings afterward for an almost entirely abandoned soundtrack for the William Friedkin film "Cruising", but encapsulated in The Germs\' one and only album is the entirety of everything that defined the short, fast life of a boy who was born Jan Paul Beahm and morphed into a frighteningly charismatic creature before he was even 20 years old. He died at age 22 as a full-blown legend among a small, but very important group of people that would, many years later, come to much greater mass attention than Darby Crash or his disciples could\'ve conceived of. In other words, &lt;i&gt;(GI) &lt;/i&gt;is one of those fantastic records that took many years to be fully recognized as the seminal classic that it is, and which had an enormous impact that no one would ever have predicted when Crash was alive. It still gives you an unmistakable feeling that all hell is breaking loose upon the earth, every time you hear it, and you can only capture that kind of lightning in a bottle once. That is, if you ever get to capture it at all.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/the-germs-gi-1979.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-CvmScjm7n2o/VX5TnhVw1kI/AAAAAAAAAqQ/g8w9s2KUe4g/s72-c/330px-Germs_-_%2528GI%2529_cover.png" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3377641655525240849</guid><pubdate>Thu, 11 Jun 2015 21:57:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-11T14:57:49.135-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Smiths "The Queen is Dead" (1986)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-_5Z8g9PStoQ/VXn1zJdM3_I/AAAAAAAAApw/yHexU2-raLs/s1600/MI0001612246.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="312" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-_5Z8g9PStoQ/VXn1zJdM3_I/AAAAAAAAApw/yHexU2-raLs/s320/MI0001612246.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The Smiths were one of those great rock bands that accomplished a whole lot in a surprisingly small window of time, in their case four albums and a slew of singles that pretty much never ranked below "very good" in terms of sheer quality, all in a five-year period. Because of how fast and quick they developed into one of the very best bands of their time, if not all time, their third album isn\'t just the moment where all the parts finally fit perfectly and everything clicked into a great, cohesive whole; it\'s the kind of fully-fledged masterwork that most bands could never even conceive of. As great as their eponymous debut, the &lt;i&gt;Hatful of Hollow &lt;/i&gt;compilation and parts of the otherwise flawed &lt;i&gt;Meat is Murder&lt;/i&gt; all were, &lt;i&gt;The Queen is Dead&lt;/i&gt; is simply perfect, which is a very rare attribute even among rock\'s most revered records.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It starts with one of the best album-openers in rock &amp;amp; roll history, the epic, surging title track prefaced by a memorably random, almost Fellini-esque snippet of audio from one of Morrissey\'s beloved early-60\'s "kitchen sink" dramas. It\'s one of Morrissey\'s greatest songs and performances, filled with positively genius turns of phrase ("She said "I know you and you cannot sing"/I said "that\'s nothing, you should hear my play piano!"), and sees him jumping across what seems to be a schizophrenic valley of wildly diffuse subject matter to arrive at an apocalyptic condemnation of the state of England at the height of the Thatcher era. It announces right off the bat that this album is the hardest-rocking thing the band has ever done, or ever will do, and what\'s more they rock like no one else but them, with Johnny Marr\'s unique web of guitars and a rhythm section (drummer Mike Joyce and bassist Andy Rourke) that was always to-die-for. Just from the work of Marr, Joyce and Rourke alone, this is the band at its most effortlessly versatile and inventive.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Right away, the title track encapsulates what really makes &lt;i&gt;The Queen is Dead&lt;/i&gt; the band\'s greatest work; it\'s their most direct and accessible album in every way, not saddled with the polarization that comes with a contentiously murky production or supposedly pedophilic lyrics (&lt;i&gt;The Smiths&lt;/i&gt;), or overwrought, equally contentious social issues (&lt;i&gt;Meat is Murder&lt;/i&gt;). Many people, fan and non-fan alike, have griped about the heavy-handed moralizing of "Meat is Murder" (the song) or "Barbarism Begins at Home", or winced at the miserablism of "Heaven Knows I\'m Miserable Now", or found "This Charming Man" much too overly precious. Far fewer among any of them could fault the brutally honest, uncharacteristically direct despair of "I Know It\'s Over"; the way "Cemetry Gates" ingeniously juxtaposes one of their bounciest and brightest tunes to a lyric deriding literary plagiarism; or how the extraordinary "There Is a Light That Never Goes Out" turns a typically melodramatic Morrissey scenario, of two outcasts in love who wish for a double-decker bus to hit them and take them away together from their unhappy home lives, into a soaring, majestic anthem that can resonate powerfully with anyone who hears it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There truly is not a bum note to be found here, even if songs like "The Boy With a Thorn in His Side", "Bigmouth Strikes Again" and "There Is a Light" are the obvious highlights as opposed to "Never Had No One Ever" or "Vicar in a Tutu", and even then the highlights take up most of this brilliantly concise and relatively short LP. Many great bands have one album in particular that stands as their finest hour, but few have ever made a towering masterpiece on the level of &lt;i&gt;The Queen is Dead&lt;/i&gt;, which is at the very least indisputably one of the most influential and important albums of the last 30 years, and to most who\'ve heard it it\'s one of the greatest albums of all time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/the-smiths-queen-is-dead-1986.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-_5Z8g9PStoQ/VXn1zJdM3_I/AAAAAAAAApw/yHexU2-raLs/s72-c/MI0001612246.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-61877337758376303</guid><pubdate>Thu, 11 Jun 2015 04:36:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-10T21:36:13.196-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Lydia Lunch "Queen of Siam" (1980)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Slrdo9QeTSk/VXkJWetxyqI/AAAAAAAAApY/JfQmuwzvYr4/s1600/Lydia_Lunch_-_Queen_of_Siam.jpeg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Slrdo9QeTSk/VXkJWetxyqI/AAAAAAAAApY/JfQmuwzvYr4/s1600/Lydia_Lunch_-_Queen_of_Siam.jpeg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;At the end of the 70\'s, few rock singers were as completely unique and ceaselessly confrontational as Lydia Lunch, when she yowled, screamed and chanted over the painfully harsh racket of Teenage Jesus &amp;amp; the Jerks, who were one of the definitive groups of New York\'s short-lived (but very influential) "no wave" movement. Quite unsurprisingly, they themselves didn\'t last long, and soon Lunch (just 20 years old by now) went into a small, obscure studio in New York to record a solo album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1980\'s &lt;i&gt;Queen of Siam&lt;/i&gt;, released on ZE Records, showed that Lunch was far from just an intriguing performance artist, and that she did indeed possess a formidably seductive persona that made her out to be a kind of demonic chanteuse rather than a brattier-than-the-norm teenager. The avant-garde elements of her earlier work aren\'t at all gone, but the overtly abrasive noise has been quite heavily tapered down in favor of a dynamic lounge jazz backdrop for her voice, which is often quite like a younger, more rambunctious Patti Smith (in a very good way).&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Her songs are at once thoroughly undefinable and vividly New York City in their sound and attitude, but the best moment comes in the form of a very, very sexy version of the Classics IV\'s "Spooky"; trailing close behind it is the pure film noir of "Lady Scarface", a positively doom-laden, near-whispered "Gloomy Sunday", and the demented little-girl meow of "Mechanical Flattery" and "Tied &amp;amp; Twist". &lt;i&gt;Queen &lt;/i&gt;is an often overlooked, modest little gem from the heady era in which it was made, and even if it isn\'t exactly a perfect album, it sure is one of those underappreciated records that a lot more people ought to hear.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/lydia-lunch-queen-of-siam-1980.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-Slrdo9QeTSk/VXkJWetxyqI/AAAAAAAAApY/JfQmuwzvYr4/s72-c/Lydia_Lunch_-_Queen_of_Siam.jpeg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1241386711985521993</guid><pubdate>Thu, 04 Jun 2015 22:57:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-06-05T22:57:29.143-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Johnny Thunders "So Alone" (1978)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FRWB5k34fNA/VXDI_W7rnFI/AAAAAAAAAn8/-jzuszT7Euo/s1600/MI0002164934.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FRWB5k34fNA/VXDI_W7rnFI/AAAAAAAAAn8/-jzuszT7Euo/s1600/MI0002164934.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Having already left an indelible imprint on rock &amp;amp; roll with the legendary New York Dolls, and then been the leader of CBGB favorites the Heartbreakers (not to be confused with Tom Petty\'s band that happened to emerge at the exact same time), Johnny Thunders was already a living legend at just 26 years old when he went into the studio in 1978 to record his first full-fledged solo album, &lt;i&gt;So Alone&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Befitting a man of such high stature in the early history of the booming punk/new wave frontier, he had quite an impressive guest list for the sessions: a pre-fame Chrissie Hynde contributing backing vocals to a version of the Dolls classic "Subway Train"; Peter Perrett, the notoriously troubled frontman of new wave heroes The Only Ones, contributing backing vocals and guitar to several songs; ex-Sex Pistols Steve Jones and Paul Cook playing guitar and drums on several songs, including quite ironically the song "London Boys", a pointed response to the Pistols\' sneering "New York"; Small Faces/Humble Pie leader and bona-fide rock legend Steve Marriott &lt;i&gt;and &lt;/i&gt;Thin Lizzy\'s Phil Lynott, both making notable cameos on Otis Blackwell\'s "Daddy Rollin\' Stone".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Make no mistake, however; this is Johnny\'s show, from the gloriously out-of-tune vocal and guitar performance on "Great Big Kiss" to the remake of the one Dolls song he had sung lead on, "Chatterbox" (slightly rewritten and retitled "Leave Me Alone"). It\'s a thoroughly entertaining, endearingly messy collection of songs, even though it clearly illustrates that Johnny was not one of rock\'s great creative rivers when it came to songwriting, what with how only four of its ten songs were written solely by Johnny himself. One of those four songs, however, stands out from the rest of the album and from anything Johnny recorded over the ensuing 13 years until his (sadly, very predictable) death from a heroin overdose in 1991. "You Can\'t Put Your Arms Round a Memory" is not only Johnny\'s single greatest song, but it\'s also one of the saddest and most heartbreaking (no pun intended) songs in the entire rock &amp;amp; roll canon. Friends of Johnny\'s have since claimed that the song was actually written years before its recording here, and before he had ever tried heroin, but now you can\'t help but hear one of the most painfully autobiographical statements any rock &amp;amp; roller had ever made up to that point, or ever would again. Heard today, it\'s nothing short of a bittersweet elegy to one of rock\'s most notorious dope casualties, penned by the man himself.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s also undoubtedly sadder still in light of how easily and quickly Johnny\'s solo career deteriorated in the 1980\'s, when it got to the point where he would sometimes stumble off the stage during gigs, and turned out just a few more unremarkable solo albums interspersed with even more spotty live recordings, both legal and otherwise. But the first time out on his own, he created hands-down the best piece of work to emerge from a former New York Doll after their original breakup, and for one album all the qualities that made Thunders a star and an icon would be on full display.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/06/johnny-thunders-so-alone-1978.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-FRWB5k34fNA/VXDI_W7rnFI/AAAAAAAAAn8/-jzuszT7Euo/s72-c/MI0002164934.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3785384564464263118</guid><pubdate>Fri, 29 May 2015 07:10:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-29T00:35:11.164-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Life Stories</category><title>We\'re Gonna Die (Send Those Curse Words to Hell!)</title><description>For two months, the kids in my little social group that hung with me at the United Action for Youth Center were all eagerly anticipating the event in which we were gonna "GET SCHOOLED!" at Public Space One in Iowa City. For one month, a couple of those kids were getting geared up to play their very first show as a band, consisting of just a guitar player and a drummer, both girls. My main buddies, for their part, were playing their third show as a band, along with a new band from out of town called Painted Disaster, a heavy indie/blues-rock band called The Projectors, the headlining HOTT, and a horrible Christian emo band whose name need not be mentioned for fear of this article possibly garnering listener attention to them, because unfortunately, they\'re still active. The day afterward was my man Detrell\'s 16th birthday, and we were also looking forward to his birthday party at the house of his girlfriend at the time, who was a member of the band playing their first show at PS1. For a one-two punch Friday and Saturday up ahead, it was indeed shaping up to be an awesome weekend.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With signs posted everywhere saying "NO ALCOHOL ALLOWED", a very sparse stage in a small room, and a whole bomb-ass passel of anticipation, an unusually large crowd (in PS1 terms) watched the newest band take the stage, the duo who knocked out songs like their cover of "Bitchin\' Camaro" by the Dead Milkmen and an original where they just kept shouting "We\'re gonna die! We\'re gonna die!" over a standard punk rock one-two one-two. My buddy\'s band, who had been in a failed band with me a year previously and then split off to form the trio that now called themselves Other Band, were up next. If memory serves, The Projectors took the stage after them, followed by Painted Disaster and the godawful Christian band, topped off by a scorching performance from HOTT. Two-fourths of HOTT were in their mid-forties, members of the original 1980\'s punk rock scene in Iowa City, and that includes the lead singer, but that 46-year-old frontman led the band through an absolute barn-burner of a performance with boundless energy and enthusiasm, and it was a perfect capper to one of the most exciting nights I\'d ever had.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;After this triumphant show, me and the members of Other Band, along with two other mutual friends, went to spend the night at Detrell\'s before we all went to UAY in the morning to hang out before most of us wound up at his girlfriend\'s house to rock out and eat pizza. But before we even got to the house, which was his grandpa\'s and tucked away in a rather secluded spot that was neighbored by only two other houses, I was immensely annoyed and irritated by something Detrell said to his visitors: "No swearing at my house!" He didn\'t want anyone saying "fuck" or "shit" in particular at his house because he "respected his grandfather too much", or something like that. I could understand if he wanted us to not be cussing it up in front of his grandpa, thus incurring the grandfather\'s costernation and scolding of Detrell, but that old dude was asleep by then. At least I thought. I was later told that he\'d caught wind of some of the cusswords that I let rip with anyway cause I absolutely despised (and still do despise, or would) being told not to say this or that in general, but &lt;i&gt;especially &lt;/i&gt;the idea of being told "watch your language" by my &lt;i&gt;friends&lt;/i&gt;. Not my mother or father or a teacher or somebody like that, but my &lt;i&gt;friends&lt;/i&gt;! It pissed me off even more when I crabbed to his Other Band co-conspirator Max on the bus down to UAY the next morning, and he responded without humor something like "oh, when someone says such and such, you can\'t do that". Man, did &lt;i&gt;that &lt;/i&gt;piss me off. His whole odd little churchboy demeanor in that moment and the way he said "you can\'t do that" festered in my mind and would continue to grate on me even thinking about it long after the fact, probably because I\'m mentally unstable and am well-known to have a bizarrely vivid memory of totally ephemeral, ridiculous shit like that. The other noteworthy thing that night was, one of the dudes there had skipped school that day and his mother  tracked him down using his cellphone signal, and she grounded him on the  spot and took him home, all without much of a scene being made.  Detrell\'s grandpa asked the rest of us if we were "supposed to be there" or not, and of course the answer was yes, though with Detrell\'s behavior I was wondering whether or not it was a good idea to come over in the first place. In any case, the real reason for Detrell\'s "rule" was most likely that his grandpa would hear the "offending words" through the thin, crappy walls of their claustrophobic, tiny dwelling and get mad at his grandson, even if he wasn\'t the one speaking those curse words. For the record, it was still totally acceptable to watch a Blink-182 documentary that was full of unapologetic vulgarity, oddly enough. &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;But gone from that house and Detrell\'s weird "rule" we were now, and after a few hours at UAY I was headed out to his girlfriend\'s house to party with two-fourths of Other Band (Detrell and then-bassist Martin, rather than Max, who had crazy parents that wouldn\'t allow him to be there or something). Of course, let\'s not forget that guitar-slingin\' girlfriend of his, or her drumming cohort, or the other couple of friends that joined us in our drunken-and-high adolescent revelry. We got totally fucked up with an ounce of weed and a twelve-pack of-&lt;i&gt;oh wait, that didn\'t happen&lt;/i&gt;. Detrell was virulently anti-substances at the time, and anyway his girlfriend\'s family were all over the place and would\'ve whooped all our asses. We all took turns jamming out on our twangers and percussion instruments in the basement, which was simply fabulous and a whole lotta fun.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;When our gang wandered to a nearby schoolyard park, we did encounter someone who appeared to be engaging in the aforementioned revelry, a high school girl who was stumbling across the park with a slightly less intoxicated friend. A few people in our group knew \'em, and mentioned that apparently one of the girls had a scary meth-addict father who beat her up. Someone jokingly suggested that I start chatting this random girl up, cause "she\'ll probably have sex with you", but not only was it an obvious joke, I also assumed that if she would she\'d probably take me back to the house with the scary dad and I might wind up getting wasted by a violent, methed-out psycho that I pictured in my head. (And no, unlike many of the "football heathens" I described in a previous piece, I would never take advantage of a drunk girl, or anyone else for that matter. Just to clarify, I ain\'t that kind of asshole.) As we all wound our way back, the birthday boy and his girlfriend were being lovey dovey, and I jibed them with a loud, manic rendition of Chuck Berry\'s "Rock &amp;amp; Roll Music" designed to annoy the shit out of them. It didn\'t work.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;About eight or nine PM that night, the party was over, and I was on the way back home being given a ride by the girlfriend\'s stepdad, who may or may not have attempted to convert me into some sort of Unitarian Church thing. It didn\'t work. &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/were-gonna-die.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4392630574487372783</guid><pubdate>Thu, 28 May 2015 19:25:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-28T12:25:11.021-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Patti Smith "Horses" (1975)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SZkk2LgZl5c/VWdc86UIfMI/AAAAAAAAAnc/F9FibkeUJ6g/s1600/PattiSmithHorses.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SZkk2LgZl5c/VWdc86UIfMI/AAAAAAAAAnc/F9FibkeUJ6g/s1600/PattiSmithHorses.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;It\'s a testament to the immense diversity of New York City\'s early punk/new wave scene in the mid-70\'s that the first two full-length albums to be released by members of this new uprising were, in order, The Dictators\' &lt;i&gt;Go Girl Crazy! &lt;/i&gt;and then Patti Smith\'s &lt;i&gt;Horses&lt;/i&gt;, both in 1975. Where the former was a gloriously lowbrow celebration of junk culture filtered through supercharged, trashy rock &amp;amp; roll, &lt;i&gt;Horses&lt;/i&gt; was (and is) practically uncategorizable. Its mixture of rock &amp;amp; roll, jazz, reggae and poetry was unlike anything that had come before it, and would seldom, if ever, be equaled even by Smith herself.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Even the cover is a stone-cold classic photograph, of a rail-thin Smith posed androgynous in men\'s clothing and displaying absolutely no overt sexuality whatsoever. To begin with, fully fledged female rockers were very scarce back in 1975, but it was an entirely different kind of \'fuck you\', to an increasingly bloated and male-orientated rock biz, to pose like &lt;i&gt;that&lt;/i&gt; on an album cover. It\'s an immediate tip-off that Smith was doing it her way, with no compromise and no limitations. It\'s as unique a piece of artwork in its own right as the music contained within, and needless to say it\'s one of the all-time great album covers.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Along with one of the great cover photos in rock history, &lt;i&gt;Horses &lt;/i&gt;also happens to begin with one of the greatest album openers ever, which is her startling revamp of Van Morrison\'s "Gloria". Before the song proper even begins, in the first few seconds of the half-sung, half-spoken "In Excelsis Deo", she delivers the line that would define the rest of her career: "Jesus died for somebody\'s sins, but not mine". Rarely has any artist ever made as powerful a statement in just one simple line like Patti Smith did here, and along with the cover it\'s the first thing that took so many people aback when first listening to the album back then, before punk proper really existed as a defined form of rock &amp;amp; roll on either side of the Atlantic. Even more extraordinary is when the impressionistic intro piece gradually and seamlessly builds into her positively apocalyptic take on the old Them song, where she doesn\'t do a gender reversal for the object of affection in Morrison\'s original and her band plays it with more dramatic fury than in any other of the innumerable versions of the tune.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;An opening that strong threatens to dwarf everything that follows, but the group effortlessly switches gears yet again for the lolloping reggae of "Redondo Beach", and then the lengthy improvisational piece "Birdland", followed by the furious "Free Money". All this on just the first half of the album, or side one in vinyl terms. "Gloria" may be one of the most radical and successful reworkings of a song that\'s ever been committed to tape, but "Land" is even more striking, where she talks about a boy named Johnny being accosted in school by a mysterious other boy in a leather jacket in a most sexual and dramatic manner, and then manages to weave this thoroughly ambiguous and abstract story in and out through Chris Kenner\'s early 60\'s hit "Land of a Thousand Dances". That\'s something that only a writer as idiosyncratic and individual as Smith could pull off.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Patti would only remain active in music for four more years and three more albums with the Patti Smith Group before breaking with the band and retiring for nearly a decade to be a full-time mom with husband Fred "Sonic" Smith of the MC5 and their son Jackson. In all her career since that time, she\'s never duplicated the sheer mastery of poetry, performance art and full-abandon rock &amp;amp; roll that she achieved with her debut album, which is why &lt;i&gt;Horses &lt;/i&gt;is one of rock\'s all-time great debuts and one of the greatest rock albums of all time, period.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/patti-smith-horses-1975.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-SZkk2LgZl5c/VWdc86UIfMI/AAAAAAAAAnc/F9FibkeUJ6g/s72-c/PattiSmithHorses.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6552695192579270682</guid><pubdate>Wed, 27 May 2015 21:51:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-27T14:51:19.317-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Dinosaur Jr. "You\'re Living All Over Me" (1987)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-BemLilqGt2Y/VWY8Rzd_dAI/AAAAAAAAAm8/yhcLxoJ9kMI/s1600/602275_10201520878926971_2044493015_n.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-BemLilqGt2Y/VWY8Rzd_dAI/AAAAAAAAAm8/yhcLxoJ9kMI/s1600/602275_10201520878926971_2044493015_n.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Rising from the ashes of the savage hardcore band Deep Wound,  Dinosaur didn\'t make the best impression with their self-titled 1985  debut album, often unsure as to what the group\'s sound really was. As  its 1987 follow-up &lt;em&gt;You\'re Living All Over Me &lt;/em&gt;proves beyond a shadow of a doubt, this developmental phase wouldn\'t last for long. For one thing, alth&lt;span class="text_exposed_show"&gt;ough  the first pressings of the album on their new label SST Records were  credited to \'Dinosaur\', a lawsuit from a 60\'s rock supergroup by that  name would force the group to change its name to Dinosaur Jr. within  about a month of the album\'s release, and thus it became the first album  to feature the group\'s \'real\' moniker.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="text_exposed_show"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s appropriate, then, that &lt;em&gt;You\'re Living All Over Me&lt;/em&gt; found the group revealing for the first time their signature sound, a  heady blend of classic rock guitar virtuosity, drawling Neil Young-esque  vocals, melody and white noise, not to mention hardcore punk fury and  even a bit of country. J Mascis\' guitar and vocal styles, both very  recognizable now as his, would make him an instant alternative/indie  rock legend, and he\'s in fine form in songs like "Little Fury Things",  "Sludgefeast" and "Raisans". These are only among the best cuts off an  album that\'s strong all the way through, through nine blissful slabs of  white-hot noise-pop and up to the bizarre, almost out-of-place closer  "Poledo", an unnerving lo-fi solo piece from bassist Lou Barlow, who  would soon go on to make similar music with the equally revered Sebadoh  after a particularly ugly split from Dinosaur in 1989.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It  would be far from the last time Dinosaur Jr. would make music this  strong, influential, prescient or unique; 1988\'s follow-up &lt;em&gt;Bug&lt;/em&gt;  (particularly the stone-cold classic "Freak Scene") and their passionate  take on The Cure\'s "Just Like Heaven" make that clear. But with over a  quarter-century\'s hindsight, it\'s also clear that &lt;em&gt;You\'re Living All Over Me&lt;/em&gt; remains Dinosaur\'s finest album, and the one that really built their  standing as one of the best and most influential bands of their time.&lt;/div&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/dinosaur-jr-youre-living-all-over-me.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-BemLilqGt2Y/VWY8Rzd_dAI/AAAAAAAAAm8/yhcLxoJ9kMI/s72-c/602275_10201520878926971_2044493015_n.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1985295315005051321</guid><pubdate>Wed, 27 May 2015 21:49:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-27T14:49:11.227-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bikini Kill "Pussy Whipped" (1993)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-J1RjjZ3O_-U/VWY7rRsy6lI/AAAAAAAAAm0/rCYSdu7aiT8/s1600/10359151_10202235128462763_5230235295721268604_n.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-J1RjjZ3O_-U/VWY7rRsy6lI/AAAAAAAAAm0/rCYSdu7aiT8/s1600/10359151_10202235128462763_5230235295721268604_n.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;By the time the controversial and hugely influential Bikini Kill put  out their first bona fide full-length album in 1993, not counting the  killer previously released &lt;em&gt;First Two Records &lt;/em&gt;compilation that includes all the material from their first EP and their half of the &lt;em&gt;Yeah Yeah Yeah Yeah &lt;/em&gt;split  with the British band Huggybear, they had already made a statement that  had seldom been made in punk rock beforehand and continues to resonate  with listeners of all gender identities and sexual orientations today.  Even before the first bar of music on their first EP, lead singer  Kathleen Hanna\'s iconic rallying cry of "we want REVOLUTION, GIRL STYLE  NOW!" set the tone for the rest of the band\'s short but seismic run, and  for the riot grrrl movement that sprung largely from their figurative  loins. The music itself was a ferocious, cacophonous skronk, and Hanna\'s  take-no-prisoners voice gave the white hot rage and indignation of the  lyrics both death-or-glory urgency and a genuinely frightening,  unsettling power. Their material from the ensuing Huggybear split EP was  even more graphic and venomous, and recorded in a more obviously lo-fi  fashion than the songs on their previous record.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Given that, in a way it makes sense that &lt;em&gt;Pussy Whipped&lt;/em&gt;, recorded at the same time that their first EP was just being released and their &lt;em&gt;Yeah Yeah Yeah Yeah &lt;/em&gt;songs  were being recorded as well, marked the point where the group began to  spread their musical and lyrical wings just a little bit. Certainly not  enough to in any way abandon or retreat from the radical feminist  politics that still fueled the band\'s every move on and offstage, but  enough to prevent them from beginning to tread the same lyrical waters  as their earlier material with an inevitable lack of the same potency it  might\'ve had the first time around. It boasts the original version of  the self-explanatory classic rock anthem "Rebel Girl", in a different  key than the more well-known, polished Joan Jett produced version that  would be released as a single in 1994, but it also includes more  personal songs like "Alien She", a complicated psychological vignette  from Kathleen about her younger, mallgoing teenage self and the  contradictions between that girl, who wants her to go to the mall and  "put the pretty, pretty red lipstick on", and the "feminist dyke whore"  who wants to kill her, although she\'s afraid she might get killed  herself. There\'s also the surprisingly soft closing track, "For Tammy  Rae", a love letter to the photographer who snapped the album\'s cover  shot, although it wasn\'t actually the first time the group had done such  a song, as evidenced by earlier ones like "Outta Me".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Brief, running at less than half an hour long, and brusque, with its spirited, sustained bark against the patriarchy, &lt;em&gt;Pussy Whipped &lt;/em&gt;is  the better of the meager two proper studio albums the group ended up  releasing before its late 90\'s disintegration, and one of the better  records of its era in general. Furthermore, not many of the original  riot grrrl groups ever produced something as consistently interesting  and varied as this album, and that makes it one of the more important  and influential records of its era in a sociopolitical sense as much as  it is musically.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/bikini-kill-pussy-whipped-1993.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-J1RjjZ3O_-U/VWY7rRsy6lI/AAAAAAAAAm0/rCYSdu7aiT8/s72-c/10359151_10202235128462763_5230235295721268604_n.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8719665894608166357</guid><pubdate>Sat, 23 May 2015 04:06:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-22T21:06:47.184-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Jungle Brothers "Straight Out the Jungle" (1988)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-D9awBzuiObY/VV4esazJhXI/AAAAAAAAAmA/RPzmxtmnxOw/s1600/Straightoutthejungle.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-D9awBzuiObY/VV4esazJhXI/AAAAAAAAAmA/RPzmxtmnxOw/s1600/Straightoutthejungle.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Arriving towards the end of a year that stands as the inarguable zenith of hip-hop\'s golden age, the Jungle Brothers\' first album not only marked the introduction of one dope crew on the East Coast scene, it also heralded the beginning of the Native Tongues movement that also included Queen Latifah, De La Soul and A Tribe Called Quest. In fact, in &lt;i&gt;Straight Out the Jungle&lt;/i&gt;\'s classic cut "Black Is Black", their high school buddy Q-Tip makes his first appearance on a record, and his introduction to the song ("Now I\'m from a tribe called \'QUEST\', and I\'m here tonight with the Jungle Brothers...") actually resulted in his group changing their name from having originally just been QUEST. It doesn\'t get much more historically important than that.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Simply put, it\'s one of the best records of the era, positively loaded with dope rhymes, smart samples and great songs. Emcees Mike Gee and Afrika Baby Bam were as smooth as Rakim or Big Daddy Kane, but their Afrocentric rhymes were as potent as those of Public Enemy and helped set the standard for socially conscious hip-hop of the late 80\'s and early 90\'s. They update Grandmaster Flash\'s "The Message" with the title track, then Marvin Gaye\'s "What\'s Going On" with their own song of the same name, and then proceed tackle everything from black unity ("Black Is Black") to safe sex ("Jimbrowski"), not to mention all the far less serious stuff that lies in-between. Many other groups of a similar bent would take on these subjects in later years, and the Jungle Brothers were not only one of the first to do just that, but would also remain one of the best, not least because the Brothers never take themselves or the subject matter too seriously. There\'s a playful, block-party vibe that hearkens back to the earliest days of rap music in New York, and it runs throughout the album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;On the production end, it\'s a wonderfully low-budget, rough-edged sound; the group\'s innovative penchant for jazzy horn samples would help kick-start the entire jazz-rap subgenre, and even their concurrent love affair with James Brown came at a time when producers and DJ\'s (most notably Eric B. and Rakim) were just starting to make the Godfather of Soul one of the most sampled artists of all time. One cut in particular, though, really sounded like no other rap record before it: "I\'ll House You", a collaboration with producer Todd Terry, was one of the very first recordings to bridge the gap between hip-hop and house music, which was just on the cusp of jumping outside of Chicago and over to the United Kingdom in 1988 and wasn\'t quite huge across the US yet to begin with. Over the next several years, similar rap/dance hybrids would burn up the charts on both sides of the Atlantic, and it planted the seed for the advent of trip-hop in the early-to-mid 90\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As you\'d understand by now, &lt;i&gt;Straight Out the Jungle&lt;/i&gt; was enormously important and influential, being the very first album from a group out of the revered Native Tongues posse, but there would only be a handful of albums out of that whole movement that would ever be as consistently engaging and outstanding as the Jungle Brothers\' first, and that\'s what matters at the end of the day when it\'s canonized as one of the great hip-hop records of all time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/jungle-brothers-straight-out-jungle-1988.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-D9awBzuiObY/VV4esazJhXI/AAAAAAAAAmA/RPzmxtmnxOw/s72-c/Straightoutthejungle.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-668894659469383585</guid><pubdate>Thu, 14 May 2015 23:10:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-14T16:10:36.041-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>3rd Bass "The Cactus Album" (1989)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uphCNxq8VqE/VVUe4W472ZI/AAAAAAAAAlU/9J9YgLGOUgk/s1600/3rdBassTheCactusAlbum.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uphCNxq8VqE/VVUe4W472ZI/AAAAAAAAAlU/9J9YgLGOUgk/s1600/3rdBassTheCactusAlbum.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Despite the almost shocking commercial and critical success of the Beastie Boys, who fused rap/hip-hop with a snotty suburban punk aesthetic and attitude, and despite the fact that the audience for rap was making huge inroads into white suburbia by 1989, hip-hop culture was still almost exclusively the province of black men at the end of the 80\'s. In its own way, 3rd Bass\' &lt;i&gt;Cactus Album &lt;/i&gt;might have been even more revelatory and prescient than the Beasties\' &lt;i&gt;Licensed to Ill&lt;/i&gt;, in that it was an LP dominated by incomparably funky sounds (courtesy of the legendary Prince Paul, in tandem with members of The Bomb Squad) and commanded by two ace emcees, and it just so happened both of those emcees were white boys from New York, and they sound like it too. Where, upon the release of their first album, many people easily mistook the Beasties for a bunch of snarky white rock kids doing a big, loud parody of black rap music, 3rd Bass came out with neither those obvious hallmarks of a punk rock past or any attempt to pretend to be black kids from the ghetto, which is mighty impressive. In doing that, &lt;i&gt;The Cactus Album&lt;/i&gt; helped open the door for white kids to start taking on hip-hop as performers and DJ\'s, rather than just eager listeners, without succumbing to embarrassing cultural appropriation or poseurism, making it an important watershed in the music\'s spread from black ghettos in America throughout the whole wide multicultural world.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;That would be enough to make 3rd Bass\' debut a landmark, but it also just so happens to be a stone-cold classic of hip-hop\'s golden age when the race card is put away entirely. It may have been a golden age that produced scores of remarkably talented individuals and fantastic music, but even then there were still few rappers of any color who were as inventive, nimble or clever as MC Serch and Pete Nice; Serch is the goofy loudmouth who came up with a nifty slang term that made for the title of the album\'s one minor hit single, "The Gas Face", and the no-nonsense Pete gives off all the flippant, hair-trigger arrogance of a young mobster. Both of them have a way with words that most rappers would still kill for today, over a quarter-century after this album was waxed.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;To many casual listeners, "The Gas Face" would probably be the most recognizable cut off the album, and taken together with a very funny music video, that\'s nothing to lament, but the real high-watermark is a pair of anthems that arrive near the end of the record, "Brooklyn-Queens" and "Steppin\' to the A.M." The former is a sunny, funny yarn about scoring with stuck-up girls out of the maligned Queens Bridge, and the latter is its nocturnal, hard-hitting flipside, which is sure to rock any party it happens to drop into to this day. Elsewhere, the boys diss their ex-labelmates the Beasties in "Sons of 3rd Bass" ("Swarm to the lyrics cause Serch is your father/Screaming "Hey Ladies", why bother?"), send up jazz legend (and unwitting rap granddaddy) Louis Armstrong in "Flippin\' Off the Wall Like Lucy Ball", and repeatedly indulge in very brief skits that draw from Wild West mythology and black &amp;amp; white television.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They wind up with one of the greatest, most audacious debut albums in rap history, and a masterwork that 3rd Bass would never approach in what little time afterward they would remain active.
