They only made one more album, 1991\'s &lt;i&gt;Derelicts of Dialect&lt;/i&gt;, and although it produced their biggest chart hit in "Pop Goes the Weasel" (savaging Vanilla Ice), it wouldn\'t quite hit the same perfect-pitch note as its predecessor when taken together as a whole. It didn\'t matter; with &lt;i&gt;The Cactus Album&lt;/i&gt;, 3rd Bass helped pave the way for a world in which hip-hop has taken rock\'s place as the dominant music of America\'s multiracial youth culture, for better or for worse, and that alone makes it one of the most important albums of the last 25 years.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/3rd-bass-cactus-album-1989.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-uphCNxq8VqE/VVUe4W472ZI/AAAAAAAAAlU/9J9YgLGOUgk/s72-c/3rdBassTheCactusAlbum.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5699298151014505371</guid><pubdate>Sun, 10 May 2015 07:45:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-10T00:45:56.488-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Billie Holiday "Lady in Satin" (1958)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-tfoSHahwowk/VU8EXmaZFNI/AAAAAAAAAko/cbeCnNbmveM/s1600/220px-LadyInSatin.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-tfoSHahwowk/VU8EXmaZFNI/AAAAAAAAAko/cbeCnNbmveM/s1600/220px-LadyInSatin.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Near the end of a relentlessly tormented life, and by turn a tumultuous singing career, Lady Day was perhaps more famous than she had ever been, thanks largely to the publication of her (now largely discredited and/or factually disputed) autobiography "Lady Sings the Blues" and its accompanying album in 1956. But her personal life was falling apart, due to increasing drug and alcohol abuse and accompanying legal problems that were brought on by a nasty Federal Bureau of Narcotics campaign that did much to eventually hasten her ugly demise. By 1958, Billie\'s ethereal pipes had taken a beating from all the hard living, and the &lt;i&gt;Lady in Satin &lt;/i&gt;LP, which was the final album to be released during her lifetime and penultimate to be recorded, painfully spotlights her condition with some of the saddest and most moving performances of her career.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;To this day, many feel that Ray Ellis\' orchestral arrangements and strings took away from the raw power of Billie\'s voice, even though it was certainly not the first time she had been accompanied by this type of backing. But from the first craggy, weary words out of her mouth on "I\'m a Fool to Want You", any such griping really ought to be put away; Holiday had always been a slightly unconventional singer who would willfully twist her voice in ways that hadn\'t been heard before in popular music, and although her perfectly imperfect take here wasn\'t really a deliberate artistic statement so much as the result of the aforementioned self-abuse, it makes for a brutally honest, devastating performance.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And the remaining ten cuts are all exactly the same way, although prime highlights include "You\'ve Changed", "Glad to Be Unhappy" and "I\'ll Be Around". The whole thing is made particularly sad by how it would come to pass that Billie\'s voice would never be this strong again, as evidenced by her train-wreck final album simply titled &lt;i&gt;Last Recordings&lt;/i&gt;, which was recorded just months before her death in July 1959 and released almost immediately thereafter. &lt;i&gt;Lady in Satin &lt;/i&gt;truly is, as The Penguin Guide to Jazz once described it, "a voyeuristic look at a beaten woman", and over half a century after she succumbed to a nasty habit and a malicious government effort to do her in, it\'s still one of the most emotionally affecting pieces of American popular music.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/billie-holiday-lady-in-satin-1958.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-tfoSHahwowk/VU8EXmaZFNI/AAAAAAAAAko/cbeCnNbmveM/s72-c/220px-LadyInSatin.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3758346766706767799</guid><pubdate>Tue, 05 May 2015 03:56:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-05-04T20:56:00.532-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Isaac Hayes "Hot Buttered Soul" (1969)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-jLYj3i4j_vQ/VUgpU-dKosI/AAAAAAAAAkE/R8qrwrvSZY4/s1600/220px-Isaac_Hayes%2C_Hot_Buttered_Soul_Album_Cover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-jLYj3i4j_vQ/VUgpU-dKosI/AAAAAAAAAkE/R8qrwrvSZY4/s1600/220px-Isaac_Hayes%2C_Hot_Buttered_Soul_Album_Cover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;It\'s bizarre to think that a bona-fide masterpiece like &lt;i&gt;Hot Buttered Soul &lt;/i&gt;would result from a record label\'s frantic attempt to get back on its feet. It was bad enough when Otis Redding and members of The Bar-Kays perished in a tragic plane crash on December 10, 1967, but to make matters worse for Stax Records, just months later their entire back catalog would become property of Atlantic Records after a messy split from the larger label. Stax exec Al Bell ordered an absolute wash-out of 27 albums and 30 singles, all to be released at once, with all of the label\'s artists to immediately record new material. Isaac Hayes was a longtime in-house songwriter and producer, who might have stayed in that position had this mandate not occurred; Hayes\' debut album as a performer, &lt;i&gt;Presenting Isaac Hayes&lt;/i&gt;, had flopped commercially and garnered little critical notice. In this rush to get Stax back on track, Hayes was able to demand complete creative control when he went into Ardent Studios in Memphis to record another album, and the end result (embellished with string arrangements quite ironically in the Motor City by Johnny Allen prior to release) was quite a good many miles away from the kind of gritty, aggressive and single-oriented southern soul the label had once specialized in.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Hot Buttered Soul &lt;/i&gt;was a landmark release in the evolution of soul music, starting off with an absolutely breathtaking reading of Dionne Warwick\'s "Walk On By" that vamps all the way up to the twelve-minute mark. The original was a high-drama mini-masterwork of the Brill Building era to begin with, but Hayes turns it into an epic, sweeping piece of pure anguish, never once losing steam across those twelve minutes, a length that was still quite rare in R&amp;amp;B music in 1969, if not completely unprecedented at that point. Soul had never been quite this grandiose or cinematic before, an innovation which would be built upon by the likes of Marvin Gaye with &lt;i&gt;What\'s Going On &lt;/i&gt;and Curtis Mayfield with his solo debut &lt;i&gt;Curtis&lt;/i&gt; over the next couple of years, not to mention the lush "Philadelphia Sound" that became hugely successful on both the R&amp;amp;B and pop charts in the mid-70\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"Walk On By" would be enough to make this record a classic, but the slightly shorter "Hyperbolicsyllabicsesquedalymistic" manages to be another stunner, another monumental vamp that captures everything great about soul music itself. It gets right back down to the nasty, funky grit of Stax\'s glory days, while at the same time continuing to shatter boundaries of song structure and length, to say nothing of its tongue-twister title. A sampled bit of rolling piano from the extended instrumental coda would later power Public Enemy\'s classic "Black Steel in the Hour of Chaos", which is no doubt how many listeners who weren\'t around yet when Hayes cut his masterpiece discovered his music when they began working backwards, using PE and the Bomb Squad\'s expertly chosen, sampleadelic reference points as a guidebook to delve into deep soul and funk. His memorable and very, very funny voice acting role as The Chef on South Park would have to take the most cake for re-introducing him to younger audiences, but I digress.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;After these two epically long highlights, which constitute side one on the original LP version of the album, the five-minute "One Woman" is the most conventional number here, and just as gorgeous as the rest of it. And just as audaciously as it begins, &lt;i&gt;Hot Buttered Soul &lt;/i&gt;ends with its most adventurous cut, the 18-minute take on Jimmy Webb\'s "By the Time I Get to Phoenix". The first eight-and-a-half minutes consist of Hayes delivering a compelling monologue which fleshes out a vividly detailed backstory for the tune, and as with "Walk On By", when he finally gets around to his rendition of the actual song he transforms it into a sweeping, orchestral epic that reaches a devastating emotional climax.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With just one album, Isaac Hayes cemented in place a whole new sound for soul music, his own penchant for lengthy onstage banter to introduce songs, and an enduring image as one of the great R&amp;amp;B love men for all time, and he made one of the best soul albums of all time on top of all that, which makes &lt;i&gt;Hot Buttered Soul &lt;/i&gt;an essential item.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/05/isaac-hayes-hot-buttered-soul-1969.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-jLYj3i4j_vQ/VUgpU-dKosI/AAAAAAAAAkE/R8qrwrvSZY4/s72-c/220px-Isaac_Hayes%2C_Hot_Buttered_Soul_Album_Cover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-187236331409107175</guid><pubdate>Sun, 26 Apr 2015 15:40:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-04-26T08:40:07.974-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Black Sheep "A Wolf in Sheep\'s Clothing" (1991)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-FjN2pQ9AZKU/VTz-a6dbixI/AAAAAAAAAjU/7w_R2beEW-4/s1600/MI0002805792.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-FjN2pQ9AZKU/VTz-a6dbixI/AAAAAAAAAjU/7w_R2beEW-4/s1600/MI0002805792.jpg" height="314" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Black Sheep describe themselves as, well, just that among the Native Tongues alternative hip-hop community (De La Soul, Tribe Called Quest, Jungle Brothers) in their bizarre, gross-out spoken intro to their excellent 1991 debut album &lt;i&gt;A Wolf in Sheep\'s Clothing&lt;/i&gt;, which just might be the funniest and most clever record from any Native Tongues group, as well as one of the best. The intro is a bit of a chuckle, but the album really begins with the absolutely hilarious, comically ultraviolent gangsta rap parody "U Mean I\'m Not" which would apparently later be a regular part of Korn\'s live repertoire. One wonders whether or not they got the joke, and with the same token one ponders that when thinking of the hordes of increasingly hackneyed, cookie-cutter thug-rappers that would eventually come to dominate the hip-hop community and become the most commercially viable aspect of the culture.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As sidesplitting as it may be at times, &lt;i&gt;A Wolf in Sheep\'s Clothing &lt;/i&gt;is hardly a comedy album. Its humor is thoroughly smart and self-deprecating, and much of the time the joke is on them and their own culture. By the looks of it, a whole lot of people are out to whoop the duo\'s asses here; despite tunes like "Strobelite Honey" and "Hoes We Knows", their misogyny level was kept at a sensible minimum compared to the likes of N.W.A. or 2 Live Crew, but just try telling that to "Ladies Against Sexist Motherfuckers" when they take these Black Sheep to task. They get the same treatment from Afrocentric thinkers in "Are You Mad?", and in both instances they manage to escape unharmed. It was an almost tiresomely (though justifiable) complaint by this point in hip-hop culture, but they take rappers who sell out to task on "To Whom it May Concern".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They tout the merits of a "Similak Child" over an ominous sample from Jefferson Airplane\'s "Today" and deliver one of the best rap singles of 1991 with "The Choice is Yours", including two versions on this record, but for the most part they simply position themselves as a couple of easygoing, bumbling playboys who are as good on the mic as they are with the ladies, as well as a pair of outcasts in a community that was increasingly split between the two extremes of gangsta rap\'s unbridled violence and the self-serious, socially conscious end of alternative rap. Coming from a goofy, yet intelligent gap in the canyon, &lt;i&gt;A Wolf in Sheep\'s Clothing &lt;/i&gt;is one of the best records of its time, a refreshing breath of fresh air that came at a time when rap music was already starting to become predictable and marketable. Lord knows the hip-hop and music world in general could use a record like this one in 2015.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/04/black-sheep-wolf-in-sheeps-clothing-1991.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-FjN2pQ9AZKU/VTz-a6dbixI/AAAAAAAAAjU/7w_R2beEW-4/s72-c/MI0002805792.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-31782502232079565</guid><pubdate>Sat, 25 Apr 2015 20:44:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-04-25T13:44:33.751-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Boogie Down Productions "By All Means Necessary" (1988)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-BHOMItPftfs/VTia7VWh5jI/AAAAAAAAAis/RfEGyvFmyT0/s1600/220px-BDPByAllMeans.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-BHOMItPftfs/VTia7VWh5jI/AAAAAAAAAis/RfEGyvFmyT0/s1600/220px-BDPByAllMeans.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;DJ Scott La Rock\'s senseless, sudden shooting death on August 27, 1987 shook his Boogie Down Productions cohort KRS-One to the core, causing him to rethink his lyrical focus and restart BDP as an essentially solo project, adopting the persona of "The Teacher" in the process and spelling out the change in attitude from the unbridled brashness of BDP\'s landmark first album &lt;i&gt;Criminal Minded&lt;/i&gt; in the opening track ("My Philosophy") of the group\'s sophomore album, &lt;i&gt;By All Means Necessary&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Where he had once been the first MC to pose on the cover of an album with a gun and one of the first to inject dancehall reggae into his rapping, KRS-One was now possibly the first to tout Krishna-Conscious vegetarianism and disdain competition in the hip-hop culture, and that\'s just in the first track. As on BDP\'s previous album, KRS has a gun in his hand on the front cover, but now he\'s only mimicking a famous photograph of Malcolm X. In "I\'m Still #1", he criticizes those who would call themselves kings of a culture that wasn\'t even two decades old back in \'88, and correctly predicts that guys like him will one day be seen as rap\'s old school. In "Illegal Business", he outlines the US government\'s ties to the drug trade in brutal and exacting terms, touching right on a contemporary wave of controversy that blighted the ass-end of Ronald Reagan\'s presidency over alleged CIA involvement in cocaine trafficking, which would come further to light in later years with investigative journalist Gary Webb\'s incendiary 1996 "Dark Alliance" series. &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;He could\'ve simplistically reversed his earlier braggadocio and morphed into an obnoxious preacher spouting cliched slogans, which critics would soon accuse him of doing at the turn of the 90\'s as he slowly faded from prominence in a then-rapidly evolving rap scene, but he loses none of the self-assured swagger that\'s been a hip-hop tradition from the beginning. Both the putdowns of "Ya Slippin\'" and his pleas for peace in the surprisingly (or perhaps unfortunately) enduring anthem "Stop the Violence" resonate today, and that\'s why &lt;i&gt;By All Means Necessary &lt;/i&gt;is undeniably as much of a stone-cold classic as its predecessor. On any side left or right of Public Enemy, politically conscious hip-hop simply doesn\'t get much better than this.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/04/boogie-down-productions-by-all-means.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-BHOMItPftfs/VTia7VWh5jI/AAAAAAAAAis/RfEGyvFmyT0/s72-c/220px-BDPByAllMeans.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8092503632616370226</guid><pubdate>Thu, 23 Apr 2015 06:56:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-04-22T23:56:23.882-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Boogie Down Productions "Criminal Minded" (1987)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MUb3lpoIMO0/VTiKlB90NuI/AAAAAAAAAic/q4wPWwgatFA/s1600/Criminal_Minded_Album_Cover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MUb3lpoIMO0/VTiKlB90NuI/AAAAAAAAAic/q4wPWwgatFA/s1600/Criminal_Minded_Album_Cover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Released in early 1987 with relatively little initial notice on the indie label B-Boy Records, as tiny as it was shady, &lt;i&gt;Criminal Minded&lt;/i&gt; upped the ante on the latent violence and aggression that was already bubbling to the surface in rap (check Schoolly D\'s "P.S.K." and Ice-T\'s "Six in the Mornin\'", both of which were released earlier), and as such it\'s still cited as one of the cornerstone records of hardcore hip-hop, as well as a key progenitor of gangsta rap. As great as the music is, its most striking innovation wasn\'t even the music itself, but the album\'s cover art; it was the very first hip-hop record with a group posed packing pistols on the front cover, making for a genuinely startling statement in the rap community of the time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Comprised initially of the fiery MC KRS-One and the skillful DJ Scott La Rock, Boogie Down Productions would soon shift to a much more socially conscious, politically-minded focus after La Rock\'s tragic murder just months after the release of &lt;i&gt;Criminal Minded&lt;/i&gt;, but here, KRS bore out blunt street reportage ("9mm Goes Bang", "Remix for P is Free") and nasty attacks, particularly on the Queens-based MC Shan ("South Bronx", "The Bridge is Over"). All of it is supported by Scott La Rock\'s brutally spare, hard-hitting beats, communicating urban decay and savage realism as effectively as their nascent West Coast gangsta contemporaries. By far the album\'s apex is "9mm Goes Bang"; KRS\' singsong reggae-rap, which as a vocal delivery made him among the first rappers to explicitly acknowledge rap\'s roots in the sounds of Jamaica, pairs with the graphic violence depicted in his words ("Me knew a crack dealer, his name was Peter/Had to buck him down with my nine milometer") to make for something that remains as disturbing as it was in 1987. In contrast to the in-your-face thuggishness of an Ice Cube or an Ice-T, he describes the gunplay and bloodshed so casually and playfully as to send a chill up anyone\'s spine.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All the unapologetic arrogance and nastiness would quickly recede into the past with La Rock\'s death and KRS-One\'s subsequent reinvention as "The Teacher", as was perhaps inevitable, since the worst aspects of ghetto life never quite make themselves as apparent as when the death and violence at its heart comes straight to your house, so to speak. But &lt;i&gt;Criminal Minded&lt;/i&gt; captures that street-level vibe better than nearly any other album of its time, and it\'s no wonder that it\'s still a stone-cold rap/hip-hop classic.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/04/boogie-down-productions-criminal-minded.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MUb3lpoIMO0/VTiKlB90NuI/AAAAAAAAAic/q4wPWwgatFA/s72-c/Criminal_Minded_Album_Cover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6551316349138704728</guid><pubdate>Thu, 23 Apr 2015 05:09:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-04-22T22:09:35.629-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Nas "Illmatic" (1994)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-lJ-IhPGHE1c/VThvNdYCMnI/AAAAAAAAAiM/JCWn88DfGcM/s1600/220px-NasIllmatic.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-lJ-IhPGHE1c/VThvNdYCMnI/AAAAAAAAAiM/JCWn88DfGcM/s1600/220px-NasIllmatic.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In hip-hop culture, authenticity and "keepin\' it real" have always been among the most important attributes of an MC who\'s a cut or two above the rest of the pack. In a roughly ten-year period that saw this nascent form of music evolve at a startlingly fast pace, producing scores of amazing talents and classic albums, few artists got their point across as forcefully and concisely as Nasir bin Olu Dara Jones did on his remarkable debut LP, 1994\'s &lt;i&gt;Illmatic&lt;/i&gt;, which still stands as one of the greatest and most influential rap albums of all time. Unlike many hip-hop full-lengths, from the junky commercial blockbusters to the acclaimed avant-garde opuses, &lt;i&gt;Illmatic &lt;/i&gt;just about makes the 40 minute mark, in contrast to the standard 50-100 minute running time that\'s common to many albums of the CD age in general. This results in a positively dynamite sequence of ten songs, not broken up by any filler material of any type, all chock-filled with detail and somber depictions of the blighted New York in which "Nasty Nas" (as he was originally dubbed when the incendiary "Halftime" was originally released as a single, over a year and a half before the album) came up.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As chronicled in the bitter "N.Y. State of Mind", Nas was a prominent member of a new generation of rappers coming up in the early-to-mid 1990\'s, who had survived the inner-city crack epidemic that was finally waning by the time they made their debut on wax and which informed the rhymes of everything from the graphic, cold accounts of fellow New Yorkers Wu-Tang Clan and The Notorious B.I.G. to the widescreen gangsta grooves of West Coasters like Snoop Dogg and Tupac Shakur. His references to shootouts, dope dealing and crackheads were anything but unprecedented by 1994, and continue to be mined endlessly as subject matter for a huge passel of MC\'s (of varying quality and/or credibility) to this day, but few people were ever as unfailingly human, compassionate or matter-of-fact in their words and in the sound surrounding them. Some of his words are so striking, they practically jump out of the speakers: "I never sleep cause sleep is the cousin of death", "I\'m out for dead presidents to represent me", "Some rest in the pieces/Some are sitting in San Quentin". It\'s a heady, startling mirror reflection of a frame of mind where you\'d better focus on the good in life, because your life could very well end as soon as you get done rocking the mic on any given night.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;One would be hard-pressed to think of compassion when listening to the hard-edged "State of Mind" or "Halftime", or the equally ethereal, grim, and underrated "Life\'s a Bitch", but in "One Love", he writes a letter to one of his mates in the lockup, who becomes a father while doing a six-year bid. Without succumbing to melodrama or cliche, it brings home the sad reality of the lengthy prison terms that so many young African-American men in particular have wound up with as a result of a life of crime, one that seemed especially all too easy (and appealing) to turn to at a time when the crack cocaine game really was the number one employer of ghetto youth. &lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There\'s absolutely no glamor or theater in this music, spare and basic, with no flash to the beats or the overall engineering. With the assistance of well-established jazz-rap producers Q-Tip, Pete Rock, Large Professor and DJ Premier, Nas creates a gritty, grim sound that, when taken with the words and his effortless flow, envelops the listener in his world. As tough as he may be, he never pretends to be larger-than-life or to be any tougher than his homeboys, many of whom were already dead by the time he began his now-storied career at the age of just 18. The indisputable authenticity and truthfulness that flow like his rhymes through the entirety of&lt;i&gt; Illmatic &lt;/i&gt;immediately made him stand out in the crowd when the album was released, and it captures a purity of expression that was never captured in quite this way again. That\'s why it\'s still not only one of the best pieces of work to come out of hip-hop, but it\'s also one of the best and most influential albums of both its time and all-time in popular music.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/04/nas-illmatic-1994.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-lJ-IhPGHE1c/VThvNdYCMnI/AAAAAAAAAiM/JCWn88DfGcM/s72-c/220px-NasIllmatic.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8040041662821890806</guid><pubdate>Mon, 06 Apr 2015 06:57:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-04-05T23:57:02.312-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Life Stories</category><title>We Were the Best (Enjoy Poverty, Dumbass Hipsters)</title><description>It\'s really hard to believe how old I am now. Being 20 years old, which I will be on the third of June this year, is by no stretch of the imagination "old", even in fashion or "entertainment industry" terms. I won\'t be able to legally buy alcohol for another whole year after that. But it still marks the end of your "teen years", those years which are supposed to be so great but usually seem to wind up as a predominantly turbulent and unhappy (though undeniably crucial) time in your life, and there\'s something to be said of the changes (most) people undergo from the age of 16 to 20.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;These changes are different for everybody, but for one thing out of my own life, I lost both my maternal grandparents in the span of two months towards the end of 2013, shortly after I would\'ve graduated high school and shortly before I would get my GED diploma in December. I was very close to them, particularly my grandma Kathy, who died suddenly of a heart attack on the 18th of October that year, in contrast to her husband Dale, who had died exactly two months to the day earlier after months of battling cancer of the esophagus and narrowly cheating seemingly certain death several times along the way. I\'m not gonna turn on the sap and tell you it was "the end of my childhood", despite the almost poignant timing, being after all just after I had legally become an "adult". But it did seem to represent the end of my boyhood in a profound enough way; by the time grandma died, I was no longer able to take those "vacations" I used to have at her house for anywhere from a few days to over two weeks at a time, thanks to having recently started working my very first paying job at Papa John\'s in Coralville, Iowa, where my orientation also just so happened to have been the day after grandpa died. Those visits had already been declining in number since the beginning of the previous year, because of what a busy social life I had at that point, and for the most part I didn\'t want to be spending a week at my grandma\'s house anymore anyway, but this was something where I was required to show up at a certain time on XYZ days and be there for a set amount of time. I hadn\'t had that type of commitment for most of my life up to then. Since I didn\'t really "need" money at that time, the job was very part-time and usually only took about 12 hours out out of an average week, but it still very much represented the dawning of a new era in my life after the near-completely responsibility-free existence I had enjoyed from early childhood thanks to being essentially unschooled, which had a few social disadvantages later on but afforded me almost unlimited free time to do whatever I wanted.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Now, as I write this, I\'m working five days a week at the Coralville Walmart, putting in a solid forty hours every week with only two measly days to myself, though there have been the occasional three-day weekends and a few "sick" call-in days that were more fib than legit. I\'m doing that many hours because these days I actually need the much more substantial pay checks in order to afford $500 per month in rent, not to mention food when my EBT card is empty, and the only reason I\'m paying only $500 is because my mother is leasing my place (not living here, though) and so I pay her a slight reduction of what the full cost is combined with utilities and the cellphone bill that my parents often lump together with "your total expenses". It\'s not such a bad life. I\'ve gotten used to it, and inevitably I\'ve learned to appreciate free time so much more than I did when there was virtually no limit to it. But when I watch a movie like "We Are the Best!", which I saw for the first time tonight and which is a great Swedish film about three thirteen-year-old girls who form a punk band in the early 80\'s, it really brings out all that misty eyed nostalgia crap from within the uber-sentimental dude that will probably always lurk just beneath the surface for the rest of my life. Specifically, for when I was 15, 16, 17 years old: hanging out with my best mates, going to&amp;nbsp; DIY punk shows with them whenever we could, sleeping over at eachother\'s houses, and all that other great stuff that I don\'t do so often nowadays. It doesn\'t hurt that in the movie, a youth center plays an important role in shaping the girls in the band and their resolve to do what they do no matter what obstacles stand in their way, and it serves as a primary focal point in its very funny and very sweet ending; I can\'t help but be reminded of how my life back in those days was centered more often than not on hanging out at the United Action for Youth center in Iowa City, which has among other things nurtured musically inclined kids for decades with a rehearsal room, recording equipment and instrument lessons. Recently on one of those "sick days", I saw "SLC Punk!" for the first time since I was 16 or 17 and it gave me the same feeling, even moreso because it had been a favorite of all of ours among the little social group I had back then.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Particularly, "We Are the Best!" reminds me of meeting a guy who\'s been a friend longer than most other people I know outside of blood relatives, and how we started a band that may have undeniably sucked ass but would undoubtedly alter our immediate futures not only as musicians, but also as people.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;I had seen this kid around the UAY center a few times before. It was mid-late November of 2010. I\'d just started hanging out there regularly again after about a year where in the span of twelve months\' time I had only been inside once for like two minutes or something. He was a scrawny black guy who was always riffing on one of the center\'s acoustic guitars. One day he had left his City High School student ID in the UAY\'s jam room, and I went outside to where he was holding court with a few friends to give it back to him. His name was Detrell Smith. The first time we really spoke was maybe two days later, when I stopped by after picking up a vinyl copy of the first Ramones album at the local record shop, which I told my dad was a "half present" for mom cause she dug the Ramones as much as I did (and still do). I was sort of not supposed to be buying stuff for myself because it was the month before Christmas and I was supposed to be buying gifts for people, a hokey "tradition" that hadn\'t really been in effect the previous November or December because my folks weren\'t giving me weekly allowance money anyway during the holiday season that year, and wouldn\'t be repeated again. I was talking to a UAY staffmember about my mom liking the Ramones, and that\'s when he chimed in with, "Your mom likes the Ramones? That\'s really cool." It was our very first conversation, and by the end of that day he had invited me to sing for a recording session he had booked in the UAY jam room/studio that was coming up in January.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There was essentially no rehearsal for the occasion, at least not with me. All that had been decided was that I was going to sing my song "I\'m Mental" along with a few other songs we were just gonna pick on the fly with him playing guitar and his friend playing the drums. It wasn\'t until I showed up for the session that I met that friend, Max Bills, who was an equally scrawny Jewish guy with blonde hair that went down past his shoulders and very briefly made me sort of half-wonder whether or not he was male or female, or if he was a transperson of some kind or another. I didn\'t even know it at the time, but Max later told me that it was essentially the first time he had ever played drums. We did "I\'m Mental", the Ramones\' "Blitzkrieg Bop" and a cover of New Zealand punk band The Rabble\'s song "I\'m Bored" where Detrell took the lead vocal. The performances were pretty much incompetent even by punk rock standards, but they were enormously fun to do, and it was my first ever recording session. We had the makings of a band, and I came up with the name The Tea Party, after the insufferable extreme-right wing group that continues to subject Americans to some of the most baseless and idiotic political banter that anyone has ever been able to muster up, but a semi obscure Canadian group already had dibs on that name and released records years before, and because I thought we were actually going to get recognition and face legal action from this other group, I changed it to The Tea Party Fascists. A month later it was changed again, to The No. Again, I had originally intended to use something else, in this case No Means No, but again another band had dibs on the name years before, in this case one that turned out to be the very well-respected and well-known punk rock trio that by sheer bizarre coincidence were also from Canada.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Under our revised moniker, we went back in the studio in March with barely any more rehearsal than had been done the first time around, and the only thing that had changed is that we were now &lt;i&gt;hilariously &lt;/i&gt;awful, not just instrumentally inept in a more basic, garden-variety way as had been the case before. Well, that, and we had added the bass player Martin Herrera, a very quiet guy who would eventually become quite surprisingly proficient after this little group fizzled out. He\'s since put down the instrument and joined the US Army, apparently. All playing live in the same room, with no vocals tracked separately, resulting in a hilariously disjointed sound to my singing on the new version of "I\'m Mental". We also did a song, sung by Detrell and Max, that they had written called "What?" on which I supplied slightly out of tune rhythm guitar to back up Detrell\'s riffing. The funniest moment was a horribly hackneyed "macho" Rolling Stones-meets-Modern Lovers-meets-Velvet Underground tune called "My Back Seat", which was this big dumbass lascivious come on to a fictional girl in the backseat of my fictional car. Detrell and Max later marked this song, with its lack of a real dynamic backbeat, terrible lyrics and all around boring ass structure, as the main reason they split off from me almost immediately after the session and started a "side-project" called Other Band which eventually roped in Martin when The No inevitably "broke up" in April upon Detrell\'s "quitting" our failed attempt at a band. We never played a gig.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;By no means had we stopped being friends, though; we still hung out all the time, although at the time Max sort of pissed me off cause he would say things like "man, your songwriting sucks" more than was needed for me to understand that "My Back Seat" was a total piece of shit. Other Band rehearsed incessantly, all summer, during which time I had stopped going to UAY as often, and eventually went up onstage at the center just a few days before Halloween to do their first ever show. As it turned out, another reason they had split to do their own thing was that they were more interested in playing hyper fast ska and pop-punk inflected stuff, a la Operation Ivy and Rancid, whereas I was already interested in many types of music aside from punk and had been for over a year prior to when we all met. As much as it wasn\'t really my thing, they mimicked that ska-punk sound with tremendous enthusiasm and were a &lt;i&gt;much&lt;/i&gt; tighter band than the one they had been in with me.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;At the beginning of 2012, I once again began going to the UAY every day it was open during the week, which was Thursday, Friday and Saturday afternoons, to be more specific 3-7pm for the first two of those days and then from noon till 5pm on Saturdays. The main reason at the time was that I had a crush on a girl I met in mid-January who would soon form another punk band that became quite popular in Iowa City, even moreso than Other Band as a matter of fact, but seeing as that crush was one-sided, would soon spawn much friction and ultimately lead to the shittiest times I had during my UAY-going years, these days I prefer to look back on the times I spent that year hanging out with Max and/or Detrell not only at UAY but also at my house, Max\'s house or Detrell\'s house depending on the night. Sometimes being at Detrell\'s wound up being a lousy night for me because at the time he lived with his grandfather, and he had this "rule" of "no swearing at my house" because he said he respected his grandpa too much or something like that, which irritated me more than it really should\'ve. On a side note, I also recorded two EP\'s of predominantly Black Flag-derived punk at the UAY during this time, and although I don\'t really like them anymore they were the only "proper" recordings that were really released out of the relative wealth of material I recorded there over the years. It was definitely at once both the peak of that whole period of life, and also the biggest downer of the time because of the other stuff that happened, which by the end of 2012 eventually encompassed other dramas that wound up ending my friendship with Max and briefly ending it with Detrell for brief periods of time over the following year after that. The last time I was at UAY was back in May 2013, weeks before I turned 18. On various occasions since then I\'ve meant to stop in and just say hi to some of the staffpeople I made good friends with, but on each of these occasions bad weather conditions usually thwarted those plans.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There\'s one memory I have of hanging out with Max, Detrell and a few others in the hours between UAY closing on a Saturday and the start of a show we were all going to later that night at Public Space One, which is a long-running art &amp;amp; music co-op type deal that was housed at the Jefferson Building in Iowa City at the time and would move to a different location in town the following summer. It was September of 2012, and football season was kicking back into gear that very day, as evidenced by the hordes of what I call "football heathens" parading through downtown all day. Their whole dumbass rah-rah "PAAAAARTAY, WOOOO!!!" thing even crept into PS1 at the show; the venue didn\'t allow drinking on the premises, nor does it today at its current location, but some guy came in super drunk already and was obnoxiously crowdsurfing and making a real jackass of himself early on during the show. Shortly before it began, as me and the rest of our little motley crew were hanging out in an alleyway some ways from PS1, a couple of musclebound and shirtless jock douchebags passed us by and one of them remarked something along the lines of: "Dumbass hipster punks, enjoy your poverty", probably accompanied by some bad fag joke or something. I said something like, "you keep passing your football around, man."&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;That was one of the funniest memories of my "youth", which is hardly over now in the grand scheme of things since I\'m only just about to turn 20, but sometimes it feels like I\'m turning into "a man" in the most predictable, boring sense already, which is mainly just because of how so much of my time is taken up by working at Walmart. I\'m still friends with Detrell, who also works pretty frequently in night stock at HyVee in Iowa City, so we\'re kind of on an even keel in that respect. The same pretty much goes for Max, I\'m told, though we\'ve hardly spoken since the end of 2012 for a variety of reasons. I\'m getting back into the swing of the scene now, albeit not in the way it used to be in that, for one thing, I\'m well past the age of the kids who are welcome to hang out at UAY on a regular basis, as are the two founding members of Other Band, who now have a different, younger bassist. I don\'t hang at PS1 anymore and am no longer a "hardcore kid", which I could sort of comfortably classify myself as for much of the time I was 17 even though I was always listening to other kinds of music as well. I guess that was the story of how at one time with those original mates, even long after we were no longer in a band together, "we were the best!" And regardless of the negative bits, considerable though some may be, I\'ll always think fondly of the whole time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/04/we-were-best-enjoy-poverty-dumbass.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-133772442507284414</guid><pubdate>Sun, 29 Mar 2015 21:45:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-29T14:49:25.989-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pere Ubu "Terminal Tower" (1985)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyJGjxWESN0/VRhmMgpzuJI/AAAAAAAAAgU/Qf1Ex88TfJU/s1600/MI0000032900.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyJGjxWESN0/VRhmMgpzuJI/AAAAAAAAAgU/Qf1Ex88TfJU/s1600/MI0000032900.jpg" height="320" width="318" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;When listening to the first three tracks on &lt;i&gt;Terminal Tower: An Archival Collection&lt;/i&gt;, it\'s still startling to think that they were all recorded in 1975, before punk proper had come to fruition and well before the experimentalism of post-punk. Even more astonishing is how Pere Ubu formed their own tiny label, Hearthan Records to release their debut single, "Heart of Darkness" backed with "30 Seconds Over Tokyo", a good number of years before the American hardcore punk underground sprang up from California, Washington D.C. and all across the country powered entirely by self-sufficient indie labels like SST, Dischord Records and Alternative Tentacles. They set themselves apart from the pack from the very beginning, and have remained one of the most thoroughly unique and undefinable, ever-shifting musical entities around, in a career that\'s been on and off for the past four decades now.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;None of the talk about how far-reaching and prescient they were would be worth a shit if their music wasn\'t as surprising, challenging and totally compelling as it is. "Heart of Darkness" starts off with a clatter of clanging guitar chords and drums before morphing into something quite heavily reminiscent of the Motorik pulse of Neu!, albeit far more outwardly sinister and disturbed, with the incomparable, oft-unintelligible mumbling of David Thomas lending just one of many idiosyncratic touches that remained obtuse and entirely their own in the years that followed. "30 Seconds Over Tokyo" is even more striking, with its Black Sabbath-esque chromatic riff, explosions of noise both from the guitars and Allen Ravenstine\'s EML synthesizer, and a resolutely bizarre rhythmic shift in the middle from a creepy-crawling, fractured waltz to some kind of country/polka mental breakdown. It\'s not often you can say that something legitimately sounds like nothing else that had ever previously been recorded, even considering the radically strange sounds of such predecessors as Zappa, Beefheart, The United States of America and the Silver Apples, and this one is of a rare breed in that respect.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"Final Solution" is much more conventionally structured, and lyrically conventional, a "dumb teen angst song" that is nonetheless augmented by all manner of avant-garde flourishes, thus making for possibly the most obvious example of Ubu\'s self-proclaimed "avant-garage" label. Obviously, despite its controversial title, it has nothing to do with the Holocaust or Nazism, but when British punkers began appropriating the swastika as a form of pure provocation not long after its recording, the band promptly dropped it from their repertoire for a number of years.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While that three-song salvo is enough of a knockout punch of frightening bliss, the remaining material on this collection is never anything other than a case in point of just that kind of blissful eccentricity, whether it\'s the almost shockingly bright chime of "Heaven" and "Not Happy" or a frighteningly intense live recording of the song "Humor Me", possibly the angriest and most aggressive song of their early days which was written about the premature alcohol-related death of original member Peter Laughner, and later included in a drastically more tame studio version on their 1978 full-length debut &lt;i&gt;The Modern Dance&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Terminal Tower&lt;/i&gt; goes beyond the quality of the best sort of compilation album; as great as many of their studio albums are, especially &lt;i&gt;The Modern Dance &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Dub Housing&lt;/i&gt;, the comp is arguably more consistent from beginning to end than any of them, and as diverse a range of both material and lineup configurations there is in the five-year span covered, it all feels like it\'s supposed to sit where it sits in the sequencing order, like it really is a proper album. It\'s concise, listenable, and there\'s still not much else out there that sounds like the music contained on it.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/pere-ubu-terminal-tower-1985.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyJGjxWESN0/VRhmMgpzuJI/AAAAAAAAAgU/Qf1Ex88TfJU/s72-c/MI0000032900.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-269270848548179886</guid><pubdate>Sun, 29 Mar 2015 04:10:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-28T21:10:14.652-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pavement "Crooked Rain, Crooked Rain" (1994)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-ML6gQk9nKYs/VRdxjbWhhpI/AAAAAAAAAgA/B_UjsNto7SI/s1600/220px-Pavement_Crooked_Rain.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-ML6gQk9nKYs/VRdxjbWhhpI/AAAAAAAAAgA/B_UjsNto7SI/s1600/220px-Pavement_Crooked_Rain.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;And so with their second full-length album, Pavement essentially pulled a Pinocchio by completing the transition from Stephen Malkmus and Scott Kannberg\'s lo-fi recording project into a proper, full-fledged rock band, which was already underway from the recording of &lt;i&gt;Slanted and Enchanted&lt;/i&gt; and subsequent extensive touring. Shortly before recording &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain, Crooked Rain&lt;/i&gt;, the group jettisoned their 40-year-old ex-hippie drummer Gary Young, who had recorded Malkmus and Kannberg\'s earliest compositions under the Pavement name in his home studio and played a huge role in defining their early reputation as a very erratic, messy live act with his bizarre behavior and drunken performances. His departure seemed to take a lot of their endearing ineptitude with him, but seeing as his replacement Steve West is as fine a drummer they could\'ve hoped for with his steadily building, exciting fills, it\'s hardly a sad loss.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The lineup change is the farthest thing from the only or the most significant thing that\'s different this time around; where &lt;i&gt;Slanted and Enchanted&lt;/i&gt; was a dense, enigmatic affair, brimming with fantastic, oftentimes melodic songs that were nonetheless thoroughly off-kilter and surrounded by all manner of murk, mystique and tape hiss, &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain &lt;/i&gt;embraces a shimmering classic-rock sound that nonetheless retains all of Pavement\'s personality. The noise and the lyrical impenetrability are entirely still here, especially the latter, yet it sounds a million times more accessible than its predecessor, right from the staggeringly gorgeous opener "Silence Kit", and a couple of songs even became moderate alt-rock hits, which of course are "Cut Your Hair" and "Range Life". One is without a doubt their most winsome "pop" moment, only to be rivaled later in the record by the positively glistening "Gold Soundz", while the other one has a then-mildly controversial verse (bagging sardonically on the Smashing Pumpkins and Stone Temple Pilots) that in hindsight seems to spell out the ever-more visible divide between the commercial "alternative rock" that Nirvana had popularized a few years earlier and the still-underground, still violently anti-commercial "indie rock" that was just beginning to define itself apart from mainstream rock of the time in the view of casual observers at least. Released as a single just after Nirvana\'s tragic dissolution and amid a sea of "bubblegrunge" imitators that would only grow in number and cringeworthiness over the latter half of the 90\'s, it captures a very different kind of shared feeling than the one spoken to in "Smells Like Teen Spirit", and in its own way it\'s just as historically important for that.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Even more impressive, the already genre-bending Pavement were trying loads of new sounds, such as country-rock ("Range Life", "Stop Breathin\'") and even a bit of jazz ("5-4=Unity"). It\'s a quantum leap forward from its predecessor, never once really sounding like the previous album in the slightest way, shape or form, and at the same time it still sounded like nothing and nobody else under the sun when it was released in early 1994. The genre-busting would come even further to the fore with the wildly eclectic &lt;i&gt;Wowee Zowee&lt;/i&gt; the following year, but it\'s definitely more focused and concentrated on &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain&lt;/i&gt;, which of course is still rightfully acclaimed as one of the greatest rock albums of the 1990\'s. Along with &lt;i&gt;Slanted&lt;/i&gt;, &lt;i&gt;Crooked Rain &lt;/i&gt;is almost inarguably the band\'s most fully realized and successful work, and it\'s all the more stunning for how successful a more accessible sound proves to be for one of the most notoriously outre bands of the era.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/pavement-crooked-rain-crooked-rain-1994.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-ML6gQk9nKYs/VRdxjbWhhpI/AAAAAAAAAgA/B_UjsNto7SI/s72-c/220px-Pavement_Crooked_Rain.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5464185358386610510</guid><pubdate>Fri, 27 Mar 2015 15:13:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-27T08:13:42.457-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Joni Mitchell "Blue" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-xqjl-E86ZJI/VRVqIvG40KI/AAAAAAAAAfs/1Vj7Xsatb-M/s1600/Bluealbumcover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-xqjl-E86ZJI/VRVqIvG40KI/AAAAAAAAAfs/1Vj7Xsatb-M/s1600/Bluealbumcover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Written partially as she vacationed in Europe following a breakup with Graham Nash, Joni Mitchell would later say that she felt essentially naked when she created her finest work, 1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Blue&lt;/i&gt;, and the album reflects her own feeling that she "had no defenses" to put up and nothing to hide. As deeply personal as the album is, it hit a raw nerve with a post-counterculture audience at the time of its release, which of course was the height of the early-70\'s singer-songwriter boom, and &lt;i&gt;Blue &lt;/i&gt;would remain the high watermark of the sensitive folksinger pantomime of the era, rivaled only by fellow Canadian Neil Young\'s &lt;i&gt;After the Gold Rush&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Unlike many other hit records of that era, however, Mitchell here expands her musical palette beyond what had been heard on her previous three albums, incorporating more wholly piano-led material than her earlier days, and utilizing her trademark unique guitar style when she does play the guitar. More importantly, the songs are often unconventionally structured and incorporate elements of jazz, paving the way for the weirder, wilder sounds she would dive into later in the decade as scores of soft-rockers heavily influenced by albums like &lt;i&gt;Blue &lt;/i&gt;made a killing on the charts.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While its reputation lies on it being the quintessential "confessional" singer-songwriter album, an idea Mitchell has often derided over the years, it\'s not quite a concept album about the aforementioned breakup, or about her newfound relationship with James Taylor, nor is it an entirely down album by any stretch of the imagination; "California", a worthy successor to "Woodstock", is proof positive of that. But the key to what makes &lt;i&gt;Blue &lt;/i&gt;so powerful is its intimacy and its honesty, which was so stark and revealing by the ears of one Kris Kristofferson that when Mitchell played him the record for the first time, the country music legend supposedly remarked, "Joni! Keep something of yourself!"&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There\'s no pretension or messing around in the opening "All I Want", or in the gorgeous love song that follows, "My Old Man". The older song "Little Green" was long thought to be an exception to the rule, once described as "impenetrable", before it came to light in the 1990s that it had been written (along with a number of Mitchell\'s other songs) about a daughter she\'d had to give up for adoption long before she became famous, who she only met for the first time after her daughter had passed her 30th birthday. "Carey" chronicles her self-imposed exile in a remote cave on the Greek island Crete, and the hauntingly spare title track was written for and about Taylor, referencing his own heroin addiction and the horse-addled collapse of the hippie counterculture at the same time. Much speculation went on about what other singer-songwriters were being referenced in her lyrics on the album, with rumors floating around about Jackson Browne and Leonard Cohen along with the confirmed Nash and Taylor, ultimately leading none other than Rolling Stone magazine to idiotically dub her "Old Lady of the Year" and make a satirical diagram of her supposed affairs and conquests, as if she was only important by way of the far more talented men she had been romantically linked to. Doubtlessly, this slut-shaming did much to confirm the waning counterculture\'s hypocrisy and inevitably flawed ideals.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Four decades later, &lt;i&gt;Blue &lt;/i&gt;is not just a classic album but a classic archetype, one that would inform the work of commercially successful female singer-songwriters long after the likes of Mitchell, Taylor and Stephen Stills had vanished from chart prominence, not to mention artists like Elliott Smith, Cat Power, PJ Harvey, Nick Cave and Red House Painters. It\'s the type of album an artist typically makes once and only once, and stands apart from the rest of their work as its own unique entity.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/joni-mitchell-blue-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-xqjl-E86ZJI/VRVqIvG40KI/AAAAAAAAAfs/1Vj7Xsatb-M/s72-c/Bluealbumcover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3192925130890111298</guid><pubdate>Fri, 27 Mar 2015 04:08:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-26T21:08:31.606-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Strokes "Is This It" (2001)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LbWA3N2o4Gc/VRTOzWvqmkI/AAAAAAAAAfY/CavZizHnsbA/s1600/220px-The_Strokes_-_Is_This_It_cover.png" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LbWA3N2o4Gc/VRTOzWvqmkI/AAAAAAAAAfY/CavZizHnsbA/s1600/220px-The_Strokes_-_Is_This_It_cover.png" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Arriving just when critics widely considered guitar bands a thing of the past, The Strokes\' positively infectious &lt;i&gt;Is This It&lt;/i&gt; breathed new life into the sounds of The Stooges, Television and (most prominently) The Velvet Underground, not to mention scores of other artists the young group weren\'t around to see in the 70\'s. One of the things that makes it such an exceptional, astounding record is that they never succumb to any urge to cop directly from any one of their inspirations, even as "New York City Cops", which was pulled from US editions of the album in light of the September 11 terrorist attacks which occurred two weeks before the album\'s American release date, cribs from Iggy Pop\'s "Lust for Life" and Julian Casablancas\' vocals consistently recall those of Lou Reed (as heard on The Velvets\' classic swan song &lt;i&gt;Loaded&lt;/i&gt;). In a time when 50-75 minute CDs had largely become the norm, at least in terms of mainstream pop/rock, The Strokes kept their first full-length (and their second release in total after the &lt;i&gt;Modern Age&lt;/i&gt; EP) down to a slender 36 minutes, with virtually no space between each song.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And each song is fantastic. It starts off with the brief title track, which beautifully spells out the album title\'s brilliant invocation of youthful dissatisfaction. The entire album effortlessly captures that boundless energy and enthusiasm that comes with a lot of classic rock debut albums and usually is never captured in quite the same way on subsequent records; some examples that come most immediately to mind include Supergrass\' &lt;i&gt;I Should Coco&lt;/i&gt;, the original UK version of &lt;i&gt;The Clash&lt;/i&gt;, The Replacements\' &lt;i&gt;Sorry Ma, Forgot to Take Out the Trash&lt;/i&gt; and even The Beatles\' &lt;i&gt;Please Please Me&lt;/i&gt;. There\'s no real studio chicanery to be found here, no frills and absolutely no bullshit, nothing to stand in the way of the pure malevolent joy that resonates through every second of music of the whole thing.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The cocky sneer of Casablancas, coupled with the bright but tough melodies and hooks, conjures up an image of your late teens/early twenties no matter how young or old you actually are, and even if you\'re listening to it for the first time in years, your brain acts like it never stopped hearing the record when the melodies kick in. Songs like "The Modern Age", "Last Nite", "Someday" and "Hard to Explain" are among the greatest rock songs to be recorded since the dawn of the new millenium, and jump out of car radios just like the best of the old AM pop radio hits of the pre-psychedelic 60\'s, although the last of the foursome perfectly incorporates a compressed drum track that sounds like mechanical percussion and (along with "Soma") makes for a sublime post-modern touch to their resolutely traditional, minimalist garage rock.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s not tied to the baggage of all the British music press hype that preceded its release. It\'s not tied to any specific sound of the past, present or future. And even though The Strokes were an unapologetically stylish, well-dressed little group, they were no shallow product of a designer\'s imagination, not with music this great. Of all the albums anyone ever called timeless, &lt;i&gt;Is This It&lt;/i&gt; is one of the truest and most deserving, and it\'s one of very few albums you can say that about to be released in the 2000s.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/the-strokes-is-this-it-2001.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-LbWA3N2o4Gc/VRTOzWvqmkI/AAAAAAAAAfY/CavZizHnsbA/s72-c/220px-The_Strokes_-_Is_This_It_cover.png" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1236580468504926146</guid><pubdate>Sun, 22 Mar 2015 07:36:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-22T00:36:47.326-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Nirvana "MTV Unplugged in New York" (1994)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-4IhbqhllJH4/VQ5jzihEnTI/AAAAAAAAAfA/rcMu-xp2vJY/s1600/Nirvana_mtv_unplugged_in_new_york.png" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-4IhbqhllJH4/VQ5jzihEnTI/AAAAAAAAAfA/rcMu-xp2vJY/s1600/Nirvana_mtv_unplugged_in_new_york.png" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;More often than not, albums culled from MTV\'s on-and-off, long-running Unplugged series serve as little more than throwaway cash-grab items that don\'t stand up apart from the TV special, episodes of which aren\'t always terribly spectacular themselves. Nirvana\'s legendary performance, taped on November 18, 1993 and aired the following month, wasn\'t your average Unplugged set to begin with; much to the costernation of MTV executives, the group played just one bona-fide hit ("Come as You Are") and ran through no less than six rather obscure cover songs from The Vaselines, Leadbelly, David Bowie and the Meat Puppets. Due to Kurt Cobain\'s drug withdrawal and the resultant moody behavior in the days leading up to the taping, which included an instance in which Cobain refused to perform at one point just one day prior to the 18th, the people setting up the engagement basically expected it to be a disaster, if it even ended up happening as scheduled. Of course, the show did go on, and the result was one of the greatest live concerts ever broadcast on television.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;They start off with a breezy run-through of the pre-fame gem "About a Girl", from 1989\'s &lt;i&gt;Bleach&lt;/i&gt;, and a positively haunting "Come as You Are", and then comes a performance unlike any the group had ever given or ever would again in what little time they would exist afterward; their take on The Vaselines\' "Jesus Doesn\'t Want Me for a Sunbeam", with bassist Krist Novoselic switching to an accordion, is one of the most gorgeous and unconventional moments of their career, and in light of Cobain\'s suicide just four months later, its hymnal feel made it even more sadly beautiful. As fantastic as it is, the chilling reading of Bowie\'s "The Man Who Sold the World" one-ups it with Cobain\'s droning, otherworldly guitar solo. Then a still-grumpy Cobain tells the rest of the band he\'s going to do the next one by himself, giving an anguished, extraordinary "Pennyroyal Tea". At its end, the ever-reliable drummer Grohl tells Kurt, "That was great!" only for Kurt to immediately snap back with an emotionless "shut up". Between-song bits like this are just as important to the album as the performances, recreating the tension surrounding its preparation and reflecting back into the music\'s somber intensity, along with the stage\'s prescient decor: with stargazer lillies, black candles and a crystal chandelier, it was nothing short of a funeral for a still-living man who nonetheless felt an internal death that was already apparent to his closest friends in the months before his actual death.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Deep cuts like "Dumb" and "Polly", which were already among the group\'s more subdued numbers in their studio incarnations, are perfectly complemented by the almost unnervingly intimate setting. After two more less-than-remarkable deep cuts ("On a Plain" and "Something in the Way"), Curt and Cris Kirkwood of the Meat Puppets come onstage with Nirvana, already augmented beyond the standard three piece with touring guitarist Pat Smear and cellist Lori Goldston, to play their own songs "Plateau", "Oh, Me" and "Lake of Fire". The three-song detour makes for a relaxed change of pace from the melancholy that hangs over the rest of the concert, but after it ends, the band finish it all off with an even sadder "All Apologies" than the studio recording and the old folk-blues "Where Did You Sleep Last Night?" (otherwise known as "In the Pines"), the latter learned via Kurt\'s beloved Leadbelly.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In the annals of popular music, there are few climaxes to an album or concert performance that have ever been as overwhelmingly powerful as the one to their version of "Where Did You Sleep"; as Kurt\'s vocal has been steadily building in intensity throughout the song, he\'s finally going to "shiver the whole night through", and when he sings "shiver", he howls it with a still-startling ferocity to the point where his voice goes up an octave and cracks like he\'s about to lose it. Remarkably, he doesn\'t, and finishes the song. It\'s no wonder that many people hear his battered, heavy heart giving out right there onstage at this exact moment.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As emotionally draining and difficult as it can be to listen to all the way through, &lt;i&gt;MTV Unplugged in New York&lt;/i&gt; is one of the most unlikely great live rock albums of all time; where other celebrated live rock albums are almost always all about the energy and volume of the music captured on disc, this one stands out for instead being a deceptively pretty, harrowing document of the misery of a deeply troubled and very talented young man who would meet a horribly tragic demise just months after it was recorded. Of all the other troubled rock &amp;amp; rollers of yore who died young, Kurt Cobain might be the only one to have had his spiraling final path into the abyss captured so vividly on film and in audio like this, and it still pops.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/nirvana-mtv-unplugged-in-new-york-1994.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-4IhbqhllJH4/VQ5jzihEnTI/AAAAAAAAAfA/rcMu-xp2vJY/s72-c/Nirvana_mtv_unplugged_in_new_york.png" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-351882387494919832</guid><pubdate>Sun, 22 Mar 2015 00:45:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-21T17:45:41.118-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Elliott Smith "Elliott Smith" (1995)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-B45lY-7yA58/VQ4KH_uyp-I/AAAAAAAAAew/RdIvOWG8NxQ/s1600/220px-Elliott_Smith_(album).jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-B45lY-7yA58/VQ4KH_uyp-I/AAAAAAAAAew/RdIvOWG8NxQ/s1600/220px-Elliott_Smith_(album).jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;On his first solo album, the half-formed, poorly recorded &lt;i&gt;Roman Candle&lt;/i&gt;, Elliott Smith didn\'t quite hit the ground running, but it was good enough to attract attention from Slim Moon\'s Kill Rock Stars label, who signed him promptly and released his eponymous second album in mid-1995. One suspects that he wasn\'t terribly confident in his abilities as a solo performer while still performing in the post-hardcore band Heatmiser, but with the attention garnered from &lt;i&gt;Roman Candle &lt;/i&gt;quickly eclipsing that band, &lt;i&gt;Elliott Smith &lt;/i&gt;shows off a singer and songwriter who\'s forgotten about whatever doubts he may have once had and comes out with a selection of infinitely superior songs to the ones on the debut album.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The opening "Needle in the Hay" would one day become perhaps Smith\'s signature song, a harrowing yet opaque account of debilitating chemical dependency that sets the tone for what can in many ways be considered a concept album about addiction, although Smith always claimed he used drug addiction as a metaphor for other forms of dependency. And indeed, all the songs have enough light and shade to give rise to your own personal interpretation of their lyrics, whatever they seem to mean to you or anyone else in particular. There are still songs like "The White Lady Loves You More" and the magnificent "St. Ides Heaven" which are quite obviously about drug addiction and alcoholism, respectively, and unfortunately Smith\'s own personal problems that would spin out of control towards the end of his life gave the assumption that all the songs were about drugs a heavy likelihood.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Just as important as the great songs is how Smith progressed musically from his first recordings, still playing nearly all the instruments himself, but incorporating ones like harmonica ("Alphabet Town") that he hadn\'t used before and using light trap drums on more of the tracks, not to mention being joined by Heatmiser bandmate Neil Gust playing electric guitar on "Single File" and Spinanes vocalist Rebecca Gates providing gorgeous harmony vocals on "St. Ides Heaven". These additions don\'t take anything away from the still strictly lo-fi presentation and the almost uncomfortable intimacy in his performances, and the approach would only be expanded on even further with the even better 1997 follow-up &lt;i&gt;Either/Or&lt;/i&gt; before Smith left Kill Rock Stars for the major label DreamWorks on the strength of his contributions to the "Good Will Hunting" soundtrack.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/elliott-smith-elliott-smith-1995.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-B45lY-7yA58/VQ4KH_uyp-I/AAAAAAAAAew/RdIvOWG8NxQ/s72-c/220px-Elliott_Smith_(album).jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8076582325361598268</guid><pubdate>Sat, 21 Mar 2015 18:48:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-21T11:48:12.407-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Can "Future Days" (1973)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KeID3BRmD-c/VQ2y6xjuitI/AAAAAAAAAec/qOF0BgB4j2I/s1600/220px-Can_-_Future_Days.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KeID3BRmD-c/VQ2y6xjuitI/AAAAAAAAAec/qOF0BgB4j2I/s1600/220px-Can_-_Future_Days.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Just as they\'d shifted from the Velvet Underground-informed, chugging rock of their earliest days to the uncompromising avant-garde experimentalism of &lt;i&gt;Tago Mago &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Ege Bamyasi&lt;/i&gt;, Can took another leap of faith into a bold new direction with 1973\'s &lt;i&gt;Future Days&lt;/i&gt;. The Karlheinz Stockhausen-influenced electronic elements that had surfaced in its two predecessors came into full shape here, with its downright lush, beautiful title track standing in marked contrast with the previous album\'s frantic opener "Pinch". While the organic jamming and interaction between musicians has gone nowhere, the ever-pulsating rhythms of Jaki Leibezeit are now fully complemented with synthesizers and full-blown soundscapes, a full-bodied, gorgeous sound that was genuinely like nothing else that had come before it and would reverberate through electronic and indie music for decades to come.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Damo Suzuki\'s vocals, which would depart from the group after the album\'s completion when Suzuki married his girlfriend, quit music and became a Jehovah\'s Witness, are softer and sweeter than they had ever been, with none of the caustic, bracing ranting or babbling that occasionally surfaced in previous recordings. With his singing so often buried by the layers of sound, &lt;i&gt;Future Days &lt;/i&gt;would rightly become regarded as a landmark in the evolution of ambient electronica, which Brian Eno would build on over the next several years with renowned records like &lt;i&gt;Another Green World&lt;/i&gt; and &lt;i&gt;Discreet Music &lt;/i&gt;before fully codifying and defining it as a genre with 1978\'s &lt;i&gt;Music for Airports&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;But &lt;i&gt;Future Days &lt;/i&gt;is much too lively and detailed to be mere background music by any stretch of the imagination, especially when the majestic, soothing tapestry of sound is briefly broken by the irresistible three-minute pop single "Moonshake", which would be the group\'s most accessible song until their 1976 fluke British hit "I Want More", which came long after Can\'s most acclaimed and influential works with Suzuki. The album culminates with the complex, sprawling 20-minute "Bel Air", a meticulous compilation of three different performances that were painstakingly edited together in a process that supposedly brought forth inter-member debates about whether or not the final product sounded "too symphonic", i.e. too much like classical-rock dinosaurs that the group detested. Of course, it\'s nothing at all like the idiotic musical masturbation of Emerson, Lake &amp;amp; Palmer, and it makes for the absolute perfect culmination of both the album and Can\'s most artistically successful period.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Can\'s renaissance trilogy came to an end with an album that was just as strong as the first two, which is something even David Bowie couldn\'t manage to do with his own Krautrock-influenced "Berlin Trilogy" at the end of the 70\'s. Indeed, &lt;i&gt;Future Days &lt;/i&gt;might just be the peak of the loose "Krautrock" genre, summing up its post-psychedelic trappings and pointing the way towards the wholly electronic sounds of Kraftwerk. It\'s simply one of the greatest and most influential albums of the 1970\'s, one of those classic records that took decades to reveal its enormous sway over future music and might still be doing so.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/can-future-days-1973.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-KeID3BRmD-c/VQ2y6xjuitI/AAAAAAAAAec/qOF0BgB4j2I/s72-c/220px-Can_-_Future_Days.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3470280632000347306</guid><pubdate>Sat, 21 Mar 2015 17:38:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-21T10:38:38.311-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Can "Ege Bamyasi" (1972)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-cYhkWwT13ak/VQ2ia5pFFgI/AAAAAAAAAeM/DvUwcGIeRak/s1600/220px-Egebamyasialbumcover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em;
