margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-cYhkWwT13ak/VQ2ia5pFFgI/AAAAAAAAAeM/DvUwcGIeRak/s1600/220px-Egebamyasialbumcover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;After the barrier-smashing sonic exploration of 1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Tago Mago&lt;/i&gt;, Can pared that album\'s sound and sprawl way back on 1972\'s &lt;i&gt;Ege Bamyasi&lt;/i&gt;, a much more relaxed, approachable record that\'s much more grounded in organic jazz and funk-based sounds while at the same time incorporating more innovative electronic elements that would come even further to the fore with the pioneering ambient sound of the ensuing &lt;i&gt;Future Days&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The opening nine-minute "Pinch" rides on a much more tense, coiled groove than any of &lt;i&gt;Tago Mago&lt;/i&gt;\'s lumbering stomps, closely recalling the hectic Afrobeat of Fela Kuti with its uptempo pacing and busy percussion. "Sing Swan Song" and "One More Night" are intrinsically left-of-center, but much more down to earth than the previous record\'s cosmic otherworldliness, which permeated even its most approachable moments. Not to say that Can were ever anything less than from another planet than the rest of the music world, but I digress. With "Vitamin C", the group come closer to conventional song structure than they had been since 1969\'s hard-rocking &lt;i&gt;Monster Movie&lt;/i&gt;; Damo Suzuki\'s vocals are incomprehensible as ever in its verses, but the chorus (yes, there actually is one) is an absolute stunner, with Suzuki crying out "Hey you! You\'re losing your vitamin C!"&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;with pure anguish and intensity, all on top of a danceable slice of dark funk that the group would only seldom approach thereafter.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s followed quite ironically by the wildly diffuse ten-and-a-half minute "Soup", the record\'s most overtly avant-garde moment, and then finishes up with two more concise numbers, "I\'m So Green" and "Spoon". The latter had been released as a stand-alone single in Germany months before the album\'s recording got underway, and it managed to make Can moderately famous in their home country when it was used as the theme music for a popular crime drama, and the single even made the German Top 10 chart, which apparently helped Can to afford to use a better recording studio than in previous sessions when they did go in to record the rest of &lt;i&gt;Ege Bamyasi&lt;/i&gt;. A quarter century later, it also gave a heavily acclaimed American indie rock group its name, but that\'s another story.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Because it has an entirely different sound and feel than its predecessor, while managing to retain all their avant-garde experimental leanings, &lt;i&gt;Ege Bamyasi &lt;/i&gt;is easily the equal of its predecessor, and it\'s no wonder that some folks even think that it\'s the superior album.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/can-ege-bamyasi-1972.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-cYhkWwT13ak/VQ2ia5pFFgI/AAAAAAAAAeM/DvUwcGIeRak/s72-c/220px-Egebamyasialbumcover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7073945777492678623</guid><pubdate>Sat, 21 Mar 2015 16:20:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-21T09:20:21.783-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Can "Tago Mago" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-fyc-MpppW4A/VQ2Pv89zJDI/AAAAAAAAAd8/knrfjhOz18E/s1600/220px-Can_-_Tago_Mago.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-fyc-MpppW4A/VQ2Pv89zJDI/AAAAAAAAAd8/knrfjhOz18E/s1600/220px-Can_-_Tago_Mago.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Taking their cue from the hypnotic 20-minute opus "Yoo Doo Right", which closed out their 1969 debut album &lt;i&gt;Monster Movie&lt;/i&gt;, Can moved sonically from a driving, menacing Velvet Underground and garage rock-influenced pulse into uncharted realms of atonal experimentation and monolithic, rambling grooves, a shift helped in no small part by the replacement of American vocalist Malcolm Mooney with the mysterious Japanese drifter Damo Suzuki. Mooney was a pretty good fit for the more raucous rock of their earlier days, but Suzuki, with his chanting words that drifted between the English and Japanese languages and included phrases that he\'d simply come up with himself, was key to the even more radical and innovative music Can would create on 1971\'s &lt;i&gt;Tago Mago&lt;/i&gt;. As solid as its predecessor was, not to mention more accessible, it was with this double-LP that Can largely solidified their legacy as one of the most influential avant-garde rock bands of all time, an album that would resonate with future generations of adventuresome post-punk and indie rock acts and will most definitely continue to do so.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Part of the reason it has such a singular legacy as an album is that it would remain Can\'s most extreme record, a lengthy, challenging work that the group wisely felt the need to pare down for its equally strong, somewhat more approachable single-LP follow-up &lt;i&gt;Ege Bamyasi&lt;/i&gt;. Where previous double-albums up to 1971 had been known for their stylistic diversity (&lt;i&gt;The White Album&lt;/i&gt;) or their epic sprawl (&lt;i&gt;Electric Ladyland&lt;/i&gt;), this one is known for testing listeners\' patience. It opens with the ominous, melancholy "Paperhouse", which starts off as a mournful dirge and turns into a rumbling stomp midway through, and the two following numbers ("Mushroom" and "Oh Yeah") may turn rock music upside down and inside out with bizarre song structures and electronic flourishes of a unique sort that had never before been heard in "rock music", but they\'re relatively concise and recognizable as tunes. The nearly 20-minute "Halleluhwah", the album\'s longest track, starts right off with a monster avant-funk groove and keeps moving all throughout with drummer Jaki Leibezeit\'s legendarily machine-like beatkeeping and Holger Czukay\'s deathless bass line, but the immediately following "Aumgn" runs up to nearly the exact same length and is by contrast a purposefully opaque flow of ambient sound that eventually builds up to a fiery climax.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Beginning the album\'s second half, i.e. the beginning of record two on vinyl,"Aumgn" signals the point where the musicians abandon anything resembling conventional structure, continuing to go thrillingly off the rails with the wild sound collage "Peking O" before returning to the chiming sound of the opening "Paperhouse" with the closing "Bring Me Coffee or Tea". It\'s not only an excellent, uncompromising collection of songs and sounds, but it\'s also a perfectly sequenced record to boot with the way it ebbs and flows. It\'s a cliche to say it, but it really does make for an adventure for first-time listeners, a sense that only increases as you discover things you hadn\'t noticed before with each subsequent listen.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/can-tago-mago-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-fyc-MpppW4A/VQ2Pv89zJDI/AAAAAAAAAd8/knrfjhOz18E/s72-c/220px-Can_-_Tago_Mago.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5123904606794728986</guid><pubdate>Sun, 15 Mar 2015 05:13:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-14T22:13:02.890-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Pretenders "Pretenders II" (1981)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-NqaX8Bksgdo/VQUUdxrbXgI/AAAAAAAAAdo/1bCPXeDDzuo/s1600/Pretenders_II_(The_Pretenders_album_-_cover_art).jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-NqaX8Bksgdo/VQUUdxrbXgI/AAAAAAAAAdo/1bCPXeDDzuo/s1600/Pretenders_II_(The_Pretenders_album_-_cover_art).jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The first Pretenders album ranks as unquestionably one of the finest rock debut albums of all time, an influential and unique record that inevitably put pressure on the group when it came time to record its follow-up, which was put together not long after the success of its predecessor and wound up falling short of expectations set by it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Pretenders II &lt;/i&gt;will always be remembered as a textbook example of a sophomore album that ends up a disappointment in the wake of an audacious debut, to be filed in the same category as everything from Cheap Trick\'s &lt;i&gt;In Color,&amp;nbsp;&lt;/i&gt;to the Dead Boys\' &lt;i&gt;We Have Come for Your Children,&amp;nbsp;&lt;/i&gt;to&amp;nbsp;The Smiths\' &lt;i&gt;Meat is Murder, &lt;/i&gt;and beyond. While the singles and deep cuts alike on the first Pretenders album were equally inspired and thrilling, ranging from the wildly unconventional "Tattooed Love Boys" and the punky "Precious" to the sighing melodies of "Kid", "Brass in Pocket" and "Lovers of Today", their second LP gets bogged down in one too many rather uninspired riffs and some surprisingly weak songwriting from Chrissie Hynde.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Hynde isn\'t as consistent as a singer or writer, and perhaps even more disappointingly, lead guitarist James Honeyman-Scott doesn\'t have as many surprises up his sleeve as he had on earlier tracks. The album does contain a few of the very best Pretenders songs, like the tough "Message of Love" and the shimmering "Talk of the Town", not to mention a top version of The Kinks\' relatively obscure "I Go to Sleep". Unfortunately, the majority of the other cuts are comparatively unmemorable efforts, like the opening "The Adultress" and "Bad Boys Get Spanked", a kinkier but considerably&amp;nbsp;less bracing sequel to "Tattooed Love Boys". By album\'s end, it\'s only gotten progressively less engaging with each of its last few numbers.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For a number of groups that have historically been afflicted with this sophomore album slump, the slump often became the impetus for a knockout punch of a third album, and indeed, the Pretenders&amp;nbsp;would triumph with the heavily autobiographical &lt;i&gt;Learning to Crawl &lt;/i&gt;in 1984, but only after the tragic and sudden deaths of James Honeyman-Scott and bassist Pete Farndon in the intermediary three years. &lt;i&gt;Pretenders II &lt;/i&gt;would consequently turn out to be the original group\'s last gasp, which gives an already disheartening LP a sense of sadness that in its&amp;nbsp;most obvious sense would be chronicled by Hynde in her later songs, particularly on &lt;i&gt;Learning to Crawl, &lt;/i&gt;but also in a sense that has only become more obvious in the decades since, seeing as the group would never again be as exceptional as it had been with Scott and Farndon.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/pretenders-pretenders-ii-1981.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-NqaX8Bksgdo/VQUUdxrbXgI/AAAAAAAAAdo/1bCPXeDDzuo/s72-c/Pretenders_II_(The_Pretenders_album_-_cover_art).jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6438263500987219882</guid><pubdate>Sun, 15 Mar 2015 05:08:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-14T22:08:09.010-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Radiohead "The Bends" (1995)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-iD74-KerYvA/VQUIhMMI5nI/AAAAAAAAAdY/a86JwocrPS0/s1600/220px-Radiohead.bends.albumart.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-iD74-KerYvA/VQUIhMMI5nI/AAAAAAAAAdY/a86JwocrPS0/s1600/220px-Radiohead.bends.albumart.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;When Radiohead first appeared in 1993 with the smash-hit "Creep", which essentially sounded like a meeting between the loud-quiet dynamics of Nirvana and the soaring, angst-ridden anthems of early Pearl Jam, most critics and observers smelled a carpetbagging rat on the same level as Stone Temple Pilots or (although they came a bit later) fellow Britishers Bush, who would surely disappear just as soon as they\'d hit it big and/or the Seattle grunge hype blew away with the next wind. Their uneven debut album &lt;i&gt;Pablo Honey&lt;/i&gt; didn\'t help matters, and so the expectations for their second album were understandably quite low, especially given that by the time they got around to the beginning of their sessions in late \'94 the "grunge boom" was indeed considered close to dead or at least officially irrelevant in the wake of Kurt Cobain\'s suicide and Pearl Jam\'s public retreat from stardom. Nobody had a clue that Radiohead were about to wave a big ol\' middle finger in the faces of all the naysayers and make a remarkable artistic leap forward in the process.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;1995\'s &lt;i&gt;The Bends &lt;/i&gt;is nearly devoid of whatever derivative parts of American alt-rock may have been floating about on its predecessor, although "Just" in particular and even the breakthrough hit ballad "Fake Plastic Trees" have a similar loud-quiet cathartic structure. For one thing, it\'s a much more sonically expansive record than any American "grunge" band would make, displaying the previously unheard influence of Queen\'s theatrical rock and the progressive rock of Pink Floyd. What really set them apart this time out is how well Thom Yorke established his distinctive, strangled falsetto, which he could of course easily morph into a snarl at a moment\'s notice. Like many bands who seem to suddenly find their voice from one album to the next, and given what we know about the band now, it sounds like their real debut album, while its predecessor has been long-since forgotten.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While "High and Dry", coupled with Oasis\' "Wonderwall", would practically make up the sonic blueprint for several regrettable post-Britpop bands that made it big in the early oughts (Coldplay and Travis are the first that come to mind), most of the album finds the group tentatively beginning to explore the themes of consumerism, alienation and isolation that would pervade its critically worshiped follow-up &lt;i&gt;OK Computer&lt;/i&gt;. On its own, however, aside from the part it now seems to play in Radiohead\'s chronological history, what makes &lt;i&gt;The Bends &lt;/i&gt;a classic is how the band breathes new life into the kind of meeting between arena-rock anthems, thoughtful lyricism and post-punk sensibilities that U2 had specialized in during the 1980\'s, and how they seamlessly marry loud rock with their increasingly abstract lyrics in a thrilling way that they would never quite repeat again. In a discography with later albums whose innovation in both sonics and marketing can easily overshadow everything else Radiohead might\'ve done before they were an almost tiresomely lauded institution of endless analytical fodder, &lt;i&gt;The Bends &lt;/i&gt;has held up surprisingly well, and still stands as one of the best and most influential rock albums of the 90\'s.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/radiohead-bends-1995.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-iD74-KerYvA/VQUIhMMI5nI/AAAAAAAAAdY/a86JwocrPS0/s72-c/220px-Radiohead.bends.albumart.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-79437808850504556</guid><pubdate>Sun, 15 Mar 2015 04:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-14T21:15:35.640-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Elliott Smith "Either/Or" (1997)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-v4c3I6nFMrQ/VQT7HTMGkTI/AAAAAAAAAdI/stKLQ4qJ-PA/s1600/MI0000128813.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-v4c3I6nFMrQ/VQT7HTMGkTI/AAAAAAAAAdI/stKLQ4qJ-PA/s1600/MI0000128813.jpg" height="320" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Elliott Smith only wound up completing five solo albums in his venerable yet tragically shortened time as one of the very best and most distinctive singer/songwriters on the planet. As the third one, 1997\'s &lt;i&gt;Either/Or &lt;/i&gt;is a dead-center middle ground with hindsight on its side between the strictly sparse, almost unnervingly intimate solo performances that dominated his first two albums (1994\'s &lt;i&gt;Roman Candle &lt;/i&gt;and \'95\'s self-titled sophomore effort) and the lush pop songcraft of the ensuing &lt;i&gt;XO &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Figure 8&lt;/i&gt;. To take a detour for a minute, there\'s more recently an added level of observation that can be applied to Smith\'s career after &lt;i&gt;Either/Or &lt;/i&gt;and why those latter two records were much more elaborately produced; they would be released by the major DreamWorks Records after Smith became a fluke fringe pop-star thanks to the inclusion of the song "Miss Misery" along with two songs from this album on the soundtrack to the acclaimed film "Good Will Hunting", which starred the recently deceased Robin Williams, who in a stroke of tragic irony seems to have met a demise unfortunately similar to how Elliott Smith most likely (and quite unexpectedly) ended his own life in 2003.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Anyway, it\'s no wonder that many people consider &lt;i&gt;Either/Or &lt;/i&gt;to be Smith\'s best album. It\'s perhaps the most cohesive collection of songs he\'d ever assemble, where he\'s still playing all the instruments himself as in the previous two albums, but he\'s playing more of them, and more often, resulting in the soaring (yet still rather understated) power-pop of "Ballad of Big Nothing" and just the right amount of creamy double-tracking applied to that whispery, thin voice of his. Regardless of the slightly busier arrangements, it\'s still as intimate and introspective as could be, still angst-ridden and given over to accounts of alcoholic shiftlessness ("Between the Bars", "2:45 AM") and self-examination ("Pictures of Me") as always. One other shift that\'s noticeable, though, is that Elliott isn\'t quite as despairing as he had been particularly on the album\'s immediate predecessor, resulting in a somewhat more diverse range of subject matter that made for a smart change of pace, which apparently was a deliberate move on Smith\'s part to avoid being pigeonholed as the perennial depressive loner.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For example, on "Angeles" (one of the selections cherry-picked for the "Good Will Hunting" soundtrack), he\'s tearing apart the cynical celebrity culture of the titular city with all the vitriol of Dylan\'s "Like a Rolling Stone" or "Ballad of a Thin Man", albeit in the same wispy voice that always made for a startling contrast with any number of his other angrier lyrics. While "Pictures of Me" is as much about himself as anyone else, its chorus ("So sick and tired of all these pictures of me") is a direct slam on those who tried (and would continue to try) to assume they knew everything about him. And with the other song used for that soundtrack, the closing "Say Yes", he even has a bona-fide love song, which he himself admitted he normally never did. It\'s probably the most unabashedly bright and optimistic song he would ever write, which in an odd sort of twist when listening to it makes you feel all the more sorry for him that he didn\'t feel or at least seem this happy most of the time in his music. It\'s deservedly one of his best-known songs, and a fine way to cap off one of the finest albums of its time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/elliott-smith-eitheror-1997.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-v4c3I6nFMrQ/VQT7HTMGkTI/AAAAAAAAAdI/stKLQ4qJ-PA/s72-c/MI0000128813.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5282431341195923607</guid><pubdate>Fri, 13 Mar 2015 17:36:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-13T10:36:23.077-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bad Brains "I Against I" (1986)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-HSKri8IeuGI/VQMUUWvenHI/AAAAAAAAAc0/yR7bqR3DDXQ/s1600/220px-BadBrainsIAgainstI.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-HSKri8IeuGI/VQMUUWvenHI/AAAAAAAAAc0/yR7bqR3DDXQ/s1600/220px-BadBrainsIAgainstI.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;After a three-year hiatus, the group that did more to pioneer hyper-fast hardcore punk in America than anyone else returned with a funk-metal masterpiece that comes closer than any of their other albums to equaling their classic 1982 debut album. While that initially cassette-only ROIR full-length was arguably the definitive hardcore album, packing heap-tons of stone-cold classic thrashers, &lt;i&gt;I Against I&lt;/i&gt; would likewise become a landmark touchstone of the sort of funky punk-metal hybrid that would become huge in the alternative rock world of the 1990\'s. Just ask a member of the Red Hot Chili Peppers, Sublime or Jane\'s Addiction.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Not unlike how an already eclectic Clash had blown open the limitations of their sound with &lt;i&gt;London Calling &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Sandanista!&lt;/i&gt;, here the notoriously versatile Bad Brains expand their musical palette further beyond the hardcore and reggae they had already proven themselves adept at with their debut and 1983\'s Ric Ocasek-produced &lt;i&gt;Rock for Light&lt;/i&gt;, with adroit stop-start rhythms and constantly varying tempos. Not only that, but the maniac frontman H.R. is showing all his vocal cards, from the well-established staccato assault of old to a fervent yelp and a plaintive croon that previously appeared only in a few select tracks from their previous two albums and is much more predominant here. Fans of the Brains\' earlier work may be disappointed by the heavy, mid-tempo rock that dominates the album\'s sound, or the way guitarist Dr. Know is showing off the virtuosic chops that he always had to their full extent for the first time, but it\'s an excellent, natural progression that never sounds as hopelessly hackneyed as many of the other seminal hardcore groups who went for a more metal sound in the mid-80\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;None of this would mean much without the stellar songs the band brings forth, which represent the pinnacle of their spiritually-fueled vision. After the bombastic intro piece, the Brains surge into the title track with the speed and ferocity of their early days before suddenly switching to a different tempo and then right back to hyper-speed all over again. In the ensuing "House of Suffering", they\'re at the same speed they\'d left off at, but H.R. is now frequently alternating his smooth croon and his ferocious yowl, a contrast that works tremendously in its favor. One of the record\'s most surprising numbers is the sighing, melodic "Secret 77", arriving in the middle of the album and not sounding quite like anything the band had recorded before, while incredibly somehow still managing to sound thoroughly like &lt;i&gt;them&lt;/i&gt; and no one else. The staggering "Sacred Love" features an excellent vocal from H.R. that was actually recorded over the phone from Lorton Reformatory, where the singer was serving a brief sentence on a pot distribution charge. The resultant deep-sea effect is astonishing and helps make the song one of the album\'s high points.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As perhaps should be expected, Bad Brains never surpassed &lt;i&gt;I Against I&lt;/i&gt;, which garnered a considerable wave of music-press attention that wound up with the group signing a deal with major label subsidy Caroline Records. It didn\'t help their momentum one bit when it ended up taking nearly another three years to deliver the record\'s follow-up, 1989\'s controversial &lt;i&gt;Quickness&lt;/i&gt;, which wound up being (at the time) their last album with H.R. and drummer Earl Hudson. Eschewing all the acrimony that has blighted the group\'s on-off career at various times, &lt;i&gt;I Against I&lt;/i&gt; is simply one of the best rock albums of its time, and rivaled only by the ROIR cassette as the Bad Brains\' finest hour.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/bad-brains-i-against-i-1986.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-HSKri8IeuGI/VQMUUWvenHI/AAAAAAAAAc0/yR7bqR3DDXQ/s72-c/220px-BadBrainsIAgainstI.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4618927851154116604</guid><pubdate>Fri, 13 Mar 2015 04:49:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-12T21:49:32.423-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Neutral Milk Hotel "In the Aeroplane over the Sea" (1998)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-8hBJW_B9kn4/VQJhWfseK4I/AAAAAAAAAcg/Tg7OeJvPDHg/s1600/220px-In_the_aeroplane_over_the_sea_album_cover_copy.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-8hBJW_B9kn4/VQJhWfseK4I/AAAAAAAAAcg/Tg7OeJvPDHg/s1600/220px-In_the_aeroplane_over_the_sea_album_cover_copy.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Jeff Mangum\'s Neutral Milk Hotel came out of the creatively fertile and massively influential Elephant 6 Collective based in Athens, GA, which peaked in the mid-90\'s but saw its creative peak towards the end of the decade with the landmark &lt;i&gt;In the Aeroplane over the Sea&lt;/i&gt;, a cryptic masterwork which made the recording-project-turned-band into top-gear indie darlings and incurred a wave of heavy press that sent a retiring Mangum into seclusion for well over a decade not long after its release in early 1998. In a lot of ways, the album (along with, for example, Wilco\'s &lt;i&gt;Summerteeth&lt;/i&gt;) set the tone for much of the rustic, traditional folk-based American indie rock which became unprecedentedly popular in the 2000s, which in itself was a quagmire that spawned a number of yawn-worthy, affectation-laden groups with metaphorical, highly elliptical lyrics not entirely unlike Mangum\'s but with far less of his eye-popping, Dylanesque flair.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As was and remains a major talking point concerning the record, &lt;i&gt;In the Aeroplane\'s &lt;/i&gt;songs were largely inspired by &lt;i&gt;The Diary of Anne Frank&lt;/i&gt;, a tragic story that tends to lose its weight among young people in America who are still regularly forced to read the book in middle or high school, and thus it confounded many hipster observers in indie rock\'s slowly waning high-irony period when Mangum talked publicly about how much it had affected him emotionally. But aside from explicit references like the distorted stomp of the classic "Holland, 1945", it\'s far from an earnest concept album relating strictly to the life of one particular, famous dead girl; not with the vividly bizarre imagery of "The King of Carrot Flowers", the title track and "Two-Headed Boy". Lines like "And your mom would stick a fork right into daddy\'s shoulder" and "Semen stains the mountaintops" are just as dense and impenetrable as anything Pavement ever wrote, simply delivered vocally with much more sincerity by Mangum than Stephen Malkmus\' quintessential slacker drawl.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Mangum\'s strained, keening wail is indeed a key and integral component of the music, lending an equal parts sorrowful and redemptive air to the highly varied material at hand that ties everything together so well. It also gives his songs an almost gospel-like fervor and intensity that those aforementioned lesser indie-folk combos often tend to miss, no matter how much the words may frequently ramble on. At a time when the mainstream music world was trumping up the largely atrocious sounds of nu-metal and rap-rock and the indie underground was still reeling from the aftershock of the alt-rock "revolution" of the early 90\'s, &lt;i&gt;In the Aeroplane over the Sea&lt;/i&gt; was an unquestionably timeless, enduring creation that stood outside of any period in time it could\'ve been recorded in, not unlike Neutral Milk Hotel\'s Athens forefathers R.E.M. with the similarly mystifying &lt;i&gt;Murmur &lt;/i&gt;fifteen years earlier. Its huge influence on hordes of currently popular artists is testament enough to it being a substantially influential piece of work, but it\'s remarkable to hear that it hasn\'t dated in the slightest and that it still stands up to being as great as the notoriously fickle, hype-begotten indie rock audience proclaimed it to be upon its release.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/neutral-milk-hotel-in-aeroplane-over.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-8hBJW_B9kn4/VQJhWfseK4I/AAAAAAAAAcg/Tg7OeJvPDHg/s72-c/220px-In_the_aeroplane_over_the_sea_album_cover_copy.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8336499368394799063</guid><pubdate>Fri, 13 Mar 2015 03:49:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-12T20:49:49.174-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Throbbing Gristle "20 Jazz Funk Greats" (1979)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-eAEjbjeO9UM/VQJOKbc03KI/AAAAAAAAAcQ/PUyWKQVfm2c/s1600/220px-20_Jazz_Funk_Greats.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-eAEjbjeO9UM/VQJOKbc03KI/AAAAAAAAAcQ/PUyWKQVfm2c/s1600/220px-20_Jazz_Funk_Greats.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With their third album, Throbbing Gristle\'s remarkably uncompromising, frighteningly bleak vision reached its full flower while simultaneously expanding the basic industrial music of their previous recordings. For one thing, the group\'s sick sense of humour is on full display with an LP cover that seems to suggest a very lightweight late-60\'s/early 70\'s English pop combo, that is until you find out that the idyllic seaside locale they\'re posing at is actually the most notorious suicide spot in Britain (Beachy Head, which is also the name of one of the album\'s tracks). Its title, of course, quite amusingly suggests yet another type of music that isn\'t actually featured within the grooves of &lt;i&gt;20 Jazz Funk Greats&lt;/i&gt;, although you can easily observe that distorted, mangled fragments of jazz and funk (not to mention disco) do creep into some of the more rhythmic pieces.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Never mind what isn\'t featured on the album, because what\'s here is some of the most inventive and disturbing music of its time, or any other period in time really. It runs the gamut from the porno-movie, bassy synth blips of the title track, which recall similar sounds that featured in Eno\'s composition "Over Fire Island" on his 1975 &lt;i&gt;Another Green World&lt;/i&gt; LP, to the one and only mildly accessible song that appears here, "Hot On the Heels of Love", which sounds like nothing short of an avant-garde reinterpretation of Donna Summer\'s disco classic "I Feel Love". That highlight of a track, arriving in the middle of the album, is immediately followed by the deeply disturbing "Persuasion", where Genesis P-Orridge dispassionately moans, "Persuasion, you gotta get some/Look at me I touch your breast/Look at me I touch your knees" over a warbly, distorted two-note bassline that repeats throughout and some frightening found-audio clips of what sounds like either a wildly erotic sexual encounter or, much more likely, a violent rape. When it comes to quite deliberately making one\'s music a very hard pill to swallow by even the standards of "indie" or underground music, Throbbing Gristle &lt;i&gt;still&lt;/i&gt; have few peers, well over three decades after this album\'s release.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Aside from its obvious thematic extremes, which took the seediness of The Velvet Underground to even uglier places, much of the sonic space is justly given over to dark ambient instrumentals that still make for an unsettling soundtrack for Halloween or just about any late, dark winter night. Not a moment too soon, the album finishes with the dissonant "Six Six Sixties", which sounds like a precursor to Sonic Youth\'s music and brings an end to one of the uttermost indelible and influential works of the post-punk era, which would indeed remain Throbbing Gristle\'s pinnacle and their best full-length. Not that they\'d have much time to carry on, mind you; they disbanded just two short years after &lt;i&gt;20 Jazz Funk Greats&lt;/i&gt;, with only one more LP up their sleeves that only saw release a full year after the group called it a day and was essentially a one-off piece of so-called "radio art" that had been commissioned by Italian national radio. The best thing you can say about &lt;i&gt;20 Jazz Funk Greats&lt;/i&gt;, to sum it all up, is ultimately that if it had been their one and only recording, their enormous influence and stature would still be secured in underground and electronic music forevermore. Throbbing Gristle were perhaps the greatest pioneers of what is now known as "industrial" experimental music, in the form that we know it today, and once the cat was out of the bag and they\'d horrified an untold swath of listeners with lyrics and sounds that had never been heard in quite this way before, there was simply no need to drag it out into a "career".</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/throbbing-gristle-20-jazz-funk-greats.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-eAEjbjeO9UM/VQJOKbc03KI/AAAAAAAAAcQ/PUyWKQVfm2c/s72-c/220px-20_Jazz_Funk_Greats.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7756135537283990016</guid><pubdate>Thu, 12 Mar 2015 03:41:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-11T20:41:30.334-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Nine Inch Nails "Pretty Hate Machine" (1989)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-5wlT3CIy2-Q/VQD-PLLbLkI/AAAAAAAAAb8/Hl2rb4jsYGw/s1600/220px-Pretty_Hate_Machine_(1989_full_artwork).jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-5wlT3CIy2-Q/VQD-PLLbLkI/AAAAAAAAAb8/Hl2rb4jsYGw/s1600/220px-Pretty_Hate_Machine_(1989_full_artwork).jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Like Nirvana\'s &lt;i&gt;Nevermind &lt;/i&gt;and much of the Red Hot Chili Peppers\' entire early oeuvre, &lt;i&gt;Pretty Hate Machine &lt;/i&gt;was, at the time of its release, a groundbreaking, unique object in music that would nonetheless eventually become a blueprint for scores of at least insufferable, often downright godawful copycats; thus it isn\'t viewed with anywhere near as much wide-eyed reverence as, say, &lt;i&gt;Revolver &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Unknown Pleasures, &lt;/i&gt;records which were certainly very influential but could never truly be imitated anyway and which had a much more subtle influence on later musicians. More than anything else, this is because Nine Inch Nails would never make an album that timeless and/or excellent, let alone this debut outing, but the existence of the likes of Linkin Park and Korn can\'t be something Trent Reznor is entirely comfortable with even now. With elements of New Order, Throbbing Gristle, Ministry and Depeche Mode all streamlined into cathartic, rock-based song structures, Reznor brought industrial music as close as it would ever come to bona-fide mainstream success, and like Nirvana\'s breakthrough that was just around the corner with punk and "grunge"-rock, it would in effect be for better and for worse.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;"Head Like a Hole" merges Reznor\'s angst-ridden (if rather predictable and ham-handed) lyrics decrying "God Money" with some very inventive music, starting off with distant synthetic percussion that strongly recalls the first few seconds of Blondie\'s "Heart of Glass" and throwing in odd sound effects that wouldn\'t have been out of place on something of Cabaret Voltaire\'s, all while never losing the song\'s headbanging, anthemic quality with its surging chorus. It\'s an impressive feat still, some 25 years after the fact, but the real centerpiece of the album is the restrained gloom of "Something I Can Never Have"; here, Reznor\'s melodramatic howl is taken down to a plaintive near-whisper, and its musical fabric encompasses only a haunting piano figure, drums that appear only sporadically and sound like they were recorded from a block away, and faint synth sounds. It\'s a tremendously ominous performance that in its entire six minutes never reaches the full cathartic bloom of the rest of the album\'s material, and it sets a tone for the darker realms NIN would later delve into headfirst.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;There\'s one thing that keeps &lt;i&gt;Pretty Hate Machine &lt;/i&gt;from being as incredible as it could\'ve been, and that would be the hit-or-miss adolescent poetry that makes up Reznor\'s lyrics. His theatrical angst-ridden arena-sized voice doesn\'t help songs that are often blighted by predictable lines that get repeated in one form or another in different songs, case in point being repetitions of themes like "You took my purity", "You took my soul", et cetera. It\'s the reason that, as surprising and inventive as the album\'s music will always be, it\'s a record that one appreciates much more if they\'re 12 or 13 years old and will never get over breaking up with their first "girlfriend" after "going out" for a whole week (!), and who are just making the terrible discovery that the real world isn\'t Ronald McDonaldland or a fairytale. Thus groups like NIN and Marilyn Manson still often serve as a gateway drug for particularly young listeners to the more cynical misanthropy of Big Black or Ministry, or to other forms of "alternative" music in general. This is definitely what really makes &lt;i&gt;Pretty Hate Machine &lt;/i&gt;such a seminal record that means what it does to so many people, and what makes it important, never minding the horrible sounds Reznor\'s innovations inadvertently begat.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/nine-inch-nails-pretty-hate-machine-1989.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-5wlT3CIy2-Q/VQD-PLLbLkI/AAAAAAAAAb8/Hl2rb4jsYGw/s72-c/220px-Pretty_Hate_Machine_(1989_full_artwork).jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6450303793166259287</guid><pubdate>Sat, 07 Mar 2015 17:33:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-03-07T09:33:06.404-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Bratmobile "Pottymouth" (1993)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-j0Who88FiWw/VPsvu6loUYI/AAAAAAAAAbY/gDw-K14CDvw/s1600/MI0000708586.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-j0Who88FiWw/VPsvu6loUYI/AAAAAAAAAbY/gDw-K14CDvw/s1600/MI0000708586.jpg" height="317" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;And so with their first album Bratmobile came out with one of the seminal records of the original, early-90\'s riot grrrl movement, and one of the best. In contrast to their compatriots in Bikini Kill, Bratmobile had a much more playful, childlike attitude and a sound that encompassed amateurish surf rock and lo-fi twee pop, as opposed to BK\'s grungy, fierce punk.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All that aside, make no mistake: this is a punk-rock record through and through, and a thoroughly feminist one at that; Allison Wolfe spits out all manner of scathing, profane and, but of course, bratty putdowns against elitist punk scenesters, misogynist men and just about anyone else that she rightly deems worthy of her wrath (key line: "GET OUTTA MY FUCKIN\' LIFE!"), albeit in a voice that often suggests a younger grrrl than the twentysomething she actually was at the time. Song titles like "Fuck Yr. Fans" and "Stab (Me and Fuck the Wounds)" give a pretty good idea of that before you\'ve even heard the music, and when you\'ve heard it you can\'t help but get an adrenaline rush quite unlike most other bands of the time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although the group\'s primary contribution to pop music history was the empowerment and encouragement they gave young women everywhere who previously would never have thought to make their own music, what really makes &lt;i&gt;Pottymouth &lt;/i&gt;so charming and engaging is how delightfully inept the group were not only by "professional" music standards, but more importantly the standards of the waning hardcore/speed-metal machismo and the metal-influenced, heavy Seattle grunge bands that were exploding commercially at the time of the record\'s release. It\'s their way of telling the underground rock boy\'s club to fuck off and step aside for a minute so they and their fellow grrrls could express themselves in whatever way they felt like, and it still sounds great and continues to inspire female would-be musicians all these years later.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/03/bratmobile-pottymouth-1993.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-j0Who88FiWw/VPsvu6loUYI/AAAAAAAAAbY/gDw-K14CDvw/s72-c/MI0000708586.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-345157005803038640</guid><pubdate>Thu, 26 Feb 2015 21:49:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-02-26T13:49:11.084-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Hole "Pretty on the Inside" (1991)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-iYPkEpqf1ls/VO-NHaOex-I/AAAAAAAAAaw/WTEwmYrp1LI/s1600/220px-Hole_-_Pretty_on_the_Inside.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-iYPkEpqf1ls/VO-NHaOex-I/AAAAAAAAAaw/WTEwmYrp1LI/s1600/220px-Hole_-_Pretty_on_the_Inside.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The incomparable, infamous Courtney Love had spent nearly a decade on the fringes of both the American and British punk/new wave subculture, befriending musicians like Julian Cope and dabbling in acting for the first time with roles in the Alex Cox pictures &lt;i&gt;Sid and Nancy &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Straight to Hell&lt;/i&gt;, and soon became dissatisfied with her life enough to go into seclusion in Alaska while stripping to support herself. At the end of the 80\'s she moved to Los Angeles and formed Hole with the quiet, enigmatic Eric Erlandson, and the group quickly gained a reputation as one of the most dissonant and challenging outfits in the city\'s perpetually evolving rock scene. In 1990 they released their debut single, "Retard Girl", on Sympathy for the Record Industry and then followed it up with "Dicknail" on Sub Pop Records, and both singles generated enough heat that the group were "talked into" a deal with Caroline Records prior to recording their first album with producers Kim Gordon and Don Fleming in early 1991.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As thrilling as the uncompromising, violent &lt;i&gt;Pretty on the Inside &lt;/i&gt;is on a pure gut level, especially songs like the scathing "Teenage Whore" and with its graphic, vividly disturbing lyrical imagery, it shows all too well that their reputation was built as much by Courtney Love\'s "you don\'t love me till you\'ve fucked me", in-your-face persona as their raw, intense sound. In other words, it\'s a very inconsistent, at times even quite incoherent album, much unlike their much more polished, melodic 1994 follow-up &lt;i&gt;Live Through This&lt;/i&gt;. This is perhaps most evident when the rather unfortunately short, thoroughly nasty title track haphazardly morphs into a meandering, sludged-out take on Joni Mitchell\'s "Both Sides Now" (titled "Clouds" here). It\'s an appreciably weird way to end the record, but it doesn\'t succeed as a convincing cover.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Oddly, this doesn\'t seem like much of a downside given that Eric Erlandson has long since confirmed the notion one gets listening to the album that Hole was more interested in making noise than anything else at this stage of their development; prior to the addition of drummer Caroline Rue, who left the group shortly after the album was released, they had played for some time already with no percussion at all, placing them more in line with a no-wave experimental mindset than the "grunge rock" milieu they found themselves inevitably lumped in with upon Courtney\'s marriage to Kurt Cobain of Nirvana, and which they seemed more suited to.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Pretty on the Inside&lt;/i&gt;, much like Nirvana\'s &lt;i&gt;Bleach&lt;/i&gt; as a matter of fact, is far from an auspicious debut album that hits the ground running, but it accomplished the legitimate goal of introducing the world to rock\'s most controversial and obstinate figure this side of Johnny Rotten or Axl Rose, and remains an influential and combative slab of feminist-minded (not quite riot grrrl, despite many claims to the contrary) noise-rock.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/02/hole-pretty-on-inside-1991.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-iYPkEpqf1ls/VO-NHaOex-I/AAAAAAAAAaw/WTEwmYrp1LI/s72-c/220px-Hole_-_Pretty_on_the_Inside.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4167675444824083738</guid><pubdate>Fri, 20 Feb 2015 17:43:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-02-20T09:43:48.036-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Wipers "Is This Real?" (1980)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-f1yEV60_G5w/VOdpkhdU1GI/AAAAAAAAAaQ/iGMx2pSi39I/s1600/Wipers_-_Is_This_Real_cover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-f1yEV60_G5w/VOdpkhdU1GI/AAAAAAAAAaQ/iGMx2pSi39I/s1600/Wipers_-_Is_This_Real_cover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Now revered as one of the key influences on grunge and alternative music that began emanating from the Pacific Northwest in the 1980\'s, Wipers passed almost unnoticed even by punk rock standards on a national level when they released their first album in January 1980. In the same year Californian groups like the Dead Kennedys, X, Black Flag and Circle Jerks made big splashes that almost immediately reverberated across the growing American underground landscape, the Wipers appeared to remain Portland, Oregon\'s best kept secret upon the release of &lt;i&gt;Is This Real?&lt;/i&gt;. The most obvious reason would have to be that the aforementioned groups were from the well-known cultural centers of San Francisco and Los Angeles, cities with larger scenes and a larger audience for their music than the tiny punk community of Portland at the time. Also unlike those other bands, Wipers did not tour nationally at the time, though they had actually played the previous year as part of an infamous show in L.A. that ended in a huge police-instigated riot.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In fact, that last part was part of singer/guitarist Greg Sage\'s original plan to not even play live at all while spitting out 15 records over a ten year period. They reneged on no live performances early on, and gained a reputation for being an intense, formidable live act in the Pacific Northwest. &lt;i&gt;Is This Real? &lt;/i&gt;captures their fury and energy with more purity than any of their subsequent records, with plenty of tracks ("Mystery", "Wait a Minute", "Is This Real?") that could be classified as punk-pop or even new wave and are surprisingly bright compared to the ominous "Alien Boy", "Return of the Rat" and "D-7". It\'s the latter group of songs that would be a seminal blueprint for Seattle "grunge", with their droning, minor-key melodies and Sage\'s distinctive fuzzy guitar work, and which made the band unique in the punk community.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Taken as a whole, &lt;i&gt;Is This Real? &lt;/i&gt;is as exciting and important a debut album as X\'s &lt;i&gt;Los Angeles&lt;/i&gt; or Dead Kennedys\' &lt;i&gt;Fresh Fruit for Rotting Vegetables&lt;/i&gt;, albeit in its own unique way, and yet the group was just getting started; its 1981 follow-up &lt;i&gt;Youth of America&lt;/i&gt; branched out into more experimental song structures and darker subject matter, and garnered Wipers attention outside the Pacific Northwest for the first time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/02/wipers-is-this-real-1980.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-f1yEV60_G5w/VOdpkhdU1GI/AAAAAAAAAaQ/iGMx2pSi39I/s72-c/Wipers_-_Is_This_Real_cover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4524812202975527389</guid><pubdate>Fri, 13 Feb 2015 07:06:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-02-12T23:06:50.032-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Breeders "Pod" (1990)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uYJ0H1yZto4/VN2ZCbRL6lI/AAAAAAAAAaA/W0Qdb4POS2A/s1600/220px-The_Breeders_Pod.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uYJ0H1yZto4/VN2ZCbRL6lI/AAAAAAAAAaA/W0Qdb4POS2A/s1600/220px-The_Breeders_Pod.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Robert Christgau, often dubbed "the dean of American rock critics", was quoted saying of The Breeders that on their 1990 debut they didn\'t sound like a real band. Elsewhere, they were derided as merely "an art project". That\'s a much too glib, narrow interpretation, of course, but it\'s true that the group came into being as merely a side-project,&lt;i&gt; &lt;/i&gt;in this case a collaboration between Pixies bassist Kim Deal and Throwing Muses guitarist Tanya Donelly, devised as a way for them to flex their songwriting muscles more than they were allowed to do in bands under the charismatic leadership of a Black Francis or Kristin Hersh.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Pod&lt;/i&gt;, recorded with Steve Albini, successfully bridges the bare-bones, angular feel of &lt;i&gt;Surfer Rosa &lt;/i&gt;(also produced by Albini) and the ethereal, otherworldly quality of the Muses\' early work, though the singing and songwriting isn\'t as equally divided as you\'d expect; come to think of it, Kim Deal dominates the record lock, stock and barrel. She handles nearly all the album\'s vocals and dominates the songwriting credits, with the exception of course of their imaginative take on The Beatles\' "Happiness is a Warm Gun", and Donelly only assists in writing one song ("Only in 3\'s"). It\'s perhaps only inevitable that it would follow this trajectory, because the group\'s very name had already been used years before as the moniker Kim and her twin sister Kelley (who would later join this new band) went under when performing as a country-folk duo as teenagers.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In any case, while it unfortunately may not be filled to the brim with songs as solid as some of Deal and Donelly\'s rather scarce songwriting contributions to their respective bands up to that time, it\'s an undoubtedly distinctive piece of work that never sounds quite like the Pixies or Throwing Muses while obviously borrowing elements of both. Deal had already established herself in the Pixies as a very different songwriter with a softer, more overtly feminine quality than Black Francis on the classic "Gigantic", but she really comes into her own here with simultaneously sweet, sexy, funny and weird songs like "Doe", "Hellbound", "When I Was a Painter", "Iris" and "Fortunately Gone". It\'s as much of a treat to listen to as it surely was for her to write and sing these songs. The only drawback to the record is that it doesn\'t fully have the consistency or the pulsating energy of &lt;i&gt;Surfer Rosa &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Doolittle&lt;/i&gt;, tending to land in oftentimes hazy, drifting territory that doesn\'t always make the songs as immediately compelling as one might hope. It\'s an extremely minor criticism, indeed, because it clocks in at barely half an hour in length anyway, so nothing drags on past its welcome in the slightest. It may not be as much of a stunner as the debut albums of the Pixies or Throwing Muses, but it\'s a modest little gem that still stands up as one of the best works that either Kim Deal or Tanya Donelly have been involved in since its recording.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/02/the-breeders-pod-1990.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uYJ0H1yZto4/VN2ZCbRL6lI/AAAAAAAAAaA/W0Qdb4POS2A/s72-c/220px-The_Breeders_Pod.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4282780212679870981</guid><pubdate>Fri, 30 Jan 2015 18:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-30T10:15:03.299-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Miles Davis "Birth of the Cool" (1957)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyD2lylWc40/VMvCr_eJSGI/AAAAAAAAAZs/mhKWVZAt6k4/s1600/MI0002421128.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyD2lylWc40/VMvCr_eJSGI/AAAAAAAAAZs/mhKWVZAt6k4/s1600/MI0002421128.jpg" height="312" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Rarely has an album had a title as prophetic and true as Miles Davis\' &lt;i&gt;Birth of the Cool&lt;/i&gt;, the 1957 compilation of sides recorded by a nine-piece ensemble in 1949 and \'50 that were originally released at that time on 78 RPM records and essentially gave birth to West Coast "cool" jazz by bridging the gap between the antic instrumentation of bebop and the lush, full-boded arrangements of big bands. It was the first of Miles\' many incomparable stylistic innovations, and it\'s stood the test of time as an absolute cornerstone of American popular music as a whole. As typical as the playing and the tenor of the music seems today, it was indeed an entirely new sound at the end of the 40\'s, and it had an impact that changed the sound of jazz and jazz-based popular music forevermore.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Old tunes like Johnny Mercer\'s gorgeous "Moon Dreams" and Jimmy Van Heusen\'s "Darn That Dream", the latter of which features vocals from Kenny Hagood and was included in issues of the album only after 1971, are given an impeccably smooth yet technically proficient take that results in a kind of mood music with a feeling, neither sonic wallpaper nor going into the hot, hectic hard bop arrangements that Davis would soon fall in love with as he abandoned the "cool jazz" he had helped create just as soon as he had already done it. As with the best jazz, it may sound inconsequential to what\'s going on outside the grooves at times, but an undercurrent of volatility and soulfulness manages to emerge from the exuberance and passion in the group\'s playing, which is an element that informed everything Miles did thereafter no matter what stylistic avenues he was trafficking in at any time or another.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For an album widely considered to be so monumental, one can\'t help but feel it\'s one of those timeless pieces of art that pretty much speaks for itself. There\'s not a whole lot of adjectives to heap on it, and it\'s a testament to the album\'s effortlessness and lack of pretension. An essential jazz item and a seminal piece of American music.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/miles-davis-birth-of-cool-1957.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-dyD2lylWc40/VMvCr_eJSGI/AAAAAAAAAZs/mhKWVZAt6k4/s72-c/MI0002421128.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3730693186990020101</guid><pubdate>Fri, 30 Jan 2015 17:38:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-30T09:38:30.323-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Who "The Who Sell Out" (1967)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-KrObbA-hFMI/VMuzkVCGEdI/AAAAAAAAAZc/GUnKZ08MB-w/s1600/The_who_sell_out_album_front.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-KrObbA-hFMI/VMuzkVCGEdI/AAAAAAAAAZc/GUnKZ08MB-w/s1600/The_who_sell_out_album_front.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With rock &amp;amp; roll itself continuing to shatter the basic sounds of its first decade of existence with stunning speed, Pete Townshend continued to push The Who from their rebellious mod roots into pop-art and psychedelic territory with &lt;i&gt;The Who Sell Out&lt;/i&gt;, released in late 1967 after they\'d finally begun to make a significant dent in the American mass audience with their legendary appearances at the Monterey Pop Festival and on "The Smothers Brothers Show" (not to mention a notoriously ill-advised tour supporting The Monkees). These ideas had already been previewed with the mini-opera "A Quick One, While He\'s Away" a year earlier, but &lt;i&gt;The Who Sell Out&lt;/i&gt; is a full realization of the concept of a song collage, stitching the album\'s songs together not by a story as was the case with "A Quick One", but by a more intriguing, Warholian conceptual move with a number of amusing faux advertisements which were meant to mimic the presentation of a UK pirate radio station of the era.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Don\'t let all that fool you; the album\'s strength is, as usual, in Townshend\'s excellent, idiosyncratic songs, which include the hit "I Can See for Miles" and the strange character sketch "Mary Anne With the Shaky Hand", and the equally amusing and heartfelt vulnerability of "Tattoo". Unfortunately, the album\'s conceptual unity begins to come undone towards the end,&amp;nbsp; but if the album\'s 1995 CD reissue is any indication with its bonus tracks that include a number of fake jingles that didn\'t make the cut, this may have been (no pun intended) a legal matter more than anything else. It is indeed a matter of public record that a number of real jingle-writers and companies in Britain initiated legal action against The Who, or were at least "offended" by their mockery in the album\'s wake, not unlike the litigation that sampling in hip-hop and electronic music incurred decades later.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Although side one is the stronger half of the album, side two is almost as strong and notably contains the first hints of Townshend\'s interest in spirituality ("I Can\'t Reach You) as well as showcasing a softer side to this group of hard rockers than had ever been made apparent before ("Sunrise"), pointing the way toward the lofty rock opera &lt;i&gt;Tommy&lt;/i&gt; and the grand, introspective stadium rock of &lt;i&gt;Who\'s Next &lt;/i&gt;and &lt;i&gt;Quadrophenia&lt;/i&gt;. To sum up, &lt;i&gt;The Who Sell Out&lt;/i&gt; was the end of The Who\'s first era and the beginning of its second, the latter of which just so happened to bring them far greater commercial success (especially in America) than the first and marked almost an entirely different band from the one behind songs like "I Can\'t Explain" and "My Generation". It\'s one of their best full-lengths, of course, and also a critical turning point from which they never fully turned back.&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/the-who-who-sell-out-1967.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-KrObbA-hFMI/VMuzkVCGEdI/AAAAAAAAAZc/GUnKZ08MB-w/s72-c/The_who_sell_out_album_front.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6328575974164285550</guid><pubdate>Fri, 30 Jan 2015 16:37:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-30T08:37:39.948-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Who "A Quick One (Happy Jack)" (1966)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-mrbkGE8lzss/VMunyGogIoI/AAAAAAAAAZQ/8IcYniUZt9s/s1600/A_quick_one.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-mrbkGE8lzss/VMunyGogIoI/AAAAAAAAAZQ/8IcYniUZt9s/s1600/A_quick_one.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Even as startlingly quick pop and rock music was evolving in the mid-to-late sixties, it must\'ve come as a surprise to Who fans how swiftly they moved from the hard, no-nonsense mod-rock of "My Generation" to the nine-plus-minute song collage "A Quick One, While He\'s Away" in the space of just one year. And that\'s not the only new move on their second album, 1966\'s &lt;i&gt;A Quick One&lt;/i&gt;; guitarist/songwriter Townshend remained the group\'s conceptual leader and focal point, but he encouraged bandmates Keith Moon, Roger Daltrey and John Entwistle to all contribute original material of their own. Daltrey\'s "See My Way" was hardly extraordinary, but in the case of Moon and Entwistle the results were surprising, to say the least.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Moony\'s notoriously zany personality comes to the fore in his two appropriately skewed cuts, the subtly hilarious "I Need You" and the wild, bizarre instrumental "Cobwebs and Strange", where the group members play wind instruments like French horn and tuba. The hilarity of the former song, which sounds like an earnest lament, seems preposterous if you don\'t pay attention to the lyrics or know its original title, "I Need You (Like I Need a Hole in the Head)"; for only god knew what reason Moon thought The Beatles were speaking a secret language behind his back, and gets back at them here by including a cheeky John Lennon imitation during an instrumental break.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Entwistle, traditionally the lowest-profile member of the group despite being well-known even then as possibly rock\'s most dexterous bass player, sings in a distinctly drier and more detached manner in comparison to Townshend\'s vulnerability and Daltrey\'s hard-man bellow, and lends a surprisingly unique bent to his own two numbers: the goofy "Boris the Spider", which became one of his signature songs and features a humorously growling chorus that perhaps had a completely subliminal influence on the vocal stylings of death metal and related subgenres some decades later, and the tough "Whiskey Man".&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Townshend\'s songs, of course, are still the strongest here, with the gorgeous power-pop classic "So Sad About Us" and, in the case of the US release of the album, the demented chaos of "Happy Jack". The album itself was retitled &lt;i&gt;Happy Jack&lt;/i&gt; in America by a record company nervous about the suggestive implications of its true title, but thankfully the original, groundbreaking title track was kept; how could it not be? Stitching together six completely different "movements" as one long piece about infidelity was, let\'s just say not anything you could call a typical rock &amp;amp; roll move even in the experimental 1966-67 era. In an unrelated note, it\'s ironic that swapping out the original\'s cover of Martha &amp;amp; the Vandellas\' classic "Heat Wave" with the more unique, superior original "Happy Jack" essentially repeated what had happened with the album\'s predecessor, where a cover of Bo Diddley\'s "I\'m a Man" was removed due to alleged prudishness on the part of the Americans and replaced with the superior "Instant Party". Odd.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;As a whole, it unfortunately doesn\'t gel into an album quite as cohesive and excellent as its predecessor, but it\'s chock-filled with little bits of unusual details and oddities like that previous album wasn\'t, and "A Quick One, While He\'s Away" set the stage for the group\'s next project, the full-fledged concept album &lt;i&gt;The Who Sell Out&lt;/i&gt;, which stretched out the idea of a song collage over an entire album stitched together by mock radio adverts.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/the-who-quick-one-happy-jack-1966.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-mrbkGE8lzss/VMunyGogIoI/AAAAAAAAAZQ/8IcYniUZt9s/s72-c/A_quick_one.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6555769094013724515</guid><pubdate>Fri, 30 Jan 2015 15:40:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-30T07:41:54.603-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Who "The Who Sings My Generation" (1965)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-RE7gXoTHOi4/VMubvmeXRXI/AAAAAAAAAZA/evojzzpLIow/s1600/220px-The_Who_sings_My_Generation.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-RE7gXoTHOi4/VMubvmeXRXI/AAAAAAAAAZA/evojzzpLIow/s1600/220px-The_Who_sings_My_Generation.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;A flange-addled wave of feedback in the key of F major is the first sound you hear, and as soon as Roger Daltrey bellows "Out in the Street", all hell breaks loose and a kind of rock harder and more chaotic than anything that had come before it (barring the early Kinks singles) begins. The Who\'s amphetamine-fueled combination of autodestructive performance art and fuck-you rock &amp;amp; roll rebellion bursts from the grooves of their 1965 debut LP, as those who never got to see them in their heyday with the incomparable Keith Moon attacking the drum kit, Pete Townshend\'s crashing windmill chords, and the entire group laying waste to their own instruments can just picture the group\'s unprecedented live fury simply by listening to the music. In a year which saw the boundaries of rock and pop music being pushed and prodded by the increasingly abstract songs of Dylan, The Byrds and The Beatles, The Who pushed pure sonic boundaries so hard that they helped lay much of the groundwork for heavy metal, punk rock and the mind-bending experiments of Jimi Hendrix.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The classics, of course, are the title track, which is so ubiquitous 50 years later that it speaks for itself at this point; the effervescent power-pop masterpiece "The Kids Are Alright"; and the lyrically innovative "A Legal Matter", the first song with Townshend taking the lead vocal himself. But the best and/or most famous cuts aren\'t where the album\'s quality begins and ends, as was the case with many of rock\'s earliest LP-length efforts and continues to be the case today with many of the albums in the Billboard 200\'s top ten or twenty. Unlike The Rolling Stones or even The Beatles, what with their early albums being positively chock-filled with cover songs, The Who only have two here, and three on the original UK version (two from James Brown on both, plus Bo Diddley\'s "I\'m a Man" on the original). The reasons for replacing the Diddley take were prudish, but substituting it with the excellent "Circles (Instant Party)" for American audiences was hardly a regrettable decision. As raucous as "Out in the Street" is, the boundaries of pure sonic chaos get pushed even further in the all-instrumental "The Ox", with the busiest drum work captured yet on a rock record at the time and grungy, overdriven guitar. And it\'s one of the band\'s most underrated qualities, but their gleefully idiosyncratic sense of humor surfaces for the first time in "It\'s Not True" ("I haven\'t got eleven kids/I weren\'t born in Baghdad/I\'m not half-Chinese either/And I didn\'t kill my dad").&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Within just a year of its recording the group would move on to more ambitious and offbeat material, as heard on its immediate follow-up &lt;i&gt;A Quick One&lt;/i&gt;, along with singles like "Substitute" and&amp;nbsp; "I\'m a Boy", and of course it would only continue to expand in &lt;i&gt;The Who Sell Out &lt;/i&gt;and the rock opera &lt;i&gt;Tommy&lt;/i&gt;, but &lt;i&gt;My Generation &lt;/i&gt;is such a perfect encapsulation of everything that made the group exciting and innovative when they first blew minds at London clubs like the Marquee that it\'s stood the test of time as one of their best albums, and one of the greatest debut albums of all time.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/the-who-who-sings-my-generation-1965.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-RE7gXoTHOi4/VMubvmeXRXI/AAAAAAAAAZA/evojzzpLIow/s72-c/220px-The_Who_sings_My_Generation.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-1111933526370515277</guid><pubdate>Sat, 17 Jan 2015 06:53:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-16T22:53:36.451-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Led Zeppelin "Houses of the Holy" (1973)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MEnjqLDAxi4/VLoAXYuV_yI/AAAAAAAAAYw/9RHRDUDjuw8/s1600/Led_Zeppelin_-_Houses_of_the_Holy.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MEnjqLDAxi4/VLoAXYuV_yI/AAAAAAAAAYw/9RHRDUDjuw8/s1600/Led_Zeppelin_-_Houses_of_the_Holy.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;After reaching their commercial and artistic peak with their monolithic fourth album, Zeppelin loosened up a bit with its 1973 follow-up &lt;i&gt;Houses of the Holy&lt;/i&gt;, their first album composed of entirely original material, while at the same time continuing to expand their music beyond its initial blues rock and folk roots. Although the album ended up taking almost the entirety of 1972 to record, interrupted as usual by heavy touring along the way, you don\'t hear any trace of exhaustion in the gripping one-note flurry that opens "The Song Remains the Same", just the first of the record\'s multi-layered epics and evidence of Jimmy Page\'s growth as a guitar player, arranger and producer.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It was unusual enough to open the album with a six and a half minute suite, but Page brings the group even closer to progressive rock with the immediately ensuing, nearly eight-minute opus "Rain Song", which was the most overtly orchestral arrangement the group had experimented with up to that point and departed entirely from anything they had previously recorded. Even the ostensibly more traditional folk-metal of "Over the Hills and Far Away" just &lt;i&gt;feels &lt;/i&gt;different from their earlier repertoire, in a superbly subtle way that only Zeppelin could pull off at the time. As much of a departure as it is at its core, much of the album is refreshingly straightforward, with the strutting rock of "Dancing Days" and the rumbling "No Quarter" for good measure.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Houses &lt;/i&gt;also features oddball style exercises like the goofy James Brown homage "The Crunge" and the lolloping reggae of "D\'yer Mak\'er", which make for a bit of much-appreciated humor after the deathly serious mysticism the group had already made a trademark with the massive success of "Stairway to Heaven". It\'s fitting that this notorious band of album-oriented rock purists ended up wanting the latter song to be released as an honest to god single with the former as its B-side, being as they\'re both from the first Zeppelin album to have all its lyrics printed. The album marked a definite turning point for the group, the point where they were now confident to do whatever the hell they wanted to do and enjoy the spoils of being without question the very biggest band in the world during the 70\'s. It\'s the fact that this new, more relaxed feeling would almost incongruously come at the same time that the arrangements became more complex and innovative than ever before that makes &lt;i&gt;Houses of the Holy &lt;/i&gt;one of their best albums.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/led-zeppelin-houses-of-holy-1973.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MEnjqLDAxi4/VLoAXYuV_yI/AAAAAAAAAYw/9RHRDUDjuw8/s72-c/Led_Zeppelin_-_Houses_of_the_Holy.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-2484444578297041920</guid><pubdate>Sat, 17 Jan 2015 06:11:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-16T22:11:03.641-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Led Zeppelin "Led Zeppelin IV" (1971)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uNkUtlEBUfw/VLn1isp8epI/AAAAAAAAAYg/6ulmcdOn1v4/s1600/Led_Zeppelin_-_Led_Zeppelin_IV.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uNkUtlEBUfw/VLn1isp8epI/AAAAAAAAAYg/6ulmcdOn1v4/s1600/Led_Zeppelin_-_Led_Zeppelin_IV.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Having established their base sound with their first two eponymous albums, then greatly expanded upon it with their third, Led Zeppelin (and, arguably, 70\'s hard rock in general) reached its absolute apex with their officially untitled fourth LP, released at the end of 1971 to much anticipation and containing a song ("Stairway to Heaven", of course) which would increasingly bore American FM radio listeners of the group as the 70\'s wore on due to its being massively overplayed. But it was played to death for good reason; "Stairway" is just one of the more remarkable tracks on what stands in hindsight as Zeppelin\'s best album from beginning to end.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Building up from a sombre first half, with its opening chords infamously appropriated from the group Spirit and its obscure 1968 instrumental "Taurus", and Robert Plant cynically decrying a "lady who\'s sure all that glitters is gold", it eventually ramps up into a galloping rocker over halfway into its eight minutes, and the buildup makes the loud finale much more satisfying. Compared to some of the other long-form FM staples of the time, it\'s one of the very few that managed to stay compelling all throughout its lengthy duration, and although it very well might not be their &lt;i&gt;best &lt;/i&gt;song, it\'s not a cause for lament that it\'s still their most famous.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And hey, that\'s only one cut, although many people who helped make the album one of rock\'s all time best sellers no doubt bought it for "Stairway" and "Stairway" alone in the 70\'s.
