The opening "Black Dog" is absolutely one of the group\'s heaviest, raunchiest numbers, an atmosphere that carries into the ensuing self-explanatory anthem "Rock &amp;amp; Roll" and then peters out in the English folk ballad "The Battle of Evermore" (featuring the criminally underrated Fairport Convention vocalist Sandy Denny in a duet with Plant). Finally, it shows off one of the greatest album-closers in rock history with the apocalyptic "When the Levee Breaks", a startling mixture of authentic blues and orchestral grandeur. As with many Zeppelin songs, it\'s a reworking of an ancient blues number, in this case from the husband and wife team of Kansas Joe McCoy and Memphis Minnie, but with the exception of Jake Holmes\' "Dazed and Confused", they\'d never reimagine someone else\'s song so successfully as something so completely separate from its origins. John Bonham\'s absolutely monolithic drums, sampled many times in the world of hip-hop, start it off and are soon accompanied by Plant\'s shrieking harmonica, and the rest speaks for itself. Dripping with all kinds of inventive production and arrangements, it\'s one of the group\'s finest moments all by itself.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Led Zeppelin IV &lt;/i&gt;(as the nominally untitled album is universally referred to) would eventually wind up being the group\'s best-selling album, not to mention the second-best-selling album in US history, and not only is it the group\'s undisputable masterpiece, but it stands as one of the most influential rock albums ever recorded, setting the groundwork for all the hard rock and heavy metal that was to follow in its wake and taking on a life of its own. As pompous as it may sound, it\'s one of the cornerstones of late-20th century popular music.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/led-zeppelin-led-zeppelin-iv-1971.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-uNkUtlEBUfw/VLn1isp8epI/AAAAAAAAAYg/6ulmcdOn1v4/s72-c/Led_Zeppelin_-_Led_Zeppelin_IV.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3805887306990797524</guid><pubdate>Sat, 17 Jan 2015 05:28:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-16T21:28:49.763-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Alexander "Skip" Spence "Oar" (1969)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ku1XVCVnQrg/VLnfCxdc2TI/AAAAAAAAAYQ/L5qLfYxsqtg/s1600/MI0001746001.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ku1XVCVnQrg/VLnfCxdc2TI/AAAAAAAAAYQ/L5qLfYxsqtg/s1600/MI0001746001.jpg" height="320" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The Moby Grape generated such a fierce momentum when they emerged in 1967 that perhaps it was sadly inevitable that they would go from being one of the most touted bands to come out of the late 60\'s San Francisco rock scene to being a mess of drugs, mental illness and managerial problems within the span of just over a year. Group leader Alexander "Skip" Spence suffered a full-on, acid-exacerbated mental breakdown, which culminated in an incident where he tried to chop down the door of bandmate Don Stevenson\'s hotel room with an axe. After a stint in a psychiatric hospital, he supposedly drove out to Nashville by motorbike immediately upon exiting the facility and got to work on recording his first (and only) solo album, 1969\'s &lt;i&gt;Oar&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;That last story has since been proven to be little more than a bit of folklore, but the album itself is still a mystifying, bizarre listen, rooted in grim, fatalistic folk and blues and (but of course) tinged with lysergic consciousness. It\'s filled with unusual sound effects, collapsing song structures, and vocals that range from a gravelly Fred Neil homage ("Cripple Creek", "Weighted Down", "Diana") to a high wisp ("Margaret/Tiger Rug", "War in Peace", "All Come to Meet Her"), and everything in-between. As outre as rock was fast becoming by the arse-end of the 60\'s, one would be hard-pressed to find many other albums of the period that sound quite like this. The nearly ten-minute closer, the frightening "Grey/Afro", was undoubtedly the album\'s most outwardly radical piece, with its funky, meandering bass, phased-out drums and incomprehensible vocals.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Quite unsurprisingly, &lt;i&gt;Oar &lt;/i&gt;didn\'t come anywhere close to commercial acceptance, and at the time of its release was actually the lowest selling item in Columbia Records\' entire catalog, but its influence carried through the next couple of decades into the hungover, despairing indie folk of Mark "Sparklehorse" Linkous and Elliott Smith, and would eventually be honored with a song-for-song tribute album in 1999. It\'s a challenging piece of work, and fans of Moby Grape\'s wiry garage rock and country rock harmonies will be disappointed to find that &lt;i&gt;Oar &lt;/i&gt;is an entirely different beast from anything the Grape ever did, but it\'s a veritable cult classic nonetheless.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/alexander-skip-spence-oar-1969.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ku1XVCVnQrg/VLnfCxdc2TI/AAAAAAAAAYQ/L5qLfYxsqtg/s72-c/MI0001746001.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6311899643111049952</guid><pubdate>Mon, 12 Jan 2015 04:44:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-11T20:57:50.236-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Neil Young "After the Gold Rush" (1970)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-PNq10XcMuzY/VLNKi4ZGOCI/AAAAAAAAAYA/632aRodIeYE/s1600/220px-After_the_Gold_Rush.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-PNq10XcMuzY/VLNKi4ZGOCI/AAAAAAAAAYA/632aRodIeYE/s1600/220px-After_the_Gold_Rush.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Coming off of his appearance with Crosby, Stills &amp;amp; Nash at the Woodstock Festival and the excellent &lt;i&gt;Everybody Knows This is Nowhere&lt;/i&gt;, at the beginning of the 70\'s Neil Young had most certainly "arrived" in both a commercial sense and that his idiosyncratic songwriting and performance abilities were now common knowledge among the rock &amp;amp; roll audience, as opposed to when he was in the Buffalo Springfield and often took a backseat to Stephen Stills when it came to singing and songwriting.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;In a chameleon-like fashion that would only become more striking as his ongoing, extraordinary career progressed, he turned away from the largely hard-rock sound of his breakthrough second solo album and expanded his musical parameters for the even more successful &lt;i&gt;After the Gold Rush&lt;/i&gt;, which also followed Young\'s first album in tandem with CSN (&lt;i&gt;Deja Vu&lt;/i&gt;). After starting the album off with the gleaming "Tell Me Why", perhaps Young\'s highest falsetto vocal on record is accompanied only by an 18 year old Nils Lofgren on piano and later by a mournful horn section in the painfully beautiful title track, which, true to its title, details the immediate aftermath of the tumultuous 60\'s using apocalyptic, surreal imagery. It\'s one of those stone-cold classic songs that\'s now become a popular music standard, much like Simon &amp;amp; Garfunkel\'s contemporary "Bridge Over Troubled Water", and like that other 1970 smash-hit it\'s one of those breathtaking songs that simply speaks for itself in a way. No other song, or album for that matter, seemed to speak so concisely to the mood of the moment in 1970, and millions of listeners shared the feeling.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;True to form, he also has markedly different numbers like the stinging protest of "Southern Man" (a kissing cousin to the then-recent CSNY single "Ohio") and the &lt;i&gt;Everybody Knows&lt;/i&gt;-esque "When You Dance I Can Really Love" up his sleeve, setting in motion his well-known refusal to stick to one style of music for very long, even across the course of just one 35-minute long-player. Other highlights in particular include the sweet country-folk ballad "Only Love Can Break Your Heart" (the album\'s other huge hit) and the heady "Don\'t Let It Bring You Down", in amongst what amounts to an entirely solid and thoroughly excellent album, as well as one of the key period piece works of its time of release.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/neil-young-after-gold-rush-1970.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-PNq10XcMuzY/VLNKi4ZGOCI/AAAAAAAAAYA/632aRodIeYE/s72-c/220px-After_the_Gold_Rush.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6255137297297677265</guid><pubdate>Mon, 12 Jan 2015 04:15:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-11T20:15:00.165-08:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Neil Young "Everybody Knows This is Nowhere" (1969)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-OyQ7ClI4uZ8/VLNCZn5s5NI/AAAAAAAAAXw/xxg28PDrikc/s1600/220px-EverybodyKnowsThisIsNowhere.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-OyQ7ClI4uZ8/VLNCZn5s5NI/AAAAAAAAAXw/xxg28PDrikc/s1600/220px-EverybodyKnowsThisIsNowhere.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Neil\'s eponymous debut album, released shortly after his exit from the short-lived but influential Buffalo Springfield at the beginning of 1969, was hardly a "much-anticipated" release when it hit the racks. Rather than it having been an inferior album, this was probably because the Springfield to begin with had never been the most commercially successful of the numerous rock bands to emerge from Los Angeles\' thriving middle-60\'s club scene, and furthermore their one ubiquitous hit ("For What It\'s Worth") was both written and sung by Stephen Stills. It would take the much stronger, more consistent &lt;i&gt;Everybody Knows This is Nowhere&lt;/i&gt;, released just four months later, to bring Young\'s immense talents to wider public notice.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This time out, he was working with the hard-driving Californian garage-rockers Crazy Horse, who were sloppier and more endearingly ragged than either Buffalo Springfield or the about-to-be-formed Crosby, Stills, Nash &amp;amp; Young. They give a grungy menace to the incendiary album-opening "Cinnamon Girl", now a much-beloved standard in Young\'s astonishingly dense repertoire; lyrically, it\'s a bit inscrutable and doesn\'t make that much literal sense, but this quality only lends an invaluable mystique to one of Young\'s hardest, crunchiest rock songs. As if "Cinnamon Girl" wasn\'t enough, Crazy Horse only continues to perfectly complement Young\'s piercing electric guitar on the lengthy, hypnotic "Down By the River" and "Cowgirl in the Sand", which clock in at over nine and ten minutes apiece and glide along on grimy, repetitive chord changes and elemental rhythms. Not discounting the country-rock title track, the gorgeous "Round &amp;amp; Round (It Won\'t Be Long)" or the mournful "Running Dry (Requiem for the Rockets)", it\'s these three key cuts (all written while Young was suffering from a high fever) that transcended most of his previous songwriting efforts and performances on record, and made a generation of rock listeners coming of age take notice.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Everybody Knows&lt;/i&gt;, as it turned out, would be just the beginning of a solid decade of compelling, often startlingly excellent music, and it still stands up as not only one of Young\'s best outings, but indeed as one of the very best rock albums of its time or any other.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/neil-young-everybody-knows-this-is.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-OyQ7ClI4uZ8/VLNCZn5s5NI/AAAAAAAAAXw/xxg28PDrikc/s72-c/220px-EverybodyKnowsThisIsNowhere.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3213369513713290650</guid><pubdate>Fri, 09 Jan 2015 01:32:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-08T18:23:45.014-08:00</atom:updated><title>Neil Young "On the Beach" (1974)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OjOTp99rHOI/VK8k-Kij5tI/AAAAAAAAAXg/nsTuZbegJq4/s1600/MI0002178817.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OjOTp99rHOI/VK8k-Kij5tI/AAAAAAAAAXg/nsTuZbegJq4/s1600/MI0002178817.jpg" height="316" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;With the release of 1970\'s &lt;i&gt;After the Gold Rush &lt;/i&gt;and 1972\'s &lt;i&gt;Harvest&lt;/i&gt;, coupled with the immense popularity of his work with Crosby, Stills &amp;amp; Nash, Neil Young had become a bona-fide superstar and a well-regarded member of the early 70\'s singer-songwriter pantomime. As soon as he reached his commercial apotheosis, with &lt;i&gt;Harvest&lt;/i&gt;, he pulled an unexpected about-face with the rambling, universally panned film/soundtrack &lt;i&gt;Journey Through the Past&lt;/i&gt; and the gloomy live album &lt;i&gt;Time Fades Away&lt;/i&gt;, neither of which have ever seen official release in any digital format at the time of this writing. Towards the end of 1973, he recorded the bulk of what would later be released as the acclaimed &lt;i&gt;Tonight\'s the Night&lt;/i&gt;, but at this time Reprise Records rejected the disarmingly dark, off-the-cuff material that Young brought forth. He stowed it away, went back into the studio, and recorded the savage, desolate masterwork &lt;i&gt;On the Beach&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Where &lt;i&gt;Tonight\'s the Night &lt;/i&gt;had been a drunken mourning for a few of Young\'s departed friends, and for the optimism of the 60\'s counterculture, &lt;i&gt;On the Beach&lt;/i&gt; saw him inching his way out of his despair without completely leaving it behind, kicking off with the upbeat "Walk On" (a weary, shrugging middle-finger to Lynyrd Skynyrd\'s "Sweet Home Alabama") and then immediately following up with the apocalyptic, &lt;i&gt;Gold Rush&lt;/i&gt;-like "See the Sky About to Rain", and the frightening "Revolution Blues". The album also sees him skewering the big, bloated business the music industry was fast becoming by 1974 ("For the Turnstiles") and serenading his actress girlfriend Carrie Snodgress ("Motion Pictures").&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The almost reluctant positivity comes to a head with the closing nine-minute opus "Ambulance Blues", a poetic refutation of conceited critics and lying politicians (among many other things), by which time the remaining darkness has already come to its conclusion with the almost-as-lengthy, dread-ridden title track. It\'s a perfect sequencing that\'s telling in light of the direction Young\'s career would take after the eventual release, a whole year after &lt;i&gt;On the Beach&lt;/i&gt;, of &lt;i&gt;Tonight\'s the Night &lt;/i&gt;in June 1975. The flipped release dates weren\'t common knowledge among rock critics of the time, so inevitably &lt;i&gt;On the Beach &lt;/i&gt;was seen as an ominous precursor to the all-encroaching despair of &lt;i&gt;Tonight\'s the Night&lt;/i&gt;, and it can honestly be seen that way if one so chooses to do so, which only helps make it one of those remarkable, dense records that reveals new things with each play. It\'s that uncertain, mysterious quality, taken along with the astonishing material at hand, that makes it one of Young\'s greatest albums, and one of the great rock albums of all time.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/neil-young-on-beach-1974_8.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OjOTp99rHOI/VK8k-Kij5tI/AAAAAAAAAXg/nsTuZbegJq4/s72-c/MI0002178817.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3132532240869619737</guid><pubdate>Mon, 05 Jan 2015 06:16:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-04T22:16:55.553-08:00</atom:updated><title>Doctors of Madness "Late Night Movies, All Night Brainstorms" (1976)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i0gOqnxEI_s/VKoZv8X1XnI/AAAAAAAAAXI/HrMmDUWzzFI/s1600/MI0002931831.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i0gOqnxEI_s/VKoZv8X1XnI/AAAAAAAAAXI/HrMmDUWzzFI/s1600/MI0002931831.jpg" height="320" width="312" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The Doctors of Madness hit their stride at the exact borderline period straddling the death of glam and prog-rock and the birth of punk rock in England, simultaneously a clear product of the recently departed era and an oft-overlooked influence on some of the first UK punk groups with the same token. On their finest hour, the sweeping &lt;i&gt;Late Night Movies, All Night Brainstorms&lt;/i&gt;, leader Richard "Kid" Strange details a bleak post-apocalyptic landscape that can\'t help but remind listeners of David Bowie\'s &lt;i&gt;Diamond Dogs &lt;/i&gt;or Lou Reed\'s &lt;i&gt;Berlin&lt;/i&gt;, albeit managing to incorporate both the sci-fi future-shock of the former and the gloomy real-life drama of the latter in an invigorating, idiosyncratic and exhilarating way.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The loose concept album is fleshed out with gorgeous, full-bodied melodies and dripping with grim imagery of death, decay, disease, neuroses and all-around dysfunction, ranging from the contemplative "I Think We\'re Alone Now" (certainly not to be confused with a certain late 80\'s pop hit) to the surging pre-punk rock of "Waiting", culminating eventually in the majestic 15-plus-minute closer "Mainlines", all augmented along the way by Celtic fiddles, shivering violins, soft acoustic guitars and painfully intense semi-orchestral arrangements. The music veers from a sensitive calm to a frenzied chaos, especially in the shifting dynamics of its two longest tracks, "Noises of the Evening" and "Mainlines", while many of Strange\'s decidedly cinematic lyrics pop out at you with either the gravity of a message from hell ("The doctors know best") or an urgent call to arms ("Tapping my feet on main street/Counting the stars in the sky/Stretching my arms and inspecting my palms/And I\'m waiting").&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It all makes for an engaging, thought-provoking LP that stands as perhaps the final statement of worth from the realm of early 70\'s glam and progressive rock, and undoubtedly one of rock\'s great forgotten albums.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/doctors-of-madness-late-night-movies.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-i0gOqnxEI_s/VKoZv8X1XnI/AAAAAAAAAXI/HrMmDUWzzFI/s72-c/MI0002931831.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-4303196958628292481</guid><pubdate>Mon, 05 Jan 2015 04:54:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2015-01-04T20:54:39.960-08:00</atom:updated><title>Tav Falco\'s Panther Burns "Behind the Magnolia Curtain" (1981))</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-B9lXwuBUoo8/VKoOm0oSVPI/AAAAAAAAAW4/UesXBdkx8II/s1600/MI0003329610.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-B9lXwuBUoo8/VKoOm0oSVPI/AAAAAAAAAW4/UesXBdkx8II/s1600/MI0003329610.jpg" height="284" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Recorded in about six hours at Memphis\' legendary Ardent Studios, the Panther Burns\' 1981 debut album is early rock &amp;amp; roll played in as shambolic, wrecked-out and amateurish a fashion as it gets. The group, led by the charismatic Tav Falco and featuring cult legend Alex Chilton in its ranks, demolishes 14 forgotten rockabilly, blues and tango obscurities as a form of post-punk, avant garde conceptual art, undercut with fractured off-kilter rhythms that often make it sound as if the drummer had never played a kit prior to being called in for this session.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;This irreverent yet faithful take on traditional American music found Falco and the Panther Burns lumped in with the "psychobilly" of The Cramps, and deservedly so, but the Panther Burns took a slightly more subtle approach to their deconstruction of early-to-mid 20th century American pop culture and music. Falco was enamored with the jagged prose of William S. Burroughs and the "Theatre of Cruelty" exponent Antonin Artaud, not B-grade horror flicks or pinup girls. With a more cerebral feel than their psychobilly peers, the rudimentary musicianship and muddy recording make for a bit of a destabilizing experience, especially when hearing the caveman smash and bash rhythms that push along the group\'s rockin\' version of the normally breezy samba standard "Aquarela do Brasil". &lt;i&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Distinctive, influential and a bit overlooked more often than not, &lt;i&gt;Behind the Magnolia Curtain &lt;/i&gt;isn\'t a sole product of either run-of-the-mill rockabilly/50\'s revivalism or fully fledged avant garde art, finding an equal parts surreal, volatile, and chaotic space of its own.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2015/01/tav-falcos-panther-burns-behind.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-B9lXwuBUoo8/VKoOm0oSVPI/AAAAAAAAAW4/UesXBdkx8II/s72-c/MI0003329610.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-5028961022066516415</guid><pubdate>Fri, 20 Sep 2013 07:16:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-09-20T00:16:40.246-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Clash "Give \'Em Enough Rope" (1978)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-I0KggyUIMnw/Ujka36iyHGI/AAAAAAAAAUk/e9pCXyRnMG4/s1600/MI0002398217.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-I0KggyUIMnw/Ujka36iyHGI/AAAAAAAAAUk/e9pCXyRnMG4/s320/MI0002398217.jpg" width="319" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;The sophomore album jinx is no bit of superstition or mystery. Any band, even one as amazing and transcendent as The Clash, is going to have a hard time following up a first LP that is not only musically excellent, but also undoubtedly a defining document of its cultural moment. The Clash\'s self titled debut album, released in April 1977 at the height of the first wave of British punk, is still rightly revered as being one of rock\'s greatest first outings, and considered by some to be the album that captured the British punk scene better than any other. As a result, when the group entered the studio a year later to record their second album, they were already sat firmly between a rock and a hard place; the likelihood of them making an album just as great or better than its predecessor was slim, simply because albums as consistent and solid as the first Clash album quite simply do not come along every time any given group makes a record. It didn\'t help matters that Blue Oyster Cult producer Sandy Pearlman sat behind the boards this time out, making for a decidedly controversial partnership that added to a critical disdain that already been festering in both the UK music press and underground fanzines to a certain extent ever since The Clash signed their contract with CBS Records in early \'77.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;Give \'Em Enough Rope &lt;/i&gt;boasts a much fuller and cleaner sound than its tinny, rough-hewn predecessor, but songs like the surging opener "Safe European Home" or "Tommy Gun" actually benefit from Pearlman\'s polish. Yes, for better or worse the album sounds totally different from their first and it most certainly is not equal that album quality-wise, but it also contains a few signs of The Clash\'s imminent musical maturity, which of course would find full flower on their follow-up tour de force &lt;i&gt;London Calling&lt;/i&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The swinging R&amp;amp;B groove of "Julie\'s Been Working for the Drug Squad", featuring piano from the recently deceased Blue Oyster Cult keyboardist Allen Lanier, recalls nothing so much as early 70\'s pub rock in the manner of Brinsley Schwarz\' "Surrender to the Rhythm", or for that matter Joe Strummer\'s own previous group The 101\'ers. It\'s hardly an unsung gem of the Clash catalog, but it\'s a welcome step in a direction apart from the thrash anthems the group had made their chief sonic trademark, and it points ahead to the diversity they would become equally renowned for in the latter half of their career. Mick Jones\' "Stay Free" recalls his heroes Mott the Hoople in terms of its sentimentality, but instead of rendering it a mournful dirge in the fashion of "The Ballad of Mott the Hoople" or "I Wish I Was Your Mother", it grafts a sweeter pop/rock sensibility onto the established sound of the group, resulting in something that\'s neither quite a ballad or a rocker but something unique, especially for The Clash.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Unfortunately, songs like these are the exception here, not the rule. While an incendiary update of the riff to The Who\'s "I Can\'t Explain" fueled the excellent non-album single "Clash City Rockers" earlier in 1978, the very same riff provides the basis for this album\'s woefully undistinguished "Guns on the Roof", one of several songs in a certain vein that fall flat here along with "Drug-Stabbing Time" and the rather conceited closer "All the Young Punks". The latter rings especially hollow, a cross subject matter-wise between that of the aforementioned "Clash City Rockers", the previous album\'s own closer "Garageland" and "White Man in Hammersmith Palais" (another excellent \'78 single), albeit possessing almost none of the bite of any of those earlier songs. Wisely, the group would largely abandon (with the exception of 1981\'s "This is Radio Clash") the hit-or-miss trend of Clash songs about The Clash for much of the rest of their career in favor of more worked-over statements about themselves and the world around them.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Ultimately, &lt;i&gt;Give \'Em Enough Rope &lt;/i&gt;is just another example of the sophomore album slump, as well as a related trend in which groups make a great first album, follow it with an inferior second one and then record a great third album. It\'s not entirely great, it\'s certainly not terrible, and it\'s worth hearing just to track the group\'s progress from the youthful fury of their first album to the dizzying multicultural stew of &lt;i&gt;London Calling&lt;/i&gt;, not to mention to hear a few great tracks, even if there aren\'t enough of those.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/09/the-clash-give-em-enough-rope-1978.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-I0KggyUIMnw/Ujka36iyHGI/AAAAAAAAAUk/e9pCXyRnMG4/s72-c/MI0002398217.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3586518514628755267</guid><pubdate>Tue, 10 Sep 2013 05:56:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-09-10T00:11:01.624-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Interviews</category><title>The World\'s First Internet Baby Interviews former DEAD BOYS bassist JEFF MAGNUM!</title><description>&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;TO BE FEATURED IN ROCK &amp;amp; ROLL FASCISTS ISSUE #2!&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-pJvphGwNHG8/Ui6gpPraqiI/AAAAAAAAASU/lfyiffJ6G4U/s1600/magnum77.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="400" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-pJvphGwNHG8/Ui6gpPraqiI/AAAAAAAAASU/lfyiffJ6G4U/s400/magnum77.jpg" width="336" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;Jeff onstage, late 1970\'s&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;div style="text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;b&gt;JEFF MAGNUM &lt;/b&gt;was one of the legendary Dead Boys&amp;nbsp; in the late 70\'s, although he wasn\'t featured on their first and best album, &lt;i&gt;Young Loud &amp;amp; Snotty&lt;/i&gt;, and now has one of his Guild Jet Star II bass guitars on display at the Rock &amp;amp; Roll Hall of Fame in Cleveland, OH. It\'s very appropriate, given that he first joined the former members of the short-lived Rocket from the Tombs in Cleveland to become a group called Frankenstein in 1975. One year later, they moved to New York, changed their name to the Dead Boys and became part of the burgeoning punk rock fray at the celebrated CBGB\'s, which of course is currently being memorialized in a film of the same name that Jeff doesn\'t care for at all. Featured below is an interview conducted via email, in which he reveals how music first moved him and how he learned about Bators\' tragic 1990 death. It should be as much a pleasure for you to read as it was for me. Enjoy.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;What is your earliest musical memory, and when did you start playing  yourself?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt; &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;In our home we had a big Telefunken High  Fidelity record player unit in the living room and my parents (&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;m&lt;/span&gt;om mostly)  bought record albums and played \'em alot!&amp;nbsp;  I was just a little kid sitting on the floor with my toys listening to  all this music, it was fun!&amp;nbsp; I  didn\'t know what a Frank Sinatra was, but it sounded OK to me.&amp;nbsp; Mom liked Erroll Garner, Sinatra, Glenn  Miller, Artie Shaw, and lotsa movie soundtrack LP\'s like "My Fair Lady" and "The  Ten Commandments"(?).&amp;nbsp; She hadda  fave LP by Joe "Fingers" Carr called "Rough-House Piano" and the song "Boogie  Woogie Rag" just drove me nuts and I\'d ask her over and over "PLAY THE FAST ONE,  MOM, PLAY THE FAST ONE!!!", she would and I just sat there diggin\' this  tune!&amp;nbsp; It\'s my fondest early musical  memory!&amp;nbsp; Apart from that, I started  teaching myself the electric bass at age 13.&amp;nbsp; I never had a lesson and figured it all  out by myself, it took forever but I think I did OK in the  end.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Were you in any other bands prior to joining the Dead Boys in 1977?&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Just your basic &lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;j&lt;/span&gt;unior &lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;h&lt;/span&gt;igh/&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;h&lt;/span&gt;igh &lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;s&lt;/span&gt;chool  cover song rock bands with friends that wanted to play. I learned at an early  age about playing in bars, getting crap thrown at you, and not getting paid at  the end of the night, but I was happy just to be playing out and still learning  the bass.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-9U7LLP3nfes/Ui6oEYGbX5I/AAAAAAAAAS0/eCgyB38YYx8/s1600/jackcasady.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-9U7LLP3nfes/Ui6oEYGbX5I/AAAAAAAAAS0/eCgyB38YYx8/s320/jackcasady.jpg" width="213" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;One of Jeff\'s inspirations: Jack Casady&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&amp;nbsp;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;What would you consider your biggest musical  influence/influences?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Once I could actually play bass, I  started listening to only the bass on rock records I liked.&amp;nbsp; John Entwistle, Bill Wyman, Jack Bruce,  all those guys.&amp;nbsp; I couldn\'t play  that stuff but I listened and learned about playing melodic bass parts, and the  bassist I learned most from was Jack Casady from Jefferson Airplane.&amp;nbsp; Alot of those songs on the early  Airplane LP\'s left me cold, but his driving, melodic bass parts made the songs  interesting to me, plus he was playing chords, octaves, using feedback,  distortion, all this stuff "traditional" &amp;nbsp;bass players were not supposed to do and  he had a unique, killer bass tone!&amp;nbsp;  I learned a ton from those early Airplane LP\'s from his playing and  applied his "driving bass" style with the Dead Boys.&amp;nbsp; To their credit, they never  complained.&amp;nbsp; And, like everybody  else, I loved The Ventures, Stooges, MC5, Alex Harvey Band, and early Alice  Cooper albums!&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div style="text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-pCsBf0YsngM/Ui6pwFOFK9I/AAAAAAAAATM/ixB4CxZBb8s/s1600/stivstage2.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-pCsBf0YsngM/Ui6pwFOFK9I/AAAAAAAAATM/ixB4CxZBb8s/s320/stivstage2.jpg" width="257" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;"A fun guy": Stiv Bators&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;How would you describe Stiv Bators as you knew him  personally?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Fun!&amp;nbsp; He was a fun guy who liked to laugh and,  onstage, he would do whatever came into his head.&amp;nbsp; He was something  else...&amp;nbsp;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-ShKSqh7LNRk/Ui6pLSm7pKI/AAAAAAAAATE/QRj7jhgEgUs/s1600/stivstage.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: right; float: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-ShKSqh7LNRk/Ui6pLSm7pKI/AAAAAAAAATE/QRj7jhgEgUs/s1600/stivstage.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;In your own experience, how would you describe the CBGB\'s scene and/or the punk  rock movement in general in the late 1970\'s?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; text-align: right;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-RCKXMb92WOA/Ui6qlUjhnCI/AAAAAAAAATQ/EQKF2vRxlrA/s1600/cbgb.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="206" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-RCKXMb92WOA/Ui6qlUjhnCI/AAAAAAAAATQ/EQKF2vRxlrA/s320/cbgb.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;CBGB\'s in the late 1970\'s&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;This has become a "hot topic" these days  what with that forthcoming idiotic CBGB movie in October. Alot of those bands,  at the time, I couldn\'t stand because they were terrible or boring or both, but  the really good NYC bands made up for it, lemme tell ya! Dead Boys played CB\'s in 1977-78 probably  more than anybody because our "Manager" owned the joint!&amp;nbsp; We hung out in that bar everynight for a  year and it was a blast!&amp;nbsp; Lotsa  friends, lotsa yuks, lotsa beer!&amp;nbsp; On  the countless Thursdays thru Sundays (2 sets a night), we would plow thru our  songs and make the quality noise, Bators would do his thing, and everyone had a  great time!&amp;nbsp;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-0A32YtxRJ3c/Ui6rtFPH4dI/AAAAAAAAATc/PuJoadfswso/s1600/MI0000810201.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="288" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-0A32YtxRJ3c/Ui6rtFPH4dI/AAAAAAAAATc/PuJoadfswso/s320/MI0000810201.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;"We Have Come for Your Children", 1978 LP&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;You didn\'t actually play on the classic "Young, Loud and Snotty", having not yet  joined the group from what I understand at the time of its recording, but what  are your memories of recording the infamous "We Have Come for Your Children"  with a fatally out-of-touch Felix Pappalardi?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;"Young Loud And Snotty" was recorded as  a demo (never meant for release) before I rejoined the band in New York, and  really great songs were wasted on that ten cent, bullshit, awful  production.&amp;nbsp; Recording the second  album was unpleasant at times because I was there to record an album, not to  take drugs and act like a bogus Keith Richards.&amp;nbsp; At one point, I was the only one in the  studio, the rest of the band gone on a drug search.&amp;nbsp; I sat there playing my bass, trying to  come up with cool parts for some of these songs and I guess Felix musta felt  sorry for me because he came into the studio, grabbed Cheetah\'s guitar and  recording engineer Steve Klein got on the drums and the 3 of us played a buncha  blues songs until they returned with their drugs and we could continue  recording.&amp;nbsp; Apart from that, when  the record was finished, Felix Pappalardi told me I should be proud of my bass  playing on the LP.&amp;nbsp; I still  am.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-I6mqD7xgqkM/Ui6sxZhctqI/AAAAAAAAATo/k5UmOzXhuT0/s1600/Night+Of+The+Living+Dead+Boys+Folder.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: right; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://4.bp.blogspot.com/-I6mqD7xgqkM/Ui6sxZhctqI/AAAAAAAAATo/k5UmOzXhuT0/s320/Night+Of+The+Living+Dead+Boys+Folder.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;Nearing the end..."Night of the Living Dead Boys"&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;What is your recollection of the 1979 demise of the Dead Boys, and/or the events  leading up to it?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;The thrill was gone.&amp;nbsp; No record label, no manager,&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt; &lt;/span&gt;and drugs  had a major hold on some of the band.&amp;nbsp;  It was not good.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; text-align: right;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-3wDUpfSf_us/Ui6tqK3AAJI/AAAAAAAAAT0/BZXDx0zmbY4/s1600/stiv+grave.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="240" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-3wDUpfSf_us/Ui6tqK3AAJI/AAAAAAAAAT0/BZXDx0zmbY4/s320/stiv+grave.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&amp;nbsp;Here lies Stiv Bators...October 22, 1949-June 4, 1990&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;If you don\'t mind discussing it, how do you&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt; &lt;/span&gt;remember first learning about Stiv\'s  unfortunately premature passing, and what was running through your mind when you  got the news?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Jimmy Zero called me the morning of his  death and told me the news.&amp;nbsp; I  laughed and told him he was full of shit and that it couldn\'t be t&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;rue&lt;/span&gt;. I was wrong.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: left; margin-right: 1em; text-align: left;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-DFTYsM6CqOk/Ui6wP_5km4I/AAAAAAAAAUM/o6BgzHAr9nA/s1600/Jeff%252BMangum%252Bjeff.png" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-DFTYsM6CqOk/Ui6wP_5km4I/AAAAAAAAAUM/o6BgzHAr9nA/s320/Jeff%252BMangum%252Bjeff.png" width="250" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;Jeff Mangum of Neutral Milk Hotel&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;D&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;o you ever get&amp;nbsp; &lt;/span&gt;confused with Jeff Mangum, leader of the recently-reunited  indie-rock darlings Neutral Milk Hotel?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;No comment.&amp;nbsp;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Do you still keep in touch with your surviving former  bandmates?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;Sadly, no.&amp;nbsp; We last played together in 2005 and I  haven\'t had any meaningful conversations with them since.&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;Do &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;you keep an eye at all on the current punk rock scene?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;People are always telling me I gotta  hear this band, or this new CD, and if I do, I usually hate it.&amp;nbsp; I am not the target audience for the  current punk rock scene. &lt;table cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="tr-caption-container" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; text-align: right;"&gt;&lt;tbody&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-1-xS4BNwdVc/Ui6yQpqHN2I/AAAAAAAAAUU/oc4ae-wgIho/s1600/magnumtoday.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-left: auto; margin-right: auto;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="320" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-1-xS4BNwdVc/Ui6yQpqHN2I/AAAAAAAAAUU/oc4ae-wgIho/s320/magnumtoday.jpg" width="213" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;tr&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;i&gt;Jeff today, at the Rock &amp;amp; &lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;R&lt;/span&gt;oll Hall of &lt;span style="font-family: Arial;"&gt;Fame in Cleveland&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;td class="tr-caption" style="text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/td&gt;&lt;/tr&gt;&lt;/tbody&gt;&lt;/table&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;What are you up to right now, musically or otherwise, and what do you see  yourself doing in the near future?&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;b&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/b&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;I was giving bass lessons for a bit, and  I actually enjoyed that.&amp;nbsp; I played  bass on three tracks on The Cyclones brilliant 2007 CD "We\'re Livin\' Like  Weasels" (&lt;a href="http://www.thecyclones.net/" target="_blank"&gt;www.TheCYCLONES.net&lt;/a&gt;).&amp;nbsp;  Life is good, I can\'t complain, and I\'m all ready for the next box of  exploding rattlesnakes!!!&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And let those exploding rattlesnakes be joyous, Mr. Magnum. I would like to thank Jeff for taking the time out to answer these twelve questions. I was surprised at a few of his answers, and all of them are very entertaining. I hope you all enjoyed reading this as much as I did making it. The second issue of the new Rock &amp;amp; Roll Fascists is soon to be released, and it will indeed feature this fantastic interview as a featured piece.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;span style="color: black; font-family: Arial; font-size: x-small;"&gt;&lt;span style="font-family: \'Arial\',\'sans-serif\'; font-size: 10pt;"&gt; &lt;/span&gt;&lt;/span&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;&lt;i&gt;&lt;/i&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/09/the-worlds-first-internet-baby.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-pJvphGwNHG8/Ui6gpPraqiI/AAAAAAAAASU/lfyiffJ6G4U/s72-c/magnum77.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>1</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-7231799329123815459</guid><pubdate>Sun, 08 Sep 2013 02:39:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-09-07T19:39:28.904-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Descendents "Milo Goes to College" (1982)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-r-pqYuY4Org/UivZk3YzFVI/AAAAAAAAASA/R4WwdTD2VVE/s1600/MI0001841105.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-r-pqYuY4Org/UivZk3YzFVI/AAAAAAAAASA/R4WwdTD2VVE/s1600/MI0001841105.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;When the Descendents first emerged as a trio of teenagers from Manhattan Beach, sporting a surf-meets-new wave sound as heard on the 1980 "Ride the Wild" single, no one could have predicted that they would wind up being one of the most influential and beloved punk rock bands of all time. When 18-year-old Milo Aukerman joined the group as lead vocalist for 1981\'s &lt;i&gt;Fat &lt;/i&gt;EP, it made all the difference in the world; while the two tracks on their debut single were fine songs in their own way, it was Aukerman\'s pencil-neck-geek persona combined with his gruff holler that truly made the Descendents the great band they were in their heyday.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It\'s a great irony that the band was to have its finest moment on record&amp;nbsp; right at the same moment that they were about to go on hiatus, due to Aukerman\'s leaving to attend college at UC San Diego. Perhaps this inspired the group to make their first (and last, until 1985) long-player a doozy, and they succeeded on that front with flying colors; 1982\'s &lt;i&gt;Milo Goes to College &lt;/i&gt;is a stone-cold classic of both hardcore and punk-pop, not to mention of punk rock and/or rock &amp;amp; roll in general.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It opens excellently with the sing-along "Myage" (Shit, don\'t need no more") and continues within just a few minutes through other great songs like the brief, oompah-stomping "I Wanna Be a Bear", the defiant "I\'m Not a Loser", the positively enraged "Parents" and the very funny "Tonyage". Later on, the college radio hit "Suburban Home" and the all-too-brief "Kabuki Girl" have you happily nursing the exit wounds they leave behind, and the final four songs ("Marriage", "Hope", "Bikeage" and "Jean is Dead") wind up being the most melodic, vulnerable tracks here, the final one being a real-life account of the suicide of one of Aukerman\'s friends ("Now you\'re gone and I\'m alone").&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The sharp contrast between the teenage snot of "I Wanna Be a Bear" and the genuinely pained "Jean is Dead" is a huge part of what makes this album such an enduring cult classic, and the record needs both of these very different kinds of songs to really work. The infectious and accurately portrayed adolescence that comes through in the likes of "Suburban Home" or "Parents" continues to inspire high school garage bands the world over, while the romantic despair of songs like "Hope" or "Bikeage" would end up being a major influence on confessional emo/post-hardcore.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;All in all, &lt;i&gt;Milo Goes to College &lt;/i&gt;is still one of the most influential punk records ever made, as well as one of the best, at least in terms of American punk and hardcore. Although there are a few lyrical droppings of the ball, most notably with the idiotic "homo"-baiting that mars the end of the otherwise magnificent "I\'m Not a Loser", they also proved themselves to be highly adept songwriters for being as young as they were at the time, and there\'s not a single clunker present. It\'s the nuclear-grade strength of these songs that holds true to this day, and will always make this an album that so many listeners will have a hard time taking off their stereo for years to come.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/09/descendents-milo-goes-to-college-1982.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-r-pqYuY4Org/UivZk3YzFVI/AAAAAAAAASA/R4WwdTD2VVE/s72-c/MI0001841105.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-6142710682075763121</guid><pubdate>Fri, 06 Sep 2013 02:38:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-09-05T19:38:21.627-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Green Day "Dookie" (1994)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MuT6qLA-IdU/UikxoKuE2SI/AAAAAAAAARw/YHgO70MeDt8/s1600/MI0003136443.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="318" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MuT6qLA-IdU/UikxoKuE2SI/AAAAAAAAARw/YHgO70MeDt8/s320/MI0003136443.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;When the first generation of enraged malcontents emerged from decaying inner cities across the globe in the mid-to-late 1970\'s, not one of those young people could in their wildest imaginations have predicted the eventuality that the raw, angry music they were playing would one day not only be accepted by an entertainment industry that initially vilified everything associated with it, but marketed by it to no end to succeeding generations of young people. The world has decided to unanimously give credit to Nirvana for breaking down the barriers between punk rock and commercial success, and it is indeed an accurate claim, but the band that really led the charge for pure punk music (or at least fully punk-derived music, as opposed to punk-influenced grunge or alternative rock) being co-opted by radio and MTV was Green Day, whose 1994 smash-hit &lt;i&gt;Dookie &lt;/i&gt;perfectly captured the mood of its pop-cultural moment. On one hand, this co-optation seemed to finally validate the merits of punk rock in the eyes of a deservedly larger audience than the one that had already known it for years; on the other hand, of course, it undoubtedly robbed the music of a great deal of its original excitement, drastically reducing its capability to shock middle America and lessening its inherent danger.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Regardless of the effects this commercial acceptance would ultimately have on punk music and culture, and the fact that the trend would eventually result in the world having to endure the dreadful likes of Good Charlotte and Sum 41, Green Day just so happened to be a stellar punk-pop outfit already, and the album that made them stars just so happens to remain one of the best and most influential albums of its time. The clean, crisp, big and bright sound of infectious singles like "Basket Case" was just what the doctor ordered to counteract the cloud of sorrow hanging over the rock world and (arguably) America in general in the wake of Kurt Cobain\'s suicide, and although songs like "When I Come Around" boasted plenty of romantic frustration and bitterness, Billie Joe Armstrong certainly didn\'t sound like any of it was going to make him want to shoot up or slit his wrists when he sang about it.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Green Day and their bouncy teen angst were, indeed, very much a welcome antithesis to the dourness of the grunge bands that had dominated the charts for two years at the point that &lt;i&gt;Dookie &lt;/i&gt;was released, which probably had a lot to do with why the album became so surprisingly successful. It also, of course, had much to do with the simple fact that nearly every song on the record is a winner, big hits and deep cuts alike, and it\'s the sheer strength of these melodic, tuneful thrashers that continues to endear the band and the album to new generations of fans to this day. It of course could never be equal to the likes of &lt;i&gt;Rocket to Russia &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Never Mind the Bollocks&lt;/i&gt;, in the grand scheme of things, but Green Day would never make an album quite this solid again, even if they\'d come very close with the considerably darker and more menacing &lt;i&gt;Insomniac &lt;/i&gt;one year later, and with that same token none of the other pop-punk groups that would follow the boys from Berkeley into the pop charts managed to come close to touching it.&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/09/green-day-dookie-1994.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-MuT6qLA-IdU/UikxoKuE2SI/AAAAAAAAARw/YHgO70MeDt8/s72-c/MI0003136443.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-8301248632388806888</guid><pubdate>Fri, 30 Aug 2013 02:10:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-08-29T19:14:05.375-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Iggy and the Stooges "Raw Power" (1973)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ul08ETZyDPY/Uh_rMxdrqFI/AAAAAAAAARA/o1sIOqvx5Mk/s1600/220px-StoogesRawPower.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ul08ETZyDPY/Uh_rMxdrqFI/AAAAAAAAARA/o1sIOqvx5Mk/s1600/220px-StoogesRawPower.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;In the music industry, as in just about any other art form or any other facet of life in general, popularity and commercial success often do not coincide with wide-ranging influence or quality. The Stooges are one of music\'s most famous (or infamous) examples, putting out two influential and now-revered albums with their original lineup in 1969-1970 only to implode due to a deadly combination of serious drug abuse and dismal record sales. It seemed as though they were doomed to be just another forgotten rock &amp;amp; roll outfit, at a time when Iggy Pop\'s outrageous onstage antics seemed absolutely impossible to successfully translate to a record-buying public that was mostly either baffled or frightened by the band\'s brutal noise.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Across the Atlantic ocean, however, they had one particularly noteworthy fan by the name of David Bowie, who claimed Iggy as an influence on his "Ziggy Stardust" persona. His star just so happened to be rising higher and higher just as Iggy\'s couldn\'t seem to get any lower into the heroin-addled abyss he and the rest of the group were mired in. Bowie and Iggy first crossed paths in 1971 in New York, at Max\'s Kansas City, and within a year their fast friendship coupled with Bowie\'s newfound superstar status led to The Stooges\' reforming with a new lineup that saw the powerfully primitive guitarist Ron Asheton replaced with the far more technically accomplished James Williamson, while Asheton reluctantly did the same for the long-gone original bassist Dave Alexander. Bowie was also to produce the band\'s new album, to be titled &lt;i&gt;Raw Power &lt;/i&gt;and released in 1973 to the same dismal sales that had plagued their earlier work.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;With the change in lineup came a marked change in sound from the primal, bluesy garage rock from hell that had made their eponymous debut album and the chaotic &lt;i&gt;Fun House&lt;/i&gt; so visceral and powerful. Along with this, Iggy had come under the influence of the androgynous glam rock fashion that Bowie had popularized, rubbing glitter all over his face, decked out in silver lame pants and leopard-skin prints. While these changes made for a slightly different band than the one that had made their first two albums, they weren\'t unwelcome ones; Williamson\'s chaotic leads on the immortal album-opening anthem "Search and Destroy" may take a great deal more experience for a learning guitarist to pull off than the sublimely simple "I Wanna Be Your Dog" or "No Fun", but along with Iggy\'s urgent vocal and Scott Asheton\'s pummeling drums, they make the song sound like it\'s the last the band will ever play. When they talk about Iggy and the Stooges (as they were now being billed on &lt;i&gt;Raw Power&lt;/i&gt;) being a punk rock band before punk existed, it\'s songs like this that they\'re really talking about going by sonic influence alone, and without a doubt it boasts some of the best-ever rock &amp;amp; roll lyrics.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And it only gets better from there. Following the take-no-prisoners onslaught of "Search and Destroy" is the equally sinister and seductive "Gimme Danger", a twisted love song where Iggy croons to his lover that he wants to feel her disease, and he knows that she\'ll cause some kind of trouble for him at some point down the road, but it turns him on. It sounds like nothing else here or in the rest of The Stooges\' catalog, and on a personal note it was the song that originally got this writer into the band a few years back. Lesser-known items "Your Pretty Face is Going to Hell" and "Penetration" may not be as well-known by non-fans as "Search and Destroy", but they seethe with manic malevolence like nothing that had come before them, and the former song has one of the best song titles in rock history.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Unlike their previous two albums, there\'s no Bo Diddley-derived ode to the then-current year here, but if "1969" and "1970" have a descendent on &lt;i&gt;Raw Power&lt;/i&gt; it\'s the raucous title track, which would be covered 20 years later by Guns \'n\' Roses for their &lt;i&gt;Spaghetti Incident? &lt;/i&gt;album and succinctly captures the chaos of the album as a whole in four minutes\' time. As their previous one had collapsed in the unstructured noise of "L.A. Blues", Iggy and the Stooges\' rock &amp;amp; roll party culminates this time with the prophetic "Death Trip", in which it sounds with 40 years\' hindsight like Iggy\'s singing "you\'re gonna save me" not to a woman, but to the countless bands yet to be formed who would immortalize his band\'s music as the immensely influential noise that it is in the years after the song\'s recording.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;It didn\'t seem that way when &lt;i&gt;Raw Power &lt;/i&gt;was released in February 1973, charting at a lamentable #182 on the Billboard 200; owing to even worse drug abuse than before as well as increasingly violent, erratic performances, within one year the band would once again be no more, their notoriously chaotic final show being captured as part of what became the posthumous and poorly-recorded &lt;i&gt;Metallic K.O.&lt;/i&gt; album. But within just half a decade, the band and their final album in particular had attracted a rabid, undeniable cult audience; it would inspire punk and punk-inspired bands in the immediately ensuing decade ranging from the Sex Pistols to The Go-Go\'s, whose Belinda Carlisle says her life was changed just by seeing &lt;i&gt;Raw Power&lt;/i&gt;\'s iconic cover shot of Iggy onstage staring balefully at the audience of a legendary London gig. After the punk era, its influence never wavered, consistently listed as a favorite album by Nirvana\'s Kurt Cobain in his journals and inspiring Henry Rollins to have its title tattooed on his shoulders. It\'s no secret that it\'s one of the most influential rock albums of all time, as well as one of the greatest and most enthralling, and although it once seemed like it was destined for obscurity, more of the world discovers those facts each and every day.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The &lt;i&gt;Raw Power &lt;/i&gt;lineup, with Mike Watt standing in for the late Ron Asheton, is now reformed and released &lt;i&gt;Ready to Die&lt;/i&gt; this past April. There isn\'t a doubt in the world that although the new album is by all accounts anything but equal to their 1973 masterwork, the guys wholeheartedly deserve the attention it\'s gotten, not to mention the adoring, much larger crowds they play for these days and their ever-growing recognition as one of rock &amp;amp; roll\'s most important bands. Enough people already recognized that in 1977, but the fact that so many more do now than did forty years ago is undeniably a well-overdue blessing.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/08/iggy-and-stooges-raw-power-1973.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-ul08ETZyDPY/Uh_rMxdrqFI/AAAAAAAAARA/o1sIOqvx5Mk/s72-c/220px-StoogesRawPower.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-929884550044403256</guid><pubdate>Sat, 17 Aug 2013 07:04:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-08-17T00:08:58.533-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Tom Petty &amp; the Heartbreakers "Tom Petty &amp; the Heartbreakers" (1976)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-83hKJDuM9nU/Ug8Qcnf_N4I/AAAAAAAAAQc/hdwdttKii1M/s1600/MI0001689737.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" height="314" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-83hKJDuM9nU/Ug8Qcnf_N4I/AAAAAAAAAQc/hdwdttKii1M/s320/MI0001689737.jpg" width="320" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;While some artists are lucky enough to score their biggest success, artistic and/or commercial, with their very first album, many more\'s humble beginnings belie the excellence they achieve later on down the line.
