In the case of Tom Petty, not only was his first LP outing not quite up to the level of quality later heard on &lt;i&gt;Damn the Torpedoes &lt;/i&gt;or &lt;i&gt;Hard Promises&lt;/i&gt;, but it also came at a time when his music was rather misunderstood by the world at large.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Because of the superstar status they would achieve in the 1980\'s, it can be difficult to comprehend today that when Tom Petty &amp;amp; the Heartbreakers first emerged with their 1976 debut album, the lion\'s share of their early press labeled them a punk/new wave act in light of their obvious lack of affinity for the progressive rock and heavy metal that largely ruled mid-70\'s FM radio, as well as perhaps the fact that the group happened to share a name with the concurrently active Johnny Thunders band responsible for such grimy punk classics as "Born to Lose" and "Chinese Rocks". In fact, a 1977 New York Times article may or may not have coined the term "pop-punk" when referring to Petty and his so-called "pop punk rock". It was at least partly because of the hasty labeling that The Heartbreakers could hardly get arrested on big-time airwaves until some time after their debut LP hit the shops.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;While they shared with the new wavers (and certain punks) a love of basic 60\'s guitar pop and certainly valued sturdy, concise songwriting over bombast and/or complexity, there was nothing truly "punk" or "new wave" about Petty; he was a rock &amp;amp; roller, pure and simple, an effortlessly cool personality whose cocky confidence came through in songs like "Rockin\' Around (With You)" and the laid-back early minor hit "Breakdown". This isn\'t to say that the rebelliousness implicit in that self-confidence was totally unrelated to the bitterly sarcastic wit of new wave\'s "angry young men" (i.e. Elvis Costello, Graham Parker and Joe Jackson), or that The Heartbreakers\' vibrant rock &amp;amp; roll was entirely removed from the supercharged punk rock of the Ramones. It\'s to say that Petty was an old-school rocker, pure and simple, and to call him anything else would just be silly. This is anything but a bad thing; it\'s the lack of subcultural or trendy baggage that has always made his music warm and approachable, which of course is in turn what makes it very consistently solid, albeit not always great.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;And indeed, much of the material on The Heartbreakers\' first album was certainly not great. While it\'s teeming with the traits and tropes that remain familiar Petty hallmarks to this day, they don\'t appear to be fully fleshed-out in most of these songs, good as all of them are and stone-cold great as two of them are. There isn\'t any definite reason for this; it\'s simply a perfect example of a debut where a band is showing its promise all over the place, but hasn\'t yet perfected its sound.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Hasn\'t perfected it, that is, until the final track. Arriving at the end of a decidedly pedestrian second side, "American Girl" was not only The Heartbreakers\' first major hit, but it also represents the moment where the band truly became more than just another bar band. A Byrds homage so obvious that Roger McGuinn himself recorded his own version for a solo LP shortly after its release as a single, the inclusion of "American Girl" is pretty much enough on its own to make owning the whole album worth it. It\'s too bad that the rest of the material isn\'t as excellent as this one track, but the whole thing is definitely good, and everybody\'s got to start somewhere, even if that somewhere isn\'t exactly great.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/08/tom-petty-heartbreakers-tom-petty.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-83hKJDuM9nU/Ug8Qcnf_N4I/AAAAAAAAAQc/hdwdttKii1M/s72-c/MI0001689737.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-3846583787656459822</guid><pubdate>Fri, 26 Jul 2013 08:56:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-07-26T01:56:36.143-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>Wipers "Over the Edge" (1983)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OMLz9ToctIU/UfI5tgYBKII/AAAAAAAAAP8/6jBL2AJSAJs/s1600/Wipers_Over_the_edge_cover.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OMLz9ToctIU/UfI5tgYBKII/AAAAAAAAAP8/6jBL2AJSAJs/s1600/Wipers_Over_the_edge_cover.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"It\'s not the truth I see/It\'s just a mockery..."&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Those  words, the first from Greg Sage\'s mouth on the Wipers\' third album,  could very well serve as an introduction one of these days to a book on  the American 80\'s underground as a whole. On the title track to &lt;em&gt;Over the Edge&lt;/em&gt;,  Wipers mastermind Sage howls desperately from that much-discussed void  of low self-esteem, alienation, disconnection, rage and despair that a  legion of hardcore punk bands were traversing by 1983, but none with  nearly as much passion as Sage mustered here.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;The endless  post-grunge namechecking by the likes of J Mascis and Kurt Cobain over  the last 25 or so years has failed to erode the distant mystique and  ominous tension at the heart of the first three Wipers LP\'s. The title  track of \'81\'s &lt;em&gt;Youth of America&lt;/em&gt;, &lt;em&gt;Edge&lt;/em&gt;\'s equally  excellent predecessor, clocked in at 10:30, then not only very rare, but  nothing short un-fucking-believable coming from an American punk band  of the era. And there was nothing to suggest this was the work of  anybody else other than a band as unique as the Wipers; it chugged along  like Krautrock at high-energy, top speed, not once descending into  self-indulgent jamming and keeping up its frantic, insistent pace all  throughout its epic sweep.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;That album\'s follow-up was something of a different affair. The longest cut on &lt;em&gt;Over the Edge &lt;/em&gt;is just under four-and-a-half-minutes long ("So Young"), and much of the moody atmosphere of &lt;em&gt;Youth &lt;/em&gt;was  heavily reined in favor of what is inarguably, in hindsight, the best  extended showcase yet put to record for Sage\'s raw-nerve anthems of  youthful alienation. In these songs, these "So Young" characters are  living in "Doom Town", about to be pushed "Over the Edge", "The Lonely  One(s)". There could be no possible confusion as to what this guy was  singing about. The "Youth of America" hadn\'t unified against their  opposition, as Sage had urged them to do with the slogan "it\'s time we  rectify this now" two years earlier, and were being crushed both  emotionally and physically across the nation, one-by-one and in small  groups, by the brutal onslaught of misunderstanding and indifference.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;&lt;em&gt;Over the Edge&lt;/em&gt; represented the absolute pinnacle of Greg Sage\'s songwriting and the  Wipers\' sheer ferocity as a whole, and that it would be the final album  to include original drummer Sam Henry and bassist Dave Koupal is  entirely appropriate. There was nowhere else to go, creatively, after  reaching this albatross of a third record, one that would be referred to  as one of the greatest punk rock albums of all time by more than just  Kurt Cobain. The Gun Club-like "Romeo" even briefly got airplay at a few  adventurous rock radio stations, some years before Cobain meant  anything to the world at large. I don\'t even need to call it the best  record Greg Sage ever played on, with the Wipers or solo, because that  should be quite obvious by now.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/07/wipers-over-edge-1983.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-OMLz9ToctIU/UfI5tgYBKII/AAAAAAAAAP8/6jBL2AJSAJs/s72-c/Wipers_Over_the_edge_cover.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item><item><guid ispermalink="false">tag:blogger.com,1999:blog-5627846526557602490.post-249862041475514182</guid><pubdate>Fri, 26 Jul 2013 08:51:00 +0000</pubdate><atom:updated>2013-07-26T01:51:58.651-07:00</atom:updated><category domain="http://www.blogger.com/atom/ns#">Music Reviews</category><title>The Jesus and Mary Chain "Psychocandy" (1985)</title><description>&lt;div class="separator" style="clear: both; text-align: center;"&gt;&lt;a href="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-AxE7ulMghp8/UfI4o-F_LwI/AAAAAAAAAPs/2hnnRGDThrQ/s1600/k05669zhy8t.jpg" imageanchor="1" style="clear: left; float: left; margin-bottom: 1em; margin-right: 1em;"&gt;&lt;img border="0" src="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-AxE7ulMghp8/UfI4o-F_LwI/AAAAAAAAAPs/2hnnRGDThrQ/s1600/k05669zhy8t.jpg" /&gt;&lt;/a&gt;&lt;/div&gt;Once upon a time, a half-dead gang of apathetic social deviants out  of Glasgow learned to love again. Through some of the most  uncompromising, brutal white noise ever committed to tape.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;For  those not informed well enough of the history of "indie" rock music  prior to 1985, there was once a time when one was either a lovelorn  balladeer or a scuzzy noise merchant, and ne\'er did the two identities  mesh as one. When the Buzzcocks fused punk rock with pop, they tempered  the punk with the pop to the point where the hybrid was commercial  enough to be successful in new wave-era Britain. But Throbbing Gristle  never recorded anything that crossed over into the lowly realms of what  was considered to be "pop" in the late 70\'s, and the obvious  distinctions between noise rock and pop rock were never in doubt of  being any less obvious.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;Enter The Jesus and Mary Chain.  After cutting demos that were much too redolent of the Ramones, another  band who fused hard rock and pop as did the British \'Cocks, for their  liking, the brothers Reid (youngest Jim &amp;amp; eldest William) cross-bred  their love of The Stooges and Velvet Underground with their equal  affection for The Ronettes and Beach Boys. The result was far from  another pop-punk fusion.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;From the &lt;em&gt;unbelievably&lt;/em&gt; blatant rip of the "Be My Baby" drum intro that opens the landmark &lt;em&gt;Psychocandy&lt;/em&gt;,  it\'s clear that a sound unlike any other is ahead. "Just Like Honey"  wasn\'t a particularly representative example of the screeching extremes  heard elsewhere on the classic debut album, but that drum intro is &lt;em&gt;not&lt;/em&gt; a carbon copy of the recorded Ronettes song it\'s copped from. Rather  than sounding like a grandiose, snapping snare, it sounds as though it  is emanating from a huge rock festival being held in an arena at the  very bottom of a canyon that\'s 1000 miles deep. Immediately thereafter,  in comes Doug Hart\'s dirt-simple bass line and a shimmering, yet also  feedback-wracked guitar, followed finally by Jim Reid\'s sighing vocal  and, eventually, an angelic backing chanteuse. The line "Eating up the  scum is the hardest thing that I can do" is followed by the effervescent  title refrain, and that sums up the motif of &lt;em&gt;Psychocandy&lt;/em&gt;.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;One  might be inclined to accuse The Mary Chain of, in the words of George  Clinton, "making love with one stroke" by using their noise-pop trick  for nearly every song on the album, barring the gorgeous "Cut Dead", but  that only serves to make the record a relentless,  can\'t-be-bothered-to-drag-on descent from one Reid snarl/sigh to the  next. None of these songs &lt;em&gt;feel&lt;/em&gt; like they\'re going past the  two-minute mark, even though nearly all of them actually do. "Never  Understand" ought to be held up as an anthem of teenage boredom fully on  par with "1969" and "Now I Wanna Sniff Some Glue", or an anthem of  individualistic pride up there with "Blue Suede Shoes". No matter, it\'s  an excellent track, and arguably the best on the album. "You Trip Me Up"  represents the true realization of the JAMC\'s melody/noise aesthetic,  riding on a lackadaisical melody that\'s almost, just faintly,  reminiscent of that for, of all tracks, "A Summer Song", but wrapped in a  fucked-up, sickened ribbon of amplifier caterwaul. &lt;em&gt;This &lt;/em&gt;was the &lt;em&gt;real&amp;nbsp; &lt;/em&gt;European "new pop" of the 80\'s.&lt;br /&gt;&lt;br /&gt;If these guys were using one stroke on &lt;em&gt;Psychocandy&lt;/em&gt;, it was evident on follow-up &lt;em&gt;Darklands &lt;/em&gt;that  they\'d apparently used that trick to such powerful effect on their  debut that they could stroke it no longer. That album expanded into more  commercial college-radio territory, with far less of &lt;em&gt;Psycho&lt;/em&gt;\'s  gloriously deafening fuzz to go with it. It\'s for that reason that it  remains such a singular, celebrated work on its own, rather than as just  The Mary Chain\'s finest record or the debut album of a great group. The  ears of both My Bloody Valentine and Dinosaur Jr. were keen and open at  the sound of &lt;em&gt;Psychocandy&lt;/em&gt;, and its impact on the independent  music scene on both sides of the Atlantic is absolutely incalculable all  the way up to the day this review is being published.</description><link>http://theworldsfirstinternetbaby.blogspot.com/2013/07/the-jesus-and-mary-chain-psychocandy.html</link><author>noreply@blogger.com (Samuel Fraser Windmill)</author><media:thumbnail height="72" url="http://2.bp.blogspot.com/-AxE7ulMghp8/UfI4o-F_LwI/AAAAAAAAAPs/2hnnRGDThrQ/s72-c/k05669zhy8t.jpg" width="72" xmlns:media="http://search.yahoo.com/mrss/"></media:thumbnail><thr:total>0</thr:total></item></channel></rss>'
